Jerusalem Talmud Peah



Chapter 1



Halakhah 1

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> These are the matters that have no measure<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">These are obligations spelled out in the Torah, so one has to fulfill them; but the Torah did not specify either minimum or maximum obligation. However, there are rabbinic minima and sometimes maxima established for all obligations.</i>: Peah<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">In harvesting a field, one is not permitted to harvest the last corner (פאה); that must be abandoned to be harvested by the poor (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 19:9" href="/Leviticus.19.9"><i>Lev</i>. 19:9</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 23:22" href="/Leviticus.23.22">23:22</a>). There is no minimum mentioned in the Torah; the Talmud will discuss whether one may declare one’s entire field as <i>Peah</i>.</i>, first fruits<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">There is an obligation to bring the first fruits of one’s land to the Temple, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 23:19" href="/Leviticus.23.19"><i>Lev</i>. 23:19</a>, 34:26; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nehemiah 10:36" href="/Nehemiah.10.36"><i>Neh</i>. 10:36</a>. No amount has been specified. This obligation is the subject of tractate <i>Bikkurim</i>.</i>, appearance<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">There is an obligation to appear in the Temple on the three holidays of pilgrimage (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 23:17" href="/Exodus.23.17"><i>Ex</i>. 23:17</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 34:23-24" href="/Exodus.34.23-24">34:23–24</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 16:16" href="/Deuteronomy.16.16"><i>Deut</i>. 16:16</a>). It is forbidden to appear in the Temple emptyhanded (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 23:15" href="/Exodus.23.15"><i>Ex</i>. 23:15</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 34:20" href="/Exodus.34.20">34:20</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 16:16" href="/Deuteronomy.16.16"><i>Deut</i>. 16:16</a>), i. e., without bringing a sacrifice. The Torah does not directly spell out the value of the sacrifice, but the verse in <i>Deuteronomy</i> requires it to be “proportional to the blessing that the Eternal has bestowed on you.” The Talmud will discuss which of the two obligations (appearance or sacrifice) is meant here.</i>, works of kindness<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">Charity has two aspects: one is giving money and valuables to the needy; this has no explicit lower and upper limit, but it does have rabbinic limits in both directions. This, in addition to the laws of <i>Peah</i>, is one of the topics of the tractate. The other aspect is giving one’s time to attend funerals, weddings, visiting the sick and mourners, to work for the public good, and similar deeds. That aspect has no limits, upper or lower, expressed anywhere.</i>, and Torah study<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">It is written of the Torah (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Joshua 1:8" href="/Joshua.1.8"><i>Jos</i>. 1:8</a>): “You should meditate upon it day and night.” Hence, there is no upper limit. The obligation of Torah study can be fulfilled by the recitation of <i>Shema‘</i>, but that recitation is also an independent obligation. Hence, Torah study <i>per se</i> has no lower limit.</i>. These are the matters whose product a person eats in this world and whose capital remains for him<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">As deeds which merit reward in the future life.</i> in the future world: Honoring father and mother, works of kindness, making peace between people; the study of Torah is worth all of these.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> Rebbi Benjamin ben Levi<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">As deeds which merit reward in the future life.</i> said: Rebbi Isaac and Rebbi Immi<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">He is Rebbi Ammi.</i> were sitting and asking: Why did they not state <i>terumah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">The gift to the Cohen from the harvest, cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Berakhot 1:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Berakhot.1.1.1"><i>Berakhot</i>, Chapter 1, Note 3</a>. There are two kinds of <i>terumah</i>. The first one, <i>terumah gedolah</i> “the great <i>terumah</i>” must be given to the Cohen as absolutely first gift after the completion of the harvest. Then a tithe of ten percent, <i>maäser</i>, must be given to the Levite who in turn has to give ten percent, one percent of the original, to the Cohen. The two <i>terumot</i> must be eaten in ritual purity by the priestly families; in contrast the tithe is a civil obligation and is profane food in the hands of the Levite. An infraction of the rules of purity for <i>terumah</i> is a deadly sin. One speaks here about <i>terumah gedolah</i> for the quantity of which the Torah does not give any rules; hence, one grain may be given for an entire silo full of grain. Today, this grain, being ritually impure, has to be burned.</i> with these? Rebbi Immi said, because of the disagreement<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">In Mishnah <i>Terumot</i> 3:5 we find a disagreement between Tannaïm. Rebbi Eliezer is of the opinion that one may not give more than 10% of the crop as <i>terumah gedolah</i>. Rebbi Ismael gives 50% as upper limit, and Rebbis Aqiba and Tarphon permit one to give as much as one likes on condition that some profane food be left over. Hence, one cannot say that <i>terumah gedolah</i> has no upper limit from the Torah, at least not according to Rebbis Eliezer and Ismael.</i>. Rebbi Yose said<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">Rebbi Yose (the fourth generation Amora) explains that even according to Rebbis Aqiba and Tarphon, who are followed in practice, <i>terumah</i> has no place in the Mishnah.</i>, one may declare one’s entire field first fruits but one may not declare one’s entire field <i>terumah</i>. They objected: Look, it mentions <i>peah</i>, but nobody may make his entire field <i>peah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Peah (Lieberman) 1:1" href="/Tosefta_Peah_(Lieberman).1.1">Tosephta 1:1</a>.</i>, but it was stated! Rebbi Yose said: The cutting of the first ear is compared to smoothing<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">In harvesting grain, the last operation is smoothing the heap of grain (so that theft could be easily detected. In modern terms, the equivalent would be storing the grain in a silo.) <i>Terumah</i> is not due before the end of the harvesting process since it is an obligation only for food. The implication is that workers may eat from the kernels before the end of the harvesting process (cutting, threshing, winnowing, and storing); after that one may not eat from it before <i>terumot</i> are separated.</i>. Before he cut the first ear, the field was not obligated for <i>peah</i>. After he cut the first ear, the entire field became obligated for <i>peah</i> and if he then wants to declare his entire field as <i>peah</i> he may do so<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">It is not “the entire field,” only “the entire field that is obligated for <i>peah</i>;” hence, the Tosephta that forbids the entire field will permit the field minus one stalk.</i>. But here, before he smoothed his grain heap, the heap was not obligated for <i>terumah</i>. When he wants to declare his entire heap as <i>terumah</i>, he may not do so since we have stated there (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Challah 1:5:5-8" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Challah.1.5.5-8"><i>Ḥallah</i> 1:9</a>): “He who says: My whole threshing floor shall be <i>terumah</i> or my entire dough shall be <i>ḥallah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ḥallah</i> is the obligatory gift to the Cohen from a (large enough) batch of bread dough, discussed in tractate <i>Ḥallah</i>. It has the status of <i>terumah</i>; hence, today is must be burned.</i> did not say anything unless he reserves part of it<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">Even though the remainder may be a single grain; the difference to <i>peah</i> is in the theoretical basis, not the practical side.</i>.”
Is the first ear obligated for <i>peah</i>? It is impossible to say that it obligated the entire field for <i>peah</i> and itself should be obligated for <i>peah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">As explained in the preceding paragraph.</i>. If he cut the first ear and it was burned, does he have to cut another one a second time<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">If he wants to give his entire field to the poor as <i>peah</i>, must the ear he cut first be in existence at the moment of dedication?</i>? Let us hear it from the following<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">A statement similar to this <i>baraita</i> is in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Peah (Lieberman) 1:9" href="/Tosefta_Peah_(Lieberman).1.9">Tosephta 1:9</a>; the reference is to Mishnah 2:7: “If he cut half the field and sold the other half, the buyer gives <i>peah</i> for everything. If he cut half the field and dedicated the other half, the one who redeems it gives <i>peah</i> for everything.” In both cases, the field is given or dedicated as is, with all obligations on the harvest, since <i>peah</i> must be given at the last corner to be harvested.</i>: “If he cut half the field and sold the other half, or he sold what he had cut, if he cut half the field and dedicated<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">To the Temple, the proceeds to be used for the upkeep of the Temple. While the material is in the hands of the Temple authorities, any private use of it is forbidden.</i> what he had cut, he gives from the leftover for everything.” If it is dedicated is it not as if it was burned<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">Since no benefit whatsoever may be derived from it at this stage.</i>? This says that if he cut the first ear and it was burned, he does not have to cut a second time<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">Because if he dedicated the first half and cut the second half for himself, he must give <i>peah</i> for the entire field from his part. If we replace the dedicated half by the burned ear, we have exactly the case that we are dealing with.</i>.
If he finished the field<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">He harvested the entire field without leaving <i>peah</i> for the poor. The reference is to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Peah (Lieberman) 1:5" href="/Tosefta_Peah_(Lieberman).1.5">Tosephta 1:5</a>: “If he did not give from standing grain, he must give from sheaves. If he did not give from sheaves, he must give from stacks. If he did not give from stacks, he must give from the grain heap. If he did not give from the grain heap before it was smoothed, he must first give <i>terumah</i> and tithes and only then <i>peah</i>.” See also Mishnah 1:6, Halakha 7:2, 4:1; an extended version is in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 94a" href="/Bava_Kamma.94a">Babli <i>Baba qama</i>94a</a>.</i>, you say that <i>peah</i> devolves on the sheaves. Would <i>peah</i> turn back to the first ear<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">That the first cut also may be <i>peah</i> from its sheaf if the entire field was cut.</i>? Rebbi Yose said, let us learn <i>peah</i> of sheaves from <i>peah</i> of standing grain. Since <i>peah</i> from standing grain does not return to the first ear<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">As explained in the preceding paragraph, the first cut is not subject to the laws of <i>peah</i> at all since it enables the laws to be applied.</i>, <i>peah</i> of sheaves likewise should not return to the first ear.
<i>Peah</i> has a minimum measure but no maximum measure. First fruits and appearance have neither minimum nor maximum measures. There are those who state: <i>Peah</i>, first fruits and appearance have no measure, neither minimum nor maximum. What is the difference between them, is it not one in sixty<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah 2 states that the rabbinic minimum for <i>peah</i> is one sixtieth of the field, 1 2/3 percent. In both formulations it is agreed that <i>peah</i> has no minimum from the Torah but it has a minimum by rabbinic usage.</i>? According to him who says that <i>peah</i> has a minimum measure but no maximum measure: What he gave<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">When he harvested but left less than 1 2/3% of the harvest standing on the field. The standing grain certainly has the status of <i>peah</i> and, no longer being the property of the farmer, is not subject to the requirement of <i>terumah</i> and tithes. [The interpretation of R. Simson of Sens is that, while as Biblical commandment there is no lower limit to what he must give, by rabbinic decree any gift of less than 1⅔% is not considered <i>peah</i> and is subject to the laws of tithes even though it is the property of the poor and not of the farmer.]</i>, he gave; if he had second thoughts and added to it, it is subject to tithes until he makes up for it<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">Any amount he adds which together with the standing <i>peah</i> does not add up to 1 2/3% of the yield of the entire field, is a voluntary gift, rather than <i>peah</i>, and has the status of grain given after threshing and smoothing the heap. The farmer is obliged to separate from it <i>terumah</i> and tithes. Only if he reaches the rabbinic threshhold of 1 2/3% [according to R. Simson of Sens, only if he gives all of 1 2/3% at one time] does the rule of <i>peah</i> apply again and the produce may be taken by the poor without further obligation.</i>. According to him who says <i>peah</i>, first fruits and appearance sacrifices have no measure, neither minimum nor maximum: What he gave already freed from the obligation; if he has second thoughts and adds, that is subject to tithes<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">To the Temple, the proceeds to be used for the upkeep of the Temple. While the material is in the hands of the Temple authorities, any private use of it is forbidden.</i>.
Rebbi Berekhiah asked: Why did we not state the dust for the straying<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">The straying wife who was seen in a secret rendez-vous with another man. The jealous husband may bring his wife to the Temple where she has to drink water mixed with some dust from the Temple floor as an ordeal (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 5" href="/Numbers.5"><i>Num</i>. 5</a>). There is no minimum amount of that dust prescribed.</i>? Why did we not state the ashes of the heifer<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">The ashes of the red heifer must be strewn on the water with which people defiled by contact with a dead body could be purified for entrance to the Temple (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 19" href="/Numbers.19"><i>Num</i>. 19</a>). Since these ashes are extremely valuable, using more than a minimum amount is a misdeed.</i>? Why did we not state the spittle of the sister-in-law<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">The childless widow who is not married by her brother-in-law becomes free to marry outside the family only by removing a shoe from the foot of the unwilling brother-in-law and spitting out before him (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 25:9" href="/Deuteronomy.25.9"><i>Deut</i>. 25:9</a>). The spittle falling to the earth must be seen by the court but no minimum is established.</i>? Why did we not state the blood of the bird for the “leper”<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">The bird needed for the ritual cleansing of the “leper” (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 14:1-7" href="/Leviticus.14.1-7"><i>Lev</i>. 14:1–7</a>). The symptoms of the “leper” have no relation to what today is called leprosy.</i>? Our<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">In old French sources, R. Simson of Sens and <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosafot on Chagigah 7a" href="/Tosafot_on_Chagigah.7a">Tosaphot <i>Ḥagigah</i> 7a</a>, this is a statement of the Amora R. Yose mentioned in the previous paragraphs, contemporary of R. Berekhiah.</i> Mishnah deals only with things to which he may add and adding is a worthy deed. As for those, even though he may add to them, doing so is no worthy deed.
“Appearance”<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">Quote from the Mishnah that introduces a new subject.</i>. Our Mishnah<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">R. Simson of Sens already noted that this is a scribal error and that the subsequent discussion shows that it is the value of the obligatory sacrifice which has a fixed minimum. Hence, one should read: “Our Mishnah is about appearance with a sacrifice but appearance in person has no measure.”</i> is about appearance with a sacrifice, but appearance in person has a measure. This agrees with what R. Joḥanan said: “One silver obolus and two silver coins are words of the Torah”<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Chagigah 1:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Chagigah.1.2.1">Mishnah Ḥagigah 1:2</a> fixes the minimum amount to be spent on the two obligatory sacrifices as, respectively, one silver obolus and two silver coins. [The two sacrifices are the holocaust for the altar and a family sacrifice of which only a small part was burned on the altar. The houses of Shammai and Hillel disagree on the distribution of these sums but not on the principle that the first has to be a silver obolus and the second two silver coins.] According to most commentators, the “silver coins” mentioned also are oboli [6 oboli equal one drachma (denar) and four drachmas equal one tetradrachma (סלע)]. In the Roman empire only the emperor could mint silver coins. The Eastern mint was at Tyre; hence, silver coins are also called Tyrian money. Before the inflation of the military anarchy, local coinage was of copper and worth one eighth of the corresponding silver coinage.<br>The following discussion will determine whether these minimal amounts have rabbinic or biblical status.</i>.
Rebbi Yasa stated before Rebbi Joḥanan: The appearance is with anything<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">One may appear in the Temple with one sacrifice for the appearance and one for the family holiday; no minimal expenditure is required. The monetary minimum is purely a rabbinic one.</i>; the sages only said: “One silver obolus, two silver coins.” He said to him: Is there anything like<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">Yerushalmi כן is contracted from כְּעֵין “in the kind of.”</i> that? Rebbi Jonah said, are not all measures determined by the sages<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">There are many biblical precepts that are applied only to some minimal amount and volume. None of these are spelled out in the Torah; they are all rabbinic formulations.</i>? They said: “The volume of an olive from a corpse<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">All rules of defilement by parts of a corpse apply only if a minimum size of the volume of one olive is present. Hence, somebody in a house in which less than that volume of a corpse is present may go to the Temple without the purification procedure of the ashes of the red heifer even though going there in impurity is a strict biblical prohibition.</i>, the volume of an olive from a cadaver<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">To bring a sin offering for having eaten from a cadaver needs this minimum amount; the same minimum applies to the impurity caused by touching or carrying pieces of the cadaver of an otherwise kosher animal.</i>, the volume of a lentil from a dead reptile<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">The minimum volume from a carcass that causes the severe impurity incurred by touching dead reptiles.</i>.” The question here is only what Rebbi Hoshaia stated: (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 23:15" href="/Exodus.23.15"><i>Ex</i>. 23:15</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 34:20" href="/Exodus.34.20">34:20</a>) “They should not be seen before Me empty-handed,” with anything<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">No minimal amount required.</i>; the sages only said: “A silver obolus, two silver coins.” The difficulty is that this<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">Two verses (and an additional one in <i>Deut</i>.) insist that one may not appear empty-handed; this must mean that a negligible amount is counted as nothing and, therefore, forbidden by the Torah.</i> is support for the opinion that [the rule] is based on words of the Torah. He said that only the sages instituted<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">The text in parentheses is the one of the Venice print here; the text in brackets is from the word-by-word parallel in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Chagigah 1:2:2-4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Chagigah.1.2.2-4"><i>Ḥagigah</i> 1:2</a> (fol. 76b). The translation is that of the <i>Ḥagigah</i> text which alone makes sense. It may be assumed that the copyist of the manuscript used for printing had before him a manuscript in which the entire sentence was only indicated by “etc.” and the copyist chose the wrong statement to complete.</i>: “One silver obolus, two silver coins.” Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun said: Rebbi Johanan follows his own opinion since Rebbi Joḥanan said that all measures<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">Including those stated by R. Jonah and all similar ones.</i> are practice taught by Moses at Sinai;
he says that “one obolus and two silver coins are the word of the Torah.” Rebbi Hoshaia follows his own opinion since Rebbi Hoshaia said: He who eats forbidden food in the present time has to note down the quantities<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">Since one is required to bring a sin-offering only if he ate more than the volume of an olive, he is forbidden to bring one if he ate less than that volume. Hence, the determination of that volume is essential. The statement of R. Hoshaia here is attributed to R. Eleazar, student and colleague of R. Johanan, in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Yoma 80a" href="/Yoma.80a">Babli <i>Yoma</i> 80a</a>. There, the interpretation is that not the rule of “volume of an olive” might change but rather its interpretation. A. Y. Greenfield, in his article “The Connection Between the Measures of an Olive and an Egg,” תחומין 14 (5754) 396–411 shows that modern interpretations of the “volume of an olive” vary on a scale of 1 to 30. Hence, once the Temple is rebuilt, an authoritative interpretation is needed even according to R. Joḥanan.</i>; maybe a new court will arise and change the measures for him; then he will know what measure he ate.
They said, Rebbi Joḥanan changed his mind about this. Rebbis Jonah and Yose said, he did not change his mind; additionally, Rebbi La said in the name of Rebbi Ammi: Ḥizqiah and Rebbi Joḥanan disagreed. Ḥizqiah said, a man may split his obligation for two animals<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote">He has to spend an obolus and two silver coins on animals for his pilgrimage but, since this is a rabbinic ordinance, it does not matter if the amount is split to pay for several animals.</i>. Rebbi Joḥanan said, nobody may split his obligation for two animals, but for each one he has to have two silver coins in his hand<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote">Since it is the practice from the days of Moses to spend two silver coins for one sacrifice of the larger kind, this has the status of a biblical command and cannot be abrogated. Similarly, a full obolus must be spend on one animal of the smaller kind.</i>.
Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish in the name of Ḥizqiah: A person adjoins an animal to an animal but no person may adjoin money to money<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote">The parallel is in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Chagigah 8a" href="/Chagigah.8a">Babli <i>Ḥagigah</i>8a</a> (and <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Chagigah 1:2:2-4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Chagigah.1.2.2-4">Yerushalmi <i>Ḥagigah</i> 1:2</a>). In the Babli, it is explained that the obligation to spend an obolus and two silver coins must be satisfied by unencumbered money. Now four years out of seven there is a second tithe on agricultural produce of the Land of Israel. That second tithe is the property of the farmer but it must be consumed in Jerusalem; it also may be redeemed by money and the money taken to Jerusalem and there spent on food to be consumed in Jerusalem. In addition, the tenth newborn calf or lamb in a herd must be taken to Jerusalem and offered as a sacrifice (only some blood is sprinkled on the altar, the rest is to be eaten by the family of the rancher.) So one may assume that many people on their pilgrimage for the holiday have money and animals of the tithe that they want to eat in Jerusalem to absolve themselves at the same time of the obligations of pilgrimage and (second) tithe. According to Ḥizqiah, on the first day of the holiday one may bring small and cheap animals as obligatory sacrifices and then use the money or the animal of tithes for additional <i>ḥagigah</i> offerings but one may not add the money of second tithes to profane money to buy one large animal for any of the two obligations.<br>In the text, the corresponding statement by R. Joḥanan is missing, viz., that one may add money (if only the basic amount is profane) but not animals (since no animal worth less than two pieces of silver is acceptable as <i>ḥagigah</i>). The statement appears in <i>Ḥagigah</i>.</i>. What may be done<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">A rhetorical introduction to the main question, corresponding to: “They asked themselves” in the Babli.</i>? There were before him ten animals, he sacrificed five of them on the first holiday. May the remainder push aside the last holiday<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote">If these animals retain the status of <i>ḥagigah</i>, they may be sacrificed on the last day of Pesaḥ or Sukkot which are also full holidays. If, however, they have only the status of sacrifices in fulfillment of a vow (since any dedication as sacrifice is a vow to bring the animal to the Temple), then they may be slaughtered only during the intermediate days of the holiday or after its conclusion, and they do not push aside holiday prohibitions.</i>? Rebbi Crispus said, Rebbi Joḥanan and Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish have differing opinions. One said, they push; the other one said, they do not push. We do not know who said what. Rebbi Zeïra said, we may explain the words of the rabbis through the words of Rebbi Joḥanan, who<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote">Aramaic דּוּ is a contraction of דְּהוּא.</i> said: A person may adjoin coins to coins but not animals to an animal. He must say, they push<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote">Since each animal was bought with money dedicated for <i>ḥagigah</i>, they all have the status of <i>ḥagigah</i>.</i>. But Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish said, a person adjoins an animal to an animal but no person may adjoin money to money. He must say, they do not push<sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote">Since one may not adjoin money, only the first animal was <i>ḥagigah</i>, the rest are not and cannot be used for holiday sacrifices.</i>. Simeon bar Abba came in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: He adds<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote">Even during the intermediate days of the holiday, when one’s biblical obligation of appearing in the Temple with a sacrifice has already been satisfied, he can add money to the original money and still the animals bought will fall under the category of <i>ḥagigah</i>. But when he declares that it is enough, the next money spent will no longer buy <i>ḥagigah</i> sacrifices. This extension of R. Joḥanan’s statement is not a logical consequence of his earlier one.</i> and pushes the holiday further until he says: I do not intend to add further.
“Works of kindness.<sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote">Quote from the Mishnah, introduction of the next subject.</i>” That means with his person<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">Charity has two aspects: one is giving money and valuables to the needy; this has no explicit lower and upper limit, but it does have rabbinic limits in both directions. This, in addition to the laws of <i>Peah</i>, is one of the topics of the tractate. The other aspect is giving one’s time to attend funerals, weddings, visiting the sick and mourners, to work for the public good, and similar deeds. That aspect has no limits, upper or lower, expressed anywhere.</i>. But with his money it has a measure. This parallels what Rebbi Simeon bar Laqish said in the name of Rebbi Jehudah ben Ḥanina<sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote">An Israeli Amora of the first generation. All his known statements were transmitted by R. Simeon bar Laqish. [In the Babli, the Amora is Rebbi Illaï, i. e., R. La who was mentioned earlier in this Halakha, a student of R. Simeon ben Laqish.]</i>: They voted at Usha<sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote">Usha in lower Galilee was the place of residence of R. Jehudah bar Illaï who, through his good relations with the Roman authorities, obtained permission for the reconstitution of the Synhedrion. This decree must have been one of the first acts of the new Synhedrion; it is explained later that the formal decree was only a formalization or reaffirmation of earlier practice.</i> that a person may give a fifth of his property for good deeds. How far down? Rebbi Gamliel bar Ininia<sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote">A Galilean Amora of the third generation, student of R. Mana I.</i> and Rebbi Abba bar Cahana; one said corresponding to <i>terumah</i> and the <i>terumah</i> of the tithe<sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Terumah gedolah</i> has to be given by estimate, not by measure (<i>Terumot</i> 1:7). A generous farmer will give one part in forty (2.5%), the average person one in fifty (2%), the stingy person one in sixty (1 2/3%) (<i>Terumot</i> 4:3). The <i>terumah</i> of the tithe has to be given exactly as 1%. Hence, the minimal amount of charity one is obliged to give from his gross income varies between 2.67% and 3.5%, with 3% recommended.</i>, the other said (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Proverbs 3:9" href="/Proverbs.3.9"><i>Prov</i>. 3:9</a>): “Honor the Eternal with your property and with the first of all your yield;” corresponding to the first of all your yield<sup class="footnote-marker">63</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Terumah gedolah</i> is called “first of your grain and oil” in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 18:4" href="/Deuteronomy.18.4"><i>Deut</i>. 18:4</a>; in this opinion one is only required to spend for charity the 2% of his gross earnings equivalent to <i>terumah gedolah</i>.</i>.
Rebbi Gamliel bar Ininia asked before Rebbi Mana: Does it mean one fifth every year? In five years he will have lost everything<sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote">But, as Rashi explains in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Ketubot 50a" href="/Ketubot.50a">Babli <i>Ketubot</i> 50a</a>, the entire rule was only enacted to make sure that nobody would bring on himself the need to accept welfare.</i>! He said to him: The first time from the capital, from there on from net gain.
Rav Huna<sup class="footnote-marker">65</sup><i class="footnote">In the parallel in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 9a" href="/Bava_Kamma.9a">Babli (<i>Bava qama</i> 9a</a>), the statements of Rav Huna and Rav Ḥabiba are both reported in the name of Rebbi Zeïra, student of Rav Huna.</i> said, for religious purposes up to a third. What does he mean, for all religious purposes or just for one purpose? They thought that this means, for all religious purposes up to one third<sup class="footnote-marker">66</sup><i class="footnote">For religious articles one may spend up to one third of one’s net worth. In that interpretation, Rav Huna would exempt religious articles from the decree of Usha.</i>. Rebbi Abun said, even for one purpose only. Rav Ḥabiba<sup class="footnote-marker">67</sup><i class="footnote">He must have been a Babylonian Amora of the third generation, a student of Rav Huna. The Rav Ḥabiba mentioned in the Babli belongs to the sixth generation, after the compilation of the Jerusalem Talmud and, therefore, he cannot be identical with the Rav Ḥabiba mentioned here.</i> in the name of the rabbis from there: What means “up to a third?” For its cost! How is that done? A man buys a religious article and sees another one which is more beautiful, until when does one bother him? Up to one third<sup class="footnote-marker">68</sup><i class="footnote">Of the original cost. If one has a religious object, such as an <i>etrog</i> or <i>tefillin</i>, he should not spend for another one more than 133% of the cost of the object he already has. In this interpretation, the statement of Rav Huna has no relation to the decree of Usha.</i>.
Rebbi Ismael stated<sup class="footnote-marker">69</sup><i class="footnote">This is a side remark: why should one spend much money on religious articles? It is a short quote of a long statement in <i>Mekhilta deR. Ismael, Parshat Hashirah</i> 3 (6 authors mentioned), <i>Mekhilta deR. Simeon bar Ioḥai</i> pp. 78–79, shortened in Massekhet <i>Sopherim</i> 3:12, <i>Massekhet Sepher Torah</i>3:10, in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shabbat 133b" href="/Shabbat.133b">Babli sources <i>Sabbath</i> 133b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nazir 2b" href="/Nazir.2b"><i>Nazir</i> 2b</a>, and, very much shortened, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sukkah 11b" href="/Sukkah.11b"><i>Sukkah</i> 11b</a>. In the latter sources, the name of R. Ismael is never mentioned.</i>: (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 15:2" href="/Exodus.15.2"><i>Ex</i>. 15:2</a>) “This is my God and I will glorify Him!” Is it possible for a human to glorify his God? Rather I shall appear beautiful before Him with religious articles. I shall make before Him a beautiful <i>lulab</i>, a beautiful <i>sukkah</i>, a beautiful <i>shofar</i>, beautiful <i>ẓiẓit</i>, beautiful <i>tefillin</i>. Abba Shaul<sup class="footnote-marker">70</sup><i class="footnote">A Tanna of the generation of students of R. Aqiba who collected the traditions of prior generations; his collection is one of the sources of the Mishnah.</i> said, I want to be like Him! Just as He is merciful and gracious, so you also should be merciful and gracious!
It happened that Rebbi Yeshebab<sup class="footnote-marker">71</sup><i class="footnote">A Tanna of the third generation, student of R. Joshua and colleague of R. Aqiba. He was one of the Ten Martyrs and was executed after the war of Bar Kokhba, at age 90, probably because he continued to teach in Judea.</i> went and let his entire property be distributed to the poor. Rabban Gamliel<sup class="footnote-marker">72</sup><i class="footnote">In the parallel <a class="refLink" data-ref="Ketubot 50a" href="/Ketubot.50a">Babli, <i>Ketubot</i> 50a</a>, it is R. Aqiba who objects and prevents him from executing his wishes.</i> sent to him: Did they not say one fifth of one’s property for good deeds? And was not Rabban Gamliel before Usha<sup class="footnote-marker">73</sup><i class="footnote">Rabban Gamliel died before the war of Bar Kokhba but the assembly at Usha was long after that war. In addition, the language of Rabban Gamliel implies that “they”, not “we”, instituted the limitation; hence, it was an old rule (but, as the text here shows, not one that was generally observed.)</i>? Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun, Rebbi Levi: That was the current practice, they forgot it<sup class="footnote-marker">74</sup><i class="footnote">The first two generations after the destruction of the Temple, at the least. The same statement by R. Yose ben R. Abun and R. Levi is quoted in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Ketubot 8:11:2-5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Ketubot.8.11.2-5"><i>Ketubot</i> 8:11</a> (fol. 32c) on another practice.</i>, but the later ones got up and agreed to the opinion of the earlier ones, to teach you that everything the Court<sup class="footnote-marker">75</sup><i class="footnote">The Synhedrion or any court whose authority is accepted by all of Israel.</i> insists on will come to be in the end, just as Moses was told on Sinai; as Rebbi Mana said (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 32:47" href="/Deuteronomy.32.47"><i>Deut</i>. 32:47</a>): “For it is not an empty word, from you,” if it is empty it is from you because you do not exert yourself about it. “Because it is your life,” when is it your life? At the time that you exert yourself!
Rebbi Tanḥuma<sup class="footnote-marker">76</sup><i class="footnote">These homilies are inserted as examples of the previous statement that deep involvement with the words of the Torah leads to such a way of life that even in situations not covered by what one has learned, he will act as it was indicated to Moses on Mount Sinai.</i> in the name of Rav Huna: It does not say (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 38:22" href="/Exodus.38.22"><i>Ex</i>. 38:22</a>) “Bezalel ben Uri ben Ḥur of the tribe of Judah made everything Moses had commanded him,” but “that the Eternal had commanded Moses.” Even matters he did not hear from Moses he did by himself in the way it was said to Moses on Sinai. Rebbi Joḥanan in the name of Rebbi Benaiah<sup class="footnote-marker">77</sup><i class="footnote">A scholar of the generation of transition between Tannaïm and Amoraïm who, in the Babylonian form ר׳ בנאה, is quoted in halakhic <i>baraitot</i>. His homilies are transmitted mostly by R. Joḥanan.</i> (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Joshua 11:15" href="/Joshua.11.15"><i>Jos</i>. 11:15</a>): “As the Eternal had commanded His servant Moses, so Moses commanded Joshua, and so Joshua executed it.” It does not say “he did not omit anything Moses had commanded him,” but “everything the Eternal commanded Moses.” Even matters he did not hear from his teacher, he did by himself in the way it was said to Moses on Sinai. Rebbi Joḥanan in the name of Rebbi Benaiah, Rebbi Ḥuna in the name of Rebbi<sup class="footnote-marker">78</sup><i class="footnote">In <i>Yalqut Shim‘oni Prophets</i>#588, the reading is “R. Jehudah” instead of Rebbi (Jehudah the Prince.)</i>: (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Malachi 2:6" href="/Malachi.2.6"><i>Mal</i>. 2:6</a>) “True teaching was in his mouth<sup class="footnote-marker">79</sup><i class="footnote">While the person described is called “Levi” by the prophet, the traditional interpretation is that the reference is to Aaron who learned Torah from Moses.</i>,” these<sup class="footnote-marker">80</sup><i class="footnote">It seems that אפילוּ here and in the next clause is a scribal error by dittography from the earlier homilies and that one should read אלוּ. This is the reading of <i>Yalqut Shim‘oni Prophets</i>#588. In the next clause this reading is also supported by the Rome manuscript of the Yerushalmi.</i> are the things he heard from his teacher, “injustice was not found on his lips,” even in matters he did not hear from his teacher.”
And the rabbis say (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Proverbs 3:26" href="/Proverbs.3.26"><i>Prov</i>. 3:26</a>): “For the Eternal will stand by your confidence and prevent your foot from being caught.” Even in matters in which you are stupid, He will prevent your foot from being caught. Rebbi Dosa said, in religious decisions<sup class="footnote-marker">81</sup><i class="footnote">The verse is one of a group praising the student of Torah. The explanation is based on identifying כסל “confidence” with כסל “stupidity”. The different opinions define the object in which one’s foot might otherwise be caught. R. Dosa’s opinion continues the statements of the earlier homilies that the true student of Torah will render the correct decision if confronted with a religious question for which he knows no precedent.</i>. The rabbis say, in sin. Rebbi Levi said, from damaging spirits. Rebbi Abba said, if you gave charity from your wallet<sup class="footnote-marker">82</sup><i class="footnote">Replacing כסל “confidence, hope” by כיס “wallet.” This opinion is mentioned last because it leads back to the main theme, the importance of charity and charitable works.</i>, the Holy One, praise to Him, will guard you from taxes<sup class="footnote-marker">83</sup><i class="footnote">An Aramaic (Syriac) word, פסא, “piece, portion”, here used in the sense of the amount apportioned by the local council among the different householders to pay the taxes imposed <i>en bloc</i> on the community by the government.</i>, penalties<sup class="footnote-marker">84</sup><i class="footnote">Greek ζημία.</i>, head taxes, and contributions<sup class="footnote-marker">85</sup><i class="footnote">Latin <i>annona</i>, obligations to deliver provisions, usually for the army.</i>.
King Monobaz<sup class="footnote-marker">86</sup><i class="footnote">A king of Adiabene, in modern Kurdistan, who converted to Judaism together with his mother Helena during the last century of existence of the Second Temple. In the parallel sources, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Peah (Lieberman) 4:18" href="/Tosefta_Peah_(Lieberman).4.18">Tosephta <i>Peah</i> 4:18</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 11a" href="/Bava_Batra.11a">Babli <i>Baba batra</i> 11a</a>, it is spelled out that he spent his treasures on emergency relief during a famine. Hence, the story here does not contradict the rule, declared as much older than Usha, that one should not give away more than one fifth of one’s property. It is rather an endorsement of deficit spending by the government during times of emergency.</i> distributed all his property to the poor. His relatives sent to him: Your forefathers added to what they had and to what their forefathers had, and you wasted yours and that of your forefathers. He said to them, that is correct! My forefathers locked it up in the earth, I locked it up in Heaven, as it is said (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Psalms 85:12" href="/Psalms.85.12"><i>Ps</i>. 85:12</a>): “Truth will sprout from the earth, but charity<sup class="footnote-marker">87</sup><i class="footnote">In the entire paragraph, צדק “justice” is identified with צדקה “charity”, and צדיק “the just” is taken to mean “giver of charity.”</i> will gaze from Heaven.” My fathers locked up treasures that bring no interest, but I locked up treasures that bring interest as it is said (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Isaiah 3:10" href="/Isaiah.3.10"><i>Is</i>. 3:10</a>): “Say to the giver of charity that he did a good deed, that they will eat the fruits of their intentions.” My forefathers collected in a place over which a (human) hand rules, but I collected in a place over which no hand rules, as it is said (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Psalms 97:2" href="/Psalms.97.2"><i>Ps</i>. 97:2</a>): “Charity and truth are the foundations of His throne.” My forefathers collected money but I collected souls, as it is said (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Proverbs 11:30" href="/Proverbs.11.30"><i>Prov</i>. 11:30</a>): “He who acquires souls is wise<sup class="footnote-marker">88</sup><i class="footnote">The entire verse reads: “The fruit of the giver of charity is a tree of life and he who buys souls is wise.”</i>.” My forefathers collected for others but I collected for myself, as it is said (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 24:13" href="/Deuteronomy.24.13"><i>Deut</i>. 24:13</a>): “Charity will be your property<sup class="footnote-marker">89</sup><i class="footnote">The plain sense of the clause is: It will be considered a good deed by you (before the Eternal, your God.)</i>.” My forefathers collected in this world but I collected for the future world, as it is said (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Proverbs 10:2" href="/Proverbs.10.2"><i>Prov</i>. 10:2</a>): “But charity will save from death.” And does he not die<sup class="footnote-marker">90</sup><i class="footnote">The last three sentences are not in the (Babylonian) parallels, where the argument is based on <a class="refLink" data-ref="Isaiah 58:8" href="/Isaiah.58.8"><i>Is.</i> 58:8</a> (speaking about him who breaks his bread with the poor): “Your charity will walk before you, the glory of the Eternal will gather you in.” The Aramaic sentence ולא מית should not be corrected into Hebrew in order to remain in style with the rest of the paragraph.</i>? But that he should not die an eternal death in the future world.
Charity and works of kindness<sup class="footnote-marker">91</sup><i class="footnote">This paragraph also appears in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sukkah 39b" href="/Sukkah.39b">Babli <i>Sukkah </i>39b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Peah (Lieberman) 4:19" href="/Tosefta_Peah_(Lieberman).4.19">Tosephta <i>Peah </i>4:19</a>. In both sources the formulation is such that the rhetorical question of the next paragraph is answered before it may be asked.</i> are as important as all commandments of the Torah. Charity applies to the living, works of kindness apply to the living and the dead. Charity applies to the poor, works of kindness apply to poor and rich. Charity is through a person's money, works of kindness through his money and his person. Rebbi Johanan bar Maria<sup class="footnote-marker">92</sup><i class="footnote">A Galilean Amora of the fourth generation.</i> in the name of Rebbi Johanan: We do not know what is more beloved, charity or works of kindness. Since it says (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Psalms 103:17" href="/Psalms.103.17">Ps. 103:17</a>): "The kindness of the Eternal is from eternity to eternity on those who fear Him, and His charity towards men," that means that works of kindness are more beloved than charity.
And Torah study<sup class="footnote-marker">93</sup><i class="footnote">Quote from the Mishnah.</i>. They asked Rebbi Joshua<sup class="footnote-marker">94</sup><i class="footnote">This also appears in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 9:9:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.9.9.2">Yerushalmi <i>Soṭa</i> 9:9</a>, the first sentence also in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Avodah Zarah 1:3" href="/Tosefta_Avodah_Zarah.1.3">Tosephta <i>Avodah zarah</i> 1:20</a>. In <a class="refLink" data-ref="Menachot 59b" href="/Menachot.59b">Babli <i>Menaḥot</i> 59b</a>, not only are different persons involved but there the question is about Greek wisdom, not the Greek language.</i>: May a person teach Greek to his son? He said to them, he may teach him at an hour that is neither day nor night as it is written (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Joshua 1:8" href="/Joshua.1.8"><i>Jos</i>. 1:8</a>): “You shall meditate about it day and night.” If it is so, a man would be forbidden to teach a profession to his son since it is written: “You shall meditate about it day and night.” But Rebbi Ismael stated (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 30:19" href="/Deuteronomy.30.19"><i>Deut</i>. 30:19</a>): “Choose life!” That refers to a profession<sup class="footnote-marker">95</sup><i class="footnote">Since only a profession will sustain a person; otherwise he will be forced to become a robber and commit capital crimes.</i>. Rebbi Abba, son of Rebbi Ḥiyya bar Abba, Rebbi Ḥiyya in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: Because of informants<sup class="footnote-marker">96</sup><i class="footnote">Greek-speaking Jews become intimate with Greek-speaking officials and will be hired as government officials. In the Babli, the verse in <i>Joshua</i> is explained not as a commandment but as a blessing, that Joshua will be able to meditate about Torah day and night.</i>. Rebbi Abbahu in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: A person may teach Greek to his daughter since it is an ornament for her. Simeon bar Abba heard that and said: Because he wants to teach his daughters, he attaches it to Rebbi Joḥanan. It should come over me<sup class="footnote-marker">97</sup><i class="footnote">A curse: if I now say that R. Joḥanan never said this but I really heard it from him and forgot. In the parallel <i>Sabbat</i> 6:1 (fol. 7c), Rebbi Abbahu retorts that a curse should come over him if he said that and did not hear it from Rebbi Joḥanan. [It seems that R. Joḥanan made that statement only in Caesarea, never in Tiberias.]</i> if I ever heard it from Rebbi Joḥanan.
“Honoring father and mother.<sup class="footnote-marker">98</sup><i class="footnote">Quote from the Mishnah to start a new section.</i>” Rebbi Abbahu in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: They asked Rebbi Eliezer, how far does honoring father and mother go? He said to them, you are asking me? Go and ask Dama ben Netinah. Dama ben Netinah was the head of the city council<sup class="footnote-marker">99</sup><i class="footnote">A mixed expression, ראש πατὴρ βουλής, “head, father of the Senate.” Cf. the Hebrew אב בית דין “father of the court,” used for the president of Jewish courts of law, and the Latin <i>pater senatus</i>. [The two stories here also appear in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 1:7:2-20" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.1.7.2-20">Yerushalmi <i>Qiddushin</i> 1:7</a> in slightly improved spelling (here the title is an unintelligible פטרכולי) and, in a shortened and less natural version, in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Kiddushin 31" href="/Kiddushin.31">Babli <i>Qiddushin</i> 31a/b</a>.]</i>. Once his mother was slapping him in front of the entire council and a slipper<sup class="footnote-marker">100</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Arukh</i> explains the word as Italian <i>pappucce</i> “houseshoes”. Rashi explains in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Yevamot 102b" href="/Yevamot.102b"><i>Yebamot</i> 102b</a>: “Thin sandals one binds under thick shoes to protect them from water; they are called <i>underschuoch</i> in (Middle High) German.” Also cf. Latin <i>chorda</i>, Greek χορδή “string, rope” (E. G.), see below, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Peah 1:1:23" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Peah.1.1.23">Note 105</a>. [In a papyrus from Oxyrhynchus from Mishnaic times, κορδίκιον is mentioned as “an article of furniture”.]</i> fell from her hand, and he handed it back to her so that she should feel no inconvenience. Rebbi Ḥizqiah said: He was a Gentile from Ascalon and the head of the city council. He never sat on the stone on which his father used to sit and when his father died, he worshipped the stone. Once the jaspis of Benjamin was lost<sup class="footnote-marker">101</sup><i class="footnote">From the breast-plate of the High priest.</i>. They<sup class="footnote-marker">102</sup><i class="footnote">The Temple authorities.</i> inquired, who would have one of similar quality? They were informed that Dama ben Netinah did. They went to him and and agreed on 100 denars<sup class="footnote-marker">103</sup><i class="footnote">In early Roman imperial money, when the coins were still honest, this amounted to 22 oz. of gold, an enormous sum since the buying power of gold was much larger than it is today. The amount mentioned in the Babli by authors living during the early military anarchy is 60’000 or 80’000 denar. According to the monetary reform of Diocletian, one Roman pound (12 ounces), 500 fine, would be 48’000 denar. At the end of the inflationary period, 100 early denar would therefore be the equivalent of 880’000 denar. Hence, the sums mentioned in the Babli are rather on the low side.</i>. He went to the upper floor to bring it and found his father sleeping. Some say that the key to the chest was in his father’s fingers; some say his father’s foot was resting on the chest. He descended to them and told them: I could not bring it to you. They thought, maybe he wants more money, and raised the price to two hundred, then to a thousand. When his father woke up from his sleep, he went up and brought it to them. They wanted to give him according to the amount mentioned last, but he refused. He said: Do I sell my father’s honor for money? I will not have any advantage from honoring my father. What reward did the Holy One, praise to Him, give him? Rebbi Yose bar Abun said: The following night, his cow gave birth to a red heifer and Israel gave him its weight in gold and took it. Rebbi Sabbatai said (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Job 37:23" href="/Job.37.23"><i>Job</i> 37:23</a>): “Justice and much charity He will not suppress.” The Holy One, praise to Him, will not wait to give the reward for good deeds to the Gentiles<sup class="footnote-marker">104</sup><i class="footnote">Since they do not believe in the Future World and, therefore, do not have part in it and must be rewarded in this word.</i>.
Rebbi Tarphon’s mother went to promenade in her backyard on the Sabbath<sup class="footnote-marker">105</sup><i class="footnote">“And her shoe-string (or, her slippers) broke.” This is missing here but appears in the parallel <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 1:7:2-20" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.1.7.2-20"><i>Qiddushin</i>1:7</a> (fol. 61b): נפסק קורדייקין שלה. [A different story is in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Kiddushin 31b" href="/Kiddushin.31b">Babli <i>Qiddushin</i>31b</a>.] Since we cannot assume that an authority of the stature of R. Tarphon had no <i>eruv</i> made if one was needed for the backyard, one might ask why he could not bring a new pair of slippers to his mother. But if the word means “shoe-string”, the reason would be clear since repairing the slippers on the Sabbath by putting in a new string would be a desecration of the Sabbath.</i>. Rebbi Tarphon went and put his two hands under her feet and she walked on them until she got to her couch. Once he fell ill and the sages came to visit him. She told them: Pray for my son Tarphon because he honors me too much. They asked her<sup class="footnote-marker">106</sup><i class="footnote">Although she spoke to them in Hebrew, they answered in Aramaic; it seems that they did not expect a woman to be educated enough to converse in Hebrew. Her explanation must have been in Hebrew since their final answer was again in Hebrew.</i>, what did he do for you? She told them what had happened. They said to her, even if he did this a million times, he did not yet reach even half of the honor that the Torah requires.
Rebbi Ismael’s mother came and complained to our teachers about him. She said to them: Scold my son Ismael because he does not honor me! At that, the faces of our teachers became saffron colored<sup class="footnote-marker">107</sup><i class="footnote">כַּרְכּוֹם “saffron,” also used as yellow dye.</i>. They said, is it possible that Rebbi Ismael does not honor his parents? They said to her, what did he do to you? She said, when he<sup class="footnote-marker">108</sup><i class="footnote">כְּדוּ = כְּשֶׁהוּא.</i> comes from the house of assembly, I want to wash his feet and drink from it<sup class="footnote-marker">109</sup><i class="footnote">The water used for washing. It is clear that R. Ismael’s mother was uneducated; she spoke Aramaic and did not know the rules of good taste. (“Water” is always a plural in Hebrew and Aramaic.)</i>, and he refuses. They said to him, since it is her wish it is her honor.
Rebbi Mana said: The millers are correct when they say that each and every person has in his chest that which is appropriate for him<sup class="footnote-marker">111</sup><i class="footnote">In moral matters, there are no universally valid standards, only individual circumstances.</i>. Rebbi Tarphon’s mother said so and they answered her appropriately, Rebbi Ismael’s mother said so and they answered her appropriately. Rebbi Zeïra was sorry and said, if only I had father or mother that I could honor them and inherit paradise<sup class="footnote-marker">112</sup><i class="footnote">In the second version of the Ten Commandments (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 5:16" href="/Deuteronomy.5.16"><i>Deut</i>. 5:16</a>), honoring father and mother is explicitly rewarded with the promise that “one will be well off” in the future world. Rebbi Zeïra’s case is identical with R. Abun’s.</i>. When he heard these two instructions, he said: Praise to the All-Merciful that I have neither father nor mother. I could not have done as Rebbi Tarphon did and I could not accept what Rebbi Ismael accepted. Rebbi Abun said, I am free from honoring father and mother. They said, his father died when his mother was pregnant and she died in giving birth.
Some person might serve his father fattened meat and inherit hell. Some person might bind his father to the grindstone and inherit paradise. How does one serve his father fattened meat and inherit hell? A person used to serve his father fattened chickens. One day, his father said to him: My son, from where do you get the money for these? He said to him: Old man, old man, eat and shut up just as dogs eat and are silent. It turns out that he serves his father fattened meat and inherits hell. How does one bind his father to the grindstone and inherits paradise? A person was a miller at a grindstone; there came a requisition for millers. He said: Father, go and grind in my stead. If it should come to pass that someone is degraded, it is better that it should happen to me rather than to you. If it should come to pass that someone is beaten, it is better that it should happen to me rather than to you. It turns out that he binds his father to the grindstone and inherits paradise.
It<sup class="footnote-marker">114</sup><i class="footnote">The exact parallel is in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sifra, Braita d'Rabbi Yishmael 2-7" href="/Sifra,_Braita_d'Rabbi_Yishmael.2-7"><i>Sifra Qedoshim</i> 2–7</a>; an almost identical quote is in Babli <i>Qidddushin</i> 30b.</i> is said (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 19:3" href="/Leviticus.19.3"><i>Lev.</i> 19:3</a>): “Everybody must fear his mother and his father,” and it is said (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 6:13" href="/Deuteronomy.6.13"><i>Deut</i>. 6:13</a>): “You must fear the Eternal, your God, and serve Him.” This brackets the fear of father and mother with the fear of Heaven. It is said (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 20:12" href="/Exodus.20.12"><i>Ex</i>. 20:12</a>): “Honor your father and your mother,” and it is said (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Proverbs 3:9" href="/Proverbs.3.9"><i>Prov.</i> 3:9</a>): “Honor the Eternal with your property.” This brackets the honor of father and mother with the honor of the Omnipresent. Is is said (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 21:17" href="/Exodus.21.17"><i>Ex.</i> 21:17</a>): “He who curses his father or his mother shall be put to death,” and it is said (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 24:19" href="/Leviticus.24.19"><i>Lev.</i> 24:19</a>): “Everybody who curses his God must bear his sin.” This brackets cursing father and mother with cursing the Omnipresent. It is impossible to speak about hitting relative to the Deity<sup class="footnote-marker">115</sup><i class="footnote">It is written (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 21:15" href="/Exodus.21.15"><i>Ex.</i> 21:15</a>): “He who hits his father or mother must be put to death.” This law cannot have a parallel in relation to the Deity.</i>. All this is logical since all three of them are partners in his creation<sup class="footnote-marker">116</sup><i class="footnote">Since father and mother contribute the animal part of his being and God gives soul, spirit, and life (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Niddah 31a" href="/Niddah.31a">Babli <i>Niddah</i> 31a</a>).</i>.
What is fear<sup class="footnote-marker">117</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sifra Qedoshim</i> 10, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Kiddushin 61b" href="/Kiddushin.61b">Babli <i>Qiddushin</i> 61b</a>. What is classified here as “honor” is described in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Kiddushin (Lieberman) 1:11" href="/Tosefta_Kiddushin_(Lieberman).1.11">Tosephta <i>Qiddushin</i> 1:11</a> as “obligation of the son towards his parents.”</i>? He may not sit in his place and may not speak in his stead<sup class="footnote-marker">118</sup><i class="footnote">If the father is present.</i> nor contradict his words. What is honor? He feeds and gives him to drink, clothes him and puts on his shoes, leads him out and in<sup class="footnote-marker">119</sup><i class="footnote">If the parent can no longer do these things.</i>. From whose money? Huna bar Ḥiyya<sup class="footnote-marker">120</sup><i class="footnote">A Babylonian Amora of the second/third generation, student of Rav Huna and Rav Jehudah, and son-in-law of Rav Jeremiah bar Abba. In the Babli, his statement is attributed to Rav Nathan bar Oshaia, a minor authority contemporary with Rav Huna bar Ḥiyya, and the statement that “some want to say” is attributed to Rav Jehudah. Even though Rav Jehudah is the much more important authority, his opinion is rejected in the Babli, in contrast to the Yerushalmi two paragraphs down.</i> said, from the old man’s. Some want to say, from his own. Did not Rebbi Abbahu say in the name of Rebbi Yose bar Ḥanina: From where do we know that even if his father tells him to throw the wallet into the sea that he should obey him<sup class="footnote-marker">121</sup><i class="footnote">In the Babli, this is one of the examples of Rebbi Eliezer about the seriousness of the obligation of honoring father and mother. The question is, why should the son be obliged to throw his own wallet (full of money) into the sea when he has to feed and clothe his parents from his own money? Hence, R. Abbahu must agree that the old parents must be cared for from their own money.</i>? That is<sup class="footnote-marker">122</sup><i class="footnote">The proof from the statement of R. Abbahu is not conclusive. There are two conditions to be fulfilled in order that the son should follow his father’s command: First, that the implications for the wealth of either parent or child are negligible and, second, that the act will have a positive influence on the mental health of the disturbed parent [otherwise, as the Babli points out explicitly, the son would infringe on the Biblical prohibition of wanton destruction and, as the verse <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 19:3" href="/Leviticus.19.3"><i>Lev.</i> 19:3</a> makes clear, one is not permitted to follow one’s parents orders to commit a sin.]</i>, if he has another one and if it helps to quiet his father’s spirit.
Both men and women<sup class="footnote-marker">123</sup><i class="footnote">Even though the verse <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 19:3" href="/Leviticus.19.3"><i>Lev.</i> 19:3</a> is formulated: “A man must fear his mother and his father,” a woman is also obligated. It is then explained why the obligation of the man only is unconditional and spelled out.</i>. Only the man has the power in his hand, but the woman does not have the power in her own hand because others have disposition over her<sup class="footnote-marker">124</sup><i class="footnote">She can only care for her parents with the consent of her husband who has the administration of her property.</i>. If she is widowed or divorced, the power is in her own hand.
The words of Rebbi Ḥiyya bar Abba disagree<sup class="footnote-marker">125</sup><i class="footnote">The text of the first sentence is that of the parallel <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 1:7:2-20" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.1.7.2-20"><i>Qiddušin</i> 1:7</a>, fol. 61b. R. Ḥiyya bar Abba disagrees with the words of Huna bar Ḥiyya that all the children have to give to their parents is their time and attention but not their money.</i>, for Rebbi Ḥiyya bar Abba said that Rebbi Yudan, son of Rebbi Simeon bar Ioḥai’s daughter<sup class="footnote-marker">126</sup><i class="footnote">He is not mentioned anywhere else in the Talmud.</i>, stated that Rebbi Simeon bar Ioḥai stated: Honoring father and mother is great because the Holy One, praise to Him, preferred it over His own honor. It is said (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 20:12" href="/Exodus.20.12"><i>Ex.</i> 20:12</a>): “Honor your father and your mother,” and it is said (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Proverbs 3:9" href="/Proverbs.3.9"><i>Prov.</i> 3:9</a>): “Honor the Eternal with your property.” When do you start to honor Him? When He was gracious to you! One gives gleanings, forgotten sheaves, and <i>peah</i>, one gives <i>terumah</i>, the First and Second Tithes<sup class="footnote-marker">127</sup><i class="footnote">The Second Tithe is not for the poor but for one’s own family, to be eaten in Jerusalem. It is supposed that at any family festival one also lets the poor eat from the festive meal. The Second Tithe is due in years 1,2,4,5 of the <i>shemiṭṭah</i> cycle, the tithe for the poor in years 3,6.</i> and the tithe for the poor, <i>ḥallah</i>, one makes a <i>sukkah</i>,<i>lulav</i>, and <i>shofar</i>,<i>tefillin</i> and <i>ẓiẓit</i>, one feeds the poor and hungry and gives to drink to the thirsty. If you have the wherewhithal, you are obliged for all of these; if you have nothing, you are not obliged even for one of them. But when it comes to honoring father and mother, whether you own property or you do not own property, you must honor father and mother, even if you are a beggar at people’s doors<sup class="footnote-marker">128</sup><i class="footnote">Who takes charity instead of giving it</i>.
Rebbi Aḥa in the name of Rebbi Abba bar Cahana: It is written (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Proverbs 5:6" href="/Proverbs.5.6"><i>Prov.</i>5:6</a>): “She<sup class="footnote-marker">129</sup><i class="footnote">The strange woman, symbolizing non-Jewish attitudes. The rewards of good deeds have been removed to the World to Come.</i> does not smooth the way of life, her tracks deviate and you will not notice it.” The Holy One, praise to Him, moved the rewards of those who fulfill the commandments (to the future world) so that they should act in faith. Rebbi Aḥa in the name of Rebbi Isaac: It is written (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Proverbs 4:23" href="/Proverbs.4.23"><i>Prov.</i> 4:23</a>): “Observe carefully all which must be kept, for from it comes life,” observe carefully all you were told in the Torah, for you do not know from which of them will life come to you. Rebbi Abba bar Cahana said, the verse equals the easiest commandment with the most difficult one. The easiest commandment is sending away the mother<sup class="footnote-marker">130</sup><i class="footnote">If one happens to find a bird’s nest, one may take the eggs or the chicks but not the mother (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 22:6-7" href="/Deuteronomy.22.6-7"><i>Deut.</i> 22:6–7</a>).</i>. The most difficult one is honoring father and mother. For both of them it is written: “Your days will be lengthened.” Rebbi Abun said, if for something that is repayment of debt<sup class="footnote-marker">131</sup><i class="footnote">Honoring father and mother for raising the child.</i> it is written (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 5:16" href="/Deuteronomy.5.16"><i>Deut.</i> 5:16</a>): “That you shall be well and that your days shall be lengthened,” so much more something that involves monetary loss and personal danger<sup class="footnote-marker">132</sup><i class="footnote">Sending away the mother is a potential loss; climbing a ladder to get the eggs or the chicks is a potential danger. [Rebbi Abun was in personal danger for refusing to worship the Roman emperor as a god; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Berakhot 5:1:2-22" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Berakhot.5.1.2-22">Yerushalmi <i>Berakhot</i> 5:1</a>).]</i>. Rebbi Levi said, this one is greater; repaying a debt is greater than fulfilling an obligation that does not involve repaying a debt. Rebbi Simeon ben Ioḥai stated: Just as their rewards are the same, so their punishments are identical. (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Proverbs 30:17" href="/Proverbs.30.17"><i>Prov.</i>30:17</a>) “The eye that scoffs at the father and despises to obey the mother,” the eye that scoffs about honoring father and mother and despises the commandment not to take the mother with the chicks<sup class="footnote-marker">133</sup><i class="footnote">In the verse it is written ליקהת “referring to obeying;” in the Talmud text, this is shortened to לקהת and, apparently under the influence of the dialect of lower Galilee, which makes no difference between ה and ת, reads לקחת “to take.” It must be noted that the writing of the letter <i>hē</i> as ה is a new development; in old texts its left leg touches the top but is displaced towards the right; it looks very much like ח.</i>, “the river ravens should pick it out,” the cruel raven<sup class="footnote-marker">134</sup><i class="footnote">Which is reputed not to feed its young (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Psalms 147:9" href="/Psalms.147.9"><i>Ps.</i> 147:9</a>, cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Ketubot 49b" href="/Ketubot.49b">Babli <i>Ketubot</i> 49b</a>).</i> should come, pick it out, and not have any enjoyment from it, “the sons of the eagle should eat it,” the merciful eagle should come and enjoy it.
Rebbi Jonathan and Rebbi Yannai were sitting together<sup class="footnote-marker">135</sup><i class="footnote">In <a class="refLink" data-ref="Ketubot 49b-50a" href="/Ketubot.49b-50a">Babli <i>Ketubot</i> 49b/50a</a>, a similar story is told about Rebbi Jonathan and Rebbi Ḥanina, but there one deals with a special situation that does not imply anything for our case here.</i>. There came a man and kissed Rebbi Jonathan’s foot. Rebbi Yannai said to him: What kindness did he pay you back for from former days? He said to him: He once came and complained to me about his son that he should support him and I told him, go, assemble a congregation and humiliate him<sup class="footnote-marker">136</sup><i class="footnote">Apply moral force to get him to support his penniless father. According to a Gaonic responsum edited by S. Asaf, the meaning of the sentence is: Prevent the congregation from praying until they deal with his complaint (Addenda and Corrigenda by Guggenheimer).</i>. He said to him, why did you not force him<sup class="footnote-marker">137</sup><i class="footnote">By a decree of the Rabbinical court, following R. Simeon bar Ioḥai.</i>? He said to him, does one force him? He said to him, you still have questions about that? Rebbi Jonathan changed his opinion and fixed it as a tradition in his own name. The result is: Rebbi Jacob bar Aḥa, Rebbi Samuel bar Naḥman in the name of Rebbi Jonathan, one forces the son to feed his father. Rebbi Yose bar Abun said, if only all traditions were so clear to me as this one: One forces the son to feed his father.
“Works of kindness<sup class="footnote-marker">138</sup><i class="footnote">Quote from the Mishnah, to introduce the next theme.</i>.” As it is written (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Proverbs 21:21" href="/Proverbs.21.21"><i>Prov.</i> 21:21</a>): “He who pursues charitable donations and works of kindness will find life, justification, and honor.” Honor in this world and life in the world to come. Rebbi Samuel bar Rav Isaac<sup class="footnote-marker">139</sup><i class="footnote">The story is told at length in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bereishit Rabbah 59" href="/Bereishit_Rabbah.59"><i>Bereshit rabba</i> 59</a>, and in a shortened version, which shows R. Zeïra in a better light, in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Ketubot 17a" href="/Ketubot.17a">Babli <i>Ketubot</i> 17a</a>. The essence of the story is that personal effort, in particular for weddings and burials, will not go unrewarded.</i> used to take branches and danced while singing<sup class="footnote-marker">140</sup><i class="footnote">From a root that appears inArabic as קלס “to dance while singing, from the standards of dignity upheld singing harmoniously.” He deviated by rabbis.</i> before brides. Rebbi Zeïra saw him and hid himself before him; he said, look at that old man, how does he make us ashamed! But when he died there were three hours of thunder and lightning in the world. An echo came from Heaven and said: Rebbi Samuel bar Rav Isaac, the worker of charity, died, come out to do charity for him! Fire came down from Heaven that was formed like a fiery branch and separated between the bier and the people; the creatures were saying: Look, his branch stood up for the old man!
“Making peace between people.<sup class="footnote-marker">138</sup><i class="footnote">Quote from the Mishnah, to introduce the next theme.</i>” It is written (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Psalms 34:15" href="/Psalms.34.15"><i>Ps.</i> 34:15</a>): “Move away from evil and do good, look for peace and pursue it.” Look for peace at your place, pursue it at another place. Rebbi Tavyome said: It says pursuing, pursuing<sup class="footnote-marker">141</sup><i class="footnote">Both the verse from Psalms here and the verse from Proverbs in the preceding paragraph use the root רדף “to pursue.” Hence, one may assume that the meaning is the same in both cases.</i>. Just as it was said there, honor in this world and life in the world to come.
“And Torah study.<sup class="footnote-marker">138</sup><i class="footnote">Quote from the Mishnah, to introduce the next theme.</i>” Rebbi Berekhiah and Rebbi Ḥiyya from Kefar Teḥumin<sup class="footnote-marker">142</sup><i class="footnote">An Amora of the third generation, possibly from Capernaum. The statement here is the only one preserved from him (unless he is identical with R. Ḥama from Kefar Teḥumin, mentioned sometimes in Midrashim.)</i>. One said that the entire world is not worth even one saying of the Torah. The other said that even all commandments of the Torah are not worth even one saying of the Torah<sup class="footnote-marker">143</sup><i class="footnote">He asserts that study is more important than doing, an attitude that is challenged below. [The contrary position is quoted without opposition in the Babli.]</i>. Rebbi Tanḥuma and Rebbi Yose ben Zimra, one said like the first and one said like the second. Rebbi Abba, father of Rebbi Abba bar Mari<sup class="footnote-marker">144</sup><i class="footnote">At first glance, it seems impossible that Rebbi Abba should be the father of Rebbi Abba bar Mari. Now it is possible that one should read: “Rebbi Abba, father of Rebbi Abba Mari.” It is also possible that Rebbi Abba bar Mari is the posthumous son of Rebbi Abba but did not want to be called Abba bar Abba to indicate by his name the unhappy circumstance of his birth; Mari would not be a personal name but a word meaning “my lord (father).”</i>, in the name of Rebbi Aḥa: One verse says (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Proverbs 8:11" href="/Proverbs.8.11"><i>Prov.</i> 8:11</a>) “all desirables do not equal it;” the other verse says (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Proverbs 3:15" href="/Proverbs.3.15"><i>Prov.</i> 3:15</a>) “your desirables do not equal it.” “Desirables” are gems and pearls, “your desirables” are the words of the Torah, for it is written (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jeremiah 9:23" href="/Jeremiah.9.23"><i>Jer.</i> 9:23</a>): “For these I desire - saying of the Eternal.<sup class="footnote-marker">145</sup><i class="footnote">The verse ends: “understanding and knowing Me,” i. e., studying Torah. Even the words of the Torah are not equal to the commandments of the Torah and executing these is of higher value than study only, parallel to the position of the Babli.</i>”
Artaban<sup class="footnote-marker">146</sup><i class="footnote">Artaban IV, the last ruler of the Parthian empire.</i> sent a priceless<sup class="footnote-marker">147</sup><i class="footnote">Greek ἀτίμητος, adj., “priceless”; τιμή “worth, price.”</i> precious pearl to our holy teacher<sup class="footnote-marker">148</sup><i class="footnote">Rav, who has this title in several passages of the Yerushalmi.</i> and said to him: Send me a thing of equal value. He sent him a <i>mezuzah</i>. He said to him: I sent you a priceless thing and you send me something worth a <i>follis</i><sup class="footnote-marker">149</sup><i class="footnote">Latin <i>follis</i>, Greek φόλλις, -εως, ὁ, in the late Roman empire a small copper coin (1/288 of a solidus or 1/1728 of an ounce of gold), under the (much later) Byzantine emperor Anastasius equal to 40 units of the smallest coin called <i>nummus</i>. Originally, <i>follis</i> means “bellows”, then “bag, small coin”, later “money bag” representing a standard value. <i>Follis</i> as a coin appears only after Diocletian’s currency reform. The use of the term reflects the time of the edition of the Yerushalmi, not the time of the actors in the story. (For a similar adaptation in a Babylonian source, cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Peah 1:1:22" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Peah.1.1.22">Note 102</a>.)</i>! He said to him, your possessions and mine together are not equal to it! Not only that, but you sent me something that I have to watch over and I sent you something that watches over you while you are sleeping, as it is written (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Proverbs 6:22" href="/Proverbs.6.22"><i>Prov.</i> 6:22</a>): “I will make you rest when you are rambling.”
Rebbi Mana understood all of them from this verse (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 32:47" href="/Deuteronomy.32.47"><i>Deut.</i> 32:47</a>): “Because it is not an empty word from you,” that is the study of Torah, “because it is your life,” that is honoring father and mother, “and by this you will have a long life,” that are works of kindness, “on the earth,” that is making peace between men.
Correspondingly<sup class="footnote-marker">150</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Peah (Lieberman) 1:2" href="/Tosefta_Peah_(Lieberman).1.2">Tosephta <i>Peah</i> 1:2</a>.</i>, there are four things that a person has to pay for in this world and the principal remains for him in the future world: Idolatry, incest and adultery, and murder. But slander is equivalent to all of these. Idolatry from where? (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 15:31" href="/Numbers.15.31"><i>Num.</i> 15:31</a>) “This soul shall certainly be cut off, its sin is inside it.” Why does the verse say “its sin is inside it?” This teaches that the soul is destroyed and its sin is still inside it. And it is written (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 32:21" href="/Exodus.32.21"><i>Ex.</i>32:21</a>): “Please, this people has committed a grave sin, they made for themselves golden gods.<sup class="footnote-marker">151</sup><i class="footnote">The first verse proves that the sin stays in the soul at death, the second verse is a paradigm that such a sin is called “grave;” the latter expression is then used to characterize the other deadly sins.</i>” Incest and adultery from where? (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Genesis 39:9" href="/Genesis.39.9"><i>Gen.</i> 39:9</a>) “How can I commit that grave evil and sin before God.” Murder from where?
