(<a class="refLink" data-ref="Genesis 4:13" href="/Genesis.4.13"><i>Gen.</i> 4:13</a>) “Cain said to the Eternal, my sin is grave, it cannot be removed.” When he comes to calumny, it does not say in the singular “grave” or “the grave” but in the plural “grave” (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Psalms 12:4" href="/Psalms.12.4"><i>Ps.</i> 12:4</a>): “May the Eternal cut off all slippery lips, all tongues peaking grave words<sup class="footnote-marker">152</sup><i class="footnote">This is the introduction to a lengthy section on the sin of calumny.</i>.”
In its written (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Genesis 37:2" href="/Genesis.37.2"><i>Gen.</i> 37:2</a>): “And Joseph reported their evil slander to their father.” What did he say? Rebbi Meir, Rebbi Jehudah, and Rebbi Simeon. Rebbi Meir said, they are suspected of eating flesh from a living animal<sup class="footnote-marker">153</sup><i class="footnote">This is forbidden for all mankind as one of the Seven Commandments to the Sons of Noah.</i>. Rebbi Jehudah said, they abuse the sons of the handmaidens and treat them like slaves. Rebbi Simeon said, they are infatuated with local girls<sup class="footnote-marker">154</sup><i class="footnote">Canaanites whom they are forbidden to marry.</i>. Rebbi Judah ben Pazi said, (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Proverbs 16:11" href="/Proverbs.16.11"><i>Prov.</i> 16:11</a>) “Balance and scales of justice are the Eternal’s; all weight-stones are His work. “What did he say, they are suspected of eating flesh from a living animal? The Holy One, praise to Him, said: So I testify for them that they first slaughter and then eat, (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Genesis 37:31" href="/Genesis.37.31"><i>Gen.</i> 37:31</a>) “they slaughtered the he-goat and dipped the coat into the blood.” What did he say, they abuse the sons of the handmaidens and treat them like slaves? (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Psalms 105:17" href="/Psalms.105.17"><i>Ps.</i> 105:17</a>) “Joseph was sold as a slave.” What did he say, they are infatuated with local girls? Lo, a she-bear will attack you, (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Genesis 39:7" href="/Genesis.39.7"><i>Gen.</i> 39:7</a>) “his master’s wife lifted her eyes to Joseph, etc.”
Rebbi Yasa in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: He who calumniates does not speak until he has disowned the principle<sup class="footnote-marker">155</sup><i class="footnote">The principle of the Jewish faith, that God is the Ultimate Judge.</i>. What is the reason? (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Psalms 12:5" href="/Psalms.12.5"><i>Ps.</i>12:5</a>) “Who said, by our tongues we shall overcome, our lips are with us, who is master over us<sup class="footnote-marker">156</sup><i class="footnote">This is a declaration of atheism.</i>?” All sins a person commits on earth, but this one they commit both in heaven and on earth. What is the reason? (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Psalms 73:9" href="/Psalms.73.9"><i>Ps.</i>73:9</a>) “They put their mouths in heaven and their tongue wanders about the earth.” Rebbi Isaac said157 (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Psalms 50:22" href="/Psalms.50.22"><i>Ps.</i> 50:22</a>): “Understand that, those who are forgetting God, lest He will tear<sup class="footnote-marker">158</sup><i class="footnote">In the Biblical text: Lest I shall tear.</i> and nobody may rescue.” Rebbi Joshua ben Levi said (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Psalms 50:20" href="/Psalms.50.20"><i>Ps.</i> 50:20</a>): "You sit, talk against your brother, you find fault with your mother’s son.” What is written there? (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Psalms 50:16" href="/Psalms.50.16"><i>Ps.</i> 50:16</a>) “God said to the evildoer, what right do you have to speak about My laws, to keep My covenant in your mouth?”
Where is a warning about calumny159? (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 23:10" href="/Deuteronomy.23.10"><i>Deut</i>. 23:10</a>) “Keep yourself from every evil word!” Rebbi La said that Rebbi Ismaël stated (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 19:10" href="/Leviticus.19.10"><i>Lev.</i>19:10</a>): “Do not walk around as slanderer among your people,” that is slandering, calumny. Rebbi Nehemiah stated that you should not be like a trader who carries things from one person to another and vice versa.
Rebbi Ḥanina said: Come and see, how hard is even a hint of calumny that the verses spoke untruth in order to make peace between Abraham and Sarah. (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Genesis 18:12" href="/Genesis.18.12"><i>Gen.</i> 18:12</a>) “Sarah laughed to herself, saying: After having wilted I became dainty<sup class="footnote-marker">160</sup><i class="footnote">Her wrinkles had disappeared and her period restarted.</i>, but my master is old.” To Abraham He does not say so, but rather (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Genesis 18:13" href="/Genesis.18.13"><i>Gen.</i> 18:13</a>): “Why now did Sarah laugh, saying how can I give birth, being old?” It is not written “my master is old” but “I am old”. Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel said<sup class="footnote-marker">161</sup><i class="footnote">The formulation in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bereishit Rabbah 100:9" href="/Bereishit_Rabbah.100.9"><i>Bereshit rabba</i> 100(9</a>) is easier to understand: We have stated: Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel said, peace is great because even the tribes spoke untruth to obtain peace between themselves and Joseph.</i>: Come and see, how hard is even a hint of calumny that the verses spoke untruth in order to make peace between Joseph and his brothers. That is what is written (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Genesis 50:16-17" href="/Genesis.50.16-17"><i>Gen.</i>50:16–17</a>): “They empowered [a messenger] to Joseph to say: Your father commanded before his death, saying: So you shall say to Joseph, please forgive etc.” We do not find that he (Jacob) commanded anything.
Rebbi Samuel bar Naḥman in the name of Rebbi Jonathan: One may calumniate disruptive persons. What is the reason? (<a class="refLink" data-ref="I Kings 1:14" href="/I_Kings.1.14"><i>1K.</i> 1:14</a>) “I shall come after you and confirm your words.<sup class="footnote-marker">162</sup><i class="footnote">While Bat Sheba told the truth, the intervention of Nathan is intended to cause trouble to Adoniah. Hence, the sin of calumny extends even to true statements unless their publication has some value for the public peace.</i>” Rebbi Zeïra asked before Rebbi Yasa: Why was Adoniah ben Ḥaggit killed? Because he asked for Avishag of Shunem<sup class="footnote-marker">163</sup><i class="footnote">What was a problem for the Yerushalmi was none for the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sanhedrin 22b" href="/Sanhedrin.22b">Babli, <i>Sanhedrin</i> 22b</a>, where the Galilean Rebbi Jacob in the name of R. Joḥanan explains that Avishag, while she was not a concubine of David, had the legal status of one and, therefore, could not be married to anybody but a king.</i>? He said to him, they were looking for a pretext to proscribe the lives of disruptive persons.
They asked before Rebbi Joḥanan: Who is guilty of calumny? He who says it and he accepts it<sup class="footnote-marker">164</sup><i class="footnote">The person who accepts information which he recognizes as calumny without protesting is as guilty as the one who disseminates it.</i>
The linen traders had an assembly<sup class="footnote-marker">165</sup><i class="footnote">The root is צמת “to take, squeeze, together.”</i>. There was one whose nickname was Bar Ḥovez (son of the cheesemaker) who did not appear. They said: What are we going to eat today? One said: cheese, then the cheeser will come. Rebbi Joḥanan said, this one was calumniating in secret<sup class="footnote-marker">166</sup><i class="footnote">By making fun of a nickname.</i>. The city councilmen<sup class="footnote-marker">167</sup><i class="footnote">Greek βουλευταί “councilmen”.</i> of Sepphoris had an assembly. There was one by the name of Joḥanan who did not appear. One said to the other, let us visit Rebbi Joḥanan today. They said<sup class="footnote-marker">168</sup><i class="footnote">This seems to be a scribal error since it follows from the next remark that a single person said so.</i>, Joḥanan will come<sup class="footnote-marker">169</sup><i class="footnote">When he hears that he is given the title of Rebbi.</i>. Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish said, this one calumniated justly.
Rebbi Abba bar Cahana said, the generation of David were all just<sup class="footnote-marker">170</sup><i class="footnote">At least on the surface they were no idolaters.</i> but because there were informers<sup class="footnote-marker">171</sup><i class="footnote">Latin <i>delator</i> “informer,” <i>delatio</i>, later form <i>delatura</i> “denunciation.”</i> among them they went to war and were falling. That is what David said (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Psalms 57:9-10" href="/Psalms.57.9-10"><i>Ps.</i> 57:9–10</a>): “My soul, among lions I am lying down with blazing fires.” “My soul, among lions,” these are Abner and Amasah who were lions in Torah; “I am lying down with blazing fires;” these are Doeg and Aḥitophel who were eager for calumny. “The people, their teeth are spear and arrows,” these are the owners of Qe‘ilah, as it is written (<a class="refLink" data-ref="I Samuel 23:11" href="/I_Samuel.23.11"><i>1Sam.</i> 23:11</a>): “Will the owners of Qe‘ilah deliver me in his hand, will Saul come?” “Their tongue is a sharp sword,” these are the people of Ziph (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Psalms 54:1" href="/Psalms.54.1"><i>Ps.</i> 54:1</a>): “When the people of Ziph came and told Saul. “At that moment, David said before the Holy One, praised be He: Master of the Universe, why should Your glory descend among them? Remove Your glory from them! That is what is written (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Psalms 57:6" href="/Psalms.57.6"><i>Ps.</i> 57:6</a>): “Rise over the Heavens, o God, higher than all earth Your glory!” The generation of Ahab were idolaters but since there was no informing among them they went to war and were victorious. That is what Obadiahu said to Elijah (<a class="refLink" data-ref="I Kings 18:13" href="/I_Kings.18.13"><i>1K.</i> 18:13</a>): “Certainly it was told to my lord what I did when Izebel slew all prophets of the Eternal etc., and I provided them with bread and water.” [If bread, why does he have to mention water<sup class="footnote-marker">172</sup><i class="footnote">Since “bread” may mean “food”.</i>, and if water, why does he have to mention bread. This teaches you that it was more difficult for him to provide them with water than with bread.] But Elijah was standing on Mount Carmel and declaring (<a class="refLink" data-ref="I Kings 18:22" href="/I_Kings.18.22"><i>1K.</i>18:22</a>): “I remained alone as prophet for the Eternal,” and everybody knew and nobody informed the king<sup class="footnote-marker">173</sup><i class="footnote">That there were another hundred prophets hidden by Obadiahu.</i>
Why is it called “triple<sup class="footnote-marker">174</sup><i class="footnote">In all Yerushalmi Targumim, calumny is called לִישָׁן תְּלִיתָאִי “triple tongue.” The same explanation is given in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Arakhin 15b" href="/Arakhin.15b">Babli <i>Arakhin</i> 15b</a>.</i>?” Because it kills three: The one who says it, the one who accepts it, and the one calumniated. And in the days of Saul, four were killed: Doeg who said it<sup class="footnote-marker">175</sup><i class="footnote">That the priests of Nob had given David the holy bread.</i>, Saul who accepted it, Aḥimelekh who was calumniated, and Abner. Why was Abner killed? Rebbi Joshua ben Levi, Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish, and the rabbis. Rebbi Joshua ben Levi said, because he made fun of the lives of his squires as it is said (<a class="refLink" data-ref="II Samuel 2:14" href="/II_Samuel.2.14"><i>2Sam.</i> 2:14</a>): “Let the squires get up and play before us, and Joab<sup class="footnote-marker">176</sup><i class="footnote">The reading of the text, אבנר, not only contradicts the reading of the verse but makes no sense here since Abner was the instigator of the duel.</i> said, let them get up.” R. Simeon ben Laqish, because he prefaced his name before that of David; that is what is written (<a class="refLink" data-ref="II Samuel 3:12" href="/II_Samuel.3.12"><i>2Sam.</i> 3:12</a>) “Abner sent messengers to David the underling, saying: Whose is the land?” He wrote: From Abner to David<sup class="footnote-marker">177</sup><i class="footnote">A letter addressed to the king would read: “To David from Abner.” The supposed inversion is intended to show that Abner considered himself a king maker, not an executor of God’s will as declared by Samuel.</i>. The rabbis say, because he did not let Shaul make peace with David. That is what is written (<a class="refLink" data-ref="I Samuel 24:5" href="/I_Samuel.24.5"><i>1Sam.</i> 24:5</a>): “My father, see, but see the corner of your coat in my hand!” He said to him, what do you want from the prattling<sup class="footnote-marker">178</sup><i class="footnote">It seems that Yerushalmi גלגל is the same as Babylonian לגלג.</i> of this one, it was torn off by thorns<sup class="footnote-marker">179</sup><i class="footnote">Rabbinic feminine סירה is the same as Biblical masculine to סיר “thorn.”</i>. But when they came to the circle, he said to him (<a class="refLink" data-ref="I Samuel 26:14" href="/I_Samuel.26.14"><i>1Sam.</i> 26:14</a>): “Can you answer, Abner?” About the corner, you said it was torn off by a thorn; were spear and pitcher cut off by a thorn? But some say, because he had it in his power to intervene for Nob, the city of priests, and he did not intervene.
(<a class="refLink" data-ref="Psalms 120:4" href="/Psalms.120.4"><i>Ps.</i> 120:4</a>) “The arrows of a hero are sharpened over hot broom coals.” All weapons kill at their place but this kills<sup class="footnote-marker">179</sup><i class="footnote">Rabbinic feminine סירה is the same as Biblical masculine to סיר “thorn.”</i> at a distance. All other charcoal that is extinguished on the outside, is also extinguished on the inside, but these, even though they are extinguished on the outside, are still burning on the inside. It happened to one that he put down burning charcoal on <i>Sukkot</i> and came and found them still burning on <i>Pesaḥ</i>.
Rebbi Samuel ben Naḥman said: One says to the snake, why when you move does your tongue flow? It said to them, that<sup class="footnote-marker">180</sup><i class="footnote">דְּהוּא = דּוּ.</i> caused it for me<sup class="footnote-marker">181</sup><i class="footnote">Because of my evil talk (and calumny about God) I was turned into a snake.</i>. What profit do you have from biting? A lion tears up and eats, a wolf tears up and eats; you, what profit do you have from biting<sup class="footnote-marker">182</sup><i class="footnote">A human who is much too big to swallow.</i>? It said to them (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Ecclesiastes 10:11" href="/Ecclesiastes.10.11"><i>Eccl.</i> 10:11</a>): “If the snake would bite without a whisper,” I would not bite unless it was said to me from heaven: bite! Why do you bite one limb and all limbs feel it? It said to them: Why do you ask me? Ask the master of the tongue<sup class="footnote-marker">183</sup><i class="footnote">The calumniator.</i> who speaks here and kills in Rome<sup class="footnote-marker">184</sup><i class="footnote">Hebrew spelling perhaps derived from Greek Ρ́ώμη, “Rome”. The Latin locative <i>Romae</i>, “in Rome”, since the word is preceded by בְּ, “in”, is probably not meant. But redundant constructions involving foreign words are found in the Yerushalmi.</i>, speaks in Rome and kills in Syria. Why are you found among fences? It said to them, I broke the fence of the world<sup class="footnote-marker">185</sup><i class="footnote">By being the first to commit adultery [cf. the author’s <i>The Scholar’s Haggadah</i> (Northvale 1995), pp. 308–309.]</i>
Merit has capital<sup class="footnote-marker">186</sup><i class="footnote">It determines the reward in the world to come. (The entire paragraph is from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Peah (Lieberman) 1:2-3" href="/Tosefta_Peah_(Lieberman).1.2-3">Tosephta <i>Peah</i>, 1:2–3</a>, also quoted <a class="refLink" data-ref="Kiddushin 40a" href="/Kiddushin.40a">Babli <i>Qiddušin</i> 40a</a>).</i> and yield<sup class="footnote-marker">187</sup><i class="footnote">The rewards in this world that are given without diminishing the rewards in the world to come.</i>; sin has capital but no yield. Merit has capital and has yield, as it is said (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Isaiah 3:10" href="/Isaiah.3.10"><i>Is.</i> 3:10</a>): “Tell the just that it is very well, that they will eat the fruit of their actions.” Sin has capital but no yield, as it is said (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Isaiah 3:11" href="/Isaiah.3.11"><i>Is.</i> 3:11</a>): “Woe to the bad criminal, the retribution for his actions will be come upon him.” How can I confirm (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Proverbs 1:31" href="/Proverbs.1.31"><i>Prov.</i> 1:31</a>): “They<sup class="footnote-marker">188</sup><i class="footnote">Evildoers.</i> will eat from the fruits of their way?” Every sin that produces fruit has yield<sup class="footnote-marker">189</sup><i class="footnote">The adulterer who produces a bastard child will be punished for the child in this world while all the punishment for adultery is reserved for the future world.</i>, that which does not produce fruit has no yield.
The<sup class="footnote-marker">190</sup><i class="footnote">The first clause of this paragraph is also in the Tosephta and Babli mentioned in Note 186. The second part is different both in the Babli and the Tosephta which both make a difference between good deeds that produced yield and those that did not. The Tosephta clearly represents the position of the Babli.</i> Holy One, praise to Him, adds good intention to deeds; the Holy One, praise to Him, does not add bad intention to deeds. The Holy One, praise to Him, adds good intention to deeds as it is written (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Malachi 3:16" href="/Malachi.3.16"><i>Mal.</i> 3:16</a>): “Then those who fear the Eternal will speak together, each man with his neighbor<sup class="footnote-marker">191</sup><i class="footnote">The proof is from the part of the verse not quoted: “The Eternal will be attentive and listen and write it into a book of remembrance before Him,” simply for the good intention.</i>.” The Holy One, praise to Him, does not add bad intention to deeds (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Psalms 66:12" href="/Psalms.66.12"><i>Ps.</i> 66:12</a>): “If I saw mischief in my heart, the Eternal would not listen.” That refers to Israel, but for Gentiles it is the reverse; the Holy One, praise to Him, does not add good intention; bad intention the Holy One, praise to Him, does add. The Holy One, praise to Him, does not add good intention, as it is written (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Daniel 6:15" href="/Daniel.6.15"><i>Dan.</i> 6:15</a>): “Until sundown he tried to rescue him;” it does not say “to save him<sup class="footnote-marker">192</sup><i class="footnote">Even though the first part of the verse, not quoted here, declares that Darius tried to save Daniel from the lions’ den.</i>.” The Holy One, praise to Him, adds bad intention: (<i>Obad.</i> 9–10) “From killing<sup class="footnote-marker">193</sup><i class="footnote">In the masoretic text, the word belongs to the previous verse, stating that nobody from Mount Seïr will escape being killed. Here the word is taken as explanation of the following verse.</i>. Because of the oppression of your brother Jacob.” But did he kill him? This teaches that he thought about killing him and the verse counts it as if he had killed him.
Rebbi Simeon ben Ioḥai stated: If a person was perfectly just all his days and at the end he rebelled, he lost all he worked for his entire life. What is the reason? (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Ezekiel 33:18" href="/Ezekiel.33.18"><i>Ez</i>. 33:18</a>) “If a just person reverts from his justness and does mischief<sup class="footnote-marker">194</sup><i class="footnote">His prior merit will not be counted.</i>.” Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish said, if he wonders about his earlier deeds. If a person was perfectly evil all his days and at the end he repents, the Holy One, praise to Him, receives him. What is the reason? (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Ezekiel 33:27" href="/Ezekiel.33.27"><i>Ez</i>. 33:27</a>) “If a sinner returns from his wickedness<sup class="footnote-marker">195</sup><i class="footnote">His prior wickedness will not be counted.</i>.” Rebbi Joḥanan said, not only that but all transgressions are counted as merits for him. What is the reason? (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Psalms 45:9" href="/Psalms.45.9"><i>Ps.</i> 45:9</a>) “Myrrh, aloe, cassia, all your faithlessness<sup class="footnote-marker">196</sup><i class="footnote">In the context of the verse, the word means “your clothes.” The Hebrew double root בגד “clothing-dealing treacherously” cannot be explained from cognate languages.</i>.” All faithless acts in which you were faithless towards Me are like myrrh, aloe, and cassia.
From one who has mostly merit and a minority of sins they<sup class="footnote-marker">197</sup><i class="footnote">The Heavenly Court.</i> collect in this world for the minority of light sins in order to give him his complete reward in the future. But to one who has mostly sins and a minority of merits they give the reward for light merits in this world in order to collect from him his complete punishment in the future world. But<sup class="footnote-marker">198</sup><i class="footnote">Similar but different statements in the name of R. Eliezer Hamodaï are in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Pirkei Avot 3:11" href="/Pirkei_Avot.3.11">Mishnah <i>Avot</i> 3:11</a>, <i>Avot dRebbi Natan A</i> 26. The meaning of the terms in this statement is explained in the next paragraph.</i> from one who tears away the yoke, who breaks the covenant, and who finds aspects in the Torah, even though he has meritorious deeds to his credit, they exact payment in this world and the capital remains for him in the future world. Idolatry and incest and adultery, Rebbi Jonah and Rebbi Yose: One says, among the easy ones<sup class="footnote-marker">199</sup><i class="footnote">The easy sins are punished in this world, the hard ones in the World to Come.</i>, and one says, among the hard ones. What are we talking about? If he repented, there is nothing that stands in the way of those who repent. But what we are talking about is one who did not repent but died in pain<sup class="footnote-marker">200</sup><i class="footnote">The question is whether an idolater or adulterer who dies in pain has received his punishment or whether he has to expect his main punishment in the World to Come. The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Yoma 86a" href="/Yoma.86a">Babli, <i>Yoma</i> 86a</a>, sides with the first opinion and reserves eternal punishment only for those who desecrate the Holy Name, i. e., bring disgrace upon the Jewish religion.</i>.
There (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sanhedrin 10:1:2-26" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sanhedrin.10.1.2-26"><i>Sanhedrin</i> 10:1</a>) we have stated: The following have no part in the Future World: He who says there is no resurrection of the dead<sup class="footnote-marker">201</sup><i class="footnote">However, in the Mishnah in <i>Sanhedrin</i>, the reading of the Yerushalmi is like the Babli, “He who says that there is no resurrection of the dead from the Torah.” The entire paragraph also appears in <i>Sanhedrin</i>.</i>, that the Torah is not from Heaven, and the Epicurean<sup class="footnote-marker">202</sup><i class="footnote">Epicure was an avowed atheist; hence, in Jewish sources, an Epicurean is an atheist.</i>. Rebbi Aqiba says, also he who reads apocryphal books<sup class="footnote-marker">203</sup><i class="footnote">Books that pretend to Biblical status but have been excluded from the canon as spurious and pernicious.</i>. And he who recites magical spells over a wound and says (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 15:26" href="/Exodus.15.26"><i>Ex.</i> 15:26</a>): “Any disease that I put upon Egypt I shall not put on you because I am the Eternal, your Healer.<sup class="footnote-marker">204</sup><i class="footnote">Using the Torah for magic or exorcisms is equivalent to not believing in the Torah.</i>” Abba Saul<sup class="footnote-marker">205</sup><i class="footnote">A Tanna of the third generation, younger than Rebbi Aqiba.</i> says, also he who pronounces the Name<sup class="footnote-marker">206</sup><i class="footnote">The Divine Name <i>YHWH</i> whose vocalization is a secret not following grammatical rules.</i> by its letters. They added him who tears away the yoke, him who breaks the Covenant, and him who finds aspects in the Torah to those who have no part in the Future World. He who breaks the yoke is one who says the Torah is obligatory but I cannot stand it. He who breaks the Covenant is one who pulls himself a prepuce. He who finds aspects in the Torah is one who says that the Torah was not given from Heaven. But did we not state separately: “He who says that the Torah is not from Heaven<sup class="footnote-marker">207</sup><i class="footnote">Has no part in the Future World.</i>?” Rebbi Ḥanania<sup class="footnote-marker">208</sup><i class="footnote">In <i>Berakhot</i> and <i>Sanhedrin</i> he is called R. Ḥanina Entanayah.</i> Entanayah stated before Rebbi Mana: That is one who publicly transgresses the words of the Torah in the manner of Joiakim, king of Judah, and his circle.
He who has a preponderance of merit inherits paradise. He who has a preponderance of sins inherits hell. Rebbi Yose ben Ḥanina said: It does not say “He lifts sins,” but rather (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 34:7" href="/Exodus.34.7"><i>Ex.</i> 34:7</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Micah 7:18" href="/Micah.7.18"><i>Michah</i> 7:18</a>) “He lifts sin.” The Holy One, praise to Him, seizes one document from the sins and the merits tilt<sup class="footnote-marker">209</sup><i class="footnote">The merits tilt the scales of justice to their side. This argument is given in cryptic brevity in Babli <i>Rosh Hashanah</i> 17a. The text follows the parallel in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sanhedrin 10:1:2-26" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sanhedrin.10.1.2-26">Sanhedrin 10:1</a> (in the Venice text, .נושה עון אין כתיב כאן)</i>. Rebbi Eleazar said (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Psalms 62:13" href="/Psalms.62.13"><i>Ps.</i> 62:13</a>): “Kindness is Yours, o Master, because You repay to everyone according to his deeds,” and if he has none, You give him from Yours. That is the opinion of Rebbi Eleazar, because Rebbi Eleazar said<sup class="footnote-marker">210</sup><i class="footnote">In Babli <i>Rosh Hashanah</i> 17a, this is quoted in the name of the House of Hillel.</i> (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 34:6" href="/Exodus.34.6"><i>Ex.</i> 34:6</a>): “And much kindness,” He turns towards kindness.
Rebbi Jeremiah said that Rebbi Samuel bar Rav Isaac asked: (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Proverbs 13:6" href="/Proverbs.13.6"><i>Pr.</i> 13:6</a>) “Justice protects the one on the straight path but sin destroys the sinner.” (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Proverbs 13:21" href="/Proverbs.13.21"><i>Pr.</i> 13:21</a>) “Evil deeds will pursue sinners but He will reward the just.” (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Proverbs 3:4" href="/Proverbs.3.4"><i>Pr.</i> 3:4</a>) “While He makes scoffers targets of scoffing, He will bestow grace on the meek.” (<a class="refLink" data-ref="I Samuel 2:9" href="/I_Samuel.2.9"><i>1Sam.</i> 2:9</a>) “He will watch over the feet of His pious, but evildoers will be silent in the darkness.” (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Proverbs 3:35" href="/Proverbs.3.35"><i>Pr.</i> 3:35</a>) “The wise will inherit honor, but shame will mark the stupid ones.” Fences are made fences and doors doors<sup class="footnote-marker">211</sup><i class="footnote">A similar thought is expressed in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shabbat 104a" href="/Shabbat.104a">Babli <i>Shabbat</i> 104a</a>: “If somebody wants to live in sin, one opens for him the door [to sin] from Heaven; if he wants to live in purity, one helps him from Heaven.”</i>. Rebbi Jeremiah in the name of Rebbi Samuel bar Rav Isaac: If a person watches himself the first, second, and third times not to commit a sin, then in the future the Holy One, praise to Him, will watch over him. What is the reason? (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Job 33:29" href="/Job.33.29"><i>Job</i> 33:29</a>) “All this God will do twice, three times, with a man.” Rebbi Zeïra said, but only if the person does not revert<sup class="footnote-marker">212</sup><i class="footnote">Change his ways from good to bad.</i>. What is the reason? It does not say “The triple thread will never snap” but rather (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Ecclesiastes 4:12" href="/Ecclesiastes.4.12"><i>Eccl.</i> 4:12</a>) “The triple thread will not quickly snap.” If you work on it, it will split.
Rebbi Ḥuna said in the name of Rebbi Abbahu: There is no forgetting before the Holy One, but for Israel He becomes forgetful. What is the reason? It is written (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 34:7" href="/Exodus.34.7"><i>Ex.</i> 34:7</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Micah 7:18" href="/Micah.7.18"><i>Michah</i> 7:18</a>) “He <i>forgets</i><sup class="footnote-marker">213</sup><i class="footnote">Replacing נשׂא “to lift, carry” by נשׁה “to forget”.</i> sin.” And so David says (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Psalms 85:3" href="/Psalms.85.3"><i>Ps.</i> 85:3</a>): “You <i>forgot</i> your people’s sin, You covered up all their misdeeds, Selah.”

Halakhah 2

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> One never gives <i>peah</i> less than one in sixty<sup class="footnote-marker">214</sup><i class="footnote">1 2/3% of the yield of the field.</i>. Even though they said that <i>peah</i> has no measure, everything is according to the size of the field, the poor, and the humility<sup class="footnote-marker">215</sup><i class="footnote">In the Rome manuscript הענבה “the size of the berries;” in the Munich manuscript of the Babli as well as the Code of Maimonides, הענייה “the answer” (of the field to the farmer’s effort.) Both Maimonides and R. Simson explain that if one part of the field has a large yield and another part a meager one, one should not give all of <i>peah</i> from the meager part. The two readings imply that the religiosity of the farmer is no matter to consider here.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> It was stated: One does not say to him, bring camels and load up. The <i>baraita</i> speaks if he wants to give more than the measure<sup class="footnote-marker">216</sup><i class="footnote">The measure is the amount fixed by the rabbis, 1 2/3%. If there is a large field and only one poor person one tells the poor to bring camels and load up the <i>peah</i>. The restriction on more than 1 2/3% is introduced because <i>peah</i> is not subject to the laws of <i>terumah</i> and tithes; hence, what is given to the poor diminishes what is given to priests and Levites. On the other hand, one does not allow the farmer to get away with less than 1 2/3% <i>peah</i>.</i>, but if he gives according to the measure then one says to the poor to bring camels and load up. If his field is large and the poor few, he gives according to the field. His field is small and the poor many, he gives according to the poor. Rebbi Simeon explained two things to make it easy for the owner: If his field is large and the poor few, he gives according to the poor; if his field is small and the poor many, he gives according to the field. The Mishnah does not say so, but “everything is according to the size of the field, the poor, and the humility (yield).<sup class="footnote-marker">217</sup><i class="footnote">The implication is that the Mishnah, which is followed in practice, requires the farmer to give more than the minimum 1 2/3% unless there is only one poor person and he would need a camel to remove the <i>peah</i>. This is also the interpretation of Maimonides (<i>Mattenot ‘Aniïm</i> 1:16).</i>”

Halakhah 3

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> One may give <i>peah</i> from the head of the field and from its middle<sup class="footnote-marker">218</sup><i class="footnote">At the start and during the actual harvest.</i>. Rebbi Simeon says, only on condition that he give the measure<sup class="footnote-marker">219</sup><i class="footnote">The rabbinically prescribed 1 2/3%; the Halakhah will discuss how this <i>peah</i> is computed. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Peah (Lieberman) 1:6" href="/Tosefta_Peah_(Lieberman).1.6">Tosephta (1:6</a>), quoted later (4:3), Rebbi Simeon explains his position that <i>peah</i> must be given at the end only, because not only is this the simple meaning of the verses speaking of the obligation of <i>peah</i>(that one may not <i>finish</i> cutting one’s field) but also to exclude favoritism and give all poor the same chance by setting a reasonably fixed time-table when they may come and pluck the remaining stalks from the field.<br>Maimonides is of the opinion that R. Simeon makes the view of the <i>tanna</i> explicit, not that he disagrees with the first statement.</i> at the end. Rebbi Jehudah says, if he left one stalk standing he adds to it<sup class="footnote-marker">220</sup><i class="footnote">The Halakhah will explain what he may add as <i>peah</i>.</i> for <i>peah</i>; otherwise, he gives only as abandoned property<sup class="footnote-marker">221</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Peah</i> is reserved for the poor (with the legal definition of the poor entitled to charitable gifts given in the last chapter); the House of Hillel rules that abandoned property is abandoned (and free from <i>terumah</i> and tithes) only if it is ownerless and abandoned to absolutely everybody. <i>Arukh</i> brings Aramaic expressions to show that the root בקר means “to be without master,” in a human moral sense also “licentious.”</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “One may give <i>peah</i> from the head of the field and from its middle.” Rebbi Yose in the name of Rebbi Simeon bar Laqish: (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 19:9" href="/Leviticus.19.9">Lev. 19:9</a>) “And at your harvesting,” why does the verse have to say “to harvest”<sup class="footnote-marker">222</sup><i class="footnote">The expression is considered redundant: “And on the occasion of your harvesting the harvest of your land, do not finish the corner of your field <i>during your harvesting</i> but abandon it for the poor and the stranger.”</i>? Even if he has still more to harvest<sup class="footnote-marker">223</sup><i class="footnote">Hence, <i>peah</i> can be given at any time after the harvest has started. (It was already discussed in Halakha 1 that the very first stalk can never be <i>peah</i>).</i>. Rebbi Jonah in the name of Rebbi Simeon bar Laqish: (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 19:9" href="/Leviticus.19.9"><i>Lev</i>. 19:9</a>) “And at your harvesting,” why does the verse have to say “to harvest”? One for the beginning and one for the end<sup class="footnote-marker">224</sup><i class="footnote">The disagreement seems to be that according to R. Jonah some <i>peah</i> must be given at the end (as explained by R. Simeon bar Ioḥai in the Mishnah) but that according R. Yose all <i>peah</i> might be given earlier. According to R. Jonah, R. Simeon explains the first sentence; according to R. Yose, he disagrees with the first sentence.</i>. Rebbi Yose in the name of Rebbi Joshua ben Levi: (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 19:9" href="/Leviticus.19.9"><i>Lev</i>. 19:9</a>) “And at your harvesting,” why does the verse have to say “to harvest”? One for sacred property and one for private persons<sup class="footnote-marker">225</sup><i class="footnote">R. Joshua ben Levi does not consider the second “to harvest” redundant but referring to a different kind of harvest.<br>הדיוֹט “private person”, Greek ἰδιώτης.</i>. Rebbi Yudan asked, do not coins redeem sacred property only when it is cut; maybe when it is standing<sup class="footnote-marker">226</sup><i class="footnote">While real estate donated to the Temple may be sold to private persons (and the proceeds used for the needs of the Temple), it is clear from many sources (e. g. Mishnah 4:5) that as long as the field is in the possession of the Temple, the rules of <i>peah</i> do not apply. Hence, any <i>peah</i> taken would have to come from a field that was bought with the grain standing, not after harvest when the grain was already cut by the Temple staff and is exempt.</i>? Rebbi Ḥaninah said, you should not say that harvest of sacred property should be treated like harvest of a private person<sup class="footnote-marker">227</sup><i class="footnote">The argument of R. Joshua ben Levi differs from that of R. Simeon ben Laqish that the second “to harvest” adds something; it eliminates harvest that is not “yours” but public property. Hence, anybody who buys a field with its crop from the Temple is required to leave <i>peah</i>, but anybody who buys the field with its crop after it was cut by the personnel of the Temple has no obligation to give <i>peah</i> from his grain (Chapter 4, Mishnah 5).</i>.
What is the standing of the first batch<sup class="footnote-marker">228</sup><i class="footnote">According to Rebbi Simeon, who says that in any case one has to give <i>peah</i> in full measure at the end, does the amount given earlier have the status of <i>peah</i> or is it simply abandoned property, not reserved for the poor?</i>? Since we have stated<sup class="footnote-marker">229</sup><i class="footnote">In the name of Rebbi Simeon himself. The implication is that the <i>peah</i> given at the end has to add up to 1⅔% of the yield cut after the earlier <i>peah</i> was given.</i> (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Peah (Lieberman) 1:5" href="/Tosefta_Peah_(Lieberman).1.5"><i>Tosephta</i> 1:5</a>): “It is <i>peah</i> and he has to give the measure at the end.” That means it becomes holy as <i>peah</i>. What means “the measure?” The measure of the entire field or the measure of the remainder? Is it possible to say, this lot becomes holy as <i>peah</i> and say, according to the measure of the entire field? Certainly the remaining measure!
Rebbi Ḥiyya<sup class="footnote-marker">230</sup><i class="footnote">R. Ḥiyya bar Abba. This paragraph deals with the last part of the Mishnah, the statement of R. Jehudah that one may add to <i>peah</i> as long as at least one stalk remains uncut on the field (and the additional <i>peah</i> is not subject to the laws of <i>terumah</i> and tithes.) Cf. also Mishnah 6.</i> in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: when it is still standing<sup class="footnote-marker">231</sup><i class="footnote">One may only add standing patches of grain, not cut grain. Rebbi Yasa is of the opinion that one even may designate grain already cut as <i>peah</i>.</i>. Rebbi Yasa in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: when it is cut. What is his intention<sup class="footnote-marker">232</sup><i class="footnote">Does R. Jehudah only say that one may add until one has given the required 1⅔% of the yield (implying that if one starts out with less than 1⅔% and then adds in one batch over that limit it is acceptable and even commendable) or does R. Jehudah apply the laws of <i>peah</i> even to produce given after the rabbinic threshold of 1⅔% clearly has already been exceeded?</i>, to free his field from the obligation or not? Let us hear from the following: Rebbi Yasa said in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: He may always add. What are we talking about? If it is his intention to free his field from the obligation, it already has been freed<sup class="footnote-marker">233</sup><i class="footnote">One could not say מוֹסיף והוֹלך “he keeps adding” if he had to stop after reaching the threshold.</i>. So we must speak about the case where it is not his intention to free his field from the obligation<sup class="footnote-marker">234</sup><i class="footnote">But the obligation was already fulfilled before he started to add.</i>!

Halakhah 4

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> They established a principle for <i>peah</i>: Everything that is food<sup class="footnote-marker">235</sup><i class="footnote">But not industrial plants like flax, cotton, or indigo.</i>, is treated as private property<sup class="footnote-marker">236</sup><i class="footnote">Not anything that is usually taken only from the commons.</i>, grows from the earth<sup class="footnote-marker">237</sup><i class="footnote">Not mushrooms that grow on rotting wood, nor hydroponics.</i>, is harvested at one time<sup class="footnote-marker">238</sup><i class="footnote">Not produce that ripens slowly so that the same field or tree has to be harvested many times; as, for example, figs.</i>, and is stored<sup class="footnote-marker">239</sup><i class="footnote">But not vegetables that are only sold green.</i>, is subject to <i>peah</i>. Grain<sup class="footnote-marker">240</sup><i class="footnote">Since קציר means not only “harvest” but also “grain”, the characteristics of crops subject to <i>peah</i> are derived from those of grain. “Grain” in the Talmud means only grain that may be leavened to make bread and in a leavened state is forbidden on Passover.</i> and legumes<sup class="footnote-marker">241</sup><i class="footnote">The talmudic definition of legumes covers all fruits other than grains which can be ground into a kind of flour: rice, millet, peas, beans, etc.</i> are included.<br>And among trees<sup class="footnote-marker">242</sup><i class="footnote">In the opinion of Maimonides, all <i>peah</i> of trees is a Biblical obligation. According to Rabbenu Simson, the only <i>peah</i> that is a Biblical obligation is that of grain, wine, and olives. Everything else is a rabbinical obligation.</i> sumac<sup class="footnote-marker">243</sup><i class="footnote">Identification of Maimonides. The leaves of the sumac (<i>Rhus coriaria L</i>.) were used in tanning leather and as black dye but the fruits, very rich in citric and related acids, were eaten and used for the preparation of lemonade. Today, ground sumac seeds mixed with thyme are used as condiment (<i>sa‘tar</i>). Hence, the fruits are subject to <i>peah</i> but not the leaves.</i> and carob, walnut and almond, vine and pomegranate, olive and date palm are subject to <i>peah</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 19:9" href="/Leviticus.19.9"><i>Lev</i>. 19:9</a>)<sup class="footnote-marker">244</sup><i class="footnote">The derivation is given, with some variations, in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sifra, Kedoshim, Chapter 1" href="/Sifra,_Kedoshim,_Chapter_1"><i>Sifra Kedoshim</i>,<i>Pereq</i>1</a><i>, Babli Ḥullin</i>137a<i>, Yalqut Shim‘oni</i> #604.</i> “And at your reaping,<sup class="footnote-marker">245</sup><i class="footnote">The full verse reads: “At your reaping the harvest of your land, do not finish reaping the corner of your field, and do not pick up isolated stalks; do abandon them to the poor and the sojourner; I am the Eternal, your God.”</i>”not only reaping; from where do we add plucking? The verse says “to harvest.” From where do we add uprooting<sup class="footnote-marker">246</sup><i class="footnote">This is the reading of the parallel sources and of S. Cirillo; the Venice print has “reaping”, which certainly is incorrect.</i>? The verse says “your harvest.” Not only grain, from where do we add legumes? The verse says “in your land.” From where do we add trees, the verse says “your field<sup class="footnote-marker">247</sup><i class="footnote">An orchard in which the trees allow for plowing between them is called “a field of trees.”</i>.”
It was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">249</sup><i class="footnote">Tosephta <i>Kilaïm</i> 1:15.</i>: One may sow vegetable seeds and tree seeds together. But he who sows with grape kernels is whipped 40 times<sup class="footnote-marker">250</sup><i class="footnote">“40 times” means 39 times, the maximal punishment for the transgression of a Biblical prohibition (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 25:3" href="/Deuteronomy.25.3"><i>Deut</i>. 25:3</a>).</i>. Rebbi Zeïra said, it is written (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 22:9" href="/Deuteronomy.22.9"><i>Deut</i>. 22:9</a>): “Do not sow your vineyard with two kinds;” the main produce<sup class="footnote-marker">251</sup><i class="footnote">The main produce are grapes and the part used for sowing is grape seed.</i> of your vineyard you should not sow with two kinds. Rebbi Yudan from Kappadokia asked before Rebbi Yose: There<sup class="footnote-marker">252</sup><i class="footnote">In the rules of ritual impurity, e. g. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Makhshirin 1:2" href="/Mishnah_Makhshirin.1.2">Mishnah <i>Makhshirin</i> 1:2</a>, all rules that apply to vegetables and grain also apply to tree fruits. “Here” refers to the rules of <i>kilaïm</i>. This shows that the main place of these paragraphs is in tractate <i>Kilaïm</i> (8:1).</i> they say that tree seeds are called seeds but here you say that tree seeds are not called seeds. He said to him: There<sup class="footnote-marker">253</sup><i class="footnote">In the answer, “there” and “here” should be switched; the first answer deals with <i>kilaïm</i> (and refers to the question dealt with there whether one transgresses the prohibition of sowing different kinds in a vineyard only if one sows there two kinds different from vines or only one.)</i> the verse excluded them since usually people do not call them “seeds,” but here the verse added (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 11:37" href="/Leviticus.11.37"><i>Lev</i>. 11:37</a>) “any sown seed that may be sown.”
Some would understand it from here: (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 24:21" href="/Deuteronomy.24.21"><i>Deut</i>. 24:21</a>) “When you harvest your vineyard.” How do you understand this<sup class="footnote-marker">254</sup><i class="footnote">The entire verse reads: “If you harvest your vineyard, you should not go back to pluck the single berries; it should be for the convert, the orphan, and the widow.” The definition of a single berry is given in Mishnah <i>Peah</i> 7:4. The question is about the meaning of the word עוֹלל “to go over it a second time”. The root עלל means “go over a second time, do anything a second time.” In Arabic, علل means “to harvest fruits a second time”. For example, עלילה means doing something following a well thought-out plan. Rebbi Jonah reads the verse in Lamentations as: “May all their evil come before You and may You <i>repeatedly</i> punish them, just as You <i>repeatedly</i> punished me for all my sins.” One is not allowed to go over the vines a second time after one has harvested the bunched grapes; that is the equivalent of <i>peah</i> for vines, (מתעלל is usually translated “to abuse, to mistreat,” it should be taken to mean “to mistreat repeatedly” with emphasis on the repetition of abuse.)</i>? Rebbi Jonah said, do not be punctilious, as you say (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Lamentations 1:22" href="/Lamentations.1.22"><i>Lament</i>. 1:22</a>) “to punish them repeatedly.” (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 24:21" href="/Deuteronomy.24.21"><i>Deut</i>. 24:21</a>) “When you shake your olive tree.” How do you understand this<sup class="footnote-marker">255</sup><i class="footnote">The verse reads: “If you shake your olive tree, do not investigate every branch afterwards; it should be for the convert, the orphan and the widow.” The way of harvesting olives for pressing oil is to shake every branch, then the ripe olives will fall off and are collected under the tree in a cloth [translation of R. Saadiah Gaon]. The root פאר appears only here in the meaning “to glean”; usually it means “to appear in splendor”. The first meaning reappears in the nouns פֻּארָה “crown of the tree”, פֹֹּארוֹת “tree branch”. Rebbi Jonah identifies first and second meanings, and reads: “Do not remove its splendor.” Then he compares the splendor of the tree, פֻּארָה, to the splendor of one’s head, the hair, that in the second verse also is called <i>peah</i>; hence it follows that the remaining olives on the tree have the status of <i>peah</i> and go under its rules. Accordingly, the rules of <i>peah</i> apply both for olive trees and vines.</i>? Rebbi Jonah said (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 19:27" href="/Leviticus.19.27"><i>Lev</i>. 19:27</a>): “Do not round off the <i>peah</i> of your head.” Since olive and vineyard are special in that they are harvested at one time for storage, so also everything that is harvested at one time for storage is obligated<sup class="footnote-marker">256</sup><i class="footnote">By the principle בנין אב משני כתובים “principle established by two verses”. If two necessary verses establish a common consequence, it will apply in general to all cases that are covered by their common antecedents (unless explicitly negated by another verse.) If the verses are partially overlapping in content, the principle is denied by some. The next paragraph will establish that there is no overlap in content in the case under consideration.</i>. But olive and vineyard are special in that they are subject to first fruits and subject to <i>peah</i>, so only that which is subject to first fruits should be subject to <i>peah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">257</sup><i class="footnote">This would exclude rice, peas, etc., which are explicitly included in the Mishnah (and would include figs).</i>. The verse says (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 19:9" href="/Leviticus.19.9"><i>Lev</i>. 19:9</a>): “Your harvest,” even the harvest of rice and millet.
It should mention the olive tree but not the vineyard. If it would mention olive tree but not vineyard, I would say that the olive tree, which is not subject to the rule of isolated berries<sup class="footnote-marker">258</sup><i class="footnote">It is stated in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 19:10" href="/Leviticus.19.10"><i>Lev.</i> 19:10</a>: “Isolated berries of your vineyard (grape berries that do not grow in a bunch but sit directly on the branch and are not part of a row of such grape berries) you should not collect but abandon to the poor.”</i>, is subject to <i>peah</i>, but that vines, which are subject to the rule of isolated berries, are not subject to <i>peah</i>. Lo, the mention of the vineyard is needed. Or if it would mention vineyard but not olive tree, I would say that vines which are subject to the rule of isolated berries are subject to <i>peah</i>, but that the olive tree which is not subject to the rule of isolated berries is not subject to <i>peah</i>. Lo, the mention of the olive tree and of the vineyard is needed.
From<sup class="footnote-marker">259</sup><i class="footnote">This is a second version of the proof that the two verses are really needed, that they are not “two parallel laws” which by their parallelism indicate that they express only these two cases and no general principle. The argument is: If the mention of <i>peah</i> for the olive tree alone would allow one to infer the duty of <i>peah</i> for all trees then from the verse in <i>Lev</i>. the duty of not collecting single fruits would apply to all trees. That is patent nonsense since most trees do not produce bunches of fruit but only isolated fruits. The sentence in parenthesis is dittography, or it may have been an attempt to explain the argument in a marginal note which subsequently entered the text.</i> the olive tree one may infer <i>peah</i> for all trees. From the vineyard one should infer the rule of isolated berries for all trees. (Just as from the olive tree one infers <i>peah</i> for all trees so one should infer the rule of isolated berries for all trees from the vineyard.) Rebbi Abun said, any property that is common to both yields an inference but any property that is [not<sup class="footnote-marker">260</sup><i class="footnote">Missing in the Venice print but clearly a scribal error already corrected by R. S. Cirillo.</i>] common to both yields no inference<sup class="footnote-marker">261</sup><i class="footnote">Since it was established above that both verses are necessary, neither of them by itself would have a generally valid inference.</i>.
It is fine according to the opinion of Rebbi Ismael. Since Rebbi Ismael established: Everything that was in a set and left the set to be applied to a new rule, is removed from the set and forms a new one<sup class="footnote-marker">262</sup><i class="footnote">This is the formulation of the Yerushalmi. In the Babylonian version, (Introduction to <i>Sifra</i> and <a class="refLink" data-ref="Zevachim 49a" href="/Zevachim.49a"><i>Zebaḥim</i> 49a</a>), the reading is: “Anything that was in a set and was taken out for an additional law, cannot be returned to its set except if the verse returns it explicitly.” The example there is about different kinds of sacrifices (חטאת, אשם) which in some respect follow the same rules, while in other aspects they do not. Therefore, a special verse is needed for taking the two kinds together to imply that they form one set of sacrifices with common rules. Here, the rule of the single berry clearly separates the vine from all other trees. Hence, it is necessary that there be a separate verse to apply the rule of <i>peah</i> to vines.</i>; it is necessary that <i>peah</i> should be mentioned for the vineyard. But for the rabbis<sup class="footnote-marker">263</sup><i class="footnote">These rabbis are not mentioned in the Babli.</i> who say, it remains in its set and keeps its special status, why is <i>peah</i> mentioned for the vineyard? Rebbi Abin said, if only the vineyard were singled out, the question would be valid. But now that vineyard and olive tree are singled out<sup class="footnote-marker">264</sup><i class="footnote">For the rule of <i>peah</i>, which is not mentioned for any other tree.</i>, if it would mention olive tree but not vineyard, I would say that the olive tree, which is not subject to the rule of isolated berries<sup class="footnote-marker">265</sup><i class="footnote">Reading of the Rome manuscript. The Leyden manuscript and Venice print have “<i>peah</i>” by an obvious scribal error.</i>, is subject to <i>peah</i>, but that vines, which are subject to the rule of isolated berries, are not subject to <i>peah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">266</sup><i class="footnote">While this argument was brought earlier, the situation is slightly different here.</i>.
Just<sup class="footnote-marker">267</sup><i class="footnote">Here starts the discussion of the conditions imposed on produce that should be subject to <i>peah</i>.</i> as you say for the harvest that its collection must be at one time in order to qualify for <i>peah</i>, should one say that the same is true for the laborer<sup class="footnote-marker">268</sup><i class="footnote">The hired agricultural laborer, who is poor, may eat while harvesting or working in the field. May he eat only from fruits that qualify for <i>peah</i>, reserved for the poor?</i>? Rebbi Jonah said, there is a difference since it says (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 23:26" href="/Deuteronomy.23.26"><i>Deut.</i>23:26</a>): “You may pluck rubbed ears,” even of something that is not stored<sup class="footnote-marker">269</sup><i class="footnote">Both conditions, that certain produce is harvested at one time, and that it usually is harvested for storage (or, in the case of wine and oil, is produced for storage), are necessary conditions for <i>peah</i>. Since rubbed ears are not stored, the verse makes a point to extend the right of the farmhand.</i>.
It was stated (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Peah (Lieberman) 1:7" href="/Tosefta_Peah_(Lieberman).1.7">Tosephta <i>Peah</i> 1:7</a>): “Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Jehudah says<sup class="footnote-marker">270</sup><i class="footnote">Since this is quoted in his name, it follows that it is not generally accepted like an anonymous statement.</i>, moist dates<sup class="footnote-marker">271</sup><i class="footnote">Arabic רֻטַב, “moist and ripe dates.” The author of <i>Kaphtor Waperaḥ</i> (Chapter 52, p. 706) notes that these dates are also called in Arabic בַּלַח, but that means “unripe dates”, the verbal root בלח means “to be dry”, the opposite of רטב.</i> are free from <i>peah</i> because the first among them does not wait for the last one. “Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Jehudah seems to say what is correct, what is the reason of the rabbis? Rebbi Zeïra said, because they all need moisture at the same time<sup class="footnote-marker">272</sup><i class="footnote">Fruit becomes eligible for the rules of the gifts to the poor when it becomes fruit, and for dates that is the moment where moisture appears around the pit, so that it can be separated from the fruit. R. Zeïra asserts that, while these dates ripen outwardly at separate times, the ripening process starts at the same time for all of them and they could be harvested and left to ripen in storage after the moisture first appears. Maimonides follows the rabbis in practice.</i>.
Rebbi Isaac ben Ḥaqolah and Rebbi Joshua ben Levi both say taro<sup class="footnote-marker">274</sup><i class="footnote">Colocasia, Greek κολοκασία, Arabic קֻלקָאס. Its root is used to produce a kind of flour in Africa; hence, it is an intermediate between a vegetable and a legume. The only problem is that of vows (e. g., if a person makes a vow to abstain from vegetables), since in matters of vows one does not follow technical usage but the meaning in local dialects. Hence, there may be places where taro is commonly subsumed under vegetables and other places where it is not.</i> is like a vegetable for tithes, the sabbatical year, <i>peah</i>, and first fruits. For vows it is questionable.

Halakhah 5

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> Forever<sup class="footnote-marker">275</sup><i class="footnote">A person who cuts his entire field without leaving <i>peah</i> has later to give <i>peah</i> to the poor. “Smoothing" is a technical term that designates the end of processing the grain harvest, when after threshing and cleaning from chaff, the grain is put into orderly heaps to be stored. The Mishnah states that the obligation of giving <i>peah</i> must be fulfilled as long as the relevant produce is in the hand of the farmer, but if it is given before smoothing then it is free from <i>terumah</i> and the two tithes. If <i>peah</i> is given later, the farmer has first to give <i>terumah</i> and tithes before he can discharge his obligation for <i>peah</i>. [In the Mishnah manuscripts there is a second sentence, that he who declares part of his crop as abandoned is freed from the relative <i>terumah</i> and tithes if it is abandoned before smoothing. This sentence is missing in the Yerushalmi and in the Munich manuscript of the Babli.]<br>The Halakhah takes the expression: “Forever he gives,” as meaning that he can give under the rules of <i>peah</i> until he has smoothed the heap, even if he gave <i>peah</i> from grain standing on the field, and avoid the obligation of <i>terumah</i> and tithes for that amount. That seems to contradict our understanding of Mishnah 3.</i> he gives as <i>peah</i> and it is free from tithes until he smoothes. He may take from the threshing floor and sow and it is free from tithes until he smoothes<sup class="footnote-marker">276</sup><i class="footnote">Since seed grain is not food.</i>. And he feeds domestic animals, wild animals, and fowl and is free from tithes until he smoothes, the words of Rebbi Aqiba<sup class="footnote-marker">277</sup><i class="footnote">In the Mishnah manuscripts the sentence is anonymous; the previous one about seed grain is attributed to R. Aqiba. From the Halakhah later on (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Peah 1:5:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Peah.1.5.4">Note 287</a>) it will be clear that the original Mishnah in the Yerushalmi also attributed only the statement about seed grain to R. Aqiba.</i>. If a Cohen or a Levite bought all the grain of a threshing floor, the tithes belong to them until it was smoothed<sup class="footnote-marker">278</sup><i class="footnote">There is nothing in the Torah which states that a Cohen could not give his own <i>terumah</i> and tithes to himself. But the rabbis ordained that Cohen and Levite have to give their tithes away in order to create a level playing field, lest Cohen and Levite farmers could drive the others out of business since they have a built-in 10% advantage in costs over the non-Levitic farmer. In the opinion of Maimonides, the rule was established in order to prevent the monopolizing of tithes by a few wealthy priests. However, if a priest bought produce before it was obligated for tithes, he may keep the tithe but not <i>terumah</i>.</i>. He who dedicates<sup class="footnote-marker">279</sup><i class="footnote">His crop or his field.</i> to the Temple and then redeems is obligated for tithes unless it was smoothed by the Temple administrator<sup class="footnote-marker">280</sup><i class="footnote">Temple property is not subject to the laws of tithes. Hence, if the obligation of giving <i>terumah</i> and tithes arose in private properly, it must be fulfilled; but if it arises in the property of the Temple, it does not apply even if the produce is later sold to private individuals.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> Rebbi Yose, Rebbi Jacob bar Zabdi in the name of Rebbi Abbahu, Rebbi Nehemiaḥ bar Uqban turned it in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: If someone separated first fruits from a smoothed heap<sup class="footnote-marker">281</sup><i class="footnote">Which now falls under the obligation of separating <i>terumah</i> and the first tithe. (While the first tithe is a civil obligation of the farmer towards the Levite, the <i>terumah</i> of the tithe is a religious obligation and it is a deadly sin to eat from the produce as long as that <i>terumah</i> of the tithe is still not separated from the rest of the grain. The first <i>terumah</i> is called the <i>great terumah</i>.) It is now asserted that First Fruits, which are brought to the Temple and will be eaten there by the priests, are not subject to <i>terumah</i> even though <i>peah</i>, which is eaten by the poor, can no longer be separated from the heap before the farmer separates the two <i>terumot</i> from his grain.</i>, it is free from the great <i>terumah</i>. Rebbi Ḥaggai said before Rebbi Yose: Does not the Mishnah say so: “Forever he gives <i>peah</i> and it is free from tithes until he smoothes.” Hence, if he smoothed he is obligated, but this does not apply to <i>terumah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">282</sup><i class="footnote">Since the Mishnah speaks only of the obligation of tithes, not <i>terumah</i>.</i>? It should say that he also is obligated for first fruits even though he did not smooth yet<sup class="footnote-marker">283</sup><i class="footnote">This is the answer of R. Yose to R. Haggai, refuting his argument: If the Mishnah is supposed to mention every detail, it certainly should have mentioned First Fruits since they are really supposed to be designated when grain or fruits are still on field or tree. Hence, “tithes” is a catchword for all obligations and the Mishnah does not prove anything for the rule under discussion.</i>! But he<sup class="footnote-marker">284</sup><i class="footnote">Rebbi Abbahu or Rebbi Joḥanan, whoever is the author of the statement about First Fruits, referred to the Mishnah <i>Terumot</i> 3:7: “From where do we know that First Fruits precede <i>terumah</i>? Each of them is called (in the Torah) <i>terumah</i> and <i>beginning</i>’, but First Fruits should precede everything because they are first before everything.”</i> is the one who said this: “Why are they called firstlings? Because they are first before everything else.” And anything which precedes something else, the latter is obligated for it<sup class="footnote-marker">285</sup><i class="footnote">This is a general principle which will be discussed at length in <i>Demai</i> 5:1. The fixed order of Biblical obligations is First Fruits (where applicable), <i>terumah</i>, First Tithe, Second Tithe. Hence, if somebody gave First Tithe before he gave <i>terumah</i>, he has to separate <i>terumah</i> from this First Tithe and he may not include <i>terumah</i> for the First Tithe in the <i>terumah</i> that he will give from the remaining grain, but <i>terumah</i> carries no obligation of tithe. As a consequence, First Fruits carry no obligation of <i>terumah</i> and even the fact that the heap now is under the obligation of <i>terumah</i> and tithes has no influence. This is the justification of R. Joḥanan/Abbahu.</i>.
Our Mishnah<sup class="footnote-marker">286</sup><i class="footnote">The Mishnah declares that “forever one may give as <i>peah</i>,” meaning even after the prescribed 1 2/3% have been given. But earlier, it was agreed that once this amount is given, there is no more <i>peah</i> to be given separately. Hence, one must consider that the amount given before the heap was smoothed was given as property abandoned to the poor. Even for the House of Shammai, calling this <i>peah</i> strictly speaking would be a misnomer but, since for the House of Shammai property abandoned to the poor follows exactly all the rules of <i>peah</i>, it is free from all obligation of <i>terumah</i> and tithes; this is an admissible freedom in the use of language.</i> is from the House of Shammai, since the House of Shammai say (Mishnah 6:1): “Property abandoned to the poor is ownerless property.”
It turns out that Rebbi Aqiba<sup class="footnote-marker">287</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to the part of the Mishnah in which R. Aqiba permits the use of grain for seeds and animal feed without the duty of tithing. While R. Aqiba disagrees with the practice of the Bene Ḥanun, he will agree that tithes are applicable only to produce used for human food.</i> is like the stores of Bene Ḥanun as it has been stated<sup class="footnote-marker">288</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sifra Deut.</i> #105, quoted in Babli <i>Bava Meẓia</i>‘ 88a where the name of the locality is corrupted to Bet Hini. It seems that these stores were located at or near the Mount of Olives and were destroyed at the start of the Roman siege of Jerusalem.</i>: Why were the stores of Bene Hanun destroyed three years before the destruction of the Temple? Because they eliminated produce from the duty of tithes since they were inferring (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 14:22" href="/Deuteronomy.14.22"><i>Deut</i>. 14:22</a>) “<i>You</i> should certainly give tithes<sup class="footnote-marker">289</sup><i class="footnote">The verse reads: “<i>You</i> should certainly give tithes from all produce of <i>your</i> seed.” Since the buyer did not sow, he is not included in the command. (As usual, only the first words of the verse are quoted; this never implies that the inference necessarily is from these words.)</i>,” that excludes the buyer, (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 14:23" href="/Deuteronomy.14.23"><i>Deut.</i> 14:23</a>) <i>“You</i> should eat,” that excludes the vendor<sup class="footnote-marker">290</sup><i class="footnote">This excludes the farmer who sells his produce which then he cannot eat. The people of Bene Ḥanun were punished since the Jewish people under Ezra and Nehemiah had accepted the duty to give tithes and observe the laws of the Land in their rabbinic interpretation (<i>Shevi‘it</i> 6:1, fol. 36b). Rebbi Aqiba will agree that according to all interpretations, produce that is not used as food is freed from the obligations.</i>.
Rebbi Joḥanan said, they fined them so that they should not run after wine presses and threshing floors<sup class="footnote-marker">291</sup><i class="footnote">This sentence refers to the statement of the Mishnah that a Cohen or a Levite, who had received or bought grain before it was made a staple in a smooth heap, has to give <i>terumah</i> to another Levite or Cohen, even though from the Torah there seems to be no reason to assume that the Cohen cannot eat his own <i>terumah</i>. The reason given is that we do not want Cohen or Levi to go and help with the harvest in order to exert moral pressure on the farmer to give <i>terumah</i> and tithes to him personally, since the Torah declares (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 5:10" href="/Numbers.5.10"><i>Num.</i> 5:10</a>): “Everybody shall have control over his own Holy Things.” The prohibition for a Cohen to exert pressure to force a non-Cohen to give him the required gifts is emphasized in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Demai (Lieberman) 5:20" href="/Tosefta_Demai_(Lieberman).5.20">Tosephta (<i>Demai</i>5:20</a>) and in both Talmudim stating that <i>terumah</i> and tithes may not be given to the Cohen or Levi as payment for helping in the harvest.<br>One does not really talk about fines here but about preemptive rules to avoid monopolization of priestly emoluments in the hands of a few individuals. The same reasoning applies to the Cohen butcher in the next paragraph.</i>.
A butcher who is a Cohen<sup class="footnote-marker">292</sup><i class="footnote">Following <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 18:3" href="/Deuteronomy.18.3"><i>Deut</i> 18:3</a>, the Cohen has to receive one front leg, the lower jaw, and the rennet bag from every slaughtered animal. Butcher here means a person who buys animals, slaughters them and then sells their meat. From the Torah, the Cohen might give the dues to himself. But as rabbinic decree this is allowed only during the first week after opening the store; all other parts destined for Cohanim he has to give away to other Cohanim.</i>. The colleagues in the name of Rebbi Joshua ben Levi: One frees him for one week. Rebbi Yose said, I went to the South and I heard Rebbi Hanan, the father of Rebbi Simeon<sup class="footnote-marker">293</sup><i class="footnote">Neither Rebbi Ḥanan nor Rebbi Simeon are known otherwise from rabbinic literature; probably the name is garbled and the next “R. Simeon” should be “R. Joshua” as indicated in the translation.</i> in the name of Rebbi (Simeon) [Joshua] ben Levi: One frees him for one week. Rebbi Yudan compares it to the statement of Rebbi Joḥanan, they fined them. Rebbi Yose said to him, if it is because of a fine, even one week should not be free for him<sup class="footnote-marker">294</sup><i class="footnote">And nobody ever prohibited Cohanim from being butchers. [R. Joseph mentioned in the text is R. Yose, the colleague of R. Yudan.]</i>. A grocer<sup class="footnote-marker">295</sup><i class="footnote">Who buys meat from a wholesale butcher to sell it in his retail store.</i> who is a Cohen, Rebbi Yudan said, one frees him for one week. Rebbi Yose said, one does not free him for one week. According to Rebbi Yose, what is the difference between the grocer and the butcher? The grocer may cheat<sup class="footnote-marker">296</sup><i class="footnote">By mixing meat from a Cohen butcher with that from non-Cohen butchers he may make his customers believe that all meat is from a Cohen butcher and, therefore, is exempt from the gifts to the Cohen. But the butcher sells only meat that he slaughtered himself and, therefore, everybody knows the amount of his obligation. Hence, since from the Torah he may take the gifts for himself, he may take them for one week.</i>, the butcher cannot cheat.
It was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">297</sup><i class="footnote">Usually, “stated” refers to a tannaïtic statement. The statement here is amoraïc; it does not refer to any tannaïtic source.</i>: Rebbi<sup class="footnote-marker">298</sup><i class="footnote">The Patriarch R. Jehudah I, in the Babli usually called “R. Jehudah the Prince”. R. Jehudah the Prince is the Patriarch R. Jehudah II, grandson of R. Jehudah I and Amora of the second generation. In the Babli, he usually is referred to as ר יהוּדה נשׂיאה.</i> and Rebbi Jehudah the Prince did not disagree about produce bought on the field from a Gentile that it is subject to tithes because the Jew takes his<sup class="footnote-marker">299</sup><i class="footnote">The Gentile’s place. Since the harvest determines the obligations and the harvest was Jewish, the obligations of Jewish law fall on the harvest.</i> place, nor about harvested produce bought from the Gentile that it is free from tithes. What did they disagree about? About him who buys harvested produce from his fellow in a year of tithes for the poor<sup class="footnote-marker">300</sup><i class="footnote">Second Tithe and tithe of the poor were defined in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Peah 1:1:30" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Peah.1.1.30">Note 127</a>. The definition of “poor” for eligibility of tithe and public assistance will be given in the last chapter.</i>. Rebbi Judah the Prince said, one takes it from him whether he be poor or rich. But Rebbi said, one takes it from the rich, one does not take it from the poor<sup class="footnote-marker">301</sup><i class="footnote">In contrast to the Cohen, the poor man who separates the tithe of the poor from the grain may then repossess it, not as owner but as poor person.</i>. What is the reason of Rebbi Jehudah the Prince? Just as nobody has any rights on the single stalks, the forgotten sheaves, and the <i>peah</i> of his own field<sup class="footnote-marker">302</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah 5:4. However, that Mishnah also denies the sharecropper the right to the tithe of the poor, hence the argument attributed to Rebbi Jehudah the Prince is redundant. While this is not stated explicitly, it is implicit in the fact that one looks for a second reason to sustain R. Jehudah’s ruling.</i>, so he should have no right to his tithe for the poor. What is the reason of Rebbi? Single stalks, the forgotten sheaves, and <i>peah</i> do not cause <i>tebel</i><sup class="footnote-marker">303</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ṭebel</i> is a technical term of unknown etymology which designates produce under obligation of <i>terumah</i> and tithes from which <i>terumah</i> has not been taken in both forms. To eat such produce is a deadly sin. It is stated in <i>Demai</i> 4:3 that eating produce under obligation for the tithe of the poor similarly is a deadly sin.</i>, but the tithe for the poor creates <i>ṭebel</i>. It became <i>ṭebel</i> already in the possession of the first owner<sup class="footnote-marker">304</sup><i class="footnote">Since the obligation for the tithes of the poor is created by the act of harvesting, the corresponding amount is no longer a property of the farmer and cannot be sold; the buyer cannot acquire it even if he is poor! Hence, the argument attributed to Rebbi does not hold.</i>! What is the reason of Rebbi Jehudah the Prince? Because of a fine<sup class="footnote-marker">305</sup><i class="footnote">That poor people should not distort the market to the detriment of farmers because they get 10% more value for their money in buying grain than rich people.</i>! What is the reason of Rebbi? Only the rich person has the power to buy<sup class="footnote-marker">306</sup><i class="footnote">There will be no distortion of markets because the poor neither have the money nor (as explained in the next sentence) can they find credit for such buys. [It might be that loans were more readily taken on in the time of R. Jehudah the Prince, in the inflationary environment of the military anarchy, than in the time of Rebbi, when currency and prices were stable under the Severan dynasty.]</i> ; it is not in the power of the poor to buy. As Rebbi Jehudah the Prince says, he may take a loan; but Rebbi says that he will not be able to borrow.

Chapter 2



Halakhah 1

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> The following separate<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">One may not give one <i>peah</i> for two fields separated by one of the enumerated obstacles.</i> regarding <i>peah</i>: The river<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">The Halakhah later makes it clear that one speaks of water even though the Biblical word נחל may also mean (dry) wady.</i>, the water canal<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">An aqueduct for drinking water or a water conduit that brings water from a river or lake to smaller irrigation canals. The irrigation canal that serves a field directly does not create an interruption.</i>, a private road, a public road, a private path<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">A path has no minimal size, but it is required that it should be in use permanently, even in the rainy season when fields are newly seeded. Paths in use only as shortcuts on fields that have been harvested do not count. The Halakhah will discuss why public roads and paths have to be mentioned when private paths and roads already make an interruption. (In most Mishnah manuscripts, private paths are mentioned after public ones, so that the singular הקבוע applies only to private paths. The discussion will show that this is not the Yerushalmi tradition.) In the opinion of Maimonides, private roads are at least 4 cubits wide, public roads at least 16 cubits, while paths are narrower. In this he adopts the definitions of the Babli. In the opinion of R. Abraham ben David, roads are used for travelling, paths for local agricultural use, independent of their width. This latter opinion agrees better with the discussions in the Yerushalmi.</i> or a public path open both summer and winter, a fallow field, a ploughed field<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">A field that has been harvested and the stubbles ploughed under while on both sides of it grain is still standing. This creates two distinct fields for purposes of <i>peah</i>.</i> and [another kind of]<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">The word אחר translated in brackets is missing in the Mishnah in the Talmud and in one of the Tosephta manuscripts (1:8), but it appears in the separate Mishnah manuscripts and in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sifra, Kedoshim" href="/Sifra,_Kedoshim"><i>Sifra Qedošim</i> 2:1</a>, and it is certainly understood here. Two wheat fields separated by a field of peas require two separate <i>peot</i>.</i> seed. He who cuts<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">Unripe grain. R. Meïr considers grain grown for animal feed a kind different from grain grown for human consumption, probably because it is exempt from <i>peah</i>.</i> for animal feed makes a separation, the words of Rebbi Meïr; but the sages say it does not create a separation unless he ploughed.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “The following separate regarding <i>peah</i>.” Because it has been said (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 19:9" href="/Leviticus.19.9"><i>Lev</i>. 19:9</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 23:22" href="/Leviticus.23.22">23:22</a>): “Your field<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">In the singular, cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sifra, Kedoshim" href="/Sifra,_Kedoshim"><i>Sifra</i> Qedošim 1:22</a>.</i>,” that he should not give from one field for another.
It cannot be connected<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">One now deals with the features that separate a field from its neighbor, such as a water canal. The problem is whether there must be a clean and total separation of two fields, or whether canals, ponds, fences, and paths separate even if they cover only parts of the boundary. The discussion centers on fences, because only for fences can we find contradictory tannaitic statements.</i>.
