If you would say that it is connected, it should separate even a field of trees<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">An orchard where trees are planted far enough apart that a plough drawn by a pair of oxen may pass between them.</i>, but we have stated there (Mishnah 3): “Everything separates for seeds but only a fence separates for trees.” Hence, by a fence it is connected and not connected! If you say it is connected, even hair pressing down<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">This is the expression of the Mishnah. A fence is no interruption if on both sides there grow trees, their branches (the “hairs”) are meshed together, and the lowest branches from both sides lie on the fence (which also may be a wall.)</i> does not separate, since we have stated: “Hair pressing down does not separate but he gives one <i>peah</i> for all!” That means that it is not connected. If you say it is connected then it should separate if it is one-sided, as we have stated<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">In a <i>baraita</i>.</i>: “If the hair was pressing down from both sides it does not separate.” Hence, from one side only it separates<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">Hence, it is undecided whether the role of the fence as divider is undisputed (unless there are “hairs” from both sides, last argument) or whether it is not better than those of the Mishnah (first argument). It is not unusual that the Yerushalmi leaves questions of this kind undecided.</i>
Rebbi Yose in the name of Rebbi Yose bar Ḥanina: If someone gave <i>peah</i> from one field for another one<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">The other field being separated from the first by one of the obstacles enumerated in the Mishnah.</i>, it does not acquire holiness<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">It is not <i>peah</i> and, hence, subject to the laws of <i>terumah</i> and tithes.</i>.
Rebbi Zeïra asked before Rebbi Yasa: If he intended to let them acquire from the boundary strip<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">Every field is surrounded by a strip, mostly of grass, usually slightly elevated over the cultivated area. On this strip one may walk between fields and also deposit agricultural implements. Rebbi Zeïra asks what is the rule if some grain grew on the boundary strip and the owner of the field wants to fulfill his duty by abandoning this grain which cannot usually be harvested. Rebbi Yasa (Rebbi Assi in the Babli) avoided answering, perhaps because he did not know the answer.<br>There is a similar problem in <i>Baba Batra</i>, dealing with real estate left by a convert to Judaism who failed to start a Jewish family. Since by conversion the convert severed his ties to his former family, in the absence of a will his property becomes ownerless at his death and can be acquired by actual possession. Then the question is whether two adjacent fields can be acquired if one performs an act of possession (such as weeding) on one of them. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Batra 3:1:2-7" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Batra.3.1.2-7">Yerushalmi (<i>Baba Batra</i> 3:1,</a> fol. 13d), R. Yasa states in the name of R. Joḥanan that if one weeded on one of two adjacent fields with the intention of thereby acquiring both fields but did not have the explicit intent to acquire the boundary strip also, he acquired only the field he weeded but no more. Then it is reported that R. Zeïra asked R. Yasa about somebody working on the boundary strip only (whether he would acquire both fields), and R. Yasa avoided answering. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 55a" href="/Bava_Batra.55a">Babli (<i>Baba Batra</i> 55a</a>), R. Assi states in the name of R. Joḥanan that boundary strip and <i>ḥaẓab</i> are dividers in respect to the property of a convert but not for <i>peah</i> whereas Ravin (Rebbi Abun) declares in the name of R. Joḥanan that it is not a divider for <i>peah</i>. For the Yerushalmi, R. Assi himself was not sure whether R. Joḥanan followed the opinion ascribed to himself in the Babli, or that ascribed to R. Abun.</i>? He avoided answering him. The opinions of Rebbi Yasa are contradictory since we have stated there (Mishnah 3:2): “The sages agree with Rebbi Aqiba about someone who sowed dill or mustard in three places<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">The Mishnah deals with someone who spot-harvests his field, cutting at separate places and letting the rest grow for an extended period of time. Rebbi Aqiba requires that each time and at each place <i>peah</i> be given, but the sages require only one <i>peah</i> for the whole field, except for (expensive) spices.</i>.” (The Mishnah means: dill in three places or mustard in three places.) Samuel said, because the first of them does not wait for the last<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">Obviously, one plot is harvested at one time; otherwise, there would be no obligation for <i>peah</i>. Samuel asserts that spice plants can be harvested at greatly varying times.</i>. Rebbi Yasa in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: Because they are usually sown in separate beds<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">Spices are never grown in large fields since they belong to intensive agriculture.</i>. And here he says so<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">In fact, he refuses to say what he should say, viz., that the boundary strip is never sown intentionally and therefore cannot be counted as <i>peah</i>.</i>? It is obvious for him that it separates<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">This is the opinion ascribed to R. Abun in the Babli.</i>. What he could not decide is whether it does become sanctified as <i>peah</i> or maybe it does not become sanctified<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">Since <i>peah</i> is exempt from <i>terumah</i> and tithes, the status of the grain growing on the boundary strip remains in limbo.</i>! Rebbi Hoshaiah stated: If someone gave <i>peah</i> from one field for another one, it does not acquire holiness. One understands that this means even the boundary strip<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">Again supporting the opinion ascribed to R. Abun in the Babli. (In the Babli, Rabin is reputed to give the correct interpretations of sentences of R. Joḥanan.)</i>.
Rebbi Yose ben Ḥanina said, rue<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">The identification of חצוּב with rue or harmel (Arabic ח̇יל) is from the Gaonic commentary to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Kelim 3:6" href="/Mishnah_Kelim.3.6">Mishnah <i>Kelim</i> 3:6</a>. In modern Hebrew, following Loew and Ben Jehudah, the word denotes the sea onion, sea leek (urginea). It is more likely that bushes such as rue were used as boundary markers rather than bulbous plants whose dispersion by seeds is beyond control. (In Arabic, ח̇צ̇ב is simply “greenery”).</i> separates for <i>peah</i>. Rav Ḥisda investigated whether they were used by Joshua to divide the Land. Rebbi Ḥanina asserted in the name of Rav Ḥisda: they were used by Joshua to fix the boundaries of the Land.
A<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">Here starts the discussion of the terms used in the Mishnah.</i> water canal, whenever it is flowing. A river even though it does not flow<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">The meaning seems to be that artificial water canals separate only if there is water flowing in them whereas natural waters separate both flowing and stagnant</i>.
If<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">The Venice print has נתכװן מכיון which is redundant. All commentators delete the first word as unintelligible but the Rome manuscript shows that the second word has to be deleted and that there is a slight shift of emphasis from the first to the second question.</i> it is correct that we have stated “a private road,” what is the need for “a public road?<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">If a private road separates, the mention of a public road is redundant; it might be that there should be no mention of the private road. The answer is that for fields, the mention of public roads is redundant but that it is needed in Mishnah 3 where it is stated that all obstacles mentioned in Mishnah 1 are not separating for trees.</i>” I tell you, even a public road does not separate for trees, only a fence does. Since we have stated “a private path,” what is the need for “a public path?” To exclude one that is permanent in summer<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">I. e., used regularly every summer through many years. Nevertheless, if it is not used during the plowing and planting period it does not count as separation.</i> but is not permanent in winter.
Rav said<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">Here starts the discussion of how large the interruption between two crops of the same kind must be so that two separate <i>peot</i> are due.</i>: Fallow land and ploughed land of a bet <i>rova‘</i><sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">In general, surface area measurements are given in the Talmudim by the amount of seed grain needed for the area in cultivation. The measurements are standardized by the tradition that the courtyard of the Tabernacle, which was 50 by 100 cubits (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 27:18" href="/Exodus.27.18"><i>Ex</i>. 27:18</a>), defined the area covered by two <i>seah</i>. One <i>seah</i> are six <i>qab</i>. Hence, the area covered by a quarter <i>qab</i>, the bet <i>rova‘</i>, is an area of 5000:48 = 104 1/6 square cubits. A cubit was at least 45 and at most 61 cm, probably 54.6 cm.</i>, other produce<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">For example, if two fields of wheat are separated by a strip of barley.</i> even the tiniest amount. Rebbi Joḥanan said: Fallow land, ploughed land, and other produce at three preliminary furrows<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">Three wide parallel furrows. According to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Isaiah 28:24" href="/Isaiah.28.24">Rashi (<i>Is</i>. 28:24</a>), in ploughing an uncultivated field one first ploughs wide and coarse “starter” furrows; for sowing one then ploughs narrow ones adapted to the particular seed. According to Maimonides (<i>Kilaim</i> 3:2), the coarse furrows serve to cover breaks in the soil which developed during the heat of summer. There is no practical difference between the two explanations. In any case, the total width of the three furrows cannot be larger than two cubits.</i>. Do they disagree<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">Is it necessary to assume that Rav and R. Joḥanan present different traditions? One tries to avoid disagreements over basic measurements as much as possible.</i>? What Rav said<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">In matters of the separating produce only.</i> refers to produce subject to <i>peah</i>, what Rebbi Joḥanan said refers to produce not subject to <i>peah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">In the next paragraph it will be explained that, at least for Rebbis Meïr and Jehudah, plots subject to the obligation of <i>peah</i> are more powerful to interrupt between fields of the same kind than those not subject to it.</i>. But did we not state that fallow or ploughed land is subject to <i>peah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">At least for the Sages who disagree with R. Meïr in the Mishnah. Since here Rav and R. Joḥanan give different measurements for identical situations, they seem to disagree.</i>? What Rav said refers to an average field, what Rebbi Joḥanan said refers to a plot 50 by 2<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">If the entire field, in this example 100 square cubits, is smaller than a <i>bet rova‘</i>, the rule of Rav becomes inapplicable and must be replaced by a smaller limit.</i>.
Rebbi Zeïra in the name of Rebbi Eleazar: Both Rebbi Meïr<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">In our Mishnah, referring to <i>peah</i>.</i> and Rebbi Jehudah<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">In <a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Menachot 10:8" href="/Mishnah_Menachot.10.8">Mishnah <i>Menaḥot</i> 10:8</a>, dealing with the prohibition of using grain from the new harvest before the presentation of the <i>‘omer</i> on the 16th of Nisan. We insist that they follow the same principle in both cases eventhough we have no statement of R. Jehudah on <i>peah</i> and no statement of R. Meïr about cutting green grain as animal fodder.</i> said the same. Just as Rebbi Meïr said that he who cuts for fodder separates, so Rebbi Jehudah will say that he who cuts for fodder separates. Just as Rebbi Jehudah said that an obligation for <i>peah</i> separates<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">This statement is not obvious and has to be proven later.</i>, so Rebbi Meïr will say that an obligation for <i>peah</i> separates. But we find that Rebbi Meïr says that an obligation for <i>peah</i> separates<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">From the Tosephta it will be deduced that everybody, including Rebbi Meïr, agrees that an independent obligation of <i>peah</i> between two fields separates the two fields into two entities from each of which one must give <i>peah</i>.</i> since we have stated<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Peah (Lieberman) 1:8" href="/Tosefta_Peah_(Lieberman).1.8">Tosephta <i>Peah</i> 1:8</a>. A field on which grain grows but which is destroyed during growth is not subject to the obligation of <i>peah</i>.</i>: “If locusts ate it, ants undermined it, or a storm or animals broke it, it is free<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. Mishnah 2:7.</i>; everybody agrees that if he ploughed<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">If he ploughed under the empty stalks remaining after the disaster. “It” in this sentence refers to the ruined field.</i> it separates, if he did not plough it does not interrupt<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">If only part of the field was destroyed it is as if the entire field was sown but part of the seed grain did not germinate; it therefore remains one field.</i>.” Who is “everybody”? Does not Rebbi Meïr say, if it is free from <i>peah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">Since it belongs to the same field and without the disaster there would not have been any separate obligation of <i>peah</i>, only separate ploughing will create two obligations. But if there were a separate obligation from the start, as in the example quoted next from R. Jehudah, then it seems that there are two obligations of <i>peah</i> created.</i>, but if it were obligated it would interrupt even if he did not plough. Parallel to what we stated there<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Menachot 10:8" href="/Mishnah_Menachot.10.8">Mishnah <i>Menaḥot</i> 10:8</a>. There is a Biblical prohibition on using new grain for human consumption before the 16th of Nisan (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 23:14" href="/Leviticus.23.14"><i>Lev.</i> 23:14</a>) and a rabbinical prohibition to harvest before that time. However, that rabbinical prohibition is waived either if it would cause monetary loss (as for fields in the region of Jericho that ripen very early) or if the grain is not used for humans. R. Simeon permits cutting for fodder anytime, R. Jehudah allows it only if the grain is less than one third ripe (since afterwards it is fit for human consumption as “green kernels”).</i>: “Rebbi Jehudah said, when? If he started before it was one-third ripe, but if it is one-third ripe one is forbidden to cut.” If he cuts<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote">After the grain is one-third ripened and the grain is potential human food. (“R. Yudan” here refers to R. Jehudah bar Illaï.)</i> he is obligated for <i>peah</i>. And anything that is obligated for <i>peah</i> separates<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote">If he harvested a strip of unripe grain in a field, he has to give <i>peah</i> from this strip. If that strip separated the remaining grain into two disjoint pieces, the two fields now are separately liable for <i>peah</i>.</i>.
Rebbi Zeïra questioned: Just as Rebbi Jehudah said, if he started before it was one-third ripe, even if he continued after it was one-third ripe he will be freed from collecting, forgotten sheaves, and <i>peah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote">R. Jehudah, in the Mishnah just quoted, only asks that one has to start cutting for fodder as long as the grain is not yet fit for human consumption, but not that one has to stop at one-third ripeness; it is clear that subsequent cutting for fodder does not turn the fodder into human food and, therefore, the farmer is free from the obligations of letting the poor collect the single stalks not bound in sheaves (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 19:9" href="/Leviticus.19.9"><i>Lev.</i> 19:9</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Ruth 2" href="/Ruth.2"><i>Ruth</i> 2</a>) and leaving sheaves forgotten on the field (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 24:19" href="/Deuteronomy.24.19"><i>Deut.</i> 24:19</a>), and <i>peah</i>.</i>, would Rebbi Meïr say that if he started before it was one-third ripe, even if he continued after it is one-third ripe he will be freed from collecting, forgotten sheaves, and <i>peah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">There is no logical necessity which would force R. Meïr to agree that wheat with edible green kernels does not constitute human food. The question is not answered since no other sources are available.</i>?

Halakhah 2

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> An irrigation canal that cannot be harvested together<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote">One cannot cut with one movement of a scythe on both banks of the irrigation canal. The exact description will be given in the Halakhah.</i>, Rebbi Jehudah says it separates. For all hills subject to weeding<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote">The language is from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Isaiah 7:25" href="/Isaiah.7.25"><i>Is</i>. 7:25</a>. It means as long as the obstacle can be used agriculturally, even if it cannot be worked with ploughs or other implements drawn by animals, it does not separate for <i>peah</i> even if such a ridge separates a field into two parts as far as mechanical farming is concerned.</i>, even if cattle can not pass by with their implements, he gives one <i>peah</i> for everything.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> They wanted to say that there is no disagreement<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote">The Mishnah does not mention any opinion differing from R. Jehudah. One might therefore think that it expresses a commonly held opinion even though in that case the Mishnah should have been anonymous.</i>. It was found stated: “A permanent irrigation canal separates<sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote">The Mishnah is defective. The parallel in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sifra, Kedoshim" href="/Sifra,_Kedoshim"><i>Sifra</i>,<i>Qedošim</i> 3:2</a> reads: “A permanent irrigation canal separates. Rebbi Jehudah says, if (it) [both banks of the canal] cannot be harvested together, it separates. For all hills subject to weeding, even if cattle cannot pass by with implements, he gives one <i>peah</i> for everything.” Hence, the majority of the Sages agree that a permanently installed irrigation canal separates for <i>peah</i> even if both banks can be cut at the same time; R. Jehudah’s opinion is in the minority.</i>”
They wanted to say: What Rebbi Jehudah talks about is one who stands on one bank and cannot cut on the other bank, but if he stands in the middle<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote">In the water of the irrigation canal.</i> and cuts on both banks it does not separate. It was found stated<sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Peah (Lieberman) 1:8" href="/Tosefta_Peah_(Lieberman).1.8"><i>Tosephta Peah</i> 1:8</a>: “An irrigation canal that cannot be harvested together, R. Jehudah says if he has to stand in the middle in order to harvest on both banks, it separates, otherwise, it does not separate.” The text before the Yerushalmi must have been similar.</i> in disagreement: “If he was standing in the middle and harvesting on both banks, it separates; on one bank it does not separate.”
Ḥiyya bar Ada in the name of Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish: If there was a rock along the length of his entire field<sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote">Bare rock, not a place for weeding or other agricultural work, not covered by the Mishnah. Here starts the discussion of the second sentence of the Mishnah.</i>, if he has to lift the plough from one side and transport it to the other side, it separates, from the other side<sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote">If he can slide the plough over the rock so that it does not lose contact with the ground.</i> it does not separate. But did we not state<sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Peah (Lieberman) 1:9" href="/Tosefta_Peah_(Lieberman).1.9">Tosephta <i>Peah</i> 1:9</a>, together with the quote at the end of the paragraph, forms one sentence there.</i>: “For terraces which are each ten hand-breadths<sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote">One cubit equals six hand-breadths. Ten handbreadths are about one yard difference from one terrace to the next.</i> higher than the other<sup class="footnote-marker">63</sup><i class="footnote">On a terraced hillside, if the agricultural machines have to be lifted up from one terrace to the next, they are distinct fields for <i>peah</i>.</i>, he must give <i>peah</i> for each one separately. Less than that he gives one <i>peah</i> for all of them.” But for less than that<sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote">A terrace wall less than three feet high.</i>, does he not have to lift the plough from one (terrace) and transport it to the other? Even for less than that he has to lift it. We had to state “ten” only because of the concluding statement: “If the heads of the terraces are connected, he gives one <i>peah</i> for all<sup class="footnote-marker">65</sup><i class="footnote">If on one side there is an incline which connects the different terraces, all counts as one field. One might think that the statement is unnecessary since we already know that anything which can be ploughed together requires one <i>peah</i> for all and the incline makes possible the ploughing without lifting the plough. However, in general we say that a height of 10 handbreadths creates a new domain. For example, regarding the laws of Sabbath, “public domain” in which one may not carry extends only up to a height of 10 handbreadths from the ground. One might have thought that a level difference of 10 handbreadths also creates a new domain for <i>peah</i>, but this is not the case.</i>.”

Halakhah 3

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> Everything separates for seeds but only a fence separates for trees. But if hair was pressing down it does not separate and he gives one <i>peah</i> for all<sup class="footnote-marker">66</sup><i class="footnote">This was explained at the beginning of Halakhah 1, notes 12–13.</i>.<br><sup class="footnote-marker">74</sup><i class="footnote">This Mishnah is the continuation of the preceding one.</i>: But for carob trees all that see one another<sup class="footnote-marker">75</sup><i class="footnote">According to Maimonides, all the carob trees a man can see standing next to one of them. According to R. Abraham ben David, all the carob trees a man can see while standing on top of one of them. According to the second opinion, fences are never a problem for carob trees.</i>. Rabban Gamliel<sup class="footnote-marker">76</sup><i class="footnote">Rabban Gamliel of Jabneh, speaking of the family of Hillel.</i> said, in my father’s house they used to give one <i>peah</i> for the olive trees they had in every direction<sup class="footnote-marker">77</sup><i class="footnote">Four <i>peot</i> for all olive trees in all four main directions.</i> and for all carobs that saw one another. Rebbi Eleazar ben Rebbi Ẓadoq said in the former’s name: also for all carob trees they had in the entire town.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “Everything separates for seeds<sup class="footnote-marker">67</sup><i class="footnote">Quote from the Mishnah. However, the discussion is purely about the second sentence in the Mishnah.</i>.” What is pressing down<sup class="footnote-marker">68</sup><i class="footnote">What is the meaning of the condition that two orchards separated by a fence are subject only to one obligation of <i>peah</i> if the leaves of trees of one orchard come down and lie across the fence to the other orchard? Does it mean that the leaves actually have to press on the fence or may they be in the air and just press down one branch on the other?</i>? It rises while pressing as in a mortar<sup class="footnote-marker">69</sup><i class="footnote">Rabbenu Simson in his commentary to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kilayim 5:3:2-4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kilayim.5.3.2-4"><i>Kilaim</i> 5:3</a> quotes the text here as כעלי במכתש “like a pestle in the mortar,” and explains, on the basis of the same language in <i>Kilaim</i>, that the branches have to press hard on the stone fence so as to create a dusty surface on the fence. Better is the interpretation of the uncorrected text, preferred by R. Moses Margalit, that the branches on both sides may be enmeshed but be higher than the fence, since the pestle is higher except for the short moments when it touches the bottom of the mortar.</i> or must it press down on the fence? From the text “hair was pressing down,” the fence does not press<sup class="footnote-marker">70</sup><i class="footnote">While the “hair” must lie on the fence, it need not act on it. There is no eroding pressure needed on the stone fence.</i>. That means it presses down on the fence.
Rebbi Mana said: Sometimes they need to be inside four cubits and sometimes they need to be inside ten handbreadths if they are planted ten to a <i>bet se‘ah</i>. How do you understand this? They<sup class="footnote-marker">71</sup><i class="footnote">The trees have to be planted close to the fence so that the stem of the tree whose branches lie on the fence is not farther than four cubits from the fence.</i> need to be within four cubits of the fence; the crowns have to be within ten handbreadths of one another<sup class="footnote-marker">72</sup><i class="footnote">In the entire orchard, the crown of any tree must have the crown of another tree within 10 handbreadths (1 2/3 cubits) distance.</i> if they are planted ten to a <i>bet se‘ah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">73</sup><i class="footnote">If the trees are planted in a regular pattern, each tree gets an area of 250 square cubits, or 15.82 cubits square. This means that the crown of each fruit tree should reach a diameter of slightly over 14.2 cubits.<br>Ten trees to a <i>bet se‘ah</i> are characteristic for a tree nursery (Mishnah <i>Sheviït</i> 1:7); adult trees form an orchard if a minimum of three trees are planted on a <i>bet se‘ah</i> (Mishnah <i>Sheviït</i> 1:2). Since Maimonides<i>does</i> not quote the rules of R. Mana in his code, he seems to consider them non-operative since trees that actually bear fruit are no longer in a tree nursery.</i>.
“But for carob trees all that see.” What defines the directions for them? The town defines the directions for them<sup class="footnote-marker">78</sup><i class="footnote">E, S, W, N are defined standing in the center of town. Chapter 5 of Mishnah <i>Erubin</i> explains that each town has to put up markers at the end of the Sabbath territory (2000 cubits from the last house of town) in the four cardinal directions.</i>. Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun said, the family of Rebbi had four oil presses in the four directions of the town and their used to give for each oil press according to its direction<sup class="footnote-marker">79</sup><i class="footnote">The prescribed percentage of the olives processed in each of the oil presses.</i>.
If there were three<sup class="footnote-marker">80</sup><i class="footnote">R. Simson of Sens reads: 30. This would explain the plural used for the three groups of trees.</i> (groups of) trees, the outer<sup class="footnote-marker">81</sup><i class="footnote">While all sources have here הראשונים “the first ones”, it is clear from the context that הראשים “the extremities” are meant.</i> ones see the middle ones, the middle ones see the outer ones, but the outer ones do not see one another, then one may give from the outer ones for the middle ones and from the middle ones for the outer ones but one may not give from the outer ones for the outer ones.

Halakhah 4

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> He who sows one species on his field gives one <i>peah</i> even though he works<sup class="footnote-marker">82</sup><i class="footnote">Literally: He makes two threshing floors. Not only the act of threshing is involved, but also the collection of threshed grain into orderly heaps. The best translation is: processing (grain on the stalk into a commercial commodity.)</i> at two different threshing floors. If he sows two species, he gives two <i>peot</i> even though he threshes them together<sup class="footnote-marker">83</sup><i class="footnote">And puts them together in the same heap.</i>. He who sows two different kinds of wheat on his field gives one <i>peah</i> if he processes them together<sup class="footnote-marker">84</sup><i class="footnote">That means that he treats them as one kind of wheat.</i>, two <i>peot</i> if he threshes them separately.<br>It happened that Rebbi Simeon from Miẓpah<sup class="footnote-marker">93</sup><i class="footnote">A Mishnah collector in the times of Rabban Gamliel I; one of a very small number of scholars who lived during Second Temple times who is always mentioned with the title “Rebbi.” {It is possible that before the destruction of the Temple, “Rebbi” did not designate a rabbi but a collector of legal statements.} Miẓpah probably is today’s Nebi Samwil, N. W. of Jerusalem.</i> sowed before Rabban Gamliel; they ascended to the stone hall<sup class="footnote-marker">94</sup><i class="footnote">The hall on the Temple Mount whose walls were formed by hewn stone and in which the high court held its sessions.</i> and asked. Naḥum the scribe<sup class="footnote-marker">95</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. Latin <i>libellaris</i>, “of books.” He was the clerk of Rabban Gamliel I’s court.</i> said: I have the tradition from Rebbi Miasha, who received it from my father, who received it from the pairs<sup class="footnote-marker">96</sup><i class="footnote">The pairs of authorities, chiefs and deputy chiefs of the high court in Jerusalem, who are mentioned in the first chapter of <i>Pirqe Avot</i>.</i>, who received if from the prophets<sup class="footnote-marker">97</sup><i class="footnote">Ḥaggai, Zachariah, Malachi, who by tradition are counted as members of the “Great Assembly.”</i>, a practice going back to Moses on Sinai, that he who sows two different kinds of wheat on his field gives one <i>peah</i> if he stores them together, two <i>peot</i> if he stores them separately.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> If he cut half the fine wheat<sup class="footnote-marker">85</sup><i class="footnote">The reading of the Venice print here is איגדו, that of the Rome manuscript אגידו. This kind of wheat is usually spelled אגרו, אגורי, אגרי; this is R. Simson’s spelling here. The spelling with <i>r</i> is probably correct. The term corresponds to Syriac איגורי דחיטתא “fine quality wheat” (Payne-Smith vol. I, col. 137). The entire exposition deals only with the case of a field sown with two kinds of wheat, as in the Mishnah.</i> and half the coarse wheat<sup class="footnote-marker">86</sup><i class="footnote">Everywhere else, this kind of wheat is called שחמתית. The elision of <i>ḥ</i> shows that these rules were formulated in the region between Haifa and Beth She‘an where ח was identified with ה. The root of the word is שחם “to be darkish” (Arabic سخم، سحم; cf. also شخم “spoiled”). This is the coarser kind of wheat.</i> to process them together and then he changed his mind and processed them separately he cannot give from the fine wheat for the coarse either the first time<sup class="footnote-marker">87</sup><i class="footnote">When he cuts half of the field, since we require that the poor have a clear sign when <i>peah</i> will be given and that is at the end of the total harvest.</i> or at the end<sup class="footnote-marker">88</sup><i class="footnote">Since actions count more than intentions.</i>. If he cut half the fine wheat and half the coarse wheat to process them separately and then he changed his mind and processed them together, he may give from the fine wheat at the end for the coarse wheat at the end, from the fine wheat the first time for the coarse wheat the first time<sup class="footnote-marker">89</sup><i class="footnote">Here also the action cancels the prior intention. He may not give from the coarse wheat for the fine since that would be stealing from the poor. He may give from the fine wheat for the coarse since he gives to the poor more than they would be entitled to.</i>. He gives from fine wheat for fine wheat if he finished harvesting his field, but if he did not finish harvesting, he may not give even from fine wheat for fine wheat<sup class="footnote-marker">90</sup><i class="footnote">As explained before, all <i>peah</i> has to be given at the end.</i>. If he cut half the fine wheat and all the coarse wheat to process them together and he changed his mind and processed them separately<sup class="footnote-marker">91</sup><i class="footnote">Not the fine and the coarse kinds separately but the first and the second harvests separately. In that case, since <i>peah</i> has to be given at the end, there is only fine wheat to be given (for the entire field) and the farmer is not obligated to separate <i>peah</i> from the coarse wheat kernels in his heap. {The emendations proposed by the commentators are unconvincing.}</i> then he gives from the fine wheat for the fine wheat and all the coarse wheat is exempted<sup class="footnote-marker">93</sup><i class="footnote">A Mishnah collector in the times of Rabban Gamliel I; one of a very small number of scholars who lived during Second Temple times who is always mentioned with the title “Rebbi.” {It is possible that before the destruction of the Temple, “Rebbi” did not designate a rabbi but a collector of legal statements.} Miẓpah probably is today’s Nebi Samwil, N. W. of Jerusalem.</i>.
Rebbi<sup class="footnote-marker">98</sup><i class="footnote">The entire following discussion on oral tradition is also in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Taanit 1:8:2-5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Taanit.1.8.2-5"><i>Taäniot</i> 1:8</a>.</i> Zeïra said in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: If you come to notice a practice of which you do not know the reason, do not push it aside as something alien since many practices were shown to Moses on Sinai and all of them were absorbed into the Mishnah. Rebbi Abin said, that is correct! If Naḥum had not come and explained it to us about the two kinds of wheat, could we have known<sup class="footnote-marker">99</sup><i class="footnote">That the rule of two kinds of wheat in one field is a practice revealed to Moses.</i>?
Rebbi Zeïra in the name of Rebbi Eleazar: (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Hosea 8:12" href="/Hosea.8.12"><i>Hos</i>. 8:12</a>) “I<sup class="footnote-marker">100</sup><i class="footnote">Words of God.</i> wrote down for him most of My teaching.”<sup class="footnote-marker">101</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 60b" href="/Gittin.60b">Babli (<i>Giṭṭin</i> 60b</a>), this is the definite statement of R. Eleazar. The later statement of R. Samuel bar Naḥman is attributed in the Babli to R. Joḥanan, and R. Zeïra follows the latter’s argument.</i> But was most of the Torah written down? Rather, more things are derived from what is written than what is (only) oral tradition. Is that so? But so it is: Things derived from what is transmitted orally are preferred over those written.
Rebbi Judah ben Pazi says: “I wrote down for him most of My teaching,” these are the admonitions<sup class="footnote-marker">102</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 26:14-46" href="/Leviticus.26.14-46"><i>Lev</i>. 26:14–46</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 28:15-69" href="/Deuteronomy.28.15-69"><i>Deut</i>. 28:15–69</a>.</i>. Nevertheless, is it not that (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Hosea 8:12" href="/Hosea.8.12"><i>Hos</i>. 8:12</a>) “they were considered foreign”.
Rebbi Avin said: If I had written down for you most of my teaching, would it not be considered Gentile? What is the difference between us and the Gentiles? These produce their books and those produce their books. These produce their parchments<sup class="footnote-marker">103</sup><i class="footnote">Greek διφθέρα, ἡ, “leather prepared for writing.” The argument is: If there were no oral Torah Jewish law would not differ from any Gentile law. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 60b" href="/Gittin.60b">Babli <i>Giṭṭin</i> 60b</a> briefly alludes to this argument.</i> and those produce their parchments.
Rebbi Ḥaggai in the name of Rebbi Samuel bar Naḥmani: Things have been said orally and things have been said in writing, and we do not know which ones are preferred. From what is written (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 34:27" href="/Exodus.34.27"><i>Ex</i>. 34:27</a>): “By the mouth of these words I concluded a covenant with you and Israel<sup class="footnote-marker">104</sup><i class="footnote">This argument is somewhat specious since the full verse reads: “The Eternal said to Moses, write down these words for yourself, because by the mouth of these words I concluded a covenant with you and Israel.” In <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 60b" href="/Gittin.60b">Babli <i>Giṭṭin</i> 60b</a>, R. Simeon ben Laqish explains the verse as part of the covenant and states that written verses may not be recited by heart and oral traditions not written down.</i>;” it follows that the oral traditions are preferred<sup class="footnote-marker">105</sup><i class="footnote">A similar argument in the name of R. Joḥanan is reported in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 60b" href="/Gittin.60b">Babli <i>Giṭṭin</i> 60b</a>.</i>.
Rebbi Joḥanan and Rebbi Yudan bar Rebbi Simeon<sup class="footnote-marker">106</sup><i class="footnote">He is Rebbi Jehudah bar Simon. The verse this homily refers to is the one quoted in the preceding paragraph.</i>. One said that if you kept what is oral tradition and kept what is written I will conclude a covenant with you, otherwise I shall not conclude a covenant with you. The other one said, if you watched all that is oral tradition and kept all that is written you will receive your reward, otherwise you will not receive any reward.
Rebbi Joshua ben Levi said<sup class="footnote-marker">107</sup><i class="footnote">The reference is to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 9:10" href="/Deuteronomy.9.10"><i>Deut</i>. 9:10</a>: “The Eternal gave to me the two stone tablets, written by the Divine Finger, <i>and</i> on them <i>like</i> all <i>the</i> words that the Eternal spoke to you on the mountain at the day of assembly.” The three italicized expressions are all unnecessary for the understanding of the sentence; these are interpreted as referring to the three divisions of oral law contained in the complete Torah. {The same derivation is found in <i>Megillah</i> fol. 74d, and in very shortened form with different emphasis in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Megillah 19b" href="/Megillah.19b">Babli <i>Megillah</i> 19b</a>.}</i>: On them, and on them; all, like all; words, the words; Bible, Mishnah, Talmud, and Aggadah. Even what a competent<sup class="footnote-marker">108</sup><i class="footnote">Arabic ות̇יק “strong, safe, secure, dependable, reliable”. The interpretations of the untrained and incompetent are worthless.</i> student will discover<sup class="footnote-marker">109</sup><i class="footnote">Since a student may not rule, or teach, before his teacher, the root of the word להורות must be “to become pregnant,” in this case, with an idea.</i> before his teacher was said to Moses on Sinai. What is the reason? (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Ecclesiastes 1:10" href="/Ecclesiastes.1.10"><i>Eccl</i>. 1:10</a>) “There is something about which one would say, look, this is new!” His colleague will answer, “it already has been forever.”
Rebbi Zeïra in the name of Samuel: One makes inferences neither from practices<sup class="footnote-marker">110</sup><i class="footnote">Rules of practice declared in the Mishnah; these may be overridden by arguments in the Talmud. The interpretation of this paragraph follows R. Samuel ben Meïr (Ṛashbam) to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 130b" href="/Bava_Batra.130b">Babli <i>Baba batra 130b</i>,<i>s. v.</i>עד</a>.</i>, nor from homiletics<sup class="footnote-marker">111</sup><i class="footnote">Homiletics, whether incorporated in the Talmud or given in separate <i>midrashim</i>. Unfortunately, since the sixteenth century this principle has been violated by Kabbalists in favor of practices mentioned in <i>Zohar</i>.</i>, nor from extraneous sources<sup class="footnote-marker">112</sup><i class="footnote">Rashbam reads תוספתות instead of תוספות, but that may be a scribal error. The “additional sources” are tannaïtic materials not given in the Mishnah, either as single traditions or as part of a collection.</i>, but only from study<sup class="footnote-marker">113</sup><i class="footnote">From the Talmud which fixes the reasons for each ruling, or from a decision made in the Talmud that practice follows the opinion of such-and-such.</i>. Rebbi Ḥalaphta ben Shaul stated: The case of two kinds of wheat is the same as the case of two kinds of barley<sup class="footnote-marker">114</sup><i class="footnote">If R. Ḥalaphta ben Shaul makes the inference that the rules for barley are identical with those for wheat, does he not violate Samuel’s statement that one does not draw inferences from stated practices but only from an argument about the underlying principles?</i>. Rebbi Zeïra said, that was part of the original practice and it was forgotten: The case of two kinds of wheat is the same as the case of two kinds of barley. But did we not state (<i>Mishnah</i> 3:3): “He who strips moist onions for the market and stores dry ones<sup class="footnote-marker">115</sup><i class="footnote">He has to give <i>peah</i> two times; this rule cannot be deduced from the stated practice regarding two kinds of wheat since one deals here with only one kind of onion. Since this Mishnah cannot be a consequence of the original <i>Halakhah</i>, neither may one assert that the ruling of R. Ḥalaphta ben Shaul was part of the original <i>Halakhah</i>. Hence, neither the statement of R. Halaphta nor the Mishnah are exempt from logical scrutiny on the basis of accepted general principles.</i>;” can you say about market and storage that this was of the original practice and was forgotten?
Rebbi Ḥananiah in the name of Samuel: One makes no inferences from a ruling<sup class="footnote-marker">116</sup><i class="footnote">If a rabbi was asked about a practical problem and he gave a certain decision, a third party may make no practical applications of that decision unless he understands the reasoning behind the decision and agrees with it.</i>. Everybody agrees that one makes no inferences from an action<sup class="footnote-marker">117</sup><i class="footnote">If one saw a rabbinic authority act in a certain way, one may not act in the same way or make inferences from this action unless one knows both the background of and the reasoning for the particular action.</i>. Rebbi Mana said to him, that means somebody who does not understand<sup class="footnote-marker">118</sup><i class="footnote">The reasoning of the rabbinic authority.</i>, but he who understands may act. He said to him, whether one understands or one does not understand, if there is a disagreement<sup class="footnote-marker">119</sup><i class="footnote">If in the Mishnah or another source there is mention of a disagreement between rabbis about the correct course, action is permitted only if there is an explicit understanding of and agreement with the reasoning behind the ruling.</i>. But if there is no disagreement, whether one understands or one does not understand<sup class="footnote-marker">120</sup><i class="footnote">In this case, one may act on the decision or imitate the action of a competent rabbinic authority even without a detailed inquiry into the arguments leading to decision or action.</i>.

Halakhah 5

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> A field harvested by Gentiles<sup class="footnote-marker">121</sup><i class="footnote">Illegally, or that they bought the crop from the Jewish farmer while the grain was still standing.</i> or robbers<sup class="footnote-marker">122</sup><i class="footnote">Greek λῃστής; in Galilean dialect, final <i>m</i> for <i>s</i> is certainly a scribal error. Since Gentiles who rob are mentioned first, the robber here is a Jew.</i>, undermined by ants, ruined by a storm or animals, is free<sup class="footnote-marker">123</sup><i class="footnote">Since the farmer gets nothing of the yield, he has no obligation to give from another field for the lost crop.</i>. If he harvested half of it and robbers harvested the other half, it is free since the obligation of produce is when it is standing<sup class="footnote-marker">124</sup><i class="footnote">The obligation of <i>peah</i> arises at the end of the harvest, when some grain is still standing. Since at that moment the grain is in the hands of the robbers, no obligation is incurred by the owner of the field.</i>. If robbers cut half of it and he harvested the other half, he must give <i>peah</i> from what he cut. If he harvested half and sold half, the buyer gives <i>peah</i> for everything<sup class="footnote-marker">125</sup><i class="footnote">Since the obligation of <i>peah</i> did arise from his cutting. However, he may ask to deduct the value of the seller’s <i>peah</i>, 1⅔% of the seller’s harvest, from the selling price.</i>. If he cut half and dedicated half<sup class="footnote-marker">128</sup><i class="footnote">(<a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Peah (Lieberman) 3:1" href="/Tosefta_Peah_(Lieberman).3.1">Tosephta <i>Peah</i> 3:1</a>). The Mishnah does not have to mention that the Gentiles harvest for themselves and not for the Jew, since for the grain harvest one may not hire Gentile workers who would pick up the single stalks not bound in sheaves.</i>, he who redeemed it from the treasurer gives <i>peah</i> for everything.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> The Mishnah<sup class="footnote-marker">127</sup><i class="footnote">Dealing with the first case, if the field was harvested by Gentiles.</i>, if they cut for themselves, but if they cut for a Jew it is obligated. And we have formulated regarding this<sup class="footnote-marker">128</sup><i class="footnote">(<a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Peah (Lieberman) 3:1" href="/Tosefta_Peah_(Lieberman).3.1">Tosephta <i>Peah</i> 3:1</a>). The Mishnah does not have to mention that the Gentiles harvest for themselves and not for the Jew, since for the grain harvest one may not hire Gentile workers who would pick up the single stalks not bound in sheaves.</i>: One does not hire Gentile workers because they do not know about gleanings.
The Mishnah<sup class="footnote-marker">129</sup><i class="footnote">Dealing with the second case, if the field was harvested by Jewish robbers.</i>, if they cut to destroy it. But if they do not cut to destroy it, it is obligated<sup class="footnote-marker">130</sup><i class="footnote">Since the robbers, while they are Jewish, are not expected to follow Jewish law and give <i>peah</i> from what they are cutting, the farmer has to give <i>peah</i> from any grain that he manages to obtain from the field. This might apply in particular to the next case, discussed in the next paragraph, when he harvested the first half of the field, in which case the language of the Mishnah may be read to mean that the farmer is totally freed from his obligation.</i>. Rebbi Hoshaia ben Shammai<sup class="footnote-marker">131</sup><i class="footnote">A fifth generation Galilean Amora from Caesarea Philippi (קצרין), a colleague of R. Mana II.</i> said, even if you say that they do not cut to destroy it, it is free since <i>peah</i> is given from standing produce and real estate cannot be robbed<sup class="footnote-marker">132</sup><i class="footnote">This is a principle repeated many times in both Talmudim, cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kilayim 7:4:2-5:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kilayim.7.4.2-5.4"><i>Kilaim</i> 7:5</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sukkah 30b" href="/Sukkah.30b">Babli <i>Sukkah</i> 30b</a>. The reason is that ownership of real estate is always documented; hence, real estate taken by force in a time of anarchy can always be recovered in court when the rule of law is reestablished.<br>According to R. S. Cirillo, since the robbers are not owners of the field, they are not owners of the grain as long as it stands. It is true that they become owners of the grain as soon as they cut it; the legal owner then has a claim for damages against them but not a claim to the actual grain. Hence, when the robbers cut the grain, there is no obligation of <i>peah</i> incurred by the farmer since neither he nor his employees are doing the harvesting. There also is no obligation on the robbers since the grain comes into their possession only after it is cut and the obligation of <i>peah</i> is already a past event. As a consequence, the field is cut without anybody incurring the obligation of <i>peah</i>.</i>.
Because<sup class="footnote-marker">133</sup><i class="footnote">The Mishnah must deal separately with the cases of robbers cutting the entire field or only part of the field because the obligation of <i>peah</i>, once incurred, may be discharged also from cut grain as explained in Mishnah and Halakhah 1:6.</i> he separates<sup class="footnote-marker">134</sup><i class="footnote">Meaning that he sets aside as <i>peah</i> for the poor.</i> from cut grain for standing grain and from standing grain for cut grain but he does not<sup class="footnote-marker">135</sup><i class="footnote">If everything is done correctly.</i> separate from cut grain for either cut or standing grain. Sometimes he has to separate from cut grain for standing grain. How can this be? If he finished his field and left something standing on it for <i>peah</i>, at the moment he cuts the first stalk from this, <i>peah</i> returns to the sheaves; he has to give from cut grain<sup class="footnote-marker">136</sup><i class="footnote">Since he stole from the poor by his action, some obligation is transferred to the grain already harvested as explained in Mishnah 1:6.</i>. Not only if he finished his field but even if he cut some and said: “From here and further on I give for <i>peah</i>,” at the moment he cuts the first stalk from this, <i>peah</i> returns to the sheaves and he separates from cut grain for standing grain.
May the seller get the <i>peah</i> which permits the sheaves<sup class="footnote-marker">137</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to the case when the farmer cuts half the harvest and then sells the field with the remaining crop. If the seller is legally below the poverty line, may he take from the <i>peah</i> the buyer is obligated to give also for the grain he himself cut? The problem is that nobody is permitted to take his own <i>peah</i>.</i>? Let us hear it from the following (Mishnah 5:4): “<sup class="footnote-marker">138</sup><i class="footnote">The Mishnah deals with the case of the sharecropper who is not permitted to take from the <i>peah</i> of the field he is working on. Then Rebbi Jehudah explains (nobody dissenting) that this is only true for the sharecropper who has leased the field and has property rights to 50%, 33%, or 25% of the crop. But if the landlord acts as the owner of the field at all times and the sharecropper has no property rights to the crop except that he will receive 50%, 33%, or 25% of the harvest after it is harvested, then the <i>peah</i> was never his and, being poor, he may go and collect from the <i>peah</i> with the rest of the poor.</i> Rebbi Jehudah said: When is this, if he received it for a half, a third, or a quarter.” What is the difference between this and the first case<sup class="footnote-marker">139</sup><i class="footnote">Of the usual sharecropper.</i>? It must be, “if you cut it, a third will be yours<sup class="footnote-marker">140</sup><i class="footnote">Only after the harvest is completed does the sharecropper have a claim.</i>.” Rebbi Abin bar Ḥiyya said, this implies that the seller may get the <i>peah</i> which permits the sheaves<sup class="footnote-marker">141</sup><i class="footnote">Since at the moment of the actual obligation of <i>peah</i> the field is already sold, the <i>peah</i> was never the seller’s and he may take his part among the other poor who come to collect.</i>. Rebbi Yose<sup class="footnote-marker">142</sup><i class="footnote">He shows that the argument of R. Abin bar Ḥiyya is faulty. While the actual obligation of <i>peah</i> arises during the buyer’s harvest, the potential obligation started with the seller’s harvest. Hence, the seller should be barred from taking his part of the <i>peah</i>. In contrast, the sharecropper who is given ownership only of a part of the harvest, not of the standing grain, has nothing at all to do with the obligation of <i>peah</i>.</i> said, there the obligation of the field did not come during his ownership, but here the obligation of the field started during his ownership. It is correct that if he cut half of the field and sold what he cut, the buyer<sup class="footnote-marker">143</sup><i class="footnote">Since he is poor and never acquired any ownership of the real estate, he may take everything a poor person is entitled to. This sentence is R. Yose’s analysis of the correct parallel to the Mishnah of the sharecropper.</i> is allowed gleanings, forgotten sheaves, and <i>peah</i>. May the buyer get the <i>peah</i> which permits the sheaves of the seller<sup class="footnote-marker">144</sup><i class="footnote">If the buyer is legally poor, may he take for himself the <i>peah</i> that he will give for the seller’s harvest? The answer might be yes, unless we are afraid of unfair competition. This is the essence of the last argument in the quarrel between Rebbi and Rebbi Jehudah II the Prince as explained at the end of Halakhah 1:6.</i>? That is the disagreement of Rebbi and Rebbi Jehudah the Prince.
Rebbi Phineas asked<sup class="footnote-marker">145</sup><i class="footnote">Here starts the discussion of the last case of the Mishnah, if part of the field was dedicated to the upkeep of the Temple.</i>: Can a harvest outside of the Land become subject to <i>peah</i>? Could you not say that a dedicated crop is exempt and (a crop) outside the Land is exempt; just as a dedicated crop may become obligated<sup class="footnote-marker">146</sup><i class="footnote">As explained in the Mishnah, if the field is sold by the Temple officials with the crop still standing.</i>, so from outside the Land it may become obligated? He found it stated: (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 19:9" href="/Leviticus.19.9"><i>Lev</i>. 19:9</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 23:22" href="/Leviticus.23.22">23:22</a>) “When you harvest the harvest of your Land,” and not the harvest outside of the Land<sup class="footnote-marker">147</sup><i class="footnote">Under any circumstance.</i>.
If the treasurer cut half of the half but did not finish to harvest the rest before somebody redeemed everything, he gives from the harvest for what was harvested<sup class="footnote-marker">148</sup><i class="footnote">As explained in the Mishnah, the buyer has to give <i>peah</i> also for the harvest of the original owner. However, since the verse speaks to individuals, “when you cut the harvest of <i>your</i> land”, the obligation is only on private individuals. It does not apply to the Temple which is the collective property of all of Israel.</i> but all that was harvested in the possession of the Temple is already exempted.

Chapter 3



Halakhah 1

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> Rectangles<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">This is the interpretation of Maimonides. R. Simson of Sens prefers to derive מלבן from לבן, “white”; a “white field” by definition is one of grain or legumes. [Then “a <i>white</i> field of grain” would be redundant.] Since מַלְבֵּן is a masculine word with a feminine plural, the gender of the numeral “one” in the Mishnah is undetermined and different manuscripts and prints vary between אחד ,אחת, usually not in a consistent manner.</i> of grain between olive trees<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">The Halakhah will explain that the disagreement is only for a standard field; there is no disagreement on fields planted wider or narrower than the standard.</i>: The House of Shammai say, <i>peah</i> from each single one, the House of Hillel say, from one for all of them. They agree<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">The House of Shammai accept the position of the House of Hillel.</i> that one gives one <i>peah</i> for all of them if the edges of the rows are touching.<br>If someone harvests his field in spots and leaves moist stalks, Rebbi Aqiba says, <i>peah</i> from each spot, but the Sages say, from one for all of them. The Sages agree with Rebbi Aqiba that he who sows dill or mustard at three different places gives <i>peah</i> from each spot<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">This is explained in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Peah 2:1:5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Peah.2.1.5">Chapter 2, Notes 17–19</a>.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> We have stated: “between olive trees.” In the House of Rebbi they stated “between fruit trees.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">Even though Rebbi edited the generally accepted Mishnah, either later generations changed the wording or, more likely, Rebbi insisted in his Yeshivah that in addition to the official Mishnah one would retain a more general version.</i>” Our Mishnah needs the one of the House of Rebbi and that of the House of Rebbi needs our Mishnah. If we had stated, but not the House of Rebbi, we would say that it said only “between olive trees”, which in themselves are subject to <i>peah</i>, but for those which are free from the obligation of <i>peah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">Since only the trees enumerated in Mishnah 1:5 are subject to <i>peah</i>, a general statement “fruit trees” includes (as a majority) those that are not subject to <i>peah</i>.</i> even the House of Shammai will agree that he gives one <i>peah</i> for all of them. Hence, our Mishnah needs that of Rebbi. Or if there were the declaration of the House of Rebbi but we had not stated ours, we would say that it refers only to those which are between trees free from the obligation of <i>peah</i>, but for trees which in themselves are subject to <i>peah</i> even the House of Hillel will agree that he gives <i>peah</i> from each single one. Hence, both our Mishnah and the <i>baraita</i> of the House of Rebbi are needed.
What are we dealing with? <sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">This text is self-contradictory; it is the confluence of two contradictory texts. The text of Maimonides (<i>Hilkhot Mattenot Aniïm</i> 3:9) seems to have been: “If they are widely spaced, also the House of Hillel will agree that he gives one <i>peah</i> for all of them. If they are densely planted, the House of Shammai will agree that he gives <i>peah</i> from each single one. But we deal with the case of 10 plants on a <i>bet se’ah</i>. The House of Shammai compare it to widely spaced, the House of Hillel compare it to densely planted,” inverting the roles of Shammai and Hillel in the first clause. But the version of R. Abraham ben David (<i>loc. cit</i>.) is: “If they are widely spaced, also the House of Shammai will agree that he gives one <i>peah</i> for all of them. If they are densely planted, the House of Hillel will agree that he gives <i>peah</i> from each single one. But we deal with the case of 10 plants on a <i>bet se’ah</i>. The House of Shammai compare it to densely planted ones, the House of Hillel compare it to widely spaced,” inverting the statements in the second clause. There is no direct manuscript evidence of any Yerushalmi manuscript for one reading over the other.</i> If they are widely spaced, even the House of Shammai will agree that he gives one <i>peah</i> for all of them<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">Following R. Abraham ben David, the House of Shammai will agree that there are too few trees to make a difference; it is one field with some holes.</i>. If they are densely planted, the House of Hillel will agree that he gives <i>peah</i> from each single one<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">Since there are few open places large enough to allow wheat to be sown (which by the laws of <i>kilaim</i> must be well separated from the trees); hence, each patch is a separate field.</i>. But we deal with the case of 10 plants on a <i>bet se’ah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">See Chapter 2, Notes 31,73.</i>. The House of Shammai compare it to widely spaced<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">This follows the text of Maimonides who argues that with widely spaced trees the owner could have sown one field. The fact that he chose to make separate rectangles proves that he wants to treat them as separate fields. (Maimonides translates מלבן by “rectangle.”)</i>, the House of Hillel compare it to densely planted ones<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">Hence, each spot sown is a separate field. R. David ben Zimra, in his commentary to Maimonides, points out that this argument supports Maimonides’s reading in the preceding paragraph. “Seeds” usually refers to grain.</i>.
What is the reason of the House of Shammai? People do not usually introduce seeds between trees<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">Hence, each spot sown is a separate field. R. David ben Zimra, in his commentary to Maimonides, points out that this argument supports Maimonides’s reading in the preceding paragraph. “Seeds” usually refers to grain.</i>. Does it turn out that the House of Shammai are like Rebbi Yose<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">Since Rebbi Yose (ben Ḥalaphta) was the leading authority of his generation in the tradition of the House of Hillel, this would be remarkable indeed.</i>? Just as Rebbi Yose said that people do not usually introduce onions between vegetables<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to the second part of Mishnah 4 in this chapter: “Rectangular beds of onions planted among other vegetables, R. Yose says: <i>Peah</i> from each bed, but the Sages say: From one bed for all of them.” According to R. Yose, onions are detrimental to other vegetables growing near them.</i>, so the House of Shammai say that people do not usually introduce seeds between trees! It is reasonable to say that the House of Shammai will agree with Rebbi Yose but Rebbi Yose will not agree with the House of Shammai<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">In a poor country, the land between trees is much too valuable to be left lying fallow. Hence, everybody will sow between the trees if at all possible.</i>. The House of Shammai will agree with Rebbi Yose that people do not usually introduce onions between vegetables. Rebbi Yose will not agree with the House of Shammai because people usually introduce seeds between trees.
If there was a fence there, it is like a tree<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">If each tree has 250 square cubits around it, one follows the House of Hillel’s opinion in the Mishnah.</i>. How about two<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">If there are two fences, do the House of Hillel still allow one <i>peah</i> for all? The question is asked but not answered [which for practical purposes is a negative answer.]</i>?
So far<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to the last clause of the Mishnah, that one gives only one <i>peah</i> if the beds grown from seeds are connected at more than one place.</i> if they are combined from one side and the other. If they were combined only on one side, either by three coarse furrows<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">See <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Peah 2:1:9" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Peah.2.1.9">Chapter 2, Note 33</a>. Is it necessary that the beds touch in the width of three coarse furrows or is any contact acceptable? The answer here again is not given, which means for practical purposes that it is negative.</i> or at any place?
“He who harvests his field in spots,” etc. Like a spotted field<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">The leopard is נמר in Hebrew; the Mishnah means: He who makes his field look like a leopard. The Talmud notes that a field which is fertilized unevenly and, hence, whose growth is spotty, is called in agricultural terminology נימרה "leopard-like." It is not clear whether we talk only of a field that is growing at different rates at different points (and, therefore, cannot be harvested at one time) or also of a field that looks normal but whose owner harvests in spots so that it looks "leopard like" at harvest time.</i>; the place where fertilizer makes plants grow early is called “spotted.”
So far if it was dry on both sides and moist in the middle<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">Since grain usually is harvested when dry, in the case contemplated the farmer will legitimately harvest the dry sides and leave the green patch in the middle for a later harvest. In this case, R. Aqiba will consider the separate dry patches as separate fields since they can be harvested separately without difficulty.</i>. How about being moist on both sides and dry in the middle<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">In this case, access to the ripe grain is difficult and one may wonder whether R. Aqiba will insist that the cutting is a separate harvest. The question is not resolved (since practice follows the majority of the “Sages”, including most of R. Aqiba’s own surviving students, about whom see the Introduction to tractate <i>Berakhot</i>.)</i>?
Rebbi Abba, Rebbi Ḥiyya<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">The tradents are R. Abba bar R. Ḥiyya bar Abba and his father.</i> in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: “Rebbi Meïr follows the argument of his teacher Rebbi Aqiba. Just as Rebbi Aqiba says that moist and dry are two different kinds, so Rebbi Meïr<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">Who in Mishnah 2:1 asserted that if somebody cuts part of his grain green for fodder and part of it dry for grain he has to give <i>peah</i> twice.</i> says that moist and dry are two different kinds.” The colleagues said before Rebbi Yose: Why should he argue like Rebbi Aqiba? He could argue even like the Rabbis since we have stated there (<i>Mishnah</i> 3:3): “He who rips out<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">See the lexical explanation in the next Mishnah.</i> moist onions for the market and keeps dry ones for storage<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">He has to give <i>peah</i> twice.</i>!” Rebbi Yose answered: We understand that market and storage make two kinds, are moist and dry two kinds<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">Hence, R. Meïr agrees with R. Aqiba, not with the other Sages.</i>?
Two kinds, half moist and half dry, even Rebbi Aqiba will agree<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">If a connected part of the field is already dry and ready for harvesting, even R. Aqiba will agree that then the harvest is the start of the harvest of the entire field and only one <i>peah</i> is due. This argument cannot apply to spot cutting.</i>. But did we not state<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah 3:2</i>: “The Sages agree with Rebbi Aqiba that he who sows dill and mustard at three different places<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">He gives <i>peah</i> for each part separately.</i> …” It is so<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">The Yerushalmi expression כיני is discussed at length in J. N. Epstein, <i>Mavo lenosaḥ hammišnah</i>, 2nd ed., Jerusalem 1964, pp. 441–508.</i>: Either dill at three places or mustard at three places<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">Even though both the Mishnah and its quote in the Halakhah read “or”, it seems that the argument refers to the reading “dill <i>and</i> mustard” found in the Parma and one of the Genizah manuscripts of the Mishnah. It is explained that any one kind of spice triggers the special rule of <i>peah</i> for spice beds.</i>. Samuel says, because the first one does not wait for the last one<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">Dill and mustard are special in that they cannot be harvested at one time. If they did not fall under the general category of legumes there would be no obligation of <i>peah</i> at all. Hence, Samuel restricts the meaning of the Mishnah to dill and mustard only; these kinds are mentioned for themselves and not as paradigms.</i>. Rebbi Yasa in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: Because they usually are being sown bed by bed<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">Spices are grown in small quantities. Hence, each bed is a separate field. For R. Johanan, dill and mustard are given as paradigms; the rule of the Mishnah applies to all spices.</i>. According to Samuel, he has to give from each stalk<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">This is almost impossible and implies a rejection of Samuel’s argument.</i>; according to Rebbi Joḥanan he has to give from each bed.

Halakhah 2

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> He who rips out<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">This translation is tentative; according to Maimonides it simply means “cuts”, based on <a class="refLink" data-ref="Isaiah 41:7" href="/Isaiah.41.7"><i>Is</i>. 41:7</a>. According to R. Simson of Sens, it means either “leaves a bald spot” where the onions have been taken out or it means “splits” his field into two parts; it is possible that the expression is intentionally ambiguous. [In <a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Arakhin 1:3" href="/Mishnah_Arakhin.1.3">Mishnah <i>Arakhin</i> 1:3</a>, the word means “to finish, to play the final coda.”] In Arabic, חלק means “to shave”; this supports the first interpretation of R. Simson, also supported by Mishnah <i>Ševiït</i> 4:4 which explains that “thinning” means taking out single plants but “ripping out” means pulling at least three together, leaving a bald spot.</i> moist onions for the market and keeps dry ones for storage gives <i>peah</i> for each batch separately; the same applies to peas<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">Peas grown for meal. Those grown to be eaten as vegetable are not subject to the laws of <i>peah</i> (Mishnah <i>Peah</i> 1:4).</i> and vineyards<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">Table grapes are considered different from grapes used either for wine making or for raisins.</i>. He who thins out gives <i>peah</i> for what is left<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">Since thinning is necessary for the better growth of many kinds, the plants taken out in thinning are not considered harvested.</i>. He who rips out with one hand<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">The meaning of this expression is not clear. According to Maimonides, it means “taking from one side only”. According to Rabbenu Simson, it means “taking from one kind only”. The sentence may refer to the start of the Mishnah: If somebody harvests young onions to sell them green with their leaves, he has to give <i>peah</i> from these green onions. But if he rips out only a few at a time with one hand, at the end, at the time of the harvest of the fully ripe onions for storage, he gives <i>peah</i> also for the volume of those green ones that he took occasionally.</i> gives from the remainder for everything.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> It is stated<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Peah (Lieberman) 1:9" href="/Tosefta_Peah_(Lieberman).1.9">Tosephta <i>Peah</i> 1:9</a>.</i>: “In patches<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">The text of the Tosephta reads: He who harvests in “patches.” According to S. Lieberman (<i>Tosefta kifšutah Peah</i> p. 131), אורּגא is the Syriac translation of ברוד “having white spots.” Z. Frankel refers the word here to Aramaic מרג “escape control”, which does not make sense. J. Levy refers to Arabic מַרַגַ “to be, to get into disorder”, to which one may add מַרג “pasture”.</i> it is obligated at the beginning and at the end.” What is “in patches?” Rebbi Jeremiah said, just as we have stated: “He who rips out moist onions for the market and keeps dry ones for storage.” Rebbi Yose<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">R. Yose (the Amora) disagrees with R. Jeremiah and asserts that green onions are not in themselves subject to <i>peah</i>; only if part of the field is left to produce onions for storage does the obligation of <i>peah</i> fall on the entire field.</i> said, for example nigella: If one sows it, it produces thin bulbs; if one plants seedlings, it produces large bulbs. For if it were not so, we would say because it is for sowing<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">In the first phase, when the plot is sown with <i>nigella</i> seed, all will be taken out for replanting. Hence, by Mishnah 1:4, it should not be subject to <i>peah</i>. However, since the seeds of the strong plant will be stored as spice, it is subject to <i>peah</i> in every harvest.</i> it should be freed from <i>peah</i>. But are wheat grains not also for sowing? Wheat is mostly for eating, that one<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote">Sown <i>nigella</i> does not produce usable spice.</i> is mostly for sowing. According to Rebbi Jeremiah, are vegetables subject to <i>peah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote">Since by equating Mishnah and Tosephta he declares an obligation of <i>peah</i> for green onions even if the field does not produce any onions for storage. This contradicts Mishnah 1:4.</i>? Its kind is collected for storage. How does Rebbi Yose deal with it<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote">The problem of green onions.</i>? It is finished and needs only to dry out.
It was stated: (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 19:9" href="/Leviticus.19.9"><i>Lev</i>. 19:9</a>) “The gathering of your harvest,” not the gathering of your plucking<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">By hand.</i>. Rebbi Zeïra, Rebbi Ḥiyya in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: He who collects<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">By hand.</i> ears for his dough in any quantity<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote">In the Babli this expression would mean “a minimal quantity.” In the Yerushalmi it means “a quantity of indeterminate size.”</i> is free from <i>peah</i>. Rebbi Eleazar says, even with a sickle<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote">It is not a harvest unless some is collected for storage, not if everything is used for immediate consumption.</i>. Rebbi Yose<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote">The Amora who in the Yerushalmi is known usually as R. Yasa and in the Babli as R. Assi.</i> said, only if he left some standing. Did we not state (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Peah (Lieberman) 1:10" href="/Tosefta_Peah_(Lieberman).1.10">Tosephta <i>Peah</i> 1:10</a>): “If he had five vines and he harvested them and brought them into his house<sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote">Not to the winepress to make wine or to his roof to make raisins.</i>, he is free from fallen berries<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote">Berries fallen from the grape bunches during harvest, which may not be taken by the harvesters but must be left for the poor.</i>, from <i>orlah</i>, from the <i>fourth year</i><sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Orlah</i> is the yield of the first three years of a newly planted tree that is forbidden for all use; the yield of the fourth year must be eaten in Jerusalem (or it may be redeemed and the money brought to Jerusalem) if there is a Temple. These obligations do not depend on the harvest; hence, they cannot belong to the text. Even though this text is found in both manuscripts of the Yerushalmi, it is not in the Tosephta where the reading is: he is free from fallen berries, forgotten sheaves, and <i>peah</i>. This is also the text implied by both Maimonides and R. Abraham ben David (הלכות מתנות עניים 4:27) and the mention of <i>peah</i> is implied by the discussion here. The corruption must have crept into a manuscript from which both the Leyden and the Rome manuscripts are derived.<br>The Tosephta does not mention a remainder; this seems to contradict R. Yasa.</i>, but he is obligated for gleanings<sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote">Single berries which do not grow either in a bunch or in a row. They may not be harvested and must be left for the poor. Since they belong to the poor from the moment of their formation, the manner of harvesting is irrelevant.</i>. Rebbi Yudan said, one for those which are fully ripe<sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote">Then any collecting is harvesting and a remnant is necessary so that the obligation of <i>peah</i> may be fulfilled.</i>, one for those which are not fully ripe<sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote">Then <i>peah</i> is not due.</i>. Rebbi Yose<sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote">Ben Zabida, the late Amora.</i> said, you may even say that both deal with the case that they are fully ripe, or both deal with the case that they are not fully ripe.
