There<sup class="footnote-marker">63</sup><i class="footnote">Without remainder.</i> if he wanted to eat them as grapes, here<sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote">A remainder is required for wine grapes.</i> if he wanted to make them into wine, he may do so. This is informed from that and that is informed from this. This is informed from that, if he wants to eat them<sup class="footnote-marker">65</sup><i class="footnote">Ears of grain. Eaten as a snack directly from the field.</i> as rubbed ears [he may do so] even if he did not leave a remainder. That is informed from this, if he wants to drink them as wine<sup class="footnote-marker">66</sup><i class="footnote">Even if the grapes are squeezed by hand to produce grape juice in the house.</i> [he may do so] only if he left a remainder.
“He who thins out gives <i>peah</i> for what is left<sup class="footnote-marker">67</sup><i class="footnote">Quote from the Mishnah.</i>.” It was stated (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Peah (Lieberman) 1:10" href="/Tosefta_Peah_(Lieberman).1.10">Tosephta <i>Peah</i> 1:10</a>): Rebbi Jehudah said: When has this been said? If he thins for sale<sup class="footnote-marker">68</sup><i class="footnote">The plants taken in thinning, even if they are marketable as produce, are certainly only vegetable that in its kind is never stored and, hence, is free from the obligation of <i>peah</i>. Nobody requires <i>peah</i> for thinnings that are discarded.</i>, but if he thins for his own use he gives from the remnant for everything<sup class="footnote-marker">69</sup><i class="footnote">Because this is the use of the produce of the field for which it was planted.</i>. Rebbi Zeïra said, that means<sup class="footnote-marker">70</sup><i class="footnote">That he does not have to give <i>peah</i> for marketable thinnings if they are marketed.</i>, if he sowed thickly in order to thin later. But if he sowed thickly without intention to thin later, not only if he uses it for himself but even for the market he gives from the remnant for everything.

Halakhah 3

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> Mother onions<sup class="footnote-marker">71</sup><i class="footnote">The Halakhah will try to explain this expression.</i> are subject to <i>peah</i>, Rebbi Yose frees. Rectangles of onions among vegetables, Rebbi Yose says <i>peah</i> from each single one, but the Sages say from one for everything<sup class="footnote-marker">72</sup><i class="footnote">For this controversy, see Halakhah 2.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> Rav says <i>pvrgrh</i><sup class="footnote-marker">71</sup><i class="footnote">The Halakhah will try to explain this expression.</i>, Samuel says sproutings<sup class="footnote-marker">72</sup><i class="footnote">For this controversy, see Halakhah 2.</i>.
Rebbi Jacob bar Abun<sup class="footnote-marker">73</sup><i class="footnote">A third generation Amora, student of R. Yose ben R. Ḥanina.</i> in the name of Rebbi Ḥanina: Rebbi Yose said only because of ownerless property<sup class="footnote-marker">74</sup><i class="footnote">Since these “mother onions” are worthless according to both definitions, anybody may pick them up. Abandoned property is not subject to <i>peah</i> since it cannot be called produce of <i>your</i> field.</i>. Rebbi Abun bar Ḥiyya asked before Rebbi Mana: Is abandoned property obligated for <i>peah</i>? He said to them: When he reacquired them one by one<sup class="footnote-marker">75</sup><i class="footnote">If the owner reasserted his right to these onions after they sprouted new growth they are not abandoned property.</i>. But did we not state: Even though one with a large harvest will not store the mother-onions, one with only a small harvest will. Hence, the reason<sup class="footnote-marker">76</sup><i class="footnote">Of the Sages.</i> is only that he collects them for storing<sup class="footnote-marker">77</sup><i class="footnote">The difference between Rebbi Yose and the Sages is a difference of opinion on matters of agricultural practice rather than on the theory of abandoned property.</i>.

Halakhah 4

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> Brothers who split<sup class="footnote-marker">78</sup><i class="footnote">The inheritance. [Since an unspecified plural has a minimum of 2 but no maximum, such a plural in legal texts always means “2”. The text should read: Two brothers who divided up an inheritance give two <i>peot</i>.]</i> give two <i>peot</i>. When they later form a cooperative, they give one <i>peah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">79</sup><i class="footnote">The Babylonian Talmud (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Chullin 135b" href="/Chullin.135b"><i>Ḥulin</i>135b</a>) notes that <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 19:9" href="/Leviticus.19.9"><i>Lev</i>. 19:9</a> reads: “When you (plural) harvest the harvest of your (plural) land, do not finish to cut the corner of your (singular) field.” This is taken to mean that <i>peah</i> is due from the owner, individual or collective [but not, as pointed out by <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sifra, Kedoshim" href="/Sifra,_Kedoshim"><i>Sifra Qedošim</i> 1(11</a>), if not all shareholders in the collective are Jews.]</i>. Two people who together bought a tree give one <i>peah</i>. If one bought the North side and the other the South side, each gives <i>peah</i> for himself<sup class="footnote-marker">80</sup><i class="footnote">Even though the care for the tree must necessarily be a common endeavor, only the property relationship matters.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> <sup class="footnote-marker">81</sup><i class="footnote">In the Venice print, this sentence belongs to the previous Halakhah. It is the consensus of all commentators that it belongs to Halakhah 5.</i> If he harvested half a field in company [with his brother] and then they split up, he<sup class="footnote-marker">82</sup><i class="footnote">According to Maimonides (<i>Mattenot Aniïm</i> 2:16), the entire sentence speaks about a man who took the harvested grain as his part and left the standing grain to his brother. He does not have to give <i>peah</i> for his part since the obligation rests on the standing grain and, if his brother would have cut the rest of the field without leaving <i>peah</i>, he cannot be asked to contribute to the <i>peah</i> that now has to be given from cut grain since he had no ownership rights at the time the last grain was cut.</i> does not give from his own part either for the initial harvest or for the final one. When they changed their minds and after splitting formed a company and harvested half a field, and then separated again, he can give from his part at the end for his partner’s part at the end<sup class="footnote-marker">83</sup><i class="footnote">Since his part is simply his part in the company proceeds, all he gives originally was company property.</i>, but not from his part at the start for his partner’s part at the start<sup class="footnote-marker">84</sup><i class="footnote">When each of them was a separate owner of half a field, since giving <i>peah</i> cannot cross ownership lines.</i>.
Rebbi Joḥanan said: If he wanted to cut half of his field and actually cut<sup class="footnote-marker">85</sup><i class="footnote">As explained in Halakhah 1:1, the obligation of <i>peah</i> starts with the first cut. But since at that point he intended only to cut half of his field, the second part has to be considered a separate field and, therefore, the obligation falls only on the first part of the field.</i> half of this half, but when he came to cut the other half he finished off his entire field, then he may give from the first cut on the intermediate ones<sup class="footnote-marker">86</sup><i class="footnote">I. e., the remainder of the first part that was not harvested the first time.</i> and from the intermediate ones on the first but not from the first<sup class="footnote-marker">87</sup><i class="footnote">The first cutting of the first part of the field.</i> on the first<sup class="footnote-marker">88</sup><i class="footnote">The first cutting of the second part of the field, that now represents a new field.</i>. Rebbi Joshua ben Levi said: If he had a field where half of it was one third ripe<sup class="footnote-marker">89</sup><i class="footnote">Grain that is less than one third ripe is not grain in any commercial sense. Once it is one-third ripe it may be used either for animal feed or for human consumption as <i>Grünkern</i>. If he now has the intention of harvesting his field when it is one-third ripe, it is as if the field were divided into two fields. The explanation of this sentence is then completely parallel to that of the preceding statement of R. Joḥanan.</i>, the other half was not yet one third ripe, and by the time he finished harvesting half of the first half, the entire field also was one third ripe, he gives from the first cut on the intermediate ones and from the intermediate ones on the first<sup class="footnote-marker">90</sup><i class="footnote">Maimonides (<i>Mattenot Aniïm</i> 2:17) reads: “And from the intermediate ones on the first and the last”. He seems to argue that the first split was by nature, not by the farmer’s design; a change in nature changes the status and at the time of the second harvest the field was counted as one again. But the first cut of the first part cannot be used to free those parts of the field that were not yet a crop at the time of cutting. Since R. Abraham ben David does not disagree, both must have read “and the last” in the text of the Yerushalmi.</i> but not from the first on the first.

Halakhah 5

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> He who sells tree trunks<sup class="footnote-marker">91</sup><i class="footnote">According to Maimonides (<i>Mattenot Aniïm</i> 3:18), the rule applies to any sale, not only of trees or tree roots, but even of standing grain.</i> in his field gives <i>peah</i> from each single one. Rebbi Jehudah says, when? If the owner of the field did not retain anything, but if he retained his field, he gives <i>peah</i> for all of them<sup class="footnote-marker">92</sup><i class="footnote">See the first and last notes to the next paragraph.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> So far when he started harvesting beforehand<sup class="footnote-marker">93</sup><i class="footnote">If the seller started harvesting, the first cut establishes the obligation of <i>peah</i> (see Halakhah 1:1). Hence, the obligation of the seller can be satisfied only if he disposes of the entire field. The discussion centers on R. Jehudah’s clarification.</i>; even if he did not start to harvest? Let us understand it from the following (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Chullin 11:2" href="/Mishnah_Chullin.11.2">Mishnah <i>Ḥulin</i> 11:2</a>): “If he bought the shearings<sup class="footnote-marker">94</sup><i class="footnote">I. e., the right to shear the sheep at a specified time in the future.</i> of another man’s sheep, if the seller retained anything, the seller is liable<sup class="footnote-marker">95</sup><i class="footnote">To give the first shearings to a Cohen, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 18:4" href="/Deuteronomy.18.4"><i>Deut</i>. 18:4</a>.</i>, otherwise the buyer is liable.” Rebbi Jeremiah in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: This is Rebbi Jehudah’s<sup class="footnote-marker">96</sup><i class="footnote">Although nobody seems to object to R. Jehudah’s clarification, the Yerushalmi does not accept the thesis of the Babli that all statements of R. Jehudah starting with “when?” are undisputed practice. In any case, it looks as if the underlying principle was the same.</i>. Is there a difference? There, both if he started to shear or if he did not start to shear<sup class="footnote-marker">97</sup><i class="footnote">Will the seller be liable as long as he retains any wool for himself.</i>, and here there will be no difference whether he started harvesting beforehand or did not start beforehand<sup class="footnote-marker">98</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Chullin 138a" href="/Chullin.138a">Babli (<i>Ḥulin</i> 138a</a>), both Rav Ḥisda and Rava agree that <i>peah</i> is due only if the harvest started since it says (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 19:9" href="/Leviticus.19.9"><i>Lev</i>. 19:9</a>): “When you harvest the harvest of your land.” But the obligation of giving the first wool to the Cohen exists independent of the act of shearing. Hence, the Babli negates the conclusion of the Yerushalmi here.</i>.
What is the reason of Rebbi Jehudah? Because the obligation of the harvest falls on the standing grain? Or because he sold to him, excluding his obligation<sup class="footnote-marker">99</sup><i class="footnote">Unless explicitly stated, the buyer does not acquire the obligations attached to the property bought. Hence, the seller is assumed to have adjusted the asking price so that it covers the cost of <i>peah</i> for the harvest sold.</i>? Let us understand it from the following: “If he bought the shearings of another man’s sheep, if the seller retained anything, the seller is liable, otherwise the buyer is liable.” Rebbi Jeremiah in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: This is Rebbi Jehudah’s. Could you say there that the obligation of the harvest falls on the standing grain<sup class="footnote-marker">100</sup><i class="footnote">In contrast to <i>peah</i>, where the verse requires that the farmer refrain from cutting the last corner of his field, the obligation to give from the fleece to the Cohen is formulated in terms of shearing; the obligation does not exist as long as the wool is still on the sheep’s back.</i>? No, it is because he sold to him, excluding his obligation! And here also it is because he sold to him, excluding his obligation<sup class="footnote-marker">101</sup><i class="footnote">Otherwise, R. Joḥanan would be in error asserting the identity of the principles underlying the Mishnah here and in <i>Ḥulin</i>.</i>.
What is the difference between them<sup class="footnote-marker">102</sup><i class="footnote">What are the practical differences that make it necessary for us to decide between the two possible explanations for R. Jehudah’s statement?</i>? If the buyer transgressed and separated <i>peah</i>. If you say, it is because the obligation of the harvest falls on the standing grain, what he gave he gave<sup class="footnote-marker">103</sup><i class="footnote">Since <i>peah</i> may be given at the start, the middle, and the end of the harvest, it is legal <i>peah</i> but does not concern the seller.</i>. But if you say that because he sold to him, excluding his obligation, he gave and may collect money from him<sup class="footnote-marker">104</sup><i class="footnote">The buyer may collect from the seller. Since the buyer has fulfilled the obligation of <i>peah</i> for his part, if he informs the seller that the latter may reduce the amount he has to give, the buyer may ask for indemnity for the reduction of the seller’s obligation.</i>. If the part of the seller burned, if it is because the obligation of the harvest falls on the standing grain, if it is burned it is burned<sup class="footnote-marker">105</sup><i class="footnote">And the buyer must give <i>peah</i> from his part since the seller cannot do it for him.</i>. But if you say that because he sold to him, excluding his obligation, if it is burned he<sup class="footnote-marker">106</sup><i class="footnote">The buyer, who bought unincumbered harvest and now has to give <i>peah</i> from what he bought, has regress on the seller for the amount of produce which goes into <i>peah</i>.</i> may collect money from him.

Halakhah 6

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> Rebbi Eliezer says, a plot of a <i>bet rova</i>‘<sup class="footnote-marker">107</sup><i class="footnote">See <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Peah 2:1:9" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Peah.2.1.9">Chapter 2, Note 31</a>.</i> is obligated for <i>peah</i>. Rebbi Joshua says, if its yield is two <i>seah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">108</sup><i class="footnote">He makes the obligation depend not on the size of the plot but the volume of the yield. Hence, the minimal size would vary with the type of grain grown. One <i>seah</i> = 6 <i>qab</i> = 24 <i>log</i>. One <i>log</i> is probably 533 cm<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup>. This makes the <i>seah</i> equal to .452 cubic feet (12.8 dm<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup>) and two <i>seah</i> about .9 cubic feet (25.6 dm<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup>).</i>. Rebbi Ṭarphon says, six by six hand-breadths<sup>109</sup><i class="footnote">One square cubit.</i>. Rebbi Joshua<sup class="footnote-marker">110</sup><i class="footnote">This Tanna is not known from any other source. Some Mishnah manuscripts have “R. Jehudah ben Bathyra”, referring to a Tanna of the third generation from Nisibis (<i>Neẓivin</i>) on the upper Tigris, today Urfa in Turkey. However, enough manuscripts from different sources have “R. Joshua” to suggest that the name should not be changed.</i> ben Bathyra said, if it is enough to cut and cut a second time<sup class="footnote-marker">111</sup><i class="footnote">The first use of sickle or scythe starts the harvest; the amount left for the second cut then can be <i>peah</i>; see the discussion at the start of Halakha 1:1.</i>; practice follows his words.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> What is the reason of Rebbi Eliezer? It says here “your field<sup class="footnote-marker">112</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 19:9" href="/Leviticus.19.9"><i>Lev</i>. 19:9</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 23:22" href="/Leviticus.23.22">23:22</a>, speaking of <i>peah</i>.</i>” and it says about <i>kilaim</i> “your field<sup class="footnote-marker">113</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 19:19" href="/Leviticus.19.19"><i>Lev</i>. 19:19</a>: “Your field you shall not sow with two different kinds.” It is assumed that a word in the Books of Moses never changes its meaning.</i>”. Just as “your field” mentioned there<sup class="footnote-marker">114</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kilayim 2:8:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kilayim.2.8.1">Mishnah <i>Kilaim</i> 2:10</a> states that two fields of different crops become forbidden if planted too closely one to the other but only if they are the minimum size of <i>bet rova</i>‘.</i> means a <i>bet rova</i>‘, so here it means <i>bet rova</i>‘. What is the reason of Rebbi Joshua? It says here “your field” and it says further on (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 24:19" href="/Deuteronomy.24.19"><i>Deut</i>. 24:19</a>): “You might forget a sheaf on the field.” Just as the field mentioned there means two<sup class="footnote-marker">115</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah 6:5 states that a forgotten sheaf is for the poor only if it is less in volume than two <i>seah</i>. Similarly, Mishnah 6:6 states that a forgotten part of the field is not for the poor if its yield is at least 2 <i>seah</i>. Rebbi Joshua seems to argue that anything that is not a field for the definition of <i>leqeṭ</i> (collecting grain forgotten be the farmer) and <i>šiḵḥah</i>(collecting forgotten sheaves) cannot be a field for the definition of <i>peah</i> since all three obligations always go together.</i>, so here also two. What is the reason of Rebbi Ṭarphon? Six by six from a vegetable patch<sup class="footnote-marker">116</sup><i class="footnote">In <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kilayim 3:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kilayim.3.1.1">Mishnah <i>Kilaim</i> 3:1</a>, the standard vegetable patch for intensive cultivation is defined as being one square cubit. R. Eliezer either is of the opinion that no vegetables are grown for storage or that onions grown for storage are such a rare case that one may take grain fields as the standard for everything. R. Ṭarphon seems to think that the smallest plot used to grow any plant for storage is the standard for all crops.</i>. “Rebbi Joshua ben Bathyra says, if it is enough to cut and cut a second time, and practice follows his words.” Does “harvesting” mean following the technique of harvesters, or is it any amount<sup class="footnote-marker">117</sup><i class="footnote">R. Joshua ben Bathyra argues that the verse: “When you harvest the harvest of your land,” means all kinds of harvest and that therefore any plot which can be harvested is subject to <i>peah</i>. The question is only whether the harvest has to be professional or whether simple plucking is enough. The verse from Psalms is taken to mean that only a cut that will fill at least the hollow of one’s hand qualifies for “harvesting.” This argument is the base of the first discussion in Halakhah 1:1.</i>? Since it is written (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Psalms 129:7" href="/Psalms.129.7"><i>Ps</i>. 129:7</a>): “The harvester did not fill his palm with it, nor the binder of sheaves his bosom,” that means that one follows the technique of harvesters.

Halakhah 7

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> Rebbi Aqiba says: Any real estate<sup class="footnote-marker">118</sup><i class="footnote">Even if its area is a fraction of a square inch.</i> is subject to <i>peah</i> and first fruits<sup class="footnote-marker">119</sup><i class="footnote">If it is used for agriculture.</i>, to write a <i>prozbol</i><sup class="footnote-marker">120</sup><i class="footnote">Greek προσβολή, ή, "falling upon, application." As a legal term, "in an auction, document recording the knocking down of a lot to a purchaser" (L. &amp; S.). Only in the Babli does the word appear with ס instead of ז; the etymology proposed would require s and not ζ but this may be a matter of local dialect. Prozbol was instituted by Hillel as a way to circumvent the annulment of debts in the Seventh Year in cases when that annulment would only be a rabbinic ordinance. Since only debts due to real persons are annulled by the Seventh Year, the creditor may deliver the debt to the court to be collected. Since the court is not a real person, the debt is not subject to annulment. The document may be written only as a lien on real estate. The details of prozbol are given in tractate Gittin.</i> based on it, and to acquire simultaneously non-guaranteed property<sup class="footnote-marker">121</sup><i class="footnote">Real estate is always sold with a title guarantee unless explicitly disclaimed. If the buyer loses the land he bought, either for lack of a valid title or because of a foreclosure of a pre-existing mortgage, he has regress on all other real estate owned by the seller. [The technical term אחריות “warranty” is אַחֵרָיוּת, corresponding to Latin <i>alienatio</i> “transfer of property to another person.”]</i> with money or contract or possession<sup class="footnote-marker">122</sup><i class="footnote">Movable property can be acquired only by some act of taking possession, either actual or symbolic. This is necessary in order to determine the exact moment when responsibility for the article is transferred, to avoid situations in which the seller takes the money and then claims that the article was lost in an accident, leaving the buyer without any chance to prove that it was lost when the responsibility was the seller’s. However, since buying real estate is a stronger form of contract, a contract or understanding to buy real estate can be extended to include movables. In that case, the moment of transfer of property rights to the real estate is determining also for the movables. The ways of acquiring real estate are either a contract witnessed by two witnesses, or payment of cash in front of witnesses, or a court ruling confirming squatters’ rights. The general theory of these matters is discussed in tractate <i>Qiddušin</i>; the exact and restrictive definition of enforceable squatters’ rights is one of the topics of tractate <i>Baba Batra</i>.</i>.<br>If someone signs over his properties<sup class="footnote-marker">148</sup><i class="footnote">In a gift document.</i> when bedridden and reserved for himself any real estate, his gift is permanent<sup class="footnote-marker">149</sup><i class="footnote">Since he reserved property, the document is a gift and not a will, on condition that it was executed under all conditions of a valid gift.</i>; if he did not reserve any real estate for himself, his gift is not permanent<sup class="footnote-marker">150</sup><i class="footnote">If he did not reserve anything for himself, it is clear that he did not expect to survive. If he survives nevertheless, the entire document becomes void.</i>. If he signs over his properties to his sons and signs over some real estate for his wife, she lost her <i>ketubah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">151</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>ketubah</i> is a mortgage on the husband’s entire property, to be paid to the wife at the termination of the marriage, after divorce or after the husband’s death. It represents the financial obligation of the husband to his wife; without such an obligation, any sexual relations between man and wife are considered illicit. The value of the obligation was fixed at 200 <i>zuz</i>(800 silver denars) for a virgin and 100 <i>zuz</i> (400 silver denars) for a previously married woman. A measure of the intended value of these sums is found in Mishnah 8:8,9, viz., that anyone with 200 <i>zuz</i> investment capital or 50 <i>zuz</i> business capital is living above the poverty line and barred from receiving charity.<br>The Halakhah explains the circumstances under which the wife may agree to be co-heir to the sons. The Mishnah appears in the context of the power of contracts involving real estate; the first part belongs to the matters treated in <i>Baba Batra</i> and <i>Giṭṭin</i>, the second part to <i>Ketubot</i>.</i>. Rebbi Yose<sup class="footnote-marker">152</sup><i class="footnote">The Tanna, ben Ḥalaphta.</i> says, if she accepted it, even if it was not in a written document, she lost her <i>ketubah</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> Rebbi Immi<sup class="footnote-marker">123</sup><i class="footnote">He is Rebbi Ammi, cf. <i>Berakhot</i>, pp. 76,85.</i> asked in the name of Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish: Think of it, if there were only one ear! Before he cut, there was no obligation; after he cut, there will be nothing left!<sup class="footnote-marker">124</sup><i class="footnote">The question refers to the rule of Rebbi Aqiba, that even the tiniest amount of real estate carries an obligation of <i>peah</i>. If the plot is only big enough for one plant, there can never be an obligation since this would fall on the harvest, not on the bare earth. Hence, R. Aqiba also should be giving a minimal size of the plot which might carry an obligation of <i>peah</i>. The answer is that there might be a plant with multiple ears which is not cut but an ear of which is plucked. Then something is left on which the obligation might fall and R. Aqiba is justified. (Cf. the second paragraph in Halakhah 1:1.)</i> Rebbi Ḥananiah in the name of Rebbi Phineas: Explain it if there was one stalk with five ears!
Rebbi Mana asked: Why does he not say: “Any crop is subject to <i>peah</i>?<sup class="footnote-marker">125</sup><i class="footnote">This is a continuation of the previous question. If <i>peah</i> is not a tax on the soil but on plants which grow on it, should not the principle be formulated in terms of the crop? The answer is that then one would need two separate sentences, one for <i>peah</i> and one for first fruits since only fruits of “your land” may be brought (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bikkurim 1:2:2-3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bikkurim.1.2.2-3"><i>Bikkurim</i>1:2</a>). Since the Mishnah was transmitted orally, that would make memorization much more difficult; a slight deviation from purely logical organization is therefore justified. The ease of memorization is a recognized principle in the formulation of Tannaïtic material.</i>” But because he has to enunciate first fruits he formulates it as “real estate.”
It was stated: “And the appearance<sup class="footnote-marker">126</sup><i class="footnote">In the Temple, see Mishnah 1:1, note 4.</i>.” Rebbi Yose in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: He who owns no real estate is freed from the appearance. Rebbi Mana asked: And why do we not say: He who has no real estate is freed from the declaration<sup class="footnote-marker">127</sup><i class="footnote">From Rashi and R. Samuel ben Meïr (Rashbam) in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Kiddushin 26a" href="/Kiddushin.26a">Babli <i>Qiddušin</i> 26a</a> and <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 150b" href="/Bava_Batra.150b"><i>Baba Batra</i> 150b</a> it is clear that in old Mishnah texts, the reading was that any real estate obligates its owner “for <i>peah</i>, first fruits, and declaration.” They negate the last term since they read it as referring to the declaration to be made in the Temple that the tithes of the last three years were given as required. Since tithes are due also from a tenant farmer or a sharecropper and, in case the farmer did not give tithes, even from the buyer of grain, and, in addition, the text does not require “tithes from your land,” there is no place for this clause in the Mishnah. The reference in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 26:15" href="/Deuteronomy.26.15"><i>Deut</i>. 26:15</a> is to את האדמה אשר נתת לנו.<br>Nachmanides quotes an explanation that the “declaration” is the one required for first fruits (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 26:3-10" href="/Deuteronomy.26.3-10"><i>Deut</i>. 26:3–10</a>); he is followed by Meïri and R. Yom Tov ben Abraham Al-Išbili. The reference would be to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 26:10" href="/Deuteronomy.26.10"><i>Deut</i>. 26:10</a>: האדמה אשר נתת לי. There is no verse מן האדמה אשר נתת לנו.</i>, since it is written (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 26:15" href="/Deuteronomy.26.15"><i>Deut</i>. 26:15</a>): “From the land that You gave us.<sup class="footnote-marker">128</sup><i class="footnote">The text is slightly misquoted; it should read: “And the Land that You gave us.”</i>” Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun<sup class="footnote-marker">129</sup><i class="footnote">What R. Mana put forward as a possibility is a tradition for R. Yose bar Abun.</i> taught a tradition in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: “He who has no real estate is freed from the declaration, since it is written: From the land that You gave us.”
Rebbi Yose in the name of Rebbi Joshua ben Levi: He who has no real estate is free from the appearance since it is said (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 34:24" href="/Exodus.34.24"><i>Ex</i>. 34:24</a>): “Nobody will illegally desire your land<sup class="footnote-marker">130</sup><i class="footnote">“When you go up to appear before the Eternal, your God, three times in the year.”</i>.” It happened that one person left his grain heap<sup class="footnote-marker">131</sup><i class="footnote">Unprotected.</i>; when he returned, he found lions surrounding it. It happened that one left a chicken coop; when he returned, he found torn cats before it. One man left his house open; when he came, he found a snake rolled up on its tail<sup class="footnote-marker">132</sup><i class="footnote">Greek κέρκος, ἡ “tail of an animal” (Explanation of De Rossi.)</i>. Rebbi Phineas told the following story: Two brothers in Askalon had Gentile neighbors. These said, if those Jews go up to Jerusalem, we will take all they have. When they went up, the Holy One, praise to Him, alotted to them two angels who came and went in their likeness. When they returned, they sent them<sup class="footnote-marker">133</sup><i class="footnote">In the entire story from here on, the singular is frequently used for the plural. This happens in several Yerushalmi passages.</i> valuable presents. They said to them: where have you been? They said to them, in Jerusalem. They said to them: Whom did you leave in your house? They said, nobody. They said: Praised be the God of the Jews Who did not let us act and did not abandon them!
From where that non-guaranteed property<sup class="footnote-marker">135</sup><i class="footnote">Movables, which cannot have a title guarantee.</i> may be acquired with guaranteed property<sup class="footnote-marker">136</sup><i class="footnote">Real estate.</i>, by money, document, or possession? Rebbi Yose<sup class="footnote-marker">137</sup><i class="footnote">He is Rebbi Yasa, Assi. The entire paragraph is paralleled in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Kiddushin 26a-27a" href="/Kiddushin.26a-27a">Babli <i>Qiddušin</i> 26a–27a</a>; there, the verse is simply quoted by Ḥizqiah himself.</i> in the name of Ḥizqiah; Rebbi Jonah, Rebbi Ḥanina Tortaya in the name of Ḥizqiah, it is written (<a class="refLink" data-ref="II Chronicles 21:3" href="/II_Chronicles.21.3"><i>2Chr</i>. 21:3</a>): “Their father gave them many gifts, silver and gold and delicacies, with fortified cities in Jehudah<sup class="footnote-marker">138</sup><i class="footnote">The language implies that the gift of valuables was contingent on the gift of cities.</i>.” So far real estate and movables were at the same place. If real estate was at one place and movables elsewhere? Rebbi Abin bar Ḥiyya said, let us hear from the following: Rebbi Eliezer<sup class="footnote-marker">139</sup><i class="footnote">Reading in the Yerushalmi and <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Batra 9:7:2-4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Batra.9.7.2-4"><i>Baba Batra</i> 9:9</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 156b" href="/Bava_Batra.156b">Babli <i>Baba Batra</i> 156b</a>, and in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bava Batra (Lieberman) 10:12" href="/Tosefta_Bava_Batra_(Lieberman).10.12">Tosephta <i>Baba Batra</i> 10:12</a>. The corresponding Mishnah, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Batra 9:7:2-4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Batra.9.7.2-4"><i>Baba Batra</i> 9:9</a> reads R. Eliezer in the Yerushalmi and also in the Munich manuscript of the Babli, as well as in the Venice and Lublin prints. It was changed to R. Eleazar (ben Shamua) in modern prints of the Babli, following the reading at one place in Babli <i>Qiddušin</i>, on the authority of Rabbenu Tam (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosafot on Bava Batra 156a" href="/Tosafot_on_Bava_Batra.156a">Tosaphot <i>Baba Batra</i> 156a</a>) for stylistic reasons. However, the overwhelming majority of manuscript sources must have priority over arguments concerning style.</i> said to them, it happened that a man from Madon<sup class="footnote-marker">140</sup><i class="footnote">A town in Galilee, on the heights overlooking the sea of Galilee, mentioned <a class="refLink" data-ref="Joshua 11:1" href="/Joshua.11.1"><i>Joshua</i> 11:1</a>. The man was in Jerusalem, fell ill, and wanted to distribute his property to people in his home town, other than the legal heirs.</i> was in Jerusalem, rich in movables. He wanted to distribute them, to give them as gifts. They said to him, you cannot do that<sup class="footnote-marker">141</sup><i class="footnote">Transfer movable property as gift without the recipients taking possession at least symbolically.</i> except if you acquire real estate. What did he do? He went and bought a rock<sup class="footnote-marker">142</sup><i class="footnote">A cheap piece of land without practical use. (In one interpretation in the Babli <i>Baba Batra</i>, he bought land the size of a <i>sela</i>‘, i. e., a tetradrachma, a silver coin about the size of a quarter. A pun may be intended, since Hebrew סלע also means “rock”.)</i> near Jerusalem and said: The Northern part I give to X with a hundred amphoras of wine, the Southern part I give to Y with a hundred amphoras of oil. The matter came before the Sages and they upheld his words<sup class="footnote-marker">143</sup><i class="footnote">It seems that the legal heirs went to court to annul the distribution. Since the amphoras could not be placed on the rock, it is clear that real estate and movables were at different places.</i>. Rebbi Ḥananiah said before Rebbi Mana: But was he not bedridden<sup class="footnote-marker">144</sup><i class="footnote">Many rules of transfer and wills are waved for seriously sick persons to assure them that their orders will be obeyed. The first example given by R. Ḥanania is generally accepted; the second one is then offered as interpretation of this Tosephta, that acquisition of movables remote from the land transferred might be valid only for bedridden persons.</i>? For in general a person might give property rights only in writing, and here even orally. In general, a person might give only if real estate and movables are at the same place, but here the real estate even was at one place and the movables elsewhere. He said to him: But is there a difference for Rebbi Eliezer? The sick person for Rebbi Eliezer is like the healthy person for the rabbis<sup class="footnote-marker">145</sup><i class="footnote">In <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Batra 9:7:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Batra.9.7.1">Mishnah <i>Baba Batra</i> 9:9</a>, R. Eliezer (Bavli in some versions R. Eleazar) explicitly states that bedridden persons are not exempt from the stringent rules of transfer; he does not allow oral bequests.</i>.
There, we have stated<sup class="footnote-marker">146</sup><i class="footnote">In fact, it has been stated here. The main place of this and the preceding paragraph is <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Batra 9:7:2-4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Batra.9.7.2-4"><i>Baba Batra</i> 9:9</a>, where “there”, i. e., <i>Peah</i>, makes sense.</i>: “Any real estate is subject to <i>peah</i> and first fruits.” What is the use of “any” real estate<sup class="footnote-marker">147</sup><i class="footnote">The question presupposes that one may not acquire movables elsewhere with the contract for real property. The Yerushalmi accepts the answer given in the previous paragraph that only R. Eliezer allows acquisition of remote movables with real estate. The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Kiddushin 26b" href="/Kiddushin.26b">Babli (<i>Qiddušin</i> 26b</a>) asks the same question after quoting R. Eliezer; the answer given here by R. Mattaniah is there ascribed to Rav Ashi, a late Amora. But the Babli adds another lengthy discussion whose explicit outcome is that movables anywhere can be acquired with a contract for real estate. This is a difference in practice between Yerushalmi and Babli.</i>? Rebbi Mattaniah said, explain it if it had space for one stalk but a pearl was hidden in it.
Who is bedridden? Anyone who did not get sick suddenly<sup class="footnote-marker">154</sup><i class="footnote">But if he gets sick suddenly, he is called מְסוּכָּן “critical”.</i>. It is customary for the relatives to visit him immediately; unrelated persons visit him after three days. If he falls ill suddenly, everybody visits him immediately. A clarification: Rebbi Ḥuna, Rebbi Phineas, and Rebbi Ḥizqiah went to visit Rebbi Yose after three days. He said to them: Do you want to sustain the <i>baraita</i> in my case<sup class="footnote-marker">155</sup><i class="footnote">To act as if you were unrelated, while you are as dear to me as any relative.</i>?
“If he reserved any real estate for himself, his gift is permanent,” even if he did not recover<sup class="footnote-marker">156</sup><i class="footnote">Since he reserved property for himself, he did not think that he would die and the transaction is a valid gift.</i>. “If he did not reserve any real estate for himself, his gift is not permanent” if he did recover<sup class="footnote-marker">157</sup><i class="footnote">He gave everything away because he thought that he would die and his action amounts to a will which is not valid as long as the testator is alive. Since the gift was not made in due form but under the special rule for sick people, it becomes invalid automatically when the testator recovers.</i>.
Rebbi Abba, Rav Huna in the name of Rav<sup class="footnote-marker">158</sup><i class="footnote">In <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Batra 9:4:2-3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Batra.9.4.2-3"><i>Baba Batra</i> 9:6</a>, this is a statement of R. Joḥanan in the name of R. Yannai, given as justification for the rules in the preceding paragraph.</i>: They<sup class="footnote-marker">159</sup><i class="footnote">The rabbis, originators of the rule.</i> declared oral instructions by a bedridden person equal to written and delivered ones<sup class="footnote-marker">160</sup><i class="footnote">A gift document becomes valid only on acceptance by the recipient or his representative.</i> by a healthy person, on condition that he had died from that particular sickness and that he describes the property and says, give field X to Y<sup class="footnote-marker">161</sup><i class="footnote">If the instructions are ambiguous or leave the implementation to a third party, they do not invalidate the claims of the legal heirs.</i>. If he says, half of the field to X and half to Y, is that designated? Or only if he says: the Northern half, the Southern half<sup class="footnote-marker">162</sup><i class="footnote">In the interpretation of Maimonides (<i>Zekhiah umattanah</i> 3:5), the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Ketubot 109b" href="/Ketubot.109b">Babli in <i>Ketubot</i> 109b</a> and <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 8b" href="/Gittin.8b"><i>Giṭṭin</i> 8b</a> accepts “half a field” as complete description which makes the gift valid and collectible. Since the Yerushalmi does not resolve the question, the law remains in doubt and, by the rule that the claimant has to prove his claim, the gift is not collectible.</i>?
Rebbi Avina in the name of Rebbi Jeremiah<sup class="footnote-marker">163</sup><i class="footnote">In the parallel <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Batra 9:4:2-3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Batra.9.4.2-3"><i>Baba Batra</i> 9:6</a>, the reading is: R. Jeremiah in the name of Rav.</i>: If he reserved movables, he did not do anything<sup class="footnote-marker">164</sup><i class="footnote">Since the Mishnah formulates that only a reservation of real estate validates the gift.</i>. Come and look: If he reserved any real estate, he has something to live on<sup class="footnote-marker">165</sup><i class="footnote">Presumably, the reservation clause means that he reserves enough to live on if he recovers. Nobody can live on the proceeds from real estate the size of a <i>sela</i>, but he can live comfortably as a dealer in gems and pearls. Hence, the exclusion of movables by R. Joḥanan does not seem to make sense.</i>; if he reserved gems and pearls, does he have nothing to live on? Rebbi Yose said, this man knows that the gift of a bedridden person transfers property rights even verbally<sup class="footnote-marker">166</sup><i class="footnote">Rebbi Yose notes that the Mishnah and the commentary of R. Jeremiah refer to a written gift of a bedridden person. Since it is unnecessary in this case to write a document, the fact that the document was written confers on it the status of a gift by a healthy person. The reservation clause is purely formal and the argument about livelyhood is irrelevant.</i>. Why did he write it with an obligation of acquisition, in order to give it the status of a gift by a healthy person.
If he reserved slaves<sup class="footnote-marker">167</sup><i class="footnote">In certain respects, slaves are treated like real estate, but in others like movables. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 150a" href="/Bava_Batra.150a">Babli (<i>Baba</i> <i>Batra</i> 150a</a>) they are defined as movable real estate. In the Babli also it seems that the exclusion of slaves has no legal consequence.</i>, about that R. Judan bar Pazi from Bar Delaiah stated something, but we do not know what he stated.
If he wrote for one and changed his mind and wrote for another<sup class="footnote-marker">168</sup><i class="footnote">A bedridden person who gave property away by a written document in which no real estate was reserved. Then he wrote a second document to supersede the first one. If all had been oral, there is no doubt that a later will would annul the prior one. If the document was simply written and nobody performed an act of acquisition, it seems that even for the Yerushalmi the document is not valid (this is the explicit position of the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 152b" href="/Bava_Batra.152b">Babli <i>Baba Batra</i> 152b</a>). Hence, somebody must have received the document for the beneficiary. According to Rav, this makes the gift absolute as explained by R. Yose earlier. According to R. Joḥanan (in the Babli, Samuel), since in any case the deed will automaticaly be rescinded if the donor recuperates, the document is not executable as long as the donor lives and, hence, it is rescindable by him.</i>, Rav says he cannot change his mind, Rav Abba bar Ḥuna and Rebbi Joḥanan say he may change his mind. What does it mean<sup class="footnote-marker">169</sup><i class="footnote">If the ownership of a slave is transferred in the gift document.</i>? If his first and second masters were Cohanim and the third<sup class="footnote-marker">170</sup><i class="footnote">The first master is the donor, the second master is the beneficiary of the second gift, and the third master the beneficiary of the first gift.</i> an Israel, in the opinion of Rav he may<sup class="footnote-marker">171</sup><i class="footnote">This is the text of all manuscripts, including R. S. Cirillo. All commentators declare the text to be faulty and switch “may” and “may not” between Rav and Rebbi Joḥanan; however, this seems to be inadmissible.</i> not eat <i>terumah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">172</sup><i class="footnote">A slave is a family member; hence, the slave of a Cohen may eat <i>terumah</i> (in ritual purity), the slave of a Jewish non-Cohen (Israel, including Levites) may not eat <i>terumah</i>. In the opinion of Rav, the Israel is the master if the donor dies and, since a gift is retroactive as explained in the second paragraph following, the slave is disqualified from the moment of the symbolic act of acquisition of the first document.</i>, in the opinion of Rav Abba bar Ḥuna in the name of R. Joḥanan he may eat <i>terumah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">173</sup><i class="footnote">Since both the donor and the legal recipient are Cohanim, the question of ownership is irrelevant.</i>. Everybody agrees that if his first master was an Israel<sup class="footnote-marker">174</sup><i class="footnote">And both recipients are Cohanim.</i>, he may not eat <i>terumah</i>; maybe the giver will recuperate.
Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun in the name of Rav Ḥuna, a <i>baraita</i> supports Rav Abba bar Ḥuna and Rebbi Joḥanan: “<sup class="footnote-marker">175</sup><i class="footnote">The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bava Batra (Lieberman) 8:9" href="/Tosefta_Bava_Batra_(Lieberman).8.9">Tosephta (<i>Baba Batra</i> 8:9</a>) has quite a different (Babylonian) version: A healthy person who wrote a will (in the abridged form only permitted to bedridden persons), a bedridden one who wrote a gift document (in the language prescribed for healthy people), even though he let somebody acquire for the recipient, they did not do anything (their documents are invalid). But if somebody wrote as agent for another person (who is sick and afraid of his family) a gift document and let it be acquired, his action is valid.</i> A healthy person who wrote a will<sup class="footnote-marker">176</sup><i class="footnote">Greek διαθήκη “will, testament”.</i> and a bedridden one who wrote a gift document<sup class="footnote-marker">177</sup><i class="footnote">In the language of a healthy person’s gift that becomes valid on acceptance.</i> may change their minds.” A healthy person who wrote a will may change his mind, even as long as he is healthy. Similarly, a bedridden person who wrote a gift document may change his mind, even while he still is bedridden.
What is a will? “It should be granted to me to live<sup class="footnote-marker">178</sup><i class="footnote">This is the reading of the Venice print and Leyden manuscript, and in all quotes of this passage by medieval authors. The Rome manuscript has לחיות “to live”; this is the reading preferred by S. Lieberman. The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 155b" href="/Bava_Batra.155b">Babli (<i>Baba Batra</i> 155b</a>) has למיקם “to get up”. In any case, it is a wish that the testator should live and, in that case, the will should cease to be in force.</i> and get up from bed, but if I should die, my properties should be given to X.” What is a gift? “All my properties are given as a gift to X from now on,” or it must be written: “as of today<sup class="footnote-marker">179</sup><i class="footnote">Since no gift document can enter into force after the death of its author. In the case of the will, it does not come into force, but there is an obligation on the heirs to fulfill the wishes of the dead. The language “from today” changes even a will into a gift.</i>.”
The sister of Rebbi Gurin<sup class="footnote-marker">180</sup><i class="footnote">An Amora of the fourth generation, student of R. Yose bar Ḥanina and R. Simeon ben Laqish. His brother’s name was Menaḥem.</i> wrote her possessions over to her brother<sup class="footnote-marker">181</sup><i class="footnote">It follows from the discussion of the case that the document contained the clause that it was valid “from today” even if it had a statement that it would become executable only after the testator’s death. The gift to the elder brother was written in the same terms.</i>. His older brother came, made peace with her, and she wrote her possessions over to him. The case came before Rebbi Immi. He said: So says Rebbi Joḥanan, one may change his mind. Rebbi Zeïra said, but does Rebbi Joḥanan not agree that if it was written “from today” one may not change his mind<sup class="footnote-marker">181</sup><i class="footnote">It follows from the discussion of the case that the document contained the clause that it was valid “from today” even if it had a statement that it would become executable only after the testator’s death. The gift to the elder brother was written in the same terms.</i>? Rebbi Abbahu came in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan, one may not change his mind. Rebbi Illaï came in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan, one may not change his mind. Then Rebbi Immi remembered, and the judgement was reversed.
Rav said<sup class="footnote-marker">182</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to the last part of the Mishnah and explains the circumstances under which the <i>ketubah</i> may be replaced by part of the inheritance.</i>, if he lets them acquire through her<sup class="footnote-marker">183</sup><i class="footnote">If the wife is the person through whose acceptance the entire document becomes valid. Then it must be assumed that she agreed to its terms.</i>. Samuel says, if he distributes in her presence<sup class="footnote-marker">184</sup><i class="footnote">And she does not object.</i>. Rebbi Yose ben Ḥanina said, they stated here a relaxation of the laws of <i>ketubah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">185</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 132" href="/Bava_Batra.132">Babli (<i>Baba Batra</i> 132</a> ab), R. Yose ben Ḥanina, while still calling it a relaxation of the rules of <i>ketubah</i>, offers a third possibility: If the husband offered the real estate as settlement of the <i>ketubah</i> and she accepted. This is not accepted in the Yerushalmi, probably to avoid the possibility that the husband exerts undue pressure on his wife in a purely private transaction.</i>, and so stated bar Qappara that a relaxation of the laws of <i>ketubah</i> is proclaimed here<sup class="footnote-marker">186</sup><i class="footnote">Both according to Rav or to Samuel. The relaxation is explained by Rebbi Abba.</i>. Rebbi Abba said, the reason of Rebbi Yose ben Ḥanina is that it applies not only to a <i>ketubah</i> of a mina<sup class="footnote-marker">187</sup><i class="footnote">100 <i>zuz</i>, the minimal <i>ketubah</i> of a previously married woman. Cf. Greek μνᾶ, ἡ, Latin <i>mina</i>, “sum or weight of 100 <i>drachmae</i>”, derived from Hebrew/Syriac/Accadic מנה.</i> or two hundred<sup class="footnote-marker">188</sup><i class="footnote">200 <i>zuz</i>, the minimal <i>ketubah</i> of a virgin.</i>, but even regarding a <i>ketubah</i> of one thousand gold denars they proclaimed a relaxation of the laws of <i>ketubah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">189</sup><i class="footnote">The value of a regular <i>ketubah</i> of 200 <i>zuz</i> is easily satisfied by real estate. But for a thousand gold denars, representing a few million $ buying power in today’s money, it is not likely that the full value will be covered by agricultural real estate.</i>.

Halakhah 8

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> He who writes over his properties to his slave makes him free<sup class="footnote-marker">190</sup><i class="footnote">Since the slave is also property, he acquires himself and, therefore, becomes his own master. The gift document is at the same time the document of manumission.</i>. If he reserved any real estate, he does not make him free<sup class="footnote-marker">191</sup><i class="footnote">Since he reserved something, in the interpretation of Maimonides (following the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 9a" href="/Gittin.9a">Babli, <i>Giṭṭin</i> 9a</a>) the document does not refer exclusively to the slave and, therefore, is not a document of manumission. Since the master is now dead, nobody else can be forced to write a document of manumission for the slave. R. Simson’s interpretation is that he writes the deed only to make sure that the slave take care to preserve the estate but he will have reserved the slave himself for his heirs.</i>. Rebbi Simeon says, the slave always becomes free unless the master says, all my properties are given to my slave X except one tenthousandth of them<sup class="footnote-marker">192</sup><i class="footnote">Since he did not spell out what is reserved, the slave himself may be reserved. But if he spelled out what is reserved, that is reserved and nothing else.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> Rebbi Yasa (Assi) in the name of Rebbi Eleazar: If he reserved some movables, he did not do anything<sup class="footnote-marker">193</sup><i class="footnote">Following both interpretations of the Mishnah, the situation is identical to the one where real estate was reserved and the document is invalid.</i>. I say that he reserved the slave’s person.
<sup class="footnote-marker">194</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to the opinion of R. Simeon who corrects the wrong attitude of the first teacher of the Mishnah. It is taken from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Peah (Lieberman) 1:13" href="/Tosefta_Peah_(Lieberman).1.13">Tosephta <i>Peah</i> 1:13</a>: “He who writes over his properties to his slave makes him free. If he reserved any real estate, he does not make him free. Rebbi Simeon says, if he said all my properties are given to my slave X except one tenthousandth of them, then he did not say anything; but [if he said all] except the hamlet X or the field Y then the slave acquired the property and acquired his freedom. When these words were said before Rebbi Yose, he said: : “He who answers correctly should be kissed on his lips.” R. Simson has in addition: “Even if the estate contains only this hamlet or that field,” meaning that the slave acquires himself (his freedom) even if he acquires no other property from the estate of his dead master. This is the interpretation of all medieval authorities.<br>Since R. Yose is the most authoritative of the teachers of his generation, his support of R. Simeon’s position makes that the valid practice, against the anonymous majority opinion of the Mishnah.</i> When the case came before Rebbi Yose, he said (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Proverbs 24:26" href="/Proverbs.24.26"><i>Prov</i>. 24:26</a>): “He who answers correctly should be kissed on his lips.”

Chapter 4



Halakhah 1

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> <i>Peah</i> is given connected to the ground<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">Unharvested.</i>. From a climbing vine<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">Climbing on a high pole or tree so that the grapes cannot be reached from the ground.</i> and a date palm, the owner brings it down and divides it up among the poor. Rebbi Simeon says, also for smooth<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">This is the reading of the Leyden manuscript and the Yerushalmi print. Some Mishnah manuscripts have חֲלַקֵּי, meaning the same, and some חֲלוּקֵי “distributed”. The majority seems to follow the reading “smooth”, which according to <i>Tiferet Israel</i> means trees without branches to hold on to, which cannot safely be harvested without a ladder.</i> nut trees. Even if 99 poor people say to distribute and one says to grab<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">Harvest from standing crop.</i>, one listens to him whose request is in keeping with practice. But for a climbing vine and a date palm it is not so; even if 99 say to grab and one says to distribute, one listens to him whose request is in keeping with practice.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 23:22" href="/Leviticus.23.22"><i>Lev</i>. 23:22</a>) “Do not finish off the corner of your field during your harvest.” This shows that <i>peah</i> is given standing on the ground. One might think this also includes climbing vine and date palms, but the verse says “harvest.” Harvest is special in that a small person can do it as well as a tall person; this excludes climbing vine and date palm where a small person cannot do it as well as a tall person<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">This tannaïtic statement is not found in any of the parallel collections.</i>.
Some<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sifra Qedošim</i> 2(5–7).</i> want to understand it from the verse (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 19:10" href="/Leviticus.19.10"><i>Lev</i>. 19:10</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 23:22" href="/Leviticus.23.22">23:22</a>) “abandon<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 19:10" href="/Leviticus.19.10"><i>Lev</i>. 19:10</a>: “Do not glean in your vineyard, neither collect single berries, <i>abandon them</i> for the poor and the stranger; I am the Eternal, your God.” <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 23:22" href="/Leviticus.23.22"><i>Lev</i>. 23:22</a>: “When you are harvesting the harvest of your land, do not finish off the corner of your field during your harvest, neither collect the gleanings of your harvest, <i>abandon them</i> for the poor and the stranger; I am the Eternal, your God.” The implication is that <i>peah</i> has to be given before any further processing.</i>,” put before them grain in its straw, fenugreek in bundles, dates in brooms<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">Dates grow in bunches that sit on the trunk; if the bunch is cut at the trunk and the dates are taken, the remainder may be used as a broom.</i>. I might think that this also includes climbing vine and date palms, but the verse says “them<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">Only those items that are described by the term “harvest”.</i>.” What argument do you have to include these and to exclude those? After the verse included, it excluded. I include those that are not dangerous and exclude those that are dangerous<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">“Connected” here means “still hanging on the tree.” Then the plucking cannot be described as “harvest”. Since this derivation by inclusion and exclusion follows the method of R. Ismael, the preceding paragraph must represent the opinion of R. Aqiba.</i>.
Is it not connected<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">The fruits still are connected to the tree. This paragraph discusses whether climbing vines and dates are an exception to the rule that <i>peah</i> has to be given for the standing crop, or whether it is only a technicality requiring the owner to harvest the <i>peah</i> himself. The answer is that there is no exception; it may well be that the name <i>peah</i> has to be given when the fruits are still hanging on the tree, but this does not change the fact that the owner must pay for taking them down.</i>? He calls it by the name <i>peah</i> when it is on the tree<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">Since produce is not exempt from tithes and may not be taken by the poor until the farmer has designated it as such, giving it the name “<i>peah</i>”.</i>. If you say not from standing produce, he calls it by the name <i>peah</i> when it is taken down. If you say he calls it by the name <i>peah</i> when it is on the tree, the removal is charged to the poor. If you say he calls it by the name <i>peah</i> when it is taken down, the removal is the obligation of the owner. But even if you say that he calls it by the name <i>peah</i> when it is on the tree, they<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">The religious authorities of earlier times.</i> charge the owner with taking it down because of the danger.
It was stated in the name of Rebbi Meïr<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">Reading of the Rome and Cirillo manuscripts. The Venice print has “Rebbi Immi”; that reading is impossible since Rebbi Immi was an Amora, not a Tanna, and the statement is a tannaïtic one. The further discussion is in the name of R. Meïr even in the Venice text.</i>: All trees are dangerous. Does Rebbi Meïr not infer from “harvest” while the rabbis likewise infer from “harvest<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">The inference drawn in the first paragraph of this Halakhah from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 23:22" href="/Leviticus.23.22"><i>Lev</i>. 23:22</a>.</i>?” Everybody infers from “harvest”, except that Rebbi Meïr says that all trees are dangerous and the rabbis say only the climbing vine and the date palm are dangerous. Rebbi Ḥananiah in the name of Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish: It has happened that five brothers died climbing five smooth nut trees<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">Accordingly, practice must follow Rebbi Meïr and Rebbi Simeon against the Mishnah. However, Maimonides follows the Mishnah both in his commentary and in his Code.</i>.
If the owner called it <i>peah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">The last part of the grain on his field; this makes it legally the property of the poor. Then he proceeded to cut it.</i> and nevertheless finished harvesting the entire field, I read for him: “Do not finish off the corner of your field during your harvest.<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">Again, the entire verse is to be understood, including the second part: “Leave it for the poor and the stranger.” Even though he has transgressed the first part of the injunction, he still is obligated for the second part, viz., to leave the cut <i>peah</i> to the poor by abandoning it on the field.</i>” If he did not call it <i>peah</i> and finished harvesting the entire field, I read for him: “Do not finish off the corner of your field during your harvest.”
If they agreed among themselves<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">The poor demanding that either the farmer should divide the produce instead of them harvesting it, or declaring that they want to climb the tree when he would be obligated to get the <i>peah</i> and give it to them. Since the Mishnah says that if 99 want to differ from the rule and one wants to comply with it (even if he acts out of selfishness because he would be able to grab more than the others), it follows that if all of them agree the agreement is valid. The Halakha notes that one does not listen to <i>him</i>, i. e., if the farmer is part of the agreement (and presumably its originator, but in any case a person of overriding influence over the poor) then the agreement is void. [This is the explanation of R. Simson of Sens. It seems that Maimonides reads הִתְנוּ בֵּינֵיהֶן שׁוֹמְעִין לָהֶן or a similar text, since in his Code (<i>Mattenot Aniïm</i> 2:16,17) he repeats twice that agreements among the poor about the manner of distribution are valid.]</i>, even so one does not listen to him.
The owner has to distribute by hand to avoid that he would see a poor person of his acquaintance and throw it before him<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">The Mishnah notes that the owner of the tree must divide <i>peah</i>; this means that he cannot simply take the fruits down and let the poor take them. Since we have the general rule of “abandon”, any exception needs a justification.</i>. Rebbi Samuel bar Eudaimon questioned<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">He asks whether the obligation to distribute only applies to those fruits which never fall under the category of “grabbing” or whether it extends to all cases where the farmer has to bring the produce before the poor, as in the case of grain already bound in sheaves. The question remains unanswered.</i>: If he finished harvesting his field, you say that <i>peah</i> returned to the sheaves. Even so, does the owner have to distribute by hand to avoid that he would see a poor person of his acquaintance and throw it before him?

Halakhah 2

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If he<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">A poor man entitled to collect <i>peah</i>.</i> took some of the <i>peah</i> and threw it on the rest<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">In order to claim possession. Since <i>peah</i> has the legal status of abandoned property, the slightest action of acquisition should be valid.</i>, he has nothing of it<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">As punishment, since all poor should have equal access to any produce left by the farmer. According to R. Simson of Sens, the Mishnah means that he retains what he already had but his action is invalid as acquisition.</i>. If he fell on it or spread his talith on it, one removes him from it<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">For the reasons given above.</i>. The same is valid for gleanings and the forgotten sheaf.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> It was stated in the name of Rebbi Meïr: “One punishes him and takes away from him both the cut and the standing produce.<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Peah (Lieberman) 2:1" href="/Tosefta_Peah_(Lieberman).2.1">Tosephta <i>Peah</i> 2:1</a> reads: “If he took some of the <i>peah</i> and threw it on the rest, he has no claim to it. Rebbi Meïr says, one punishes him and takes away from him both the cut and the standing produce.” This proves that the language of the Mishnah, the first sentence of the Tosephta, implies that he does not lose the <i>peah</i> he previously collected. Maimonides, who in his commentary to the Mishnah writes that he loses that <i>peah</i> also, explains the Halakhah, not the Mishnah.</i>” That refers to intentional misconduct; does it apply even to action in error or even to small sheaves<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">If the poor collector already had made a small sheaf of his <i>peah</i>. The question is not answered; for practical purposes, it is answered in the negative.</i>?
Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish in the name of Abba Cohen Bar Dalaia<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">A Tanna of the first generations: Abba, a priest of the course of Dalaiahu, the 23rd of the 24 priestly divisions.</i>: A person acquires a find within four cubits of himself<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">If somebody sees an abandoned object and intends to acquire it, it becomes his property when he is closer than 4 cubits to it, and nobody has the right to run past him and grab it. Since the Mishnah obviously declares that principle inoperative with regard to <i>peah</i>, there seems to be a contradiction between two rabbinic principles. The Babli (<i>Baba Meẓi‘a</i> 10a/b) resolves the problem by restricting the principle of Abba Cohen to the public domain; it does not apply to private property such as a field. The Yerushalmi disagrees; since <i>peah</i> is abandoned property, as proved in the preceding Halakhah, it must be assumed that the ground on which <i>peah</i> grows is temporarily also abandoned to the poor.<br>The expression “himself” will be given special importance in the following discussion.</i>. What is the reason? (<a class="refLink" data-ref="I Chronicles 22:14" href="/I_Chronicles.22.14"><i>1Chr</i>. 22:14</a>) “Look, in my poverty<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">How can anybody (King David) who has the means of dedicating such enormous wealth, call himself “poor”? The explanation is that the wealth never actually came into his possession but that he dedicated it as soon as it became his property by intent and proximity.</i> I prepared for the House of the Eternal 100’000 <i>kikkar</i><sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">One <i>kikkar</i> equals 3’000 <i>sheqel</i>, and, since the <i>sheqel</i> in the Talmud is defined as 2 drachmas, the <i>kikkar</i> is equal to the Greek talent of 6’000 drachmas. The 14 g silver <i>sheqalim</i> from the Jewish revolt were “sacred <i>sheqalim</i>” of double weight, or four civil denars. Honest silver denars or drachmae from the early principate weigh about 3.5 g. Taking civil <i>sheqalim</i>, one <i>kikkar</i> would be about 21 kg and David would have prepared 2 metric tons of gold and 20 metric tons of silver for the Temple.</i> of gold, 1’000’000 <i>kikkar</i> of silver, bronze and iron unweighed because it was so much, I prepared wood and stones, and you<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">Solomon.</i> should add to them.”
Rebbi Jonah said that Rebbi Hoshaiah asked: What are we talking about? If this was within four cubits from him, he was rich<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">After he dedicated all he had, he was poor. But if one has the means to dedicate billions, he cannot say that he is in poverty at the act of dedication.</i>. If it was outside of four cubits, may anybody dedicate anything that is not his<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">If A says to B that he dedicated a piece of B’s property for charity or religious purposes, his words are null and void and have neither legal nor religious implications.</i>? We confirmed it<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">The argument in the previous paragraph is still valid if David never came to possess a single piece of gold or silver worth more than 200 <i>zuz</i>; then he could assemble all this wealth for the Temple without ever ceasing to be legally poor.</i> if he dedicated it piece by piece.
Rebbi Abun said, what means “in my poverty?” That there is no wealth before Him Who commanded and the world came into existence<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">The expression “in my poverty” is homiletic and carries no legal meaning; the verse is inapplicable to our situation and the statement about the four cubits is a rabbinical institution without Biblical foundation.</i>! Another explanation: בְּעוֹנְיִי “in my deprivation<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">The other uses of עוני in the Bible show that this is the usual meaning of the word. This also excludes the verse from being used as proof in our context.</i>,” because he fasted and donated the price of his meal to Heaven.
Rebbi Jacob bar Idi objected before Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish. Did we not state: (Mishnah <i>Baba Meẓi‘a</i> 1:4) “If somebody saw a find and fell on it, when another person came and grabbed it, he who grabbed it had the rights to it?” He answered him: Explain it if the first one did not say that his four cubits should acquire it for him<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">The Babli (<i>Baba Meẓi‘a</i> 10b) explains that deeds speak louder than words and that by falling on the object the finder showed that he did not wish to acquire it by simple speech. In that case, it can be acquired only by grabbing, i. e., holding on to it and moving it. The Yerushalmi does not go so far; it only requires that an object should be within four cubits of the finder and that the latter should clearly express his intention of acquiring the object. Afterwards, he can do as he wishes.</i>. But did we not state<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">In our Mishnah.</i>: “If he fell on it or spread his talith on it, one removes him from it?” He said to him, that is the same, if he did not say that his four cubits should acquire it for him. But did not Rebbi Ḥiyya state<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">In our Tosephta, the statement is anonymous. The tradition that the Tosephta is originally from the school of Rebbi Ḥiyya seems to be an old Yerushalmi one.</i>: (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Peah (Lieberman) 2:2" href="/Tosefta_Peah_(Lieberman).2.2">Tosephta <i>Peah</i> 2:2</a>) “If two<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">In the Tosephta: “Two poor persons.”</i> were pushing<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">Since כתש means “to hew to pieces”, probably the translation should be: “Thrash one another.” In any case, they quarrel by physical force rather than words; it may be assumed that neither of them intended to acquire the forgotten sheaf by speech.</i> one another because of a sheaf and another poor person came and grabbed it from before them, he is entitled to it. He said to him, it is the same, he<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">Neither of the first two quarellers.</i> did not say that his four cubits should acquire it for him.
Rebbi Yasa said in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">The entire discussion belongs to the parallel in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 8:3:2-9" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.8.3.2-9"><i>Giṭṭin</i> 8:3</a> (fol. 49c), where it is stated in Mishnah 2 that a divorce document, of which it is written (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 24:1" href="/Deuteronomy.24.1"><i>Deut</i>. 24:1</a>): “He wrote her a divorce document and delivered it into her hands,” can be delivered by the husband’s throwing the document close to her (within four cubits) in the public domain, with the wife’s knowledge. Then Mishnah 3 adds: “The same rule applies to marriage and the settlement of debts,” meaning that if a woman agrees to marry a certain man and the groom, while declaring that the wedding gift is given for the purpose of marriage, instead of delivering it into the bride’s hand throws it close to her in the public domain in front of two witnesses, the marriage is valid. Similarly, if the creditor asks the debtor to throw to him the amount owed, if the debtor did that and it landed within the creditor’s four cubits in the public domain, the debtor has discharged all his obligations. On this, Rebbi Joḥanan comments that a gift can be acquired only by actual possession, not by four cubits in the public domain.</i>: That refers to divorce documents, but it does not apply to a gift. Rebbi Joḥanan adds something<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">His statement about gifts is far from trivial.</i>, Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish adds something. Rebbi Joḥanan adds something. Since a find which cannot be acquired by the knowledge of another person<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote">If two persons walk together, one sees a find and tells the second one to take it up for him, and the second one lifts it up with the intention of acting as the first person’s agent, the first one acquired it (Mishnah <i>Baba Meẓi‘a</i> 1:3). However, in that case the find must actually be taken up; the four cubits of the agent and his declaration are invalid. If the second person decides to take the find for himself, the four cubits and his declaration of intent of acquisition are valid and the find is his.</i> can be acquired within four cubits, should this rule not apply <i>a fortiori</i><sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote">Hence, his statement excluding gifts is necessary.</i> to a gift that is acquired by the knowledge of another person<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote">Without the donor’s intent of giving the gift, there is no gift and, hence, none can be acquired. [However, if A says to B: acquire the gift for C, the action of B is valid for C since one may let another person profit without the latter’s knowledge (explanation of R. Moses ben Ḥabib)].</i>? Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish adds something. Since a gift which cannot be acquired within four cubits<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">R. Simeon ben Laqish’s premise is R. Joḥanan’s conclusion and vice-versa. If the arguments were made by one person, they would be self-contradictory; for the position of two people they are acceptable.</i> can be acquired by the knowledge of another person, should this not <i>a fortiori</i> apply to a find<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote">Hence, the statement of R. Simeon ben Laqish in the name of Abba Cohen, that four cubits acquire for a person, is necessary insofar as it means acquisition for himself and not for another individual.</i>?
Rebbi Zeïra objected before Rebbi Yasa: But did we not state<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 8:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.8.3.1">Mishnah <i>Giṭṭin</i> 8:3</a>.</i>: The same applies to marriage? He answered him, divorce documents and marriage contracts have the same rules<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote">The statement of R. Joḥanan about exclusion of gifts could as well have been formulated for marriage contracts.</i>. But did we not state: The same applies to debt? He said to him, because if he<sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote">The creditor tells the debtor to throw the repayment into the sea. If the debtor follows instructions, he has discharged his obligations.</i> said to him: Throw it into the sea and your debt will be forgiven, it would be forgiven. But if that is true, even if it fell down close to the debtor, the debtor should have the benefit! But we have stated<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote">In the same Mishnah, speaking of the debtor throwing the money to the creditor. Nothing of four cubits was mentioned in the first explanation.</i>, if it falls down close to the debtor, the debtor is still obligated. For he said to him, throw it so that it will enter my domain, and it did not yet enter his domain<sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote">If the money fell outside the creditor’s four cubits in the public domain, the debt is not discharged since the condition was not satisfied. However, if the creditor stands on the seashore and the money falls close to him into the sea, the debtor has discharged his obligation. The problem raised in the return question is considered to be different and receives a different answer.</i>.
