Rebbi Abbahu said, all those objections that Rebbi Zeïra raised before Rebbi Yasa, Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish raised before Rebbi Joḥanan. Did he accept them from him? He solved them with those same solutions.

Halakhah 3

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> One does not harvest <i>peah</i> with sickles and one does not uproot it with axes so that they should not injure one another. Three investigation periods<sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote">Periods when the poor will go to the fields to see where they may collect <i>peah</i>. According to the interpretation of Maimonides (<i>Mattenot Aniïm</i> 2:17), the poor are not permitted to collect at other times. The root of the word is בעי “to question”. (The Halakhah reads אבעיות). The Halakhah will clarify the meaning of the three periods.</i> are there during the day, in the morning, at noontime, and in the evening. Rabban Gamliel says, they decreed this only to say that one may not do less. Rebbi Aqiba says, they decreed this only to say that one may not do more<sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote">The farmer is not allowed to declare <i>peah</i> at any other time, when not all poor have the opportunity to be present.</i>. In Bet Namer<sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote">Identified with Biblical Bet Nimrah (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 32:2" href="/Numbers.32.2"><i>Num</i>. 32:2</a>) on the Jordanian side of the Jordan, a <i>tell</i> called <i>Nymreïn</i>.</i> they insisted on collecting<sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>hiph</i>‘<i>il</i> form, given by the overwhelming majority of manuscript sources, is a causative: The farmer made his farmhands cut the grain along ropes stretched lengthwise along the field. In this way, the entire end-strip of the field became <i>peah</i>, given piecemeal during the harvest.</i> along a rope and were giving <i>peah</i> from every strip<sup class="footnote-marker">63</sup><i class="footnote">Greek ὄγμος, Byzantine pronunciation <i>oymos</i>, “furrow in plowing, swathe in reaping,” also ὤγμος, “strip of cultivated land.”</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> What are אֹבָּעְיוֹת? Rebbi Abun said, just as you say (<i>Ob</i>. 6): “How was Esau searched out, his hidden places investigated<sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote">The root בעה in Obadiah has a meaning close to Rabbinic Aramaic בעי.</i>.”
“In the morning, at noontime, and in the evening.” In the morning because of nursing women. At noontime because of the children. In the evening, because of the decrepit<sup class="footnote-marker">65</sup><i class="footnote">The old people; from the root משש “to totter.”</i>.
“Rabban Gamliel says, they decreed this only to say that one may not do less;” if he wants to add, he may add. “Rebbi Aqiba says, they decreed this only to say that one may not do more;” does this mean that if he wants to give less frequently he may not do so?
“Those of Bet Namer insisted on collecting along a rope and were giving <i>peah</i> from every strip.” It was stated: Abba Shaul says, one mentions them disapprovingly and one mentions them approvingly. One mentions them disapprovingly because they only gave <i>peah</i> of one percent<sup class="footnote-marker">67</sup><i class="footnote">And not 1⅔% as required by rabbinical ordinance.</i>. And one mentions them approvingly because they insisted on collecting along a rope and were giving <i>peah</i> from every strip.
It was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">69</sup><i class="footnote">There are parallels in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Peah (Lieberman) 1:6" href="/Tosefta_Peah_(Lieberman).1.6">Tosephta <i>Peah</i> 1:6</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sifra, Kedoshim" href="/Sifra,_Kedoshim"><i>Sifra Qedošim</i> 1:10</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shabbat 23a" href="/Shabbat.23a">Babli <i>Šabbat</i> 23a</a>. In all these parallels, there are four reasons given, meaning that R. Simeon explains the reason of the Biblical law. In the Babli, R. Simeon is reputed to deduce laws from the reasoning behind Biblical precepts; the Yerushalmi does not seem to accept this but gives reasons separate from the verse. (The Tosephta in our hands is essentially a Babylonian compilation; <i>Sifra</i> is Palestinian material edited in Babylonia.) It is clear that R. Simeon supports here his argument in Mishnah 1:3 that <i>peah must</i> be given at the end, because of Biblical precept and rabbinical ordinance.</i> in the name of Rebbi Simeon: For five reasons one should give <i>peah</i> only at the end of one’s field, because of robbing the poor, because of idling the poor, because of cheats, because of bad impression, and because the Torah said (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 19:9" href="/Leviticus.19.9"><i>Lev</i>. 19:9</a>): “Do not finish off the corner of your field.” Because of robbing the poor, that no man should see a free hour and say to his poor relative: Come, and take this <i>peah</i> for yourself. Because of idling the poor, that the poor should not sit around, watch the entire day, and say: Now he is giving <i>peah</i>, now he is giving <i>peah</i>, but rather they should go, collect on another field, and come at the moment of finishing. Because of the cheats, that one should not say: I already gave, and then he chooses the good and brings out the bad<sup class="footnote-marker">70</sup><i class="footnote">We are not concerned here with people who willingly transgress the ordinances of the Torah. Rather, we speak about so-called pious people who find ways to minimize their obligations. In this case, the farmer harvests the entire field without giving <i>peah</i>. Then he is obligated to give <i>peah</i> from the cut grain but here he has the possibility to select stalks with small kernels for the poor, whereas he has no such control if he refrains from cutting the last corner.</i>. Because of bad impression, that the passers-by should not say: Look, this man harvested his field and did not leave <i>peah</i> for the poor. And because the Torah said: “Do not finish off the corner of your field.”

Halakhah 4

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> A Non-Jew who harvested his field and later converted is free from gleanings, forgotten sheaves, and <i>peah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">71</sup><i class="footnote">For <i>peak</i> and gleanings it is written: “At <i>your</i> harvesting,” and for the forgotten sheaf it says: “If <i>you</i> harvest <i>your</i> harvest,” and at the time of the harvest he still was an outsider.</i>. Rebbi Jehudah obligates him for forgotten sheaves because that applies only at the time of binding the sheaves<sup class="footnote-marker">72</sup><i class="footnote">To refrain from going back to collect a forgotten sheaf cannot apply as long as there are no sheaves. Hence, if the man converts between cutting the grain and binding the sheaves, the obligation comes to him as a Jew and he is bound by it.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> Rebbi Jehudah said it well, what is the reason of the Rabbis? Rebbi Yose in the name of Rav, Rebbi Ḥizqiah, Rav Jehudah in the name of Samuel: It is written (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 24:19" href="/Deuteronomy.24.19"><i>Deut</i>. 24:19</a>): “If you forget a sheaf on the field.” If you forget the standing grain<sup class="footnote-marker">73</sup><i class="footnote">The verse reads: “If <i>you</i> cut <i>your</i> harvest on <i>your</i> field and then you forget a sheaf on the field, do not return to collect it.” The obligation attaches not to sheafmaking but to harvesting. The <i>Sifri Debarim</i> 282 reads: “‘Your harvest,’ except if it was cut by Gentiles. From that they said: A Non-Jew who harvested his field and later converted is free from gleanings, forgotten sheaves and <i>peah</i>. Rebbi Jehudah obliges him for forgotten sheaves because that applies only at the time of binding the sheaves.”</i>. What applies to forgetting while it is standing also applies to forgetting sheaves, but what does not apply to forgetting while it is standing, does not apply to forgetting sheaves.
If a Jew and a Gentile were partners in the standing grain, the part of the Jew is obligated but the part of the Gentile is free. Rebbi Ḥizqiah in the name of Rav Jeremiah: If it was divided up<sup class="footnote-marker">75</sup><i class="footnote">The parallel in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Peah (Lieberman) 2:9" href="/Tosefta_Peah_(Lieberman).2.9">Tosephta <i>Peah</i>2:9</a> reads: “If a Jew and a Gentile were partners in the standing grain, the part of the Jew is obligated but the part of the Gentile is free. Rebbi Ismael said: If a Jew and a Gentile were partners in standing grain, it is free from <i>peah</i>. When is that? Only if the Gentile has veto power; if the Gentile has no veto power then it is obligated for <i>peah</i>.” This means that if the Jew may harvest his part without getting the Gentile’s permission, it is not a partnership as far as harvest is concerned but only a partnership for the growing period; accordingly, it is totally the Jew’s harvest and is obligated. The Yerushalmi seems to apply the expression “when is that?” and the following to the beginning statement, not to the opinion of Rebbi Ismael.</i>.

Halakhah 5

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If somebody dedicated<sup class="footnote-marker">77</sup><i class="footnote">As a monetary gift to the Temple. The Temple is the common property of all of Israel, hence the specific term “<i>your</i> (personal) harvest” is not applicable and produce harvested by the Temple administration is free from all obligations. The Temple did acquire real estate and other valuables only for redemption or sale, to be converted into currency.</i> standing produce and redeemed it standing, it is obligated<sup class="footnote-marker">78</sup><i class="footnote">Since at harvest time it is in private hands. The same applies in the next case; produce already obligated does not lose the obligation by dedication.</i>; in sheaves and redeemed in sheaves, it is obligated; standing and redeemed in sheaves it is free since at the time of obligation<sup class="footnote-marker">79</sup><i class="footnote">I. e., at harvest time.</i> it was free. Parallel to this, if somebody dedicated his produce before the time of tithes<sup class="footnote-marker">80</sup><i class="footnote">This will be explained in the Halakhah.</i> and redeemed it, it is obligated; after the time of tithes, it is obligated. But if he dedicated it before it was finished, the treasurer finished it<sup class="footnote-marker">81</sup><i class="footnote">Or, depending on the case, it grew to become obligated while in the hand of the Temple treasurer.</i>, and then the owner redeemed it, it is free since at the time of obligation it was free.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> And why is it stated twice<sup class="footnote-marker">82</sup><i class="footnote">The statement about tithes appears both here and in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Challah 3:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Challah.3.3.1">Mishnah <i>Ḥallah</i> 3:3</a>. The entire discussion also appears there in Halakhah 3.</i>? Rebbi Jonah, Rebbi Ḥiyya<sup class="footnote-marker">83</sup><i class="footnote">Rebbi Ḥiyya bar Abba.</i>, Rebbi Joshua ben Levi in the name of Bar Pedaiah<sup class="footnote-marker">84</sup><i class="footnote">Rebbi Jehudah bar Pedaiah, Amora of the first generation in Galilee, nephew of Bar Qappara and teacher of R. Joshua ben Levi (and also R. Joḥanan).</i>: One for smoothing<sup class="footnote-marker">85</sup><i class="footnote">The total obligation for heave and tithes starts only at the end of the harvest, when the grain is put into orderly heaps whose surface is smoothed (so that theft can easily be detected.) In modern terms, it would mean that the grain is stored in a silo.</i>, one for one third<sup class="footnote-marker">86</sup><i class="footnote">Grain that is one-third ripe may be used as food, <i>Grünkern</i>. From this moment on, grain kernels may be eaten on the spot without giving heave and tithes, but they may not be used for household purposes without first separating the dues for Cohen and Levite.</i>. Rebbi Yose said, Rebbi Abba and the colleagues. The colleagues say, one for smoothing, one for one third<sup class="footnote-marker">87</sup><i class="footnote">But they do not know which refers to what.</i>. Rebbi Abba explains: At <i>Ḥallah</i> for smoothing<sup class="footnote-marker">88</sup><i class="footnote">Since <i>ḥallah</i> is the heave of bread dough (in a sizeable amount), it does not qualify for “eating on the spot” but must refer to the finished harvest.</i>, at <i>Peah</i> for one third.
Our Mishnah is from Rebbi Aqiba since Rebbi Aqiba said that you go after the first third. And they differed: A field that was one-third ripened in the possession of a Gentile, and a Jew bought it after that time, Rebbi Aqiba said it is free<sup class="footnote-marker">90</sup><i class="footnote">The produce was in the hands of the Gentile at the time of the start of the obligation; so there is no obligation.</i>, but the Sages say he is obligated for the additional growth<sup class="footnote-marker">91</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Chullin 137a" href="/Chullin.137a">Babli, <i>Ḥullin</i> 137a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 77a" href="/Gittin.77a"><i>Giṭṭin</i>77a</a>, the positions of R. Aqiba and the Sages are inverted.</i>. How is that? Explain it for Rebbi Aqiba (after smoothing<sup class="footnote-marker">92</sup><i class="footnote">In <i>Maäserot</i> 5:4, the same statement is quoted and there it is said in the name of R. Eleazar that even the Sages obligate only for the Second Tithe or the tithe of the poor. These two alternative obligations are attached only to smoothed, stored grain; their obligation does not start with grain that is only one-third ripe.</i>) [in a disagreement<sup class="footnote-marker">93</sup><i class="footnote">That our Mishnah really follows the opinion of R. Aqiba, which, therefore, becomes the operative practice. [Since the Babli switches the names in the statement, it is the position of the Babli that our Mishnah represents the majority opinion of the Sages.]</i>], or according to everybody if he harvested immediately<sup class="footnote-marker">94</sup><i class="footnote">And there is no additional growth during the ownership of the Jew.</i>.

Halakhah 6

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> He<sup class="footnote-marker">96</sup><i class="footnote">The owner of the field.</i> who collected <i>peah</i> and said: “This is for X, the poor man,” Rebbi Eliezer says, he made him acquire it; but the Sages say, he should give it to the first poor person he meets. Gleanings, forgotten sheaves, and <i>peah</i> of a Non-Jew<sup class="footnote-marker">97</sup><i class="footnote">From the language of the Mishnah it is not clear whether this is from a field (in the Land of Israel) belonging to a Non-Jew, or whether these are gleanings from the field of a Jew collected by a Non-Jew and given to a poor Jew. The first possibility is preferred by the Yerushalmi, the second by the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 47a" href="/Gittin.47a">Babli (<i>Giṭṭin</i> 47a</a>). Regularly collected <i>peah</i>, gleanings, and forgotten sheaves are exempt from heave and tithes.</i> are obligated for tithes, except if the Non-Jew declared it to be abandoned property.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> Rebbi Joshua ben Levi says: They disagree about a rich owner<sup class="footnote-marker">98</sup><i class="footnote">Since he may not take <i>peah</i> for himself, he may not take it for others. The Babli (<i>Baba Meẓi‘a</i> 9b) explains that R. Eliezer disagrees: since everybody can make himself poor by giving away all his property, he is a potential receiver of charity and may collect, but he may not keep it as long as he is not poor.</i>. But a poor owner, since he has the right to take it<sup class="footnote-marker">99</sup><i class="footnote">For himself; he may acquire it legally even according to the Sages. That the poor farmer may not keep his own <i>peah</i> is a rabbinic institution that does not influence the status of the <i>peah</i> taken by any poor person.</i>, the other person acquired it.
Rebbi Zeïra said: Rebbi Eleazar<sup class="footnote-marker">100</sup><i class="footnote">The Amora.</i> (about one’s friend), Rebbi Joḥanan, and Rebbi Joshua ben Levi said the same thing. Rebbi Eleazar, as Rebbi Zeïra said in the name of Rebbi Eleazar: A man may acquire a find for his friend<sup class="footnote-marker">101</sup><i class="footnote">If a man finds abandoned or lost property, he may pick it up to acquire it for another person. This essentially is giving a gift of something that is not his property while it was lying in the street, and he picked it up with the intention of not acquiring it himself. It is enough that he could have picked it up for himself to empower him to give it directly to the beneficiary. This is the exact parallel to the poor farmer collecting <i>peah</i>, not to give later to somebody else, but so that the other should be the immediate owner of every collected stalk. [Later in this Halakhah and in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Metzia 10a" href="/Bava_Metzia.10a">Babli (<i>Baba Meẓi‘a</i> 10a</a>), this appears as a statement of R. Joḥanan. Since a statement X in the name of Y usually means that X did not hear the statement directly from Y, it is to be assumed that R. Zeïra heard the statement of R. Eleazar in the yeshivah of R. Joḥanan.]</i>. Rebbi Joḥanan, as we have stated there (Mishnah Baba Meẓiah 1:5): “The finds of his minor sons and daughters<sup class="footnote-marker">102</sup><i class="footnote">They have no independent legal status but can act for their father. The same holds for slaves and their master.</i>, of his Gentile slaves and slave girls, and of his wife<sup class="footnote-marker">103</sup><i class="footnote">It is a rabbinic ordinance that the wife’s finds and earnings belong to the husband, in exchange for his obligation to totally support his wife. If the wife is an earner, that clause may be abrogated by mutual consent.</i>, belong to him. The finds of his adult sons and daughters<sup class="footnote-marker">104</sup><i class="footnote">Who are legal persons in their own right.</i>, of his Jewish slaves and slave girls<sup class="footnote-marker">105</sup><i class="footnote">They are not really slaves but indentured servants for at most 6 years and do not lose their legal status. (The institution of Jewish slavery ended with the deportation of the first of the 10 tribes, since it was valid only as long as the distribution of land under Joshua was in force.)</i>, and of his divorced wife in case that he had not yet paid her <i>ketubah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">106</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Peah 3:7:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Peah.3.7.1">Chapter 3, Note 151</a>. As long as the <i>ketubah</i> is not paid, the ex-husband has to support his ex-wife, but her earnings and finds are hers to keep.</i>, belong to them. Rebbi Joḥanan said, if they<sup class="footnote-marker">107</sup><i class="footnote">The adult children. If they live in the father’s house and are supported by him, their earnings go to the father. Hence, if they find something, it is automatically assumed that they acquire the found property for their father.</i> are not dependents. But if they are dependent on their father, their finds belong to him. Rebbi Joshua ben Levi, as Rebbi Joshua ben Levi said: They disagree about a rich owner. But if the owner is poor, since he has the right to take it, the other person acquired it.
It was stated: If one hires a worker to do all kinds of work<sup class="footnote-marker">108</sup><i class="footnote">The duties are not specified. If the worker is hired for a specific kind of work, e. g., for ploughing, the find belongs to the worker (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Metzia 10a" href="/Bava_Metzia.10a">Babli <i>Baba Meẓi‘a</i> 10a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Metzia 12b" href="/Bava_Metzia.12b">12b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Metzia 118a" href="/Bava_Metzia.118a">118a</a>).</i> for him, the worker’s findings belong to his employer. Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish asked: If he wants to quit, he may quit<sup class="footnote-marker">109</sup><i class="footnote">In the Yerushalmi, this is a statement of R. Joḥanan (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Metzia 6:2:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Metzia.6.2.2"><i>Baba Meẓi‘a</i>6:2</a>); there Rav is of the opinion that in a hiring contract both parties may dissolve the relationship without notice (but the party that changes the contract is liable for damages arising for the other party.) In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Metzia 10a" href="/Bava_Metzia.10a">Babli (<i>Baba Meẓi‘a</i>10a</a>), Rav states that a worker can quit without notice since it is written (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 25:42" href="/Leviticus.25.42"><i>Lev</i>. 25:42</a>): “(The Children of Israel) are My slaves,” and not slaves of slaves. Hence, any contract that restricts the ability of the worker to leave his place of work is invalid.</i>; and you say that the find belongs to the employer<sup class="footnote-marker">110</sup><i class="footnote">If the findings are worth more than the daily wages, the finder could simply quit his job before picking up the find and then the employer would have no rights.</i>? Rebbi Jacob bar Aḥa<sup class="footnote-marker">111</sup><i class="footnote">Since R. Jacob bar Aḥa quotes himself in our text, his name here seems to be a scribal error.</i> said that Rebbi Yasa asked, why do we need that of Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish, did we not understand that Rebbi Jacob bar Aḥa said that Rebbi Joḥanan and Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish disagree? Rebbi Joḥanan said, a man may acquire a find for his friend. But Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish said, a man cannot acquire a find for his friend<sup class="footnote-marker">112</sup><i class="footnote">R. Simeon ben Laqish has no need for his objection since, for him, the question does not arise at all. In the Babli (<i>loc. cit</i>.), the objection to R. Joḥanan’s rule is raised and answered, that as long as the journeyman does not quit, all he does that day is to the employer’s benefit.</i>.
Rebbi Redifa<sup class="footnote-marker">113</sup><i class="footnote">An Amora of the last generation of Galilean Amoraïm who collected sayings of the Sages of the preceding generation.</i>: Rebbi Jonah and Rebbi Yose disagree. One says, he who may take may acquire<sup class="footnote-marker">114</sup><i class="footnote">He who may take for himself may acquire for another person without first taking possession himself.</i>; the other says, he who may give may acquire<sup class="footnote-marker">115</sup><i class="footnote">He who may distribute property may acquire for a third person directly since he could acquire and then give it away.</i>. He who says “he who may take” certainly includes him who may give. He who says “he who may give,” excludes him who may take.
A Mishnah disagrees with him who says that he who may take may acquire, since we have stated<sup class="footnote-marker">116</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 1:5:2-9" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.1.5.2-9"><i>Giṭṭin</i> 1:6</a>. This paragraph is elliptic and almost incomprehensible. The Mishnah reads: “If someone says, give this divorce document to my wife or this document of manumission to my slave, and then wants to change his mind in either case, he may do so in the opinion of R. Meïr. But the Sages say, in the case of a wife, but not in the manumission of slaves since one may favor a person without his knowledge.” Since the divorce is considered bad for the wife, it can become valid only with her knowledge. R. Meïr thinks that manumission also may be bad for the slave who will have to accept all duties of a free Jew; the Sages consider manumission always to be good. With the wife’s knowledge, however, the emissary, a man, can acquire the divorce document for her. But as a man, he is not in a position ever to receive a divorce document.</i>: “Give this divorce document to my wife …,” because he may receive the divorce document of his minor daughter<sup class="footnote-marker">117</sup><i class="footnote">The minor daughter who was formally betrothed (קידושין) but not yet actually married, can become free to marry another man only by divorce. The bill of divorce must be accepted by her father, or by herself on the instruction of the father, since she becomes of age only by actual marriage. Hence, a man, while he cannot receive the document in question, is in theory able to receive some bills of divorce.</i>. “And the document of manumission to my slave …,” since he may receive his own document of manumission<sup class="footnote-marker">118</sup><i class="footnote">We have to assume that the emissary is a fellow slave who could receive his own manumission.</i>. But did we not state<sup class="footnote-marker">119</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 6:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.6.1.1">Mishnah <i>Giṭṭin</i> 6:1</a>: “If someone says: Receive this divorce document for my wife, or convey this divorce document to my wife, and wants to change his mind, he may do so. If the woman said: Receive the divorce document for me, if he {the husband} wants to change his mind {after handing over the document}, he may not do so {since the transaction was done with the wife’s knowledge.}” That is the case if the emissary of the wife is a free man; she is divorced the moment the document comes into her emissary’s hand. But since a slave has no “hand” in the legal sense, how can this Mishnah be reconciled with the one quoted first?</i>: “Receive this divorce document for my wife or bring this divorce document to my wife; if he wants to change his mind … he may not do so.” Is a slave empowered to bring a divorce document? Explain it by different cases<sup class="footnote-marker">117</sup><i class="footnote">The minor daughter who was formally betrothed (קידושין) but not yet actually married, can become free to marry another man only by divorce. The bill of divorce must be accepted by her father, or by herself on the instruction of the father, since she becomes of age only by actual marriage. Hence, a man, while he cannot receive the document in question, is in theory able to receive some bills of divorce.</i>.
The following Mishnah disagrees with him who says that he who may take may acquire, since we have stated there<sup class="footnote-marker">122</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Maäser Šeni</i> 5:9. The Mishnah discusses the case of a man who was away on a trip at the time (Passover Eve) when the tithes must be given. Rabban Gamliel, Rebbi Joshua, Rebbi Aqiba, and Rebbi Eleazar ben Azariah were travelling on a ship on that day (probably to or from Rome on official business) when Rabban Gamliel designated the first tithe to Rebbi Joshua, a Levite, and the tithe of the poor to Rebbi Aqiba. In order not to transgress the Biblical command of distributing the tithes, Rebbi Aqiba must have been able to let the poor acquire their tithe while on the ship.</i>: “One tithe that I will measure in the future is given to Aqiba ben Joseph that he should let the poor acquire it, and its place is rented to him.” But is Rebbi Aqiba entitled to take? Explain it, before he got rich<sup class="footnote-marker">123</sup><i class="footnote">When he was a student, his wife supported him because she wanted him to become a scholar. But because he was poor, his very rich father-in-law disowned them.</i>. And even if you say, after he became rich<sup class="footnote-marker">124</sup><i class="footnote">This is the more likely scenario because in this setting he already was one of the most prominent rabbis.</i>, when he was an administrator<sup class="footnote-marker">125</sup><i class="footnote">Of the public charity funds. Cf. Greek πρόνοος, ον adj., “careful, prudent”; also late form προνοητής, οῦ, ὁ, “supervisor, administrator” (E. G.).</i>, and the hand of the administrator is equal to the hand of the poor.
The word of Rebbi Joshua ben Levi is that he who may take may acquire, since Rebbi Joshua ben Levi said: They disagree about a rich owner. But in the case of a poor owner, since he has the right to take it, the other person acquired it.
Rebbi Ḥizqiah, Rebbi Jeremiah, in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan<sup class="footnote-marker">127</sup><i class="footnote">Here starts the discussion of the last part of the Mishnah: Gleanings, forgotten sheaves, and <i>peah</i> of a Gentile are obligated for tithes, except if the Gentile declared it to be abandoned property.</i>: <sup class="footnote-marker">128</sup><i class="footnote">Add: “The Mishnah is only understandable …”</i> According to him<sup class="footnote-marker">129</sup><i class="footnote">The opinions of R. Jehudah and R. Simeon in <i>Demaï</i> 3:4, 5:9. The opposite opinion in <i>Demaï</i> 5:9 (fol. 24d), <i>Kilaïm</i> 7:4 (fol. 30d) and in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 47a" href="/Gittin.47a">Babli, <i>Giṭṭin</i> 47a</a>, is attributed to R. Meïr. The Yerushalmi clearly accepts the opinion of RR. Jedudah and Simeon. Cf. also <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Demai 5:8:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Demai.5.8.1"><i>Demay</i>, Chapter 5, Note 102</a>.</i> who says that a Gentile may acquire real estate in the Land of Israel to free it from tithes<sup class="footnote-marker">130</sup><i class="footnote">It is explained in <i>Demaï</i>(<i>loc. cit</i>.) and elsewhere that the Biblical duties of heave and tithes ceased with the Babylonian exile. The returnees from Babylonia took these duties upon themselves and future generations in a solemn covenant with God (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Nehemiah 10:1" href="/Nehemiah.10.1"><i>Neh</i>. 10:1</a>) in those regions (Judea, the Southern Plains, Galilee, and Golan Heights) settled by the returnees; hence the problem is not one of Biblical precepts but of the meaning of this covenant. According to the position taken here, the obligation of <i>peah</i>, gleanings, and forgotten sheaves is a later Rabbinic obligation on the land as far as Jews are concerned (<i>Demaï</i> 3:4, fol. 23d). Since the Gentile has no obligation to give to the poor, he cannot designate anything under these names. But since he is the owner, he may legally abandon the produce just as a Jew might abandon it.</i>. But according to him who says that a Gentile may not acquire real estate in the Land of Israel to free it from tithes, even if he declared it abandoned property it is obligated<sup class="footnote-marker">131</sup><i class="footnote">According to R. Meīr, the obligation is on the Land, not on the owner. If the agricultural real estate is owned by a Gentile, the obligation obviously cannot be enforced since the Gentile has no obligation to abide by the covenant. However, if the produce is then bought by a Jew, it will be a deadly sin for him to consume anything from it before he has given the heave and, at least, the priest’s part of the tithe. The only exception is abandoned property (by Mishnah 6:1, this must be abandoned to any taker, not just the poor.) Now, <i>peah</i>, gleanings, and forgotten sheaves also are free of heave and tithes but these can be given only by persons obligated to give. Hence, <i>peah</i>, gleanings, and forgotten sheaves designated by Gentiles do not have the legal status of <i>peah</i>, gleanings, and forgotten sheaves, and are not exempt; neither does abandoning the property help since nothing the Gentile does can possibly influence the duties upon the produce.</i>. Rebbi Yose in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan<sup class="footnote-marker">132</sup><i class="footnote">Note that “in the name of” always implies an indirect transmission of statements; it might be that R. Jeremiah deals only with the statement about abandoned property and R. Yose with that about <i>peah</i>, gleanings, and forgotten sheaves.</i>: <sup class="footnote-marker">128</sup><i class="footnote">Add: “The Mishnah is only understandable …”</i> According to him who says that a Gentile may not acquire real estate in the Land of Israel to free it from tithes<sup class="footnote-marker">133</sup><i class="footnote">The obligation of <i>peah</i>, gleanings, and forgotten sheaves is on the Land and is activated as soon as the produce comes into Jewish hands. R. Yose is of the opinion that the Gentile owner still may legally abandon the produce.</i>. But according to him who says that a Gentile may acquire real estate in the Land of Israel to free it from tithes, the Sages decreed special leniency for gleanings<sup class="footnote-marker">134</sup><i class="footnote">If the Gentile designated some produce as <i>peah</i>, or left gleanings on the field, he clearly abandoned this to the poor. The general rule is that abandoned property must be abandoned to rich and poor alike (Mishnah 6:1), but in the case before us the rabbis made an exception; it is the leniency mentioned here. This is part of Nehemiah’s covenant; such a leniency would be impossible for a Biblical obligation.</i>.
Rebbi Eleazar<sup class="footnote-marker">135</sup><i class="footnote">As student and colleague of R. Joḥanan, he certainly cannot quarrel with Rebbis Ḥizqiah and Ḥanina, but he must question R. Joḥanan directly. In the first version, one accepts only the statement of R. Ḥizqiah as authoritative; in the second version, one accepts both statements of R. Joḥanan as genuine but referring to different parts of the Mishnah, as explained above.</i> asked, does he not acquire movables? Not on the last statement this was asked, but on the following: Rebbi Ḥizqiah, Rebbi Jeremiah, in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: According to him who says that a Gentile may acquire real estate in the Land of Israel to free it from tithes. But according to him who says that a Gentile may not acquire real estate in the Land of Israel to free it from tithes, on this Rebbi Eleazar asked, does he not acquire movables<sup class="footnote-marker">136</sup><i class="footnote">It cannot be said that even abandoned property is subject to tithes since everybody agrees that the yield of the Land legally belongs to the Gentile owner and his declaration of abandoned property is unquestionably valid in Jewish law.</i>? Rebbi Ḥanina in the name of Rebbi Phineas, also on the preceding one, on that of Rebbi Yose he asked: Rebbi Yose in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: According to him who says that a Gentile may not acquire real estate in the Land of Israel to free it from tithes. But according to him who says that a Gentile may acquire real estate in the Land of Israel to free it from tithes, on this Rebbi Eleazar asked, does he not acquire movables<sup class="footnote-marker">137</sup><i class="footnote">Since the produce belongs to the Gentile according to everybody (<i>Demaï</i>5:9), even for R. Meïr, and certainly for RR. Jehudah and Simeon, there cannot be an obligation for heave and tithes on any <i>peah</i>, against the statement in the Mishnah. Since the question is not answered, R. Eleazar’s position is accepted.</i>?

Halakhah 7

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> <sup class="footnote-marker">138</sup><i class="footnote">From here to Mishnah 5:6 follow the rules of gleanings.</i>: What are gleanings? That which falls down at harvest time. If in harvesting he cut what his hands grabbed, or he uprooted a handful, when he was stung by a thorn and it fell to the ground, this belongs to the owner<sup class="footnote-marker">139</sup><i class="footnote">Anything falling down in an accident is not “gleanings from your harvest.” This reason is given in an extended version of the text of the Mishnah in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sifra, Kedoshim" href="/Sifra,_Kedoshim"><i>Sifra Qedošim</i> 2:5</a>. The somewhat awkward wording היה קוצר קצר is also used there; it cannot be emended away.</i>. From inside the hand and/or<sup class="footnote-marker">140</sup><i class="footnote">Whether to translate “and” or “or” is discussed in the Halakhah.</i> inside the sickle it belongs to the poor<sup class="footnote-marker">141</sup><i class="footnote">Since this falls down in the course of a professional harvest. From the back of hand or sickle it is accidental. From the tip it is a matter of opinion.</i>. From the back of his hand or the back of the sickle, it belongs to the owner. From fingertips and the tip of the sickle, Rebbi Ismael says, to the poor, Rebby Aqiba says, to the owner.<br>Antholes inside the standing produce belong to the owner<sup class="footnote-marker">145</sup><i class="footnote">I. e., the kernels taken by the ants and stored in their holes.</i>. In those appearing after the harvesters left, the tops are for the poor<sup class="footnote-marker">146</sup><i class="footnote">The kernels which can be seen from the outside should have been picked up at harvest time; those for which the hole has to be dug out are out of the reach of the poor and, hence, belong to the owner.</i> and the bottoms for the owner. Rebbi Meïr says, everything is for the poor because gleanings in doubt<sup class="footnote-marker">147</sup><i class="footnote">Since the lower lying grains become visible when the upper layer is taken, the boundary between upper and lower grains is not fixable and, hence, cannot legally exist.</i> are gleanings.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> It was stated: The gleanings of your harvest, not what anybody harvests with his bare hands<sup class="footnote-marker">142</sup><i class="footnote">See Chapter 3, Halakhah 3: The gathering of your harvest, not the gathering of your plucking.</i>. And equally, the dropped berries of your vineyard, and not what drops from hand-picking<sup class="footnote-marker">143</sup><i class="footnote">If single berries are plucked rather than whole bunches cut off with a knife.</i>.
Rav Cahana and Rav Taḥlifa<sup class="footnote-marker">144</sup><i class="footnote">This probably should be <i>Rebbi</i> Taḥlifa, a colleague of Rav Cahana (II) in the yeshivah of Rebbi Joḥanan.</i>. One said, both from inside the hand and inside the sickle; the other one said, from inside the hand, even if it is from behind the sickle.
Rebbi Jehudah in the name of Rebbi Samuel<sup class="footnote-marker">148</sup><i class="footnote">It probably should read: Rav Jehudah in the name of Samuel. It is very unlikely that the reference should be to the fourth generation Rebbi Jehudah (Yudan) and the third generation Rebbi Samuel, student of Rebbi Abbahu.</i>: The tops are for the poor, viz., white ones<sup class="footnote-marker">149</sup><i class="footnote">Completely dry kernels belong to the poor if they were not picked up before the surrounding grain was harvested. Green kernels were taken by the ants before the harvest and, therefore, are not possibly gleanings that fell down during harvest time. Hence, “top” and “bottom” do not have to be taken literally.</i>. The bottom ones are for the owner, viz., green ones. Rebbi Meïr says, everything is for the poor because gleanings in doubt are gleanings, since no threshing will end without some green ones<sup class="footnote-marker">150</sup><i class="footnote">Accordingly, all green kernels lying on the ground also belong to the poor. Rebbi Meïr considers an event as legally possible even if the probability of the event is much smaller than 50%; the statement “that it is impossible” is not to be taken too literally.</i>.
Rebbi Joḥanan said: It is by Rebbi Jehudah ben Ḥagra<sup class="footnote-marker">152</sup><i class="footnote">In the parallel in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Chullin 134a" href="/Chullin.134a">Babli <i>Ḥulin</i>134a</a>, he appears as R. Jehudah bar Agra (from Kefar Acco), a Tanna of the fourth generation. There in <i>Ḥulin</i>, R. Jehudah ben Agra explicitly formulates that <i>peah</i>, gleanings, and forgotten sheaves in case of doubt belong to the poor, confirming the interpretation of R. Joḥanan.</i>, as it was stated: “A Gentile who converted but had cut grain before he converted is free<sup class="footnote-marker">153</sup><i class="footnote">From the obligation of gleanings, even if he converted before the grain was bound into sheaves. The poor may collect gleanings only after the cut grain is bound into sheaves.</i>; after he converted, he is obligated. If it is in doubt, he is free. Rebbi Jehudah ben Ḥagra obligates him<sup class="footnote-marker">154</sup><i class="footnote">If he is Jewish at the moment the field is ready to be searched by the poor, he cannot collect gleanings after his conversion.</i>.” Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish said, it<sup class="footnote-marker">155</sup><i class="footnote">Even Rebbi Meïr will agree with the Sages against R. Jehudah ben Ḥagra since our question is not one of doubt but of principle.</i> agrees with everybody: A Jew, who is obligated in principle, is also obligated in doubtful cases; a Gentile, who is not obligated in principle, is not obligated in doubt. Rebbi Joḥanan said: So did Rebbi Meïr argue with Rebbi Jehudah ben Ḥagra, do you not agree with me that gleanings in doubt are gleanings<sup class="footnote-marker">156</sup><i class="footnote">And you obligate the convert because of a legal doubt, viz., whether the obligation of leaving the gleanings is incurred at the moment of cutting, in which case there would be no obligation, or at the moment the field is abandoned to the poor, in which case the convert is obligated.</i>? Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish: So did Rebbi Meïr argue with the Sages, do you not agree with me that gleanings in doubt are gleanings<sup class="footnote-marker">157</sup><i class="footnote">But the case of R. Jehudah ben Ḥagra is not comparable to that of the Mishnah.</i>?
From where that gleanings in doubt are gleanings? Rebbi Samuel ben Naḥman in the name of Rebbi Jonathan: “Poor and rich, justify him<sup class="footnote-marker">158</sup><i class="footnote">There is no such verse in Scripture. The consensus of the commentators is that it should read: (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Psalms 82:3" href="/Psalms.82.3"><i>Ps</i>. 82:3</a>) “Do justice for the poor and needy.” One does justice in also giving him the gifts that are in doubt.</i>” in his gifts. Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish in the name of Bar Qappara: (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 23:6" href="/Exodus.23.6"><i>Ex</i>. 23:6</a>) “Do not bend the lawsuit of your destitute.” In his lawsuit, you may not bend<sup class="footnote-marker">159</sup><i class="footnote">The judge may not say: His opponent has deep pockets; let me rule for the poor; then he will need no public assistance.</i>, but you may bend for him in his gifts<sup class="footnote-marker">160</sup><i class="footnote">To rule for him also in doubtful cases.</i>. Rebbi Joḥanan said, he<sup class="footnote-marker">161</sup><i class="footnote">The poor acquires as his right, not as a possibility as in the previous argument.</i> acquires in this case, as Rebbi instructed us (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 19:10" href="/Leviticus.19.10"><i>Lev</i>. 19:10</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 23:22" href="/Leviticus.23.22">23:22</a>): “abandon,” put something before him of your own<sup class="footnote-marker">162</sup><i class="footnote">The term “abandon” first refers to the gifts that belong to the poor as of right; the second mention in the same verse refers to the doubtful cases when one has to renounce his rights; a similar argument is found in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sifra, Kedoshim" href="/Sifra,_Kedoshim"><i>Sifra Qedošim</i> 2:7</a>. The same applies for the triple expression quoted by R. La. It is clear from here that Practice has to follow R. Meïr; this is also the decision of Maimonides (<i>Mattenot Aniïm</i> 4:9).</i>. Rebbi La said, it is written (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 24:19-21" href="/Deuteronomy.24.19-21"><i>Deut</i>. 24:19–21</a>): “It shall be the sojourner’s, the orphan’s, and the widow’s;” give him both from yours and from his!

Chapter 5



Halakhah 1

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> For a grain stack under which gleanings were not collected, all ears that touch the ground are for the poor<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">Since gleanings cannot be taken from under the stack, the farmer robbed the poor by not letting them search before he used that space. He is punished by an extreme application of the principle that “gleanings in doubt belong to the poor.”</i>. If the wind dispersed any sheaves<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">In this case, the farmer has the obvious right to rake the dispersed stalks together. But thereby he also takes the gleanings for the poor and has to make up for this.</i>, one estimates what amount of gleanings that would have yielded, and he gives that to the poor. Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel says, he gives to the poor the average fall<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">Which has been determined once and for all and is explained in the Halakhah.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> Here you say that “one estimates what amount of gleanings that would have yielded, and he gives that to the poor.” But there, you say “all ears that touch the ground are for the poor.” Rebbi Abbahu in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: They fined him because he put the stack on the gleanings of the poor. That is for intentional transgression; it is also for unintentional error, for sheaves, for a wheat stack on top of barley, even if it was stacked by others without his knowledge, even if he called the poor and they did not come<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">The translation follows the interpretation of Maimonides (<i>Mattenot Aniïm</i> 4:7), that this is a declarative sentence, a rule promulgated by the Court without exceptions. One might also read the sentence as a question: are these also for the poor? Since the question is not answered, the practical answer would be in the negative.</i>.
Rebbi Immi in the name of Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish: This is from the House of Shammai, since the House of Shammai say that property abandoned to the poor is abandoned<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">As explained in the next Mishnah, we require that the poor be given produce ready for immediate use. While harvested grain becomes subject to heave and tithes only after being gathered into orderly heaps, a farmer who really wants to create problems gives tithes from unthreshed grain. Hence, there already is a potential obligation on the produce after harvesting and the grain which is given in lieu of gleanings should be subject to heave and tithes. On the other hand, abandoned property is not “your harvest,” and as such is free from heave and tithes. In Chapter 6, the House of Shammai rule that property abandoned only to the poor, such as <i>peah</i> and gleanings and grain given in lieu of <i>peah</i> and gleanings, is considered legally abandoned. The House of Hillel disagree and restrict the notion of abandoned property to things abandoned to everybody, rich and poor alike. Since the Mishnah does not require heave and tithes to be given for grain given in lieu of gleanings, it seems to disagree with the House of Hillel who determine our practice.</i>. Rebbi Yasa said to him: We understand that it is free from tithes because property abandoned by a court order is abandoned<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">The grain given in lieu of gleanings is given by a decree of the High Court, not by personal choice. Hence, it is legally abandoned.</i>. It is written (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Ezra 10:8" href="/Ezra.10.8"><i>Ezra</i> 10:8</a>): “Anybody who will not come within three days conforming to the decree of the rulers and the elders, all his property shall be devoted to destruction<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">If the decree of the Court did not make the property abandoned, its destruction would have to be considered theft. Since Ezra was a teacher of the Law, his rulings have to be accepted.<br>From here on to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Peah 5:1:6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Peah.5.1.6">Note 24</a>, the text also appears in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shekalim 1:2:2-5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shekalim.1.2.2-5"><i>Šeqalim</i> 1:2</a>.</i>.” From where that it<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">Property abandoned by decree of court.</i> is free from tithes? Rebbi Jonathan, the son of Rebbi Isaac bar Aḥa<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">According to the interpretation of A. Zacut (<i>Sefer Yoḥasin</i>) of an unclear note in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 114a" href="/Pesachim.114a">Babli <i>Pesaḥim</i> 114a</a>, R. Isaac bar Aḥa is quoted as “R. Isaac” without a patronymic.</i> understood it from the following<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">Tosephta Sanhedrin 1:2, Babli Sanhedrin 12a, Yerushalmi Sanhedrin 1:2 (Note 207), Nedarim 6:3 (Note 83). (Addenda and Corrigenda by Guggnheimer)</i>: One intercalates<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">Since the Jewish year is both lunar and solar, but 12 lunar months are only approximately 254 days, in 19 years there have to be seven intercalary years of 13 months. For details see the author’s <i>Seder Olam</i>(Jason Aronson, Northvale NJ, 1998).</i> years neither in the Sabbatical year, nor in the year following the Sabbatical; but if they did intercalate it is intercalated. The one month he adds, is it not free from tithes<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">Since the spontaneous growth of the Seventh Year may be taken by everybody, it is not “your harvest” and, hence, free from heave and tithes even if taken by the owner of the land. If the Supreme Court declared the year intercalary against the rules, it is nevertheless a valid 13 months year and all produce is legally abandoned property.</i>? That refers to the Sabbatical year. What about the year after the Sabbatical? Rebbi Abun said, not to prolong the prohibition of new grain<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">New grain may be eaten only after the <i>Omer</i> sacrifice on the 16th of Nisan. An intercalation of a month, which always falls in early spring, unnecessarily postpones the harvest of new grain.</i>.
<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">This paragraph is an aside, taken from <i>Šebiït</i> 6:4, also <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nedarim 6:8:2-15" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nedarim.6.8.2-15"><i>Nedarim</i>6:13</a>. It explains why our calendar today does not take the Sabbatical year into consideration when determining intercalary months in the 19 year cycle. (Since 19 and 7 are relatively prime, the Sabbatical rules would force seven different calendars to be made!)</i> Rebbi Zeïra in the name of Rebbi Abbahu: That is only before Rebbi permitted the importation of vegetables from outside the Land<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">The soil outside the Land of Israel is unclean (cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Amos 7:17" href="/Amos.7.17"><i>Amos</i> 7:17</a>). In former times Jews did not use vegetables from outside the Land since it might have particles of soil still clinging to it. But after the last remnants of the ashes of the red heifer disappeared, these laws became inoperative and it was possible for everybody, even the most scrupulous, to eat imported vegetables. This has to be dated to the times of Rebbi (Jehudah the Prince); cf. commentary to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Berakhot 1:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Berakhot.1.1.1">Mishnah <i>Berakhot</i> 1:1</a>.</i>. But after Rebbi permitted the importation of vegetables from outside the Land, the Sabbatical year is as any other year<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">Since there is enough food for everybody.</i>. It was stated: One does not intercalate in the Sabbatical Year but only in other years of a Sabbatical period, but if they did intercalate it is intercalated. Rebbi Mana said, that refers to earlier times when years were in order<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">Taxes were paid on income, which means that in Sabbatical years the farmer did not have to pay taxes.</i>, but now that years are not in order<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">Now that the government levies taxes on arable land without regard to the actual yield, the farmer has to grow crops on his land even in the Sabbatical year on the authority of R. Mana (<i>Šebiït</i> 4:2).</i>, the Sabbatical year is like any other year.
It was stated: The house of Rabban Gamliel intercalated immediately after the end of the Sabbatical year<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">Reading of the parallel in <i>Seqalim</i> 1:2, in the Venice print מד</i>. Rebbi Avin said, from this<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">All the previous arguments which prove that the action of the court can free produce from the Biblical obligations of heave and tithes are not relevant since there is a Biblical obligation to manipulate the calendar so that Passover should fall in the month of the Spring equinox. Hence, the obligation to intercalate is biblical and not rabbinic.</i> you do not infer anything. (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 16:1" href="/Deuteronomy.16.1"><i>Deut</i>. 16:1</a>) “Watch the spring month.” Watch it that it should come in its renewal.
What text implies this<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">Which Mishnah text implies that property decreed ownerless by the court is legally abandoned property.<br>In <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shekalim 1:2:2-5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shekalim.1.2.2-5"><i>Šeqalim</i> 1:2</a>, the author of the objection is R. Immi. This is the better text since R. Yasa was a colleague of R. Immi, much earlier than R. Mana.</i>? “For a grain stack under which gleanings were not collected, …” Rebbi Mana in the name of Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish: This is from the House of Shammai. Rebbi Yasa said to him: We understand that according to everybody, this is a fine<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">As R. Joḥanan had said, this is a fine imposed on the farmer and has no connection with the duties imposed on produce. The Mishnah does not say that all ears which touch the ground are gleanings, but that all ears which touch the ground must be abandoned to the poor. Hence, they are not gleanings and are exempt from heave and tithes only for the House of Shammai. (Maimonides follows the earlier opinion of R. Yasa that it is produce abandoned by court order and free from heave and tithes.)</i>. Following the House of Hillel, the poor give tithes and eat.
<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">Here starts the discussion of the opinion of Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel, that the gleanings of the poor are given as a fixed percentage of the harvest.</i> Rebbi Zeïra, Rebbi Abbahu in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: Four <i>kab</i> per <i>kor</i><sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Metzia 105b" href="/Bava_Metzia.105b">Babli, <i>Baba Meẓi‘a</i> 105b</a>, this is also given by R. Abbahu; the reading (of an otherwise unattested) “R. Abuh” in the current editions of the Babli is a late printer’s error according to <i>Diqduqe Soferim Baba Meẓi‘a</i> p. 155. A <i>kor</i> is 30 <i>seah</i> or 180 <i>qab</i>.</i>. Rebbi Zeïra asked before Rebbi Abbahu: Four <i>qab</i> per <i>kor</i> or per <i>bet kor</i><sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">Do you mean 4 <i>qab</i> in volume for any <i>kor</i> in volume, 1 in 45, or 4 <i>qab</i>(about 4.5 U. S. pints) for a field of area 1 <i>bet kor</i> = 30 <i>bet seah</i> = 30×2500 square cubits = 75’000 square cubits (570.25 acres)? The first option is unlikely because that would make gleanings 2.2% of the harvest, much more than the larger <i>peah</i>. Since an ear of grain can be expected to yield at least 15 grains, the second option would mean gleanings of 4 <i>qab</i> for at least 2’700 <i>qab</i> harvested.</i>? He said to him, per <i>bet kor</i>. There<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Metzia 9:4:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Metzia.9.4.1">Mishnah <i>Baba Meẓi‘a</i> 9:5</a>: “He who received a field as a sharecropper, and it failed to grow, nevertheless is required to tend it as long as the yield will be sufficient to form one heap. Rebbi Jehudah says: What is the definition of a heap? Rather, if it is enough to drop it.” Rashi explains there, “enough to use it as seed.” R. Jehudah complains that “heap” cannot be a legal definition [even though R. Abbahu in the Babli, R. Joshua ben Levi in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Metzia 9:4:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Metzia.9.4.3">Yerushalmi (<i>Baba Meẓi‘a</i>9:5,</a> fol. 12a), define it as a mound in which a shovel stays upright.]</i>, we have stated: “What is the definition of a heap? Rather, if it is enough to let it fall<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">This is the same expression as the one used here by Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel. The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Metzia 105b" href="/Bava_Metzia.105b">Babli <i>Baba Meẓi‘a</i>105b</a> declares, again in the name of R. Joḥanan, that this means four <i>qab</i> per <i>bet kor</i>.</i>. Rebbi Abbahu said, enough to let it fall as seed<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">I. e., the field produces at least as much grain as was used for sowing. This is also R. Abbahu’s definition in <i>Baba Meẓi‘a</i>; it is much more than the נפלה defined here. How may R. Abbahu give two different definitions for the same legal expression? The question is not answered. Maybe this is the reason why Maimonides, in his Code, does not follow Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel, even though his opinions are almost always the practice to be followed.</i>.” And here, he says so!

Halakhah 2

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> <sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">Here start the laws of forgotten sheaves and other forgotten harvests.</i>: A single ear on the field to be harvested and its head reaches to the grain that is still standing, if it can be cut together with standing grain, it belongs to the owner, otherwise it is for the poor<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">If it can not be cut in one movement of the sickle together with some stalks of grain still standing in bunches, it is like a forgotten sheaf and one may not return to take it.</i>. An ear of gleanings that was mixed up in a stack of grain, he gives tithes for one ear and gives it to him<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">Since the farmer is required to give to the poor something that may be consumed right away, after it is mixed up in the stack it cannot be given away until at least the heave of the tithe has been given. Hence, one ear has to be given to the Cohen as heave, the other one to the poor in lieu of gleanings. In this sentence, “he” is the farmer and “him” refers to the poor.</i>. Rebbi Eliezer said, how can the poor man exchange something that never was in his possession<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">The procedure of the first Tanna does not work, since the poor man does not receive gleanings but grain in lieu of gleanings. But since the gleanings never were in the poor man’s possession, the poor person has no right to exchange his gleanings for the stalk which the owner gives to him and if the owner later uses that ear of gleanings in his stack he still robs the poor.</i>? But he has to give the poor rights to the entire stack<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">This is known as “a gift on condition that it must be returned,” which legally is a gift as long as it is with the recipient. In our case, the poor has to get possession of the stack only long enough to accept any other stalk in lieu of the gleaning he should be given; then the stack reverts to the owner and now the latter may give the heave of the tithe to the Cohen and a stalk to the poor.</i>; then he gives tithes for one ear and gives it to him.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> What is standing grain that saves standing grain<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">In Mishnah 6:7 it is stated that standing grain saves other standing grain which the farmer forgot to cut. The question is, how far away may an entire uncut patch be from the main uncut field that it should not be forgotten? The answer is the same as in our Mishnah, that the head of one of the stalks of the forgotten patch may be bent, without breaking the stem, to touch the as yet uncut part of the field.</i>? Rebbi Joḥanan said, the same as we have stated: a single ear on the field to be harvested and its head reaches to the standing grain. Rebbi Yose said, only if the harvested field surrounds it<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">Otherwise, it never can be counted as forgotten. In contrast to the next two conditions which restrict the case in which it is not forgotten, this one restricts the possible applications of the rules of forgotten produce.</i>, and only if its head reaches the standing grain, and only if it can be cut together with standing grain. If it can be cut together with standing grain but standing grain cannot be cut with it<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">If the angle of the sickle with the ground is too large when the harvester stands close to the unharvested grain, the harvester cannot start cutting the single stalk and reach the standing patch with one sweep of the sickle.</i>, it is saved. If there were two, the inner one can be cut together with the outermost standing grain, but the outer one cannot be cut with the standing grain; the inner one is saved and saves<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">It saves the outer one if the outer one could be cut together with the inner one and the inner one could be cut together with standing grain. Hence, the notion of being saved is transitive for any row of stalks that could be cut in pairs.</i>.
Rebbi Hoshaia said, when I was mashing olives with the great Rebbi Ḥiyya, he said to me: Any olive that you can reach when stretching out your hand is not forgotten<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">After shaking the olive tree you may go around over all branches you worked on and take the olives still on the tree. It is only when you move your ladder that the remainder becomes property of the poor.</i>. Rebbi Joḥanan said, if he passed it over and forgot it, it is forgotten<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">If in his mind he is finished with one branch, he may not return to it.</i>. The Mishnah disagrees with Rebbi Hoshaia: A single ear on the field to be harvested and its head reaches to the grain. Can you not stretch out your hand and take a single ear on the harvested field<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">The condition that the ear must touch the standing stalks is certainly much more restrictive than that a harvester, standing in front of the grain, cannot pluck by hand on the other side.</i>? Rebbi Illai in the name of Rebbi Hoshaia: They said that about the second row<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">The Mishnah speaks of a stalk left over from cutting the first row. If in harvesting the second row one returns to the place of that ear, it is as when in plucking leftover olives one returns to a harvested spot after moving the ladder and then even R. Ḥiyya agrees that this is forgotten property.</i>.
Can something that is apt to be saved but was forgotten become legally forgotten? Let us hear from the following: “When he removed<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">עימר as a verbal form “removing the sheaf” is defined by <a class="refLink" data-ref="Rashi on Shabbat 127a" href="/Rashi_on_Shabbat.127a">Rashi in <i>Šabbat</i>127a</a>. R. S. Lieberman points out that this parallels Biblical דִּשֵּׁן “removed the ashes”, from the noun דשן “ashes.”</i> the first, second, and third sheaves<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">He not only bound them but he also removed them to a storage place or threshing floor. This statement puts the standard row of sheaves at three sheaves. Hence, the full second row would be sheaves 4,5,6.</i> but forgot<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">To remove the fourth after it was bound.</i> the fourth, some state: If he took away the fifth one, it (the fourth) becomes legally forgotten, but some state: If he waited long enough that he could have taken away the fifth one, it becomes legally forgotten.” Rebbi Avin bar Ḥiyya said, he who says, “if he removed the fifth one,” (speaks of the case) where there is a sixth one<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">Because sheaves in a row are saved by the next sheaf just as stalks in a row are saved by the next stalk. Hence, the fourth can become legally forgotten only if the fifth, which saves it from being legally forgotten, is removed.</i>, but he who says, “if he waited long enough that he could have removed the fifth one,” (speaks of the case) where there is no sixth one<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote">Since there is no row of three sheaves, they will be taken to be removed individually and not as a row. It suffices that the fifth could have been finished and removed.</i>. Before he took away the fifth one, would not the fourth one be apt to be considered starting a row<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote">If there are six sheaves, the fourth does not become legally abandoned until the sheaf which saves it is removed.</i>? That means that something that is apt to be saved but was forgotten can become legally forgotten.
Can something that is apt to save but was forgotten become legally forgotten? Let us hear from the following<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah 6:6 reads: “Standing grain in the volume on two <i>seah</i> (about .9 cubic feet) cannot be legally forgotten.” Then Mishnah 6:8 adds: “One <i>seah</i> of cut grain and one <i>seah</i> of uncut grain do not combine.” That means, both of them may become legally forgotten.</i>: “One <i>seah</i> of cut grain and one <i>seah</i> of uncut grain.” Here the cut grain should save the uncut grain<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote">If they are contiguous and would combine. In Halakhah 6:8 it is pointed out that Mishnah 6:8 seems to follow the ruling of Rabban Gamliel in Mishnah 6:5 that sheaves which add up to a volume of 2 <i>seah</i> are exempt from the law of forgotten sheaves. Then there is no reason why cut and uncut grain should not be combined.</i>. That means that something that is apt to save, but was forgotten, can become legally forgotten. Rebbi Jonah<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote">In Halakhah 6:8, “Rebbi Jonathan.” The reading here is the more likely one since Rebbi Jonathan is a very early Amora.</i> said, explain it if he was cutting and removing the sheaves row by row<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote">The field contains much more than two rows of a <i>seah</i> each. Hence, the two do not have to be adjacent in space or time and the Mishnah has no implication for our question.</i>. He already forgot the standing grain before he might forget the sheaves.
How does he do it<sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote">Here starts the discussion of the second sentence of the Mishnah, the requirement that the farmer give tithes before he delivers the replacement for the gleanings to the poor. The problem is that heave and tithes can be given only from produce subject to the laws of heave and tithes. Gleanings are exempt from these laws and, therefore, cannot be used as either heave or tithes.</i>? He brings two ears and says: If this one is gleanings, all is fine; but if not, then its tithes are contained in the other one, and he gives him<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote">The poor person. The other one is given to the priest.</i> the first one. Do we not have to worry that the one used to contain tithes in it was gleanings? Rebbi Jonah said, he brings two ears and says: If this one is gleanings, all is fine; but if not, then its tithes are contained in the other one, and he gives him one of them<sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote">He must treat the two ears equally and make the declaration twice. He then is restricted to give the ear only to a poor person who may eat tithes, i. e., a Levite or a Cohen. The other ear is then to be given as tithes, not necesarily to a poor person. [Interpretation of this elliptic statement given by R. David ben Zimra to Maimonides, <i>Mattenot Aniïm</i> 4:10.]</i>.
Rebbi Abbahu<sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote">Here we discuss the last part of the Mishnah, the disagreement between the Sages and R. Eliezer on whether it is possible to transfer property of a single ear or whether it is necessary first to give the entire stack to the poor.</i> in the name of Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish, this follows Rebbi Yose<sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote">Bar Ḥalaphta, the Tanna.</i>, as we have stated there<sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote">The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Bekhorot 2:6" href="/Mishnah_Bekhorot.2.6">Mishnah <i>Bekhorot</i> 2:6</a> deals with a sheep whose first lambs are male twins of which it is unknown which one was the firstborn. It is then decreed that one of them be given to the Cohen, and the other one (who might be the firstborn and, if without blemish, should be brought as sacrifice) should graze until it develops a bodily defect and then can be eaten. The first Tanna (representing R. Meïr) insists that the second animal is subject to the rule of obligatory gifts to the Cohen as profane meat. Rebbi Yose disagrees, and in a <i>baraita</i> (also <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bekhorot 18a" href="/Bekhorot.18a">Babli <i>Bekhorot</i>18a</a>) explains that every animal whose exchange is in the hand of the Cohen is freed from the rule of obligatory gifts. The Babli explains, in an apparent disagreement with the interpretation of the Yerushalmi, that this is not a general principle but it underlines the special status of the second animal which, as a potential sacrifice, may not be shorn or used for work.</i>: “For Rebbi Yose says that everything whose replacement is in the hand of the Cohen is freed from the obligatory gifts, but Rebbi Meïr obligates him.” Rebbi Abba said, the words of Rebbi Yose imply that he has to empower the priest<sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote">It is not enough to designate an ear as tithes; it must actually be delivered into the hand of the Cohen.</i>. Rebbi Yose<sup class="footnote-marker">63</sup><i class="footnote">The later Amora.</i> said, the statement of Rebbi Abba contradicts Rebbi Simeon bar Laqish since we have stated here: “He has to give the poor rights to the entire stack; then he gives tithes for one ear and gives to him.” Rebbi Abba said, the words of Rebbi Yose imply that he has to empower the priest, hence<sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote">This is the Amora Rebbi Yose’s inference.</i> this is not from the words of Rebbi Yose. Rebbi Mana said, the Mishnah itself testifies that it is by Rebbi Yose<sup class="footnote-marker">65</sup><i class="footnote">Everybody agrees that transfer of property must take place, but not necessarily delivery; the Tanna R. Yose requires only transfer of property rights.</i>. The first Tanna wants to say that he transfers as property to the poor not the entire stack but only one ear. The other Tanna wants to say that he transfers the entire stack as property to the poor. The first Tanna wants to say that this<sup class="footnote-marker">66</sup><i class="footnote">The symbolic transfer of the entire stack to the poor as a “gift on condition that it be returned” is considered a subterfuge without legal validity.</i> is like the transfer of property from one’s right hand to his left hand; it is no transfer. The other Tanna wants to say that it is not transferring property from one’s right hand to his left hand; it is transfer<sup class="footnote-marker">67</sup><i class="footnote">And is legally valid.</i>.
Rebbi Zeïra stated<sup class="footnote-marker">68</sup><i class="footnote">These three words are not in the text of R. Simson of Sens and not in that of R. Solomon Cirillo. The text of R. Simson is certainly smoothed in the sense of standard Hebrew grammar; the text of R. S. Cirillo may be smoothed.</i>: Rebbi Abbahu in the name of R. Joḥanan: The reasoning of Rebbi Eliezer is inverted! There<sup class="footnote-marker">69</sup><i class="footnote">In chapter 4, Mishnah 6, Rebbi Eliezer declares that any person can collect for a poor one, so that the second one is the immediate owner and the collector never has ownership. How can this be squared with the statement of R. Eliezer in the current Mishnah?</i> he makes him acquire, and here he says thus? He answered “according to your reasoning<sup class="footnote-marker">70</sup><i class="footnote">A standard expression in both Talmudim. Rebbi Eliezer points out a logical flaw in the reasoning of his opponents; the objection is irrelevant for himself, by Mishnah 4:6.</i>,” in your reasoning that you say it is by exchange<sup class="footnote-marker">71</sup><i class="footnote">Since the Sages oppose R. Eliezer in Mishnah 4:6, holding that the non-poor X can never acquire gifts to the poor for the poor Y, how can the owner transfer ownership in gleanings by exchange? R. Eliezer’s procedure with the formal gift is needed only for his opponents in 4:6; for himself he will rule that the owner may acquire the gleanings for the poor recipient.</i>. “How can the poor man exchange something that never was in his possession? But he has to give the poor man rights to the entire stack; then he gives tithes for one ear and gives to him.”

Halakhah 3

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> One does not use a waterwheel with buckets<sup class="footnote-marker">72</sup><i class="footnote">After the harvest, before the poor had cleaned out vineyard (or field), to avoid hurting the vines (or to prepare the field for the next season.)</i>, the words of Rebbi Meïr. But the Sages do permit it, because it is impossible<sup class="footnote-marker">73</sup><i class="footnote">To do otherwise, since the vineyard would dry up. [This translation follows תוספתא כפשוטה p. 155; it is not much different from the explanation of <i>Arukh</i> (s. v. טפח) in the name of R. Daniel Gaon.]</i>. A householder wandering from one place to another who was in need of taking gleanings, forgotten sheaves, <i>peah</i>, and tithes of the poor<sup class="footnote-marker">74</sup><i class="footnote">Since his money had run out.</i> should repay them once he comes home, the words of Rebbi Eliezer<sup class="footnote-marker">75</sup><i class="footnote">Taking into account his property at his place of residence, at no time was he eligible for public assistance.</i>. But the Sages say, he was poor at that moment<sup class="footnote-marker">76</sup><i class="footnote">Having no money with him and unable to get credit.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> It was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">77</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Peah (Lieberman) 2:20" href="/Tosefta_Peah_(Lieberman).2.20">Tosephta <i>Peah</i> 2:20</a>. There, the reading is: “R. Jehudah says, he collects (the part of the poor) and deposits it on the fence.”</i>: He who waters his field before a poor person had entered it, if his potential loss is greater than the poor man’s, it is permitted, but if the poor man’s loss is greater than his, it is prohibited. Rebbi Jehudah says, in both cases he deposits them on the fence and the poor man comes and takes what is his.
The reasoning of Rebbi Jehudah is inverted: There<sup class="footnote-marker">78</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah 7:5: “If somebody is pruning vines, just as he is pruning his, he may prune the poor’s, the words of Rebbi Jehudah. Rebbi Meïr says, he is empowered to do it on his own vines, but not on the poor’s.” One prunes new shoots of vines at the start of the growing season; at that moment, the poor have rights only to the grapes growing singly, not in bunches, directly on the branch.</i>, he says that just as he prunes for himself, so he prunes for the poor; but here he says thus<sup class="footnote-marker">79</sup><i class="footnote">He collects and puts the poor’s part aside.</i>! There, they caused it for themselves since they did not come<sup class="footnote-marker">80</sup><i class="footnote">Nobody is wasting time to pluck budding single grape berries. In Halakhah 7:5, there is an another answer given: Since by pruning one makes the vine grow more grape bunches, the poor’s part also will be increased in the future.</i>, but here they came.
The reasoning of the rabbis is inverted: There<sup class="footnote-marker">81</sup><i class="footnote">In Mishnah 7:5, R. Meïr, who disagrees with R. Jehudah, is identified with the anonymous opponent of Rebbi Jehudah in the Tosephta quoted at the start of this Halakhah.</i>, they say if his potential loss is greater than the poor’s, and here they say thus<sup class="footnote-marker">82</sup><i class="footnote">In our Mishnah, they permit using the water wheel to mechanically water the vineyard.</i>! There, it is possible<sup class="footnote-marker">83</sup><i class="footnote">To prune only after the poor collected their share.</i>, but here it is impossible.
Now Rebbi Meïr says, one does not use the water wheel, and one estimates the loss to the owner. Now the rabbis say, one uses the water wheel and estimates the loss to the poor<sup class="footnote-marker">84</sup><i class="footnote">So why does R. Meïr insist on not watering, if all accounts can be satisfied with money?</i>. To whom does he pay? Rebbi Jonah said, to the poor of his place, because otherwise, what could we say<sup class="footnote-marker">85</sup><i class="footnote">There is no individual claimant; hence, there would be no payment unless the head of the local charity institution brings action in the name of all local, potentially empowered, poor.</i>? Rebbi Ḥiyya bar Ada said, it is needed for the measure of justice<sup class="footnote-marker">86</sup><i class="footnote">Even the administrator of charity could not bring action unless specifically empowered to do so. It is therefore not superfluous to restrict the potential claimants to those represented by the administrator of charity.</i>.

Halakhah 4

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If he<sup class="footnote-marker">87</sup><i class="footnote">If the farmer takes from the poor their gleanings or <i>peah</i> in exchange for grain from his storage, what he takes is not subject to tithes, since gleanings and <i>peah</i> are exempt, but what the poor take is subject to heave and tithes.</i> switches with the poor, what he receives is exempt but what the poor get is obligated.
