If two persons received a field as sharecroppers<sup class="footnote-marker">88</sup><i class="footnote">That means, both are poor. [The meaning of the word אריס is “cultivator of the soil”, from Arabic اريس, designating a tenant farmer. In both Talmudim, the designation is restricted to sharecroppers.]</i>, one gives his part and his tithes of the poor to the other one<sup class="footnote-marker">89</sup><i class="footnote">Nobody may take tithes of the poor, gleanings, etc., from his own harvest even if he is poor. Since the sharecropper has a proprietary interest in the growing plants, he may not take any of the gifts to the poor. But if two poor people work two sides of one field, each one is entitled to all the gifts to the poor accruing from the other’s half.</i>, and vice-versa. He who receives a field to harvest is barred from its gleanings, forgotten sheaves, and <i>peah</i>. Rebbi Jehudah said, when is this so<sup class="footnote-marker">90</sup><i class="footnote">The Babli postulates that everywhere R. Jehudah is mentioned with the question אימתי, he does not disagree with the anonymous text of the Mishnah but gives it a restrictive definition. The Yerushalmi seems to agree with this.</i>? When he received it for one-half, one third, or one quarter<sup class="footnote-marker">91</sup><i class="footnote">The standard terms of a sharecropper’s contract.</i>; but if he told him, one third of what you will harvest will be yours<sup class="footnote-marker">92</sup><i class="footnote">In this case, the sharecropper has no property claim to the standing grain; the harvest is the landowner’s and the sharecropper may take the gifts to the poor from this crop. However, tithes of the poor, like any other tithes, are an obligation on the threshed grains and there the sharecropper is already an owner and the tithes of the poor are prohibited to him.</i>, he may take gleanings, forgotten sheaves, and <i>peah</i> but not the tithes of the poor.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> It is stated<sup class="footnote-marker">93</sup><i class="footnote">Tosephta <i>Maäser Šeni</i> 4:7 in a slightly different wording. A third version, in which the answer to R. Jehudah is missing, is given in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Kiddushin 32a" href="/Kiddushin.32a">Babli <i>Qiddushin</i> 32a</a>.</i>: A father and his son<sup class="footnote-marker">94</sup><i class="footnote">The grownup son who earns his own money.</i>, a man and his relative, two brothers, two associates redeem Second Tithe<sup class="footnote-marker">95</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Peah 1:1:30" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Peah.1.1.30">Chapter 1, Note 127</a>. In the times of the Temple, if the accumulated amount of Second Tithe was too large, the produce could be redeemed and the money taken to Jerusalem to be spent on similar food (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 14:25" href="/Deuteronomy.14.25"><i>Deut.</i> 14:25</a>). After the destruction of the Temple, Second Tithe has to be redeemed for a symbolic amount since the redemption money then has to be destroyed. However, it is spelled out in the last chapter of <i>Leviticus</i> that a person who redeems any of his dedications has to add another 25% to the redemption sum. A redemption by a third party is not subject to this surtax. It is therefore in everybody’s interest to let a third party redeem Second Tithes.</i> for one another and give tithe of the poor to one another<sup class="footnote-marker">96</sup><i class="footnote">If they qualify as poor.</i>. Rebbi Jehudah said, a curse should come upon him who gives tithe of the poor to his father<sup class="footnote-marker">97</sup><i class="footnote">Even if his father can no longer work and has no savings, the son should not treat him as receiver of charity.</i>. He said, why? If they were both poor<sup class="footnote-marker">98</sup><i class="footnote">If the son himself qualifies for public assistance as a poor person, it is acceptable that he give the tithe of the poor of his plot to his father.</i>!
What<sup class="footnote-marker">99</sup><i class="footnote">This paragraph is almost identical with one in Chapter 2, Halakha 7, with references to “here” and “there” switched as required. The name of the tradent has been switched from the correct “Abin bar Ḥiyya” to a non-existent “Ḥiyya bar Abin”. The discussion is about the ruling by R. Jehudah that the sharecropper may take the gifts to the poor if his share is only produce which is already cut.</i> is the difference between this and the first case<sup class="footnote-marker">100</sup><i class="footnote">Of the usual sharecropper who has rights to the growing plants.</i>? It must be “if you cut it, a third will be yours<sup class="footnote-marker">101</sup><i class="footnote">Only after the harvest is completed does the sharecropper get rights.</i>.” Rebbi Abin bar Ḥiyya said, that implies that the seller may get the <i>peah</i> which permits the sheaves<sup class="footnote-marker">102</sup><i class="footnote">In the situation of Mishnah 2:7, at the moment of the actual obligation of <i>peah</i> the field is already sold, the <i>peah</i> was never the seller’s and he may take from it if he is poor.</i>. Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun<sup class="footnote-marker">103</sup><i class="footnote">He shows that the argument of R. Abin bar Ḥiyya is faulty. While the actual obligation of <i>peah</i> arises during the buyer’s harvest, the potential obligation started with the seller’s harvest. Hence, the seller should be barred from taking his part of the <i>peah</i>. In contrast, the sharecropper who is given ownership only of a part of the harvest, not of the standing grain, has nothing at all to do with the obligation of <i>peah</i>.</i> said, there the obligation of the field did come during his ownership, but here the obligation of the field did not come during his ownership.
Rebbi Abbahu bar Naggari<sup class="footnote-marker">104</sup><i class="footnote">An Amora whose time and place cannot be determined. His reasoning parallels R. Yose’s: Gleanings etc. have to be abandoned on the field and it was not the sharecropper’s field. But tithes, including the tithe of the poor, have to be given after threshing, and in that poor and rich are in the same situation.</i> said, there is a difference for gleanings, forgotten sheaves, and <i>peah</i> because they have to be abandoned.

Halakhah 5

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If someone sells his field, the seller is permitted<sup class="footnote-marker">105</sup><i class="footnote">If he is poor at harvest time.</i> but the buyer is barred. Nobody should hire a worker on condition that his son may collect gleanings after him<sup class="footnote-marker">106</sup><i class="footnote">And for this pay him smaller wages, since he is paying his debts with the money of the poor.</i>. He who does not let the poor collect gleanings, or who lets one person collect but not the other, or helps one of them, robs the poor. On him it was said (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Proverbs 22:28" href="/Proverbs.22.28"><i>Prov.</i> 22:28</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Proverbs 23:10" href="/Proverbs.23.10">23:10</a>): “Do not displace an eternal boundary<sup class="footnote-marker">107</sup><i class="footnote">The implication is from the second part of the verse: “Nor intrude on the land of orphans.” On <a class="refLink" data-ref="Proverbs 22:28" href="/Proverbs.22.28"><i>Prov.</i>22:28</a>, Rashi comments: “Do not displace an eternal boundary,” do not change established usage; our teachers said: He who puts a basket under the vine at harvest time so that the single berries fall into it, on him it is said, do not displace an eternal boundary. On <a class="refLink" data-ref="Proverbs 24:10" href="/Proverbs.24.10"><i>Prov.</i> 24:10</a>, Rashi writes, “Nor intrude on the land of orphans,” the gleanings, forgotten sheaves, and <i>peah</i> which belong to them.</i>.”
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> Our Mishnah is about someone who sold the field with the standing crop. But if someone sold the standing crop and retained the field for himself, for him I read “your field”, for the other one I read “your harvest.<sup class="footnote-marker">109</sup><i class="footnote">Since <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 24:19" href="/Deuteronomy.24.19"><i>Deut.</i> 24:19</a> reads: “When you are harvesting your harvest on your field,” both the owner of the field and the owner of the harvest are forbidden to take of the gleanings and <i>peah</i>.</i>”
“By doing so, he robs the poor.” The owner who does that robs the poor<sup class="footnote-marker">110</sup><i class="footnote">Either by not giving at all or by limiting the chances of the disfavored.</i>. The worker who does that robs the owner<sup class="footnote-marker">111</sup><i class="footnote">He robs the owner of the merit, either leading him into sin by offering to work for lower wages on condition that his son get all gleanings and <i>peah</i>, or, if the owner does not know that he lets his son take everything, by not letting him fulfil the Biblical commandments. The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Metzia 12a" href="/Bava_Metzia.12a">Babli (<i>Baba Meẓi‘a</i> 12a</a>) disagrees and postulates as standard labor practice that the worker’s minor son may collect gleanings after his father. The Babli must restrict the meaning of the Mishnah to the case that the worker agrees to take wages smaller than customary.</i> and the poor, and about him it was said: “Do not displace an eternal boundary.”
Rav Jeremiah and Rav Joseph<sup class="footnote-marker">112</sup><i class="footnote">This homily is based on a reading in the Mishnah, not אַל תַּסֵּג גְּבוּל עוֹלָם but אַל תַּסֵּג גְּבוּל עוֹלִים “do not displace the boundary of the rising.” This reading is attested to by Maimonides in his Commentary and is found in most Mishnah manuscripts of Babylonian type. One might assume that the scribes of most of the Mishnah manuscripts of Yerushalmi type were more versed in Biblical verses and copied from memory rather than from the text before them. As explained before, the part important for the<br>Mishnah is the second part of the verse. Here, by a change of vocalization, one author wants to give a better parallel to “orphan” than “eternal”.</i>, one said these are the ones who came up from Egypt, the other said these are the ones who lost their property; a blind man is called “plenty of light.<sup class="footnote-marker">113</sup><i class="footnote">Those coming down in the world are called “rising”.</i>” Rebbi Isaac said, (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Isaiah 58:7" href="/Isaiah.58.7"><i>Is.</i> 58:7</a>) “degraded poor you shall bring to your house<sup class="footnote-marker">114</sup><i class="footnote">He objects to the previous homily since a verse explicitly called the poor “degraded.”</i>.” Rebbi Abin said, if you do this<sup class="footnote-marker">115</sup><i class="footnote">To bring the poor into your house. This homily no longer is connected to the Mishnah.</i>, I will credit it to you as if you had presented First Fruits in the Temple. It says here, “you shall bring,” and it says there (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 23:19" href="/Exodus.23.19"><i>Ex.</i> 23:19</a>): “The first fruits of your land you shall bring to the Eternal’s Temple<sup class="footnote-marker">116</sup><i class="footnote">See the commentary of I. A. Rabin to <i>Mekhilta Mišpatim</i> 20 (p. 335) that there is a clear disagreement between Babylonian and Galilean Amoraïm in the interpretation of “to bring” in this verse, and the interpretation given here is Babylonian.</i>.”

Halakhah 6

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> A sheaf forgotten by the workers but not by the owner, by the owner but not by the workers, or the poor stood before it or covered it with straw<sup class="footnote-marker">117</sup><i class="footnote">Anything the poor hide in any way when the sheaves are loaded to be brought to the barn.</i>, is not a forgotten sheaf.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> A sheaf forgotten by the workers but not by the owner is not a forgotten sheaf since it is written (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 24:19" href="/Deuteronomy.24.19"><i>Deut.</i> 24:19</a>): “When you harvest and you forget<sup class="footnote-marker">118</sup><i class="footnote">The verse reads: “When you are harvesting your harvest on your field and forget a sheaf on the field, you may not return to take it.” Since “harvesting a harvest” is redundant language, it is split into two. “Your harvest” refers to the owner, “harvesting” refers to the farmhands who do the actual work. Both have to forget. (This implies that the owner must have known of the sheaf.)</i>.” If the owner forgot but the workers did not, it is not a forgotten sheaf since it is written: “When you are harvesting and you forget.” Rebbi Simeon ben Jehudah<sup class="footnote-marker">119</sup><i class="footnote">A fifth generation Tanna, from Kefar Acco, student of R. Simeon (bar Ioḥai). His statement also appears in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Peah (Lieberman) 3:1" href="/Tosefta_Peah_(Lieberman).3.1">Tosephta <i>Peah</i> 3:1</a>.</i> says in the name of Rebbi Simeon: Even if donkey drivers<sup class="footnote-marker">120</sup><i class="footnote">In the Tosephta and the original scribe (who usually is better than the corrector) of the Leyden manuscript: “others”.</i> passing by on the road saw a sheaf forgotten by the workers but not by the owner<sup class="footnote-marker">121</sup><i class="footnote">The language is difficult but is attested to by the Yerushalmi manuscripts and R. Simson of Sens. According to R. Saul Lieberman, it means that the owner never knew of the sheaf and he was not on the field during harvest. In our Tosephta manuscripts, “and not by the owner” is missing. R. Moshe Margalit writes that in his Tosephta manuscript, “by the workers” also is missing; there is only a reference to “them” who forgot. From the text of Maimonides (<i>Mattenot Aniïm</i> 5:1) it seems that he read: “was forgotten by the workers and by the owner.”</i>, it is not a forgotten sheaf unless everybody forgot it.
If he stood in town and said: I know that the workers are forgetting sheaves at place X; if they forgot it is not a forgotten sheaf<sup class="footnote-marker">123</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Peah (Lieberman) 3:1" href="/Tosefta_Peah_(Lieberman).3.1">Tosephta <i>Peah</i> 3:1</a>, reading of the Vienna manuscript. The Erfurt manuscript and printed editions have “this is a forgotten sheaf;” this is the Babylonian version (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Metzia 11a" href="/Bava_Metzia.11a"><i>Baba Meẓi‘a</i> 11a</a>). The parallel in the Babli in every case adopts a position opposite to that taken by the Yerushalmi. The explanation given here follows the analysis in <i>Tosephta Kifshutah</i> 1, p. 159–160.</i>. If he stood on the field and said: I know that the workers are forgetting sheaves at place X; [if they forgot] it is a forgotten sheaf, since it says (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 24:19" href="/Deuteronomy.24.19"><i>Deut.</i> 24:19</a>): “On your field and you forget a sheaf.” On the field you forget, but in town you do not forget. Rebbi Zeïra in the name of Samuel<sup class="footnote-marker">124</sup><i class="footnote">In the Rome manuscript, “Rebbi Zeïra, Rav Jehudah in the name of Samuel.” Since Rav Jehudah was Samuel’s student and R. Zeïra’s teacher, the formula of indirect quote “in the name of” there is superfluous. The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Metzia 11a" href="/Bava_Metzia.11a">Babli (<i>Baba Meẓi‘a</i> 11a</a>) has: “Rav Jehudah said, Samuel said,” as direct transmission.</i>: The same holds in regard to found objects<sup class="footnote-marker">125</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Metzia 1:4:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Metzia.1.4.1">Mishnah <i>Baba Meẓi‘a</i> 1:4</a>: “If he saw them running after a lost object, a deer with broken bones, pigeon chicks not yet able to fly, and he said: My field should acquire this for me, it acquires for him” (on condition that the field be either fenced in or that something is growing which makes it inaccessible to the public.) In the case of sheaves, if he is not standing on the field and wants the field to acquire the sheaf, it is valid. However, if he stands in or near the field, the field cannot acquire for him since the sheaf is his and nobody can acquire anything he already owns. Hence, if he stands in his field and claims that sheaf but later forgets it, the earlier remembrance will not help him since, in that case, the field will not acquire for him. In our case he must be able to go and reach the slowly moving animals before they leave his field.</i>. Where are we holding? If he may touch it, in the city, even inside his field<sup class="footnote-marker">126</sup><i class="footnote">Does he acquire only if the deer is so severely injured that he still will find it inside his field when he goes there from town? In the name of R. Joḥanan, the answer given confirms the questioner’s opinion.</i>. Rebbi Abba bar Cahana, Rebbi Yasa in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: Only if he can touch them, even inside his field.
If it was completely covered by straw<sup class="footnote-marker">127</sup><i class="footnote">Not intentionally covered by the poor, but a sheaf lying covered under straw, which nobody noticed when the sheaves were taken in.</i>? Let us hear from the following: “The blind<sup class="footnote-marker">128</sup><i class="footnote">If the farmer is blind, his disability does not change the laws.</i> who forgot makes a forgotten sheaf.” Is a blind person not like a sheaf completely covered by straw? Rebbi Jonah said, if he remembered the straw<sup class="footnote-marker">129</sup><i class="footnote">Since he will come back to collect the straw, he must stumble upon the sheaf. The sheaf is not forgotten since he will be returning to it.</i>. Rebbi Jonah’s position is parallel to Rebbi Zeïra’s; just as Rebbi Zeïra said<sup class="footnote-marker">130</sup><i class="footnote">Chapter 6, Halakhah 3, speaking about two sheaves to be taken by one of the workers and forgotten by the farmer. The farmer personally lifted the upper one; by lifting he acquired it permanently. The lower one was covered and, therefore, cannot be a forgotten sheaf. Rebbi Zeïra explains that the lower, covered sheaf is forgotten unless the farmer remembered the upper, already acquired one.</i>, if he remembers the upper one, so Rebbi Jonah said, if he remembered the straw.

Halakhah 7

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> He<sup class="footnote-marker">131</sup><i class="footnote">Translation and interpretation follows in the main Maimonides in his Commentary on the Mishnah. “He” is the farmer, the owner of the crop.</i> who makes sheaves as helmets<sup class="footnote-marker">132</sup><i class="footnote">As explained in the Halakhah, sheaves bound together at the top, just under the ears, looking like hats.</i>, round bundles<sup class="footnote-marker">133</sup><i class="footnote">Bound together at the bottom. Loew accepts a derivation parallel to Arabic כמז “collecting and making round bundles with one’s hands.”</i>, wheels<sup class="footnote-marker">134</sup><i class="footnote">Grain bundles in the shape of wheels.</i>, and small sheaves<sup class="footnote-marker">135</sup><i class="footnote">Needed to make stacks, not to be taken directly to the threshing floor.</i> is not subject to the law of forgotten sheaves. If he takes them from there to the threshing-floor, the rules on forgotten sheaves apply. If he makes sheaves for a stack, it is subject to the rules on forgotten sheaves. If he takes them from there to the threshing-floor, the rules on forgotten sheaves do not apply. This is the principle: Everybody who makes sheaves for a place that is the end of his work<sup class="footnote-marker">136</sup><i class="footnote">As harvester; from there a new phase of the work starts.</i> is subject to the rule on forgotten sheaves, from there to the threshing-floor is not subject to the rule on forgotten sheaves<sup class="footnote-marker">137</sup><i class="footnote">That transport is no longer part of the harvest.</i>. To a place that is not the end of his work it is not subject to the rule on forgotten sheaves, from there to the threshing-floor<sup class="footnote-marker">138</sup><i class="footnote">The end of the harvest.</i> is subject to the rule on forgotten sheaves.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> Rebbi Jonah says, from the top, as you say (<a class="refLink" data-ref="I Samuel 17:5" href="/I_Samuel.17.5"><i>1Sam.</i> 17:5</a>): “A brass helmet on his head.” Round bundles, Rebbi Avinah said, from the bottom, as you say (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 32:34" href="/Deuteronomy.32.34"><i>Deut.</i> 32:34</a>): “It is hidden with me.” <sup class="footnote-marker">139</sup><i class="footnote">With the different meanings of the Hebrew/Aramaic root חרר, this could mean 1) something burned (a charcoal-broiled cake), 2) a round mass, 3) a hole, 4) freedom. Hence, it is necessary to spell out that meaning 2) is meant.</i>חֲרָרָה a wheel. Small sheaves, Rebbi Joḥanan said (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 24:19" href="/Deuteronomy.24.19"><i>Deut.</i> 24:19</a>): “When you will be harvesting the harvest of your field and you forget a sheaf on the field,” just as harvest has no other harvest after it<sup class="footnote-marker">140</sup><i class="footnote">On the same field. This would imply that he who cuts part of his field green either for fodder or for <i>Grünkern</i>, is not subject to the law of the forgotten sheaf.</i>, so too a sheaf, which has no other sheaf-making after it.

Chapter 6



Halakhah 1

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> The House of Shammai say, property abandoned to the poor is abandoned<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">It is free from any obligation of heave and tithes.</i>, but the House of Hillel say, it is not abandoned unless it is abandoned also to the rich, as in a Sabbatical year<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">I. e., what grows on the fields in a Sabbatical year without being planted or sown, which is exempt from heave and tithes.</i>. If all sheaves of a field are of one <i>qab</i> each, except one which is of four <i>qab</i> and they forgot about that one, the House of Shammai say, it cannot be a forgotten sheaf<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">Because it may be unbundled into several sheaves and, therefore, is not a final sheaf as required by the preceding Mishnah.</i>, but the House of Hillel say, it can be a forgotten sheaf.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> Rebbi Ḥiyya<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">Rebbi Ḥiyya bar Abba.</i> in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: The reason of the House of Shammai (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 19:10" href="/Leviticus.19.10"><i>Lev.</i> 19:10</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 23:22" href="/Leviticus.23.22">23:22</a>)<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">“Do not go over your vineyard a second time, nor pick up the single berries of your vineyard; to the poor and the sojourner relinquish them.” Why does it say “relinquish,” and not “give”?</i> “for the poor and the sojourner.” Why does the verse say, “relinquish them”? There is another relinquishing like this one. Just as this one is for the poor and not the rich, also what is spoken of elsewhere<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">Any meaning of עזב must conform with this paradigm.</i> is for the poor and not for the rich.
Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish said, the reason of the House of Hillel, (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 23:11" href="/Exodus.23.11"><i>Ex.</i>23:11</a>)<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">Speaking of the produce of the Sabbatical year.</i> “let drop,” why does the verse say, “and abandon it”? There is another abandoning like this one. Just as this one is for poor and rich alike, also what is spoken of elsewhere<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">Any meaning of נטש must conform with this paradigm.</i> is for poor and rich alike.
How<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">As in most Talmudic discussions, if themes 1–2 are introduced in order, they will be discussed as 2–1. The verse whose interpretation is ascribed to the House of Shammai is also the base of R. Joḥanan’s interpretation for the House of Hillel.</i> do the House of Hillel deal with the reason of the House of Shammai? “Relinquish <i>them</i>” is a restriction, <i>these</i> are for the poor, not for the rich, but what is spoken of elsewhere<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">Where עזב is used, it does not have to conform to the paradigm.</i> is for poor and rich alike. How do the House of Shammai deal with the reason of the House of Hillel? “Let it drop and abandon <i>it</i>” is a restriction, this abandoning is for poor and rich alike, but what is spoken of elsewhere<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">Where נטש is used, it does not have to conform to the paradigm.</i> is for the poor but not for the rich. Rebbi Avin said, the Mishnah supports Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">Since the House of Shammai disappeared soon after the destruction of the Temple and R. Joḥanan and R. Simeon ben Laqish lived almost 200 years later, their deductions are intellectual exercises which moreover have been shown here to be contradictory, as the same principles applied to two different verses lead to contradictory results. But R. Simeon ben Laqish’s argument very likely is historically true.</i>: “Abandoned also to the rich, as in the Sabbatical year.”
Property abandoned to animals but not to humans, to Gentiles but not to Jews, to rich but not to poor, everybody agrees that it does not have the status of abandoned property<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">And is subject to the laws of heave and tithes.</i>. For humans but not for animals, for Jews but not for Gentiles, for the poor of one town but not for the poor of any other town<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">This shows that the entire discussion is about the opinion of the House of Shammai. However, in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Peah (Lieberman) 3:1" href="/Tosefta_Peah_(Lieberman).3.1">Tosephta <i>Peah</i> 3:1</a> it is stated that “the House of Shammai agree with the House of Hillel that if he abandoned property for humans but not for animals, for Jews but not for Gentiles, it is legally abandoned.” This, then, must be taken as the Babylonian position.</i> is a disagreement between Rebbi Joḥanan and Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish. In the opinion of Rebbi Joḥanan, this abandoning is legal abandoning<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">In his opinion, restrictive abandoning is permitted.</i>. In the opinion of Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish, this abandoning is not legal abandoning<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">Since he deduces the rules from the Sabbatical year, and there animals and the stranger are explicitly mentioned as beneficiaries.</i>. Rebbi Lia said, they disagree explicitly: Rebbi Joḥanan said, this abandoning is legal abandoning. Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish said, this abandoning is not legal abandoning.
Rebbi Abin bar Ḥiyya said, that which was said, if it was abandoned to the poor but grabbed by the rich is a difference between Rebbi Meïr and Rebbi Yose<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">The main source is <i>Nedarim</i>, Babli 43a ff., Yerushalmi 4:10. The Mishnah speaks of a person A who made a vow not to use anything belonging to another person B. According to R. Meïr, B may abandon some property which A may take, but according to R. Yose, the abandoned property still belongs to B (who is also responsible for any damage caused by or on this property) until someone picked it up; hence, A may not take up the abandoned property.</i>. According to Rebbi Meïr, who asserts that [it is legally abandoned] as soon as a person abandons anything from his property, [in our case] it is legally abandoned<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">The rich person, while acting in an improper way, nevertheless acquired the abandoned article.</i>. According to Rebbi Yose, who says that nothing may leave the hands of its owners except if it is taken up [by another person, in our case] it is not legally abandoned<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">Since under the terms of the abandonment, the rich person will not acquire it even if he picks it up.</i>.
So far<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">These two words belong to <i>Nedarim</i>, not here. The question is, whether a person may abandon real property on condition that it automatically revert to the previous owner unless picked up by another person in a certain interval of time. In order to avoid declarations of abandonment simply for the purpose of evading heave, tithes, and taxes, restrictions might have been placed by the Rabbinic Court on the right of reversal.</i>, if he abandoned it for a longer period of time. But if he abandoned only for a short time? Let us hear from the following<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">The two passages in quotation marks are from a text close to Tosephta <i>Ma‘serot</i> 3:11; see also <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nedarim 43b-44a" href="/Nedarim.43b-44a">Babli <i>Nedarim</i> 43b–44a</a>.</i>: “If he abandoned his field, he may cancel his action during two or three days<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">But after that, the renunciation becomes absolute.</i>.” Rebbi Simeon Dima stated before Rebbi Zeïra: Even after three he may cancel his action. He said to him, since you say after three days, is it the same after three or after many? The language of a <i>baraitha</i> supports Rebbi Zeïra: “About when is this said<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">That after two days the act becomes irrevocable.</i>? If he abandoned in an unspecified way. But if he said: My field shall be abandoned one day, one week, one month, one year, a sabbatical period, as long as nobody took it over, either he or another person, he may cancel. But after somebody acquired it, either he or somebody else, he cannot cancel.” This means that short or long periods are the same. It also means that they were not worried about dishonesty<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">Since the probability that nobody else took the property is very small.</i>. That obviously answers Rebbi Zeïra’s question, since Rebbi Zeïra had said, are short and long times the same?
What are we discussing<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">This deals with the last part of the Mishnah, quoted by R. Jonah, that an outsize sheaf left on the field is not “forgotten” for the House of Shammai.</i>? If it should be recognizable, two would be enough. If for a row<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to Mishnah 4, which states that two sheaves constitute forgotten sheaves, but three sheaves do not. On this, the Halakhah specifies that the three sheaves must form a row in order to escape the law of forgotten sheaves.</i>, three would be enough. One scholar quoted this statement by Rebbi Joḥanan before Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish: Anything he can split and make into a row following the House of Shammai<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">At the end of Mishnah 4, the House of Shammai is quoted as giving three sheaves to the poor, four sheaves to the farmer.</i>. Rebbi Jonah: That goes by proportionality; “if all sheaves of a field are of one <i>qab</i> each, except one which is of four <i>qab</i> and they forgot about that one,” if all sheaves of a field are of two <i>qab</i> each, except one which is of eight <i>qab</i>.

Halakhah 2

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> A sheaf that was near a closure<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">Where an opening in a fence was closed with wood or other materials. This is the concurrent explanation of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Rashi on Bava Metzia 25b" href="/Rashi_on_Bava_Metzia.25b">Rashi (<i>Baba Meẓi‘a</i>25b</a>), Rabbenu Ḥananel (<i>loc. cit</i>.), and Maimonides (Commentary on the Mishnah) and, therefore, Geonic tradition. Ben Jehudah derives the word from the root גוף, גפף “to close an opening.” For example, מגופה is a clay block with which the top of an amphora containing wine or olive oil is sealed. The deviating explanation of R. Simson of Sens, “a stone fence made without mortar,” is preferred by modern Talmudic dictionaries. Schwab translates: “fence of pales,” close to the Modern Hebrew acception: “fence made of a weave of reeds or rods.”</i>, a stack, cattle<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">Cattle rather than horses were used to draw agricultural implements.</i>, or vessels, if it was forgotten, the House of Shammai say, it is not a forgotten sheaf, but the House of Hillel say, it is a forgotten sheaf.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> It is difficult about the House of Shammai; closure and stack are definite things, and they say it is not a forgotten sheaf. It is difficult about the House of Hillel; cattle and vessels are not definite things, and they say it is a forgotten sheaf<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">The background of the question is not clear. It is stated in the paragraph after the next that in one interpretation the Mishnah deals only with the case that the sheaf was already taken up to be brought to the threshing floor but then was deposited somewhere and forgotten. It is stated as a general principle of the House of Shammai that the farmer already acquired final possession of the sheaf by taking it up. The House of Hillel is of the opinion that the Biblical ordinance of the forgotten sheaf overrides everything until the grain is removed from the fields. The operating term in this respect is (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 24:19" href="/Deuteronomy.24.19"><i>Deut</i>. 24:19</a>) “and <i>you</i> will forget,” i. e., it must be you who forgets. We have to assume that the House of Shammai in principle also will agree to such an interpretation. Then we would say that it is difficult to forget something deposited near a landmark; hence, if it is forgotten, the House of Shammai are not justified not to declare it a forgotten sheaf. On the other hand, if the sheaf was deposited near movable objects and is not easily found again, the House of Hillel should have accepted the action of prior acquisition. (Interpretation of <i>Tosafot Yom Tov</i> on the Mishnah.)<br>But the position of this set of questions before the interpretation of the Mishnah seems to indicate that the question is asked in a general way, rather than about a sheaf which was already taken up. Therefore, most commentators, from R. Simson to R. Z. Frankel, prefer to change the text and switch the names of Hillel and Shammai. There is no textual basis for this. The best interpretation of the text as it stands is the one of R. Moshe Margalit, who explains as follows: “It is difficult about the House of Shammai; (<i>if their reason is that</i>) closure and stack are definite things (<i>location not easily forgotten</i>), and they say (<i>even for cattle and vessels</i>,<i>which are easily moved</i>) it is not a forgotten sheaf! It is difficult about the House of Hillel, (<i>if their reason is that</i>) cattle and vessels are not stationary, and they say (<i>even for closure and stack</i>) it is a forgotten sheaf!”</i>.
“By its place, if it was standing beside the wine press or beside a breach in the fence<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">This is a quote from Mishnah 7:1: “Every olive tree which has a special name on the field, for example ‘a dripping olive tree’ at harvest time, if it was forgotten, is not subject to the law of the forgotten sheaf. How is this said? By its name, its yield, or its place.… By its place, if it was standing beside the wine press or beside a breach in the fence.”</i>.” This Mishnah follows the House of Shammai, since the House of Shammai say, it is not a forgotten sheaf<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">If the sheaf was forgotten near a well-defined breach in the fence. This would imply that practice has to follow the House of Shammai, against the general rule to follow the House of Hillel.</i>. Rebbi Yose said, it is everybody’s opinion. There a stationary object<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">The olive tree.</i> is beside a stationary one, but here a movable object<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">The sheaf, which according to the House of Hillel is considered forgotten in the legal sense.</i> is beside a stationary one.
<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">With some minor deviations, this is <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Peah (Lieberman) 3:2" href="/Tosefta_Peah_(Lieberman).3.2">Tosephta <i>Peah</i> 3:2</a>.</i> Rebbi Ilaï<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">A Tanna of the third generation, student mostly of R. Eliezer, but also of the other great leaders of the second generation. Through his son, Rebbi Jehudah, the teachings of Rebbi Eliezer became so influential in the Mishnah.</i> said: I asked Rebbi Joshua, about which sheaves do the Houses of Shammai and Hillel disagree? He said to me, this is the teaching: The sheaf which was near a closure, a stack, cattle, or vessels, if it was forgotten, the House of Shammai say it is a forgotten sheaf, but the House of Hillel say it is not a forgotten sheaf<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">I. e., the Mishnah deals with the status of these sheaves under various circumstances. The text here switches the positions of the Houses of Hillel and Shammai. In the Tosephta, the rest of the sentence starting with “the House of Shammai say,” is missing. Hence, either R. Joshua disagrees with the Mishnah which is formulated in the tradition of R. Eliezer, or there is a scribal error. While in general, the opinion of R. Joshua prevails against that of R. Eliezer, here R. Eliezer’s opinion is endorsed in the strongest terms by R. Eleazar ben Azariah and should determine practice (Maimonides <i>Mattenot Aniïm</i> 5:3).</i>. But when I came to Rebbi Eliezer, he told me that the Houses of Shammai and Hillel did not disagree that a sheaf near a closure, a stack, cattle, or vessels, if it was forgotten, was a forgotten sheaf. What did they disagree about? About a sheaf he took up<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">And removed the sheaf away from the original field; since the owner cannot acquire his own property on his own field, he must have moved on the public road at least for a few steps. Then the only question is whether the commandment of the forgotten sheaf overrides the acquisition.</i> and put beside a closure, beside a stack, beside cattle, beside vessels, and forgot about it; in that case the House of Shammai say it is not a forgotten sheaf because he already acquired rights to it. But the House of Hillel say it is a forgotten sheaf. And when I came and lectured about these things before Rebbi Eleazar ben Azariah, he said to me, by the Covenant<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">An oath formula.</i>, these are the words that had been spoken to Moses on Horeb.
There<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Ketubot 8:1:2-4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Ketubot.8.1.2-4"><i>Ketubot</i> 8:1</a>; there this paragraph and the next one appear.</i> we have stated: “A woman who inherited property before she was betrothed<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">A Jewish marriage is performed in two stages. The first, <i>qiddushin</i>, which for lack of a better English equivalent was translated as “betrothed,” requires the groom to hand over to the bride an object of value (today, a gold ring) and to declare before two witnesses that the woman is betrothed to him. From that moment on they are married as far as penal law is concerned. In antiquity, the propective bride (who probably was in her early teens) did not prepare any trousseau. Hence, after <i>qiddushin</i> she was given adequate time to prepare and then was married to live with her husband in a second public ceremony, <i>nissuïn</i>, the execution of the <i>ketubah</i> document, in which the groom mortgages all his possessions and earnings for the upkeep of his wife and the care of his children, followed by the public recitation of seven benedictions (in the presence of 10 adult males). From that moment on, the couple is required to live in intimacy. Today, the two ceremonies are separated only by the reading of the <i>ketubah</i> and, sometimes, by a rabbi’s sermon. The period between <i>qiddushin</i> and <i>nissuïn</i> is one in which the groom is married, but as he is forbidden marital relations with his wife, as yet has no financial responsibility. After <i>nissuïn</i>, since the wife has a claim on his property enforceable in court, he receives administration of her estate.</i>, the Houses of Shammai and Hillel both are of the opinion that she may sell or give away and her actions are valid. If she inherited after she was betrothed, the House of Shammai say, she may sell, but the House of Hillel say, she may not sell<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">Since the husband will have a monetary interest in the property after <i>nissuïn</i>.</i>.” Rebbi Phineas asked before Rebbi Yose, why did we not state it with the leniencies of the House of Shammai and the stringencies of the House of Hillel<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">In the fourth chapter of <i>Iddiut</i>, the few cases in which the House of Shammai are more lenient than that of Hillel are enumerated. The first two Mishnaiot of the present chapter are included in that list, the one from <i>Ketubot</i> is not.</i>? He said to him, the Mishnaiot come only for circumstances that are either stringent on both sides or lenient on both sides. But here it is a stringency on one side<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote">On the wife, who is restricted in her actions. The law is lenient for the husband, who gets veto power over the actions of his wife before he mortgages all his property to her.</i> and a leniency on the other side. But did we not state: “The House of Shammai say, property abandoned to the poor is abandoned?” Is this not lenient for the poor and stringent for the householder, and it was stated! It is lenient for the poor<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote">In that non-poor persons may not acquire it.</i> and not stringent for the householder, since it was abandoned by his intent. He said to him, did we not state: “The sheaf that was near a closure, a stack, cattle, or vessels, if it was forgotten,” is this not lenient for the householder and stringent for the poor, and it was stated! He said to him, it is lenient for the householder but not stringent for the poor, because they did not acquire it yet. You may also say here<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote">In the case of <i>Ketubot</i>.</i>, it is lenient for the woman and not stringent for the husband since he did not yet acquire property rights to it. He<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">Rebbi Yose.</i> said to him, since he became betrothed to her, the inheritance fell to both of them.
Rebbi Jehudah said<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote">This paragraph belongs only to <i>Ketubot</i>; it was copied with the preceding paragraph.</i>, they argued before Rabban Gamliel: Because betrothed she is his wife and married she is his wife; just as the sale by the latter is void, the sale by the former also should be void. He said to them, we are ashamed of the new ones<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote">To require the husband’s consent to any financial or business transaction by his wife.</i>, now you want to roll old ones over us<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote">Extending the husband’s rights to <i>qiddushin</i>.</i>! The new ones, after <i>nissuïn</i>, the old ones before <i>nissuïn</i>, when she was married in the second ceremony.

Halakhah 3

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> Heads of rows<sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote">When sheaves are arranged in rows, the first sheaf in each row is always exempt from the law of forgotten sheaves since either one starts from it or one ends at it; in neither case could one return to it.</i>, the sheaf next to it proves<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote">If sheaves were arranged in parallel rows, and the sheaves were then removed by rows in one direction. If a sheaf from one was left standing but in the orthogonal direction there was a sheaf standing next to it, the standing sheaf now belongs to a row in the other direction; it is not forgotten until it is isolated in all directions from the rest of the sheaves still on the field. (By Mishnah 4, two isolated sheaves may be “forgotten”, a group of three is not.)</i>, and a sheaf that was taken up to be brought to town<sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote">The House of Hillel agree with that of Shammai that as soon as the sheaf is taken up to be removed from growing areas, the laws of the forgotten stalk no longer apply.</i> and then was forgotten, they all agree that they never are forgotten sheaves. The following describe heads of rows: Two who started in the middle of the row, one turned North and the other turned South, and they forgot before them or after them. The one before them is a forgotten sheaf<sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote">Any sheaf that at the start of the collection was in front of one of the workers, if forgotten, is subject to the law of forgotten sheaves. But if a sheaf was between the two, in the back of both of them, it is not forgotten since it never was considered, and it remains the head of an East-West row.</i>, the one after them is not a forgotten sheaf. A single person who started at the head of a row and forgot before and after himself. The one before him is not a forgotten sheaf<sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote">If he leaves sheaves before him but does not jump over one, these belong to East-West rows and do not have the status of being forgotten.</i>, but the one after him is a forgotten sheaf because it falls under (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 24:19" href="/Deuteronomy.24.19"><i>Deut</i>. 24:19</a>) “do not return to take it.” This is the principle: Anything that falls under “do not return to take it,” is a forgotten sheaf, but anything that does not fall under “do not return to take it,” is not a forgotten sheaf.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> From where about heads of rows<sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote">That they are exempt from the laws of forgotten sheaves.</i>? Rebbi Jonah said, it is written (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 24:19" href="/Deuteronomy.24.19"><i>Deut.</i> 24:19</a>): “When you are harvesting your harvest on your field and you forget,” what you harvest you can forget<sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote">But you cannot forget what you only start to harvest. Hence, uncut stalks at the start of a row to be cut are not for the poor.</i>. That refers to the start of rows of standing grain. End of rows of standing grain? Rebbi Jonah said, “do not return to take it.” From the place you came from you should not return to take it<sup class="footnote-marker">63</sup><i class="footnote">At the end of the row one goes to the next row; since one is at the end of the field he cannot retrace his steps to the end of the row.</i>. So far, about starts of rows of sheaves, ends of rows of sheaves? Rebbi Jonah said, we learn starts of rows of sheaves from starts of rows of standing grain and ends of rows of standing grain from ends of rows of sheaves<sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote">The verse speaks of the forgotten sheaf but is formulated in terms of harvesting standing grain. Just as forgotten standing stalks at beginning and end of rows are not for the poor, neither are sheaves at beginning and end of rows.</i>.
“The sheaf next to it proves<sup class="footnote-marker">65</sup><i class="footnote">Quote from the Mishnah.</i>.” How is that? (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Peah (Lieberman) 3:4" href="/Tosefta_Peah_(Lieberman).3.4">Tosephta <i>Peah</i> 3:4</a>): “If he had ten rows of ten sheaves each and he removed one row of them in a North-South direction; when he forgot one of them it is not forgotten because it is considered East-West<sup class="footnote-marker">66</sup><i class="footnote">It is connected to the part of the field not yet worked over in the orthogonal direction.</i>.” When he then removed the sheaves East-West and forgot one of them, let us hear from the following<sup class="footnote-marker">67</sup><i class="footnote">Word-by-word parallel to Chapter 5, Halakhah 2.</i>: When he removed the first, second, and third sheaves<sup class="footnote-marker">68</sup><i class="footnote">He not only bound them but he also removed them to a storage place or threshing floor. This statement assumes that a standard row of sheaves are three sheaves. Hence, the full second row would be sheaves 4,5,6.</i> but forgot<sup class="footnote-marker">69</sup><i class="footnote">To remove the fourth after it was bound.</i> the fourth, some state: If he removed the fifth, it becomes legally forgotten, but some state: If he waited long enough that he could have removed the fifth, it becomes legally forgotten. Rebbi Avin bar Ḥiyya said, he who says, “if he removed the fifth” if there is a sixth<sup class="footnote-marker">70</sup><i class="footnote">Because sheaves in a row are saved by the next sheaf just as stalks in a row are saved by the next stalk. Hence, the fourth can become legally forgotten only if the fifth, which saves it from being legally forgotten, is removed.</i>, but he who says, “if he waited long enough that he could have removed the fifth” if there is no sixth<sup class="footnote-marker">71</sup><i class="footnote">Since there is no row of three sheaves, they will be taken to be removed individually and not as a row. Hence, it is enough that the fifth could have been finished and removed.</i>. Before he took away the fifth, would not the fourth be apt to be considered starting a row<sup class="footnote-marker">72</sup><i class="footnote">If there are six sheaves, the fourth does not become legally abandoned until the sheaf which saves it is removed.</i>? If this is named “forgotten,” then also in our case<sup class="footnote-marker">73</sup><i class="footnote">Of sheaves first taken N-S, then E-W.</i> it is “forgotten.”
If there was an oversize sheaf<sup class="footnote-marker">74</sup><i class="footnote">And he decided to split it into regular size sheaves.</i> and he removed a sheaf from the outside next to the inside, it is considered a row<sup class="footnote-marker">75</sup><i class="footnote">If only part of the sheaf is removed, that part must be bound in a separate sheaf. We must assume that there is another sheaf standing nearby, so that there are three sheaves standing close together which form a row for the House of Hillel (Mishnah 4), or that the farmer splits his oversized sheaf into three, working from the outside in (which is the natural thing to do.)</i>. If he removed from the inside next to the outside, may it be considered a row<sup class="footnote-marker">76</sup><i class="footnote">We still have a minimum of three sheaves (see preceding Note) but since the farmer works from the inside out he is going back and, by R. Jonah’s argument at the start of the Halakhah, the sheaf formed by the outside stalks should not be protected as “head of row.”</i>? If he removed from the top beside the bottom it is considered a row<sup class="footnote-marker">77</sup><i class="footnote">That means, he takes off the top stalks to form a separate sheaf, the bottom part taking the place of the original oversized sheaf. This certainly is legitimate. However, if the farmer contrives a way to pull the lower stalks out of the sheaf, the question is as before, whether any of the resulting sheaves can be protected as heads of a row. These purely theoretical questions are not answered.</i>. If he removed from the bottom beside the top, is it considered a row?
When Rav descended there<sup class="footnote-marker">78</sup><i class="footnote">“There” always means Babylonia; going to Babylonia is “descending,” going to the Land of Israel is “ascending.” The story appears in the same context in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sotah 45a" href="/Sotah.45a">Babli <i>Sotah</i> 45a</a>, the actor being Abbaie, three generations after Rav; the Yerushalmi version is also in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 9:2:2-9" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.9.2.2-9"><i>Sota</i> 9:2</a>.</i>, he declared: I am this place’s Ben Azai<sup class="footnote-marker">79</sup><i class="footnote">Ben Azai, one of the most outstanding students of Rebbi Aqiba, was a walking encyclopedia and used to stroll through the markets of Tiberias, ready to immediately answer any question of Jewish learning. He died during mystical studies before he could marry R. Aqiba’s daughter. In the Talmudim, several sages are reported to have tried to imitate Ben Azai but all of them were quickly confronted with a question for which they gave the wrong answer or did not know any answer at all. The story is inserted here because a few paragraphs down hidden sheaves will be discussed.</i>. There came an old man and asked him, two slain people, one on top of the other<sup class="footnote-marker">80</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 21:1-9" href="/Deuteronomy.21.1-9"><i>Deut.</i> 21:1–9</a>, about the purification ceremony when a person is found murdered and the murderer is not found. Then a calf’s neck is broken in a ravine not used for agriculture.</i>? Rav was of the opinion that one breaks the neck. He told him, one does not break the neck. He asked him, why? He said to him, not the lower one for he is hidden<sup class="footnote-marker">81</sup><i class="footnote">The relevant verse is <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 21:1" href="/Deuteronomy.21.1"><i>Deut.</i> 21:1</a>: “If a corpse is found on the land the Eternal, your God, gives to you.…” The lower body is not “found”; he is only discovered when the other body is removed. The upper body is not found <i>on the land</i>.</i>, not the upper one because he floats. When he ascended here, he came to Rebbi, who told him: He told you correctly, “if he is found,” and not “if they are found.”
The great Rebbi Jehudah<sup class="footnote-marker">82</sup><i class="footnote">He probably is Jehudah bar Ḥiyya, son of the great R. Ḥiyya and twin brother of Ḥizqiah.</i> said: If a water canal crossed the entire field, if he has to lift the plough on one side and put it on the other side, it is not considered one row, otherwise it is considered one row<sup class="footnote-marker">83</sup><i class="footnote">If the irrigation canal is so shallow that the farmer can plough right across, the rows on both sides form just one row together. But if the plough has to be lifted and carried to the other side, then even if the furrow on one side is the exact continuation of the one on the other side, there are legally two rows and four ends that are protected from the law of forgotten sheaves.</i>. If he cut half a row and came back the next day it is considered a row<sup class="footnote-marker">84</sup><i class="footnote">As in the following examples, anytime the harvest or the work on the sheaves was interrupted, the new start is counted as head of row and exempt from becoming “forgotten.”</i>, when he sat down to eat, when he sat down to sleep, when his friend called him, when it got dark.
“<sup class="footnote-marker">85</sup><i class="footnote">An almost identical text is <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Peah (Lieberman) 3:3" href="/Tosefta_Peah_(Lieberman).3.3">Tosephta <i>Peah</i> 3:3</a>. R. Eliahu Fulda explains that חבירו refers to a friend of the farmer; he and his friend both carry a sheaf. This is impossible since one speaks about “upper and lower” sheaf. The other commentators, from Rashi on the Babli to R. S. Lieberman on the Tosephta, do not note the incongruity of having the farmer taking up one sheaf and forgetting two. One has to explain that חבירו is another sheaf; now two are lying on top of one another. {This is good Hebrew style, cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 26:3" href="/Exodus.26.3"><i>Ex.</i> 26:3</a> where gobelins are “sisters”.} When the upper one is taken up, it is acquired. The lower one then lies uncovered in the field and its status is the subject of the disagreement in the Tosephta. (Problem noted by E. G.)</i> A sheaf he took to bring to town, put it on another one, and forgot about both, the lower one is a forgotten sheaf but the upper one is not a forgotten sheaf. Rebbi Simeon<sup class="footnote-marker">86</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sotah 45b" href="/Sotah.45b">Babli, <i>Sotah</i> 45b</a>, this is quoted in the name of R. Simeon ben Jehudah who earlier in 5:6 quoted the tradition of R. Simeon (bar Ioḥai.)</i> said, neither of them is a forgotten sheaf; not the lower one because it is covered<sup class="footnote-marker">87</sup><i class="footnote">R. Simeon follows the opinion of R. Jehudah in Mishnah 9 that covered sheaves do not fall under the law of forgotten sheaves.</i> and not the upper one because he already had acquired it.” Rebbi Zeïra said, if he remembers<sup class="footnote-marker">88</sup><i class="footnote">If he somehow later remembers the upper one, it is his by prior acquisition as spelled out in the Mishnah. If he never remembers the upper one, then the lower one is not covered by anything different from itself and, therefore, cannot legally qualify as hidden.</i>. Rebbi Zeïra parallels Rebbi Jonah, just as Rebbi Jonah said<sup class="footnote-marker">89</sup><i class="footnote">Chapter 5, Halakhah 6, speaking about a sheaf covered under straw which is not forgotten as long as the farmer has the intention of collecting the straw since then the sheaf will automatically be discovered.</i>, if he remembers the straws, so Rebbi Zeïra said, if he remembers the upper one.
(<a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Peah (Lieberman) 3:4" href="/Tosefta_Peah_(Lieberman).3.4">Tosephta <i>Peah</i> 3:4</a>) “A field whose sheaves are mixed up, if one was forgotten it is not a forgotten sheaf until he removes all around it<sup class="footnote-marker">90</sup><i class="footnote">The principle of the Mishnah is applied here to a field whose sheaves are not in orderly rows. Nevertheless, a sheaf is not legally forgotten unless it is completely isolated.</i>.”

Halakhah 4

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> Two sheaves may qualify as forgotten sheaves, three may not. Two heaps of olives or carob may qualify as “forgotten sheaves”, three may not. Two plants<sup class="footnote-marker">91</sup><i class="footnote">Definition of Maimonides and R. Simson, cf. Arabic חוּץ “palm leaves.” R. Isaac ben Malchisedek Simponti translates by Italian <i>manelle</i> “sheaves.”</i> of hemp may qualify as “forgotten sheaves”, three may not. Two berries may qualify as dropped berries<sup class="footnote-marker">92</sup><i class="footnote">Refers to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 19:10" href="/Leviticus.19.10"><i>Lev.</i> 19:10</a>: “The dropped berries in your vineyard you shall not collect; abandon them to the poor and the stranger.”</i>, three may not. Two stalks may be gleanings, three may not, following the House of Hillel. About all of these, the House of Shammai say three are for the poor and four for the proprietor.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> Rebbi Abun bar Ḥiyya in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: If he made them like a Gamma (Γ)<sup class="footnote-marker">93</sup><i class="footnote">The sentence combines the Mishnah and the end of the previous Halakhah: A sheaf is not forgotten unless it became isolated from the others. Then how can two sheaves become forgotten sheaves? If they stood like the ends of a Γ separated by a missing sheaf at the angle.</i>.
He said<sup class="footnote-marker">94</sup><i class="footnote">The Mishnah speaks of “heaps of olives,” not single olives.</i> only “heaps”, not olives. What is the difference between heaps and olives? Heaps are the end of the harvest<sup class="footnote-marker">95</sup><i class="footnote">Hence, by Mishnah 5:7, the law of forgotten sheaves applies. Once the olives are put in orderly heaps, the work of the harvest is finished and that of the oil press starts.</i>, single olives are not the end of the harvest.
Rebbi Hoshaia said<sup class="footnote-marker">96</sup><i class="footnote">For comment, see Chapter 5, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Peah 2:1:10" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Peah.2.1.10">Halakhah 2, Note 40</a>.</i>, when I was mashing olives with the great Rebbi Ḥiyya, he said to me: Any olive you can reach when stretching out your hand is not forgotten. Rebbi Joḥanan said, if he passed it over and forgot it, it is forgotten.
Rebbi Eleazar in the name of the great Rebbi Ḥiyya: Half a bunch of grapes are dropped berries. Rebbi Ḥiyya stated: Half a bunch of grapes or an entire bunch are dropped berries. But did we not state: “Two berries are dropped berries?” Rebbi Immi in the name of Rebbi Ḥiyya: If he cuts and deposits them under the vine<sup class="footnote-marker">97</sup><i class="footnote">Where the grapes become dusty and cannot be put in the basket to be transported either to the wine press or to market.</i>.
If they are made like a shepherd’s flute<sup class="footnote-marker">98</sup><i class="footnote">Greek σίφων, -ωνος, ὁ, “pipe, water spout; a musical instrument” (I. Löw), same as Biblical (pl.) סופניה (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Daniel 3:10" href="/Daniel.3.10"><i>Dan.</i>3:10</a>); Gesenius and Lysowski explain as bagpipe. A shepherd’s flute made from reeds of different lengths glued together; the question is whether a plant (probably hemp) that grows different stalks of different lengths from one root is counted as one (and is under the law of forgotten sheaves) or as several (and is not).</i>? Menahem<sup class="footnote-marker">99</sup><i class="footnote">A Galilean Amora of the third generation. His time may be determined from the fact that he quotes R. Ammi and is mentioned as teacher of R. Ḥaggai.</i> in the name of Rebbi Jonathan: Only if he cuts them equally<sup class="footnote-marker">100</sup><i class="footnote">They may be counted as separate plants if they are cut equally near the ground. But stalks cut together count as one.</i>.
Rebbi Abin said, the reason of the House of Shammai (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 24:19" href="/Deuteronomy.24.19"><i>Deut.</i>24:19</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 24:20" href="/Deuteronomy.24.20">20</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 24:21" href="/Deuteronomy.24.21">21</a>): “It shall belong to the stranger, the orphan, and the widow<sup class="footnote-marker">101</sup><i class="footnote">Since there are three categories mentioned, each of them should have the possibility to get a separate piece.</i>.” The reason of the House of Hillel (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 19:10" href="/Leviticus.19.10"><i>Lev.</i> 19:10</a>): “For the poor and the stranger.” Rebbi Mana said, both of them explained the same verse, “it shall belong to the stranger, the orphan, and the widow.” The House of Shammai say, for the poor<sup class="footnote-marker">102</sup><i class="footnote">All three, as given by R. Abin.</i>, and the House of Hillel say, for the proprietor<sup class="footnote-marker">103</sup><i class="footnote">Since there is only one connective “and”, only two belong together. However, there are three categories of people entitled to collect gleanings and leftovers.</i>.

Halakhah 5

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> A sheaf containing two <i>seah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">104</sup><i class="footnote">25.6 liter, about .9 cu. ft., cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Berakhot 3:4:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Berakhot.3.4.4"><i>Berakhot</i>, Chapter 3, Note 164</a>. Halakhah 6 spells out that the grain kernels alone, without the straw, are meant.</i> that was forgotten is not a “forgotten sheaf.” Two sheaves that together are two <i>seah</i>, Rabban Gamliel says, they are for the proprietor, but the Sages say, they are for the poor. Rabban Gamliel said to them: Does large size of the sheaves increase or decrease the power of the proprietor? They said to him, it increases it. He said to them: Since a sheaf containing two <i>seah</i> that was forgotten is not “forgotten sheaf,” it is only logical that two sheaves that together are two <i>seah</i> should not be “forgotten sheaf” either. They said to him, if they said that about one sheaf that is like a stack<sup class="footnote-marker">105</sup><i class="footnote">Exempt from the laws of “forgotten sheaves,” Mishnah 6:2. This applies to sheaves as large as a stack.</i>, do you want to say the same about two sheaves that are like bundles?<br>Standing produce of two <i>seah</i> which was forgotten is not “forgotten sheaf.” If it does not have two <i>seah</i> but it might bring two <i>seah</i>, even if it were oats<sup class="footnote-marker">113</sup><i class="footnote">Arabic קוּרטֻמָאן “oats”, with short kernels (Translation of Maimonides). Arukh translates טופח by Arabic גֹֻלֻבָּאן, peas. Barley grains are three to an inch. The field would not be under the laws of “forgetting” if it would yield .9 cu. ft. if all grains were the size of barley grains (in the shell, hairs attached.)</i> it is considered like barley grains.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> Rebbi Eleazar said, it is written (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 24:19" href="/Deuteronomy.24.19"><i>Deut.</i> 24:19</a>): “When you are harvesting your harvest on your field and you forget a sheaf on the field,” a sheaf to which you may stretch out your hand and take it<sup class="footnote-marker">106</sup><i class="footnote">But not one where you need both hands.</i>. Some Tannaïm stated<sup class="footnote-marker">107</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sifry Deut.</i> 283.</i>: “And you forget a sheaf,” but not a stack.
How does it work out<sup class="footnote-marker">108</sup><i class="footnote">What is the practical difference between R. Eleazar who treats the oversized sheaf as sheaf and the Tanna who treats it as a stack?</i>? If he forgot one sheaf next to it, if you make it a sheaf, everybody agrees that it is “forgotten sheaf.<sup class="footnote-marker">109</sup><i class="footnote">Then you have two sheaves which for everybody are subject to “forgotten sheaves.”</i>” If you make it a stack, a difference between Shammai and Hillel<sup class="footnote-marker">110</sup><i class="footnote">This should read: the Houses of Shanmai and Hillel. The House of Shammai in Mishnah 6:2 freed the sheaf next to a stack from “forgotten sheaves;” the House of Hillel did not.</i>. If he forgot two sheaves, if you make it sheaves, a difference between the Houses of Shammai and Hillel<sup class="footnote-marker">111</sup><i class="footnote">For the House of Hillel, one has three sheaves and it is an exempted row; for the House of Shammai one would need four.</i>; if you make it a stack it will not be considered a row<sup class="footnote-marker">112</sup><i class="footnote">Then one only has two sheaves which even for the House of Hillel are not a row.</i>.
Rebbi Jonah said (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 24:19" href="/Deuteronomy.24.19"><i>Deut.</i> 24:19</a>): “When you are harvesting your harvest on your field and you forget a sheaf on the <i>field</i>,” a sheaf containing two <i>seah</i> that was forgotten is not “forgotten sheaf.” Standing produce<sup class="footnote-marker">114</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>field</i> of the verse.</i> containing two <i>seah</i> that was forgotten is not “forgotten sheaf.” Rebbi Yose said, considering only the ears<sup class="footnote-marker">115</sup><i class="footnote">But not the straw.</i>. If they were thin, one looks at them as if they were long, if they were scorched, one looks at them as if they were full.

Halakhah 6

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> Standing produce saves sheaves and standing produce<sup class="footnote-marker">116</sup><i class="footnote">Standing produce which was not forgotten protects produce nearby that was forgotten from becoming legally “forgotten.” “Nearby” was defined earlier as close so that the ears of one may touch the other.</i>. But a sheaf<sup class="footnote-marker">117</sup><i class="footnote">A sheaf which either is not forgotten or which itself is exempt from the laws of the “forgotten sheaf.”</i> saves neither sheaves nor standing produce. What is standing produce that saves sheaves? Everything that is not forgotten, even if it is only one stalk.<br>A <i>seah</i> of uprooted grain and a <i>seah</i> of not uprooted grain<sup class="footnote-marker">131</sup><i class="footnote">They do not combine to form the two <i>seah</i> that exempt from the laws of forgotten sheaves.</i>, also trees<sup class="footnote-marker">132</sup><i class="footnote">Harvested and hanging fruits do not combine.</i>, garlic and onions do not combine<sup class="footnote-marker">133</sup><i class="footnote">They belong to the same family but different species.</i>. Rebbi Yose says, if a poor man’s right comes in the middle<sup class="footnote-marker">134</sup><i class="footnote">If at any time after the cutting of the first part the poor had the right to collect <i>peah</i>, gleanings, or forgotten sheaves, the two parts cannot be combined. In vineyards, the poor have the right to collect the single berries that grow on the vines. But for other trees, the poor have no rights until the end of the harvest; for R. Yose, harvested and hanging fruits of other trees combine.</i>, they do not combine, otherwise they do combine.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> Rebbi Illa said, it is written (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 24:19" href="/Deuteronomy.24.19"><i>Deut.</i> 24:19</a>): “When you are harvesting your harvest on your field and you forget a sheaf on the <i>field</i>,” a sheaf surrounded by harvest and not a sheaf surrounded by standing produce. And why a sheaf surrounded by harvest and not a sheaf surrounded by standing produce? Under a sheaf surrounded by harvest is the field<sup class="footnote-marker">119</sup><i class="footnote">Since the harvest is not finished.</i>, under a sheaf surrounded by standing produce is straw.
It was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">120</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Peah (Lieberman) 3:5" href="/Tosefta_Peah_(Lieberman).3.5">Tosephta <i>Peah</i> 3:5</a>; there (and in the manuscript of R. S. Cirillo) the name of the first Tanna is R. Meïr.</i>: “The standing produce of his neighbor saves his own<sup class="footnote-marker">121</sup><i class="footnote">If his forgotten patch of produce is close to the neighbor’s field that was not yet harvested.</i>, the standing produce of a Gentile saves that of a Jew, the standing wheat saves barley, the words of Rebbi. But the Sages say, only his own produce saves and only its own kind.”
Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel stated<sup class="footnote-marker">122</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Peah (Lieberman) 3:6" href="/Tosefta_Peah_(Lieberman).3.6">Tosephta <i>Peah</i> 3:6</a>. In the edition of R. S. Lieberman, the punctuation and interpretation of the abbreviation are incorrect. The text of the Tosephta is garbled and the good text of the Yerushalmi was misunderstood by R. Simson and the other commentators.</i>: Just as standing produce saves the sheaf, so the sheaf saves the standing produce. This is logical: If standing produce, on which the power of the poor is enhanced<sup class="footnote-marker">123</sup><i class="footnote">Since he has the right to <i>peah</i>, gleanings, and forgotten sheaves.</i>, saves if any of it was forgotten, it is only logical that the sheaf should save, since on it the power of the poor is reduced<sup class="footnote-marker">124</sup><i class="footnote">It is reduced to the forgotten sheaf.</i>. Rebbi<sup class="footnote-marker">125</sup><i class="footnote">The son of Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel.</i> answered him: If standing produce, on which the power of the poor is enhanced, saves if any of it<sup class="footnote-marker">126</sup><i class="footnote">The sheaf, on which the power of the poor is weak.</i> was forgotten, is it logical that the sheaf, on which the power of the poor is reduced, if any of it was forgotten should save<sup class="footnote-marker">127</sup><i class="footnote">The standing produce, to which the claim of the poor is much stronger!</i>? From the words of both of them we infer that sheaf is saved by sheaf<sup class="footnote-marker">128</sup><i class="footnote">If both sheaves are close together. For Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel it follows from his logical argument, for Rebbi it follows since the claim of the poor to the sheaf is reduced.</i>, but standing produce is not saved by standing produce<sup class="footnote-marker">129</sup><i class="footnote">For neither of them is there an argument that anything should save standing produce to which the claim of the poor is enhanced.</i>.
Hence<sup class="footnote-marker">130</sup><i class="footnote">This paragraph is part of the next Halakhah in the manuscripts and first prints. But it belongs here, as discussion of whether “even if it is only one stalk” means that if a single stalk is forgotten, nothing is saved. The answer is that it is enough if one stalk was not forgotten.</i>, if he forgot, is it “forgotten sheaf”? Explain it if he forgot the standing produce first.
Hence, if all<sup class="footnote-marker">135</sup><i class="footnote">Adding up to two <i>seah</i>.</i> is cut it is for the proprietor. Our Mishnah follows Rabban Gamliel<sup class="footnote-marker">136</sup><i class="footnote">Who asserted in Mishnah 5 that sheaves which together add up to 2 <i>seah</i> do not fall under the law of the forgotten sheaf. Since the Mishnah is formulated as anonymous statement, practice has to follow Rabban Gamliel.</i>.
Then<sup class="footnote-marker">137</sup><i class="footnote">If we follow Rabban Gamliel. The same text, with a different thrust, is found Chapter 5, Halakhah 2.</i> the cut grain should save the uncut grain. This means that something that is apt to save, but was forgotten, can become legally forgotten. Rebbi Jonathan<sup class="footnote-marker">138</sup><i class="footnote">There, R. Jonah.</i> said, explain it if he was cutting and removing the sheaves row by row. He already forgot the standing grain before he might forget the sheaves<sup class="footnote-marker">139</sup><i class="footnote">And Rabban Gamliel will not have the two combined if they are separated in space and time.</i>.
Does it mean that it really comes, or only that it is able to come<sup class="footnote-marker">141</sup><i class="footnote">Does it mean that Rebbi Yose excludes combining only if the poor actually could exercise their rights, or even if there is only a theoretical possibility.</i>? Let us hear from the following<sup class="footnote-marker">142</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Peah (Lieberman) 3:5" href="/Tosefta_Peah_(Lieberman).3.5">Tosephta <i>Peah</i> 3:5</a>: “According to R. Yose, Ḥananiah, the nephew of R. Joshua, said that whenever a poor man’s right comes in between, for example grain and vineyard, they do not combine. Whenever a poor man’s right does not come in between, for example fruits of trees, they do combine.”</i>: “For example grain and vineyard.” And is the vineyard on the spot<sup class="footnote-marker">143</sup><i class="footnote">It is true that there is no manual grain harvest without gleanings; the poor always have the right to enter after cutting and binding of the sheaves. But there are vines which do not produce single berries; in a vineyard, the right of the poor is conditional.</i>? That means, even if it is able to come<sup class="footnote-marker">144</sup><i class="footnote">It includes all vineyards and mechanical harvesting.</i>!

Halakhah 7

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> Grain cut for animal feed<sup class="footnote-marker">145</sup><i class="footnote">Cut as fodder (in contrast to grain cut for human consumption but used as fodder) means that it was cut green. In the same way, grain cut to bind sheaves must still have flexible stalks; it cannot be dry and does not qualify as “harvest.”</i> or to bind sheaves, and similarly garlic used to bind<sup class="footnote-marker">146</sup><i class="footnote">Garlic leaves used to bind bundles of garlic or onions.</i> as well as bundles of garlic and onions<sup class="footnote-marker">147</sup><i class="footnote">Onions and garlic are bound or plaited in small bundles for later use but these are then bound together in large bunches for transport. The small bundles are not made for transport to storage and by Mishnah 5:7 do not fall under the law of the forgotten sheaf.</i> are not subject to “forgotten sheaves.” Anything hidden in the ground<sup class="footnote-marker">148</sup><i class="footnote">Plants whose edible part, or main part, is the one hidden in the ground.</i>, such as <i>luf</i><sup class="footnote-marker">149</sup><i class="footnote">In classical Arabic, לוּף means serpentary, snake weed, <i>Arum Dracunculus L.</i>, a plant with a large bulb. This is the identification given by Arukh, based on a Gaonic source. Maimonides (Commentary on the Mishnah) declares it “doubtless to be a kind of onion.” <a class="refLink" data-ref="Rashi on Shabbat 126b" href="/Rashi_on_Shabbat.126b">Rashi (<i>Shabbat</i> 126b</a>) declares it to be a kind of legume. The determination by Arukh must be accepted. (In modern Arabic, לוּף <i>liffa</i> is <i>Luffa cylindrica Roem</i>, dishcloth gourd, which is inedible.)</i>, garlic, and onions, Rebbi Jehudah says, they are not subject to “forgotten sheaves”, but the Sages say, they are subject to “forgotten sheaves.”
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> Rebbi Jonah said<sup class="footnote-marker">150</sup><i class="footnote">At the moment the decision is made to use certain produce as fodder, not for human consumption, it becomes exempt from the law of the forgotten sheaf.</i>, not only if he gave it but even if he took it with the intention of giving it.
There<sup class="footnote-marker">151</sup><i class="footnote">The discussion is paralleled in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Kamma 6:5:2-3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Kamma.6.5.2-3"><i>Baba Qama</i> 6:5</a> in a different formulation.</i> we have stated: “If someone sets fire to a stack in which were hidden vessels, Rebbi Jehudah says, he pays for all that was in it.
