But the Sages say, he only pays for a stack of wheat or barley<sup class="footnote-marker">152</sup><i class="footnote">Both Babli and Yerushalmi are agreed that the Mishnah refers only to a man who lights a legal fire on his own field and it burns out of control. But an arsonist has to pay for everything under any circumstance. The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 61b" href="/Bava_Kamma.61b">Babli (<i>Baba Qama</i> 61b</a>) states that the Sages agree that whoever started the fire is responsible for agricultural implements usually hidden in stacks. Hence, in the opinion of the Babli but not the Yerushalmi, the Sages free him only from the responsibility for unexpected losses.</i>.” The argument of Rebbi Jehudah is inverted; there he says to include the hidden things, but here he says to exclude the hidden things. There (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 22:5" href="/Exodus.22.5"><i>Ex.</i> 22:5</a>) “and a<sup class="footnote-marker">153</sup><i class="footnote">This is the Biblical text, not “the stack” as given here. “If fire gets out of control and a stack, or the standing grain, or the field is consumed, the person who caused the fire should certainly pay.”</i> stack or standing grain was consumed.” I understand that since it says “standing grain” it includes a stack. Why does the verse add “a stack?” In order to include the hidden things. But here (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 24:10" href="/Deuteronomy.24.10"><i>Deut.</i> 24:10</a>) “your field,” in the open, to exclude anything hidden. The argument of the rabbis is inverted; there they say to exclude the hidden things, but here they say to include the hidden things. There, “or standing grain or a field,” just as a field is in the open, so all must be in the open. But here, “your field,” in the open, to exclude that which is hidden; “your harvest,” in the open, to exclude that which is hidden. That is a restriction after a restriction and every restriction after a restriction is only<sup class="footnote-marker">154</sup><i class="footnote">There is an אלא missing in the text or the אין is superfluous (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Yevamot 12:1:2-17" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Yevamot.12.1.2-17"><i>Yebamot</i>12:1</a>). The principle that a restriction after a restriction is an extension is universally accepted in both Talmudim; in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Horayot 1:1:2-10" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Horayot.1.1.2-10"><i>Horaiot</i> 1:1</a> it is generalized to the statement that any sequence of restrictions of an even number of elements is an extension; for an odd number of elements it is a restriction.</i> to add the hidden things.

Halakhah 8

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> He who harvests at night or removes the stacks, or a blind person<sup class="footnote-marker">155</sup><i class="footnote">All of whom are likely to miss some standing produce or some sheaves.</i>, are subject to the law of forgotten sheaves. But if he<sup class="footnote-marker">156</sup><i class="footnote">The one who harvested or removed the sheaves at night, and the blind (Maimonides <i>Mattenot Aniïm</i> 5:8).</i> intended only to take the large pieces, he is not subject to the law of forgotten sheaves. If he said: “I harvest on the understanding that I shall take what I might forget,” he is subject to the law of forgotten sheaves.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> So is the Mishnah: He who harvests at night or removes the stacks at night, or a blind person day or night.
Rebbi Jonah said<sup class="footnote-marker">157</sup><i class="footnote">He refers to the second sentence in the Mishnah.</i>, not only for the large pieces, but also for the thin pieces. Since he is going to check for the large ones, the thin ones likewise are not subject to the law of forgotten sheaves.
If he said: “I harvest on the undertanding that I shall take what I might forget,” he is subject to the law of forgotten sheaves, since he made a condition<sup class="footnote-marker">158</sup><i class="footnote">Not a condition in the legal sense, since a legal condition needs a positive and a negative and he cannot say, “if I cannot take everything then what I harvest shall not be harvested.” Probably, “condition” should be read as “understanding.”</i> against what is written in the Torah, and if anybody makes a condition against what is written in the Torah, his condition is invalid<sup class="footnote-marker">159</sup><i class="footnote">Except in the matter of civil contracts.</i>.

Chapter 7



Halakhah 1

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> Every olive tree that has a special name on the field, for example “a dripping olive tree<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">Maimonides identifies this as “from Beth Netofa (in Galilee);” then it should be vocalized נְטוֹפָה. But the form נְטִיפָה in the Halakhah requires נְטוּפָה here. The addition “in its time” can only mean that the olives start dripping even on the tree, at the end of the growing season, for a short time every year.</i>” in its time, if it was forgotten, is not subject to the law of the forgotten sheaf. When does this apply? For its name, its production, and its place. By its name, if it was dripping<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">Literally, “pouring” or “from a town Shipkoun”.</i> or from Beth Shean. By its production, if it produced much. By its place, if it stood next to the wine-press or near a hole in the wall. For all other olive trees, two are subject to the law of the forgotten sheaf, three are not. Rebbi Yose says, there is no law of the forgotten sheaf for olive trees.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> Rebbi La said, it is written (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 24:19" href="/Deuteronomy.24.19"><i>Deut</i>. 24:19</a>): “You will forget a sheaf on the field,” a sheaf you forget forever; this excludes one you certainly will remember after some time<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">Because it has a specific name or property.</i>.
Rebbi Jeremiah asked: If it was marked in his mind, is it as if it were marked<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">If the olive grower gave the tree a private name in his mind, is that as good as a publicly known name (or, at least, a name known to his workers).</i>? If it stood in the shadow<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">Maimonides (<i>Mattenot Aniïm</i> 5:24) reads בצד “next to”, which probably is the correct reading.</i> of a date palm, the date palm marks it. If two of them were dripping, each of them marks the other<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">These three cases are agreed on; the question is about the next case.</i>. If the entire field was of dripping olive trees? Let us hear from the following: “Rebbi Yose says, there is no law of the forgotten sheaf for olives.” Rebbi Simeon bar Yaqim<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">An Amora of the third generation, student of R. Joḥanan and colleague of R. Eleazar, mentioned as R. Simeon bar Elyaqim in the Babli.</i> said, Rebbi Yose said this only in former times when olives were scarce because Hadrian, the evil one, had come and devastated the entire land<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">In the war of Bar Kokhba.</i>. But today when olive trees are everywhere, they are under the law of the forgotten sheaf.
<i>Šofkānī</i> drips oil. But did we not state “dripping,” so it must be that it yields much oil. But did we not state: “By its production, if it yields a lot.” So, <i>Šofkānī</i><sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">Root שפך “to pour out.”</i> yields much oil. <i>Nĕṭūfah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">Root נטף “to drip.”</i> drips oil. Its production, it yields a lot, viz., it produces a lot of olives. <i>Bês̄ānī</i>, some say, really from Beth She’an<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">Biblical בית שאן, Talmudic בישן.</i>; some say, it puts the other trees to shame<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">Root בוש “ashamed,” taken in causative sense, “to put to shame.”</i> by producing fourfold, similar to what we have stated: If all sheaves of the field are of one <i>qab</i>, except one which is of four <i>qab</i> and was forgotten. Since it produces more than others, it is considered marked. On condition that it produces every year? Since it produces most years it is considered marked.
“By its place, if it stood next to the wine-press or near a hole in the wall.” Is our Mishnah following the House of Shammai, since the House of Shammai say, property abandoned to the poor is abandoned<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">If the Mishnah did accept the ruling of the House of Shammai as generally accepted practice, it would be more than astonishing. However, the quote from the House of Shammai (Mishnah 6:1) seems to be wrong; it would have to be Mishnah 6:2: “The sheaf that was near a closure, a stack, cattle, or vessels; if it was forgotten, the House of Shammai say, it is not a forgotten sheaf, but the House of Hillel say, it is a forgotten sheaf.” In contrast, everybody seems to agree that an olive tree standing near a closure is not forgotten.</i>? Rebbi Yose<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">The Amora.</i> said, it is everybody’s opinion; there it is movable near immovable, here it is immovable near immovable.
“<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">Repeated from above.</i> Rebbi Yose says, there is no law of the forgotten sheaf for olives.” Rebbi Simeon bar Yaqim said, Rebbi Yose said this only in former times when olives were scarce because Hadrian, the evil one, had come and devastated the entire land. But today when olive trees are everywhere, they are under the law of the forgotten sheaf.
Rebbi Yose<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">The Amora. The reference here is to Halakhah 6:4 which specifically excludes single olives from the law of forgotten sheaves. The next reference to “Rebbi Yose” is to the Tanna.</i> said, nobody applied the law of forgotten sheaves to olive trees except Rebbi Aqiba, for he drew inferences from “behind you, behind you.” Then there should be no “forgotten sheaf” for olive trees following Rebbi Yose who does not draw inferences from “behind you.<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">It says in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 24:20-21" href="/Deuteronomy.24.20-21"><i>Deut</i>. 24:20–21</a>: “If you shake your olive tree, do not pick single olives <i>behind you</i>; they shall be for the stranger, the orphan, and the widow. If you harvest your vineyard, do not pick unripe berries <i>behind you</i>; they shall be for the stranger, the orphan, and the widow.” <i>Behind you</i> must refer to things forgotten, since one harvests always in front of himself.</i>” They objected, is not a sheaf subject to “forgotten sheaves”, and there it is not written “behind you.” Since it is written (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 24:19" href="/Deuteronomy.24.19"><i>Deut</i>. 24:19</a>): “Do not return to take it,” it is as if “behind you” were written there.
Rebbi Jonah had a problem<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">The problem refers to the next Mishnah which states that once one started to harvest an olive tree, even if it was marked by some of the peculiarities enumerated, it becomes subject to the law of forgotten sheaves. Rebbi Yose denies any applicability of that law to olive trees. Since practice does not follow R. Yose, the question is not resolved.</i>: That dripping olive tree, since it is marked, in the opinion of Rebbi Yose even if he started with it it, should it be as if he had not started with it?

Halakhah 2

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> An olive tree standing between three rows of two rectangles, when forgotten, is not subject to the law of forgotten sheaves<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">Maimonides explains in his Mishnah commentary that the trees are planted in checkerboard fashion, that each “white” square has a tree while the “black” squares are planted with vegetables; we still say that the middle one is obscured by the surrounding trees, disregarding the vegetable beds. R. Isaac Simponti accepts this explanation but takes the rectangles to denote distance, not necessarily that they are planted. [However, in his Code (<i>Mattenot Aniïm</i> 3:25) Maimonides accepts the explanation, also given by R. Simson, that the olive tree is a single tree surrounded on three sides by three rows of trees, each row consisting of two trees, so that the surrounding trees hinder the access to the single tree. R. Simson rejects the idea that the tree should not be subject to the law because of difficulty of access.]</i>. An olive tree which yields two <i>seah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">Olives, not oil.</i>, when forgotten, is not legally forgotten. When does this apply<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">This question does not refer to the tree yielding two <i>seah</i> but to the marked trees mentioned in Mishnah 1. After one started harvesting, these are not protected unless they yield two <i>seah</i>.</i>? Only if he did not start with it, but if he started harvesting it, even a <i>dripping olive tree</i> in its time, when forgotten, is subject to the law of the forgotten sheaf. Whenever he<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">Maimonides, in his commentary and his Code (<i>Mattenot Aniïm</i> 1:12) refers “him” to the poor. Once they have permission to search for olives under the tree, they may take the olives that are still on the tree, but not before (even though ususally olives are not harvested by climbing the tree but by shaking it.) R. Simson and R. Abraham ben David (<i>Mattenot Aniïm</i> 1:12) refer “him” to the owner; as long as anything under the tree (the main harvest) is not legally forgotten, everything on the tree is his, independent of his state of mind regarding the olives remaining on the tree.</i> has under it, he has at its crown. Rebbi Meïr says, from the moment that the <i>maḥba</i> is used<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">According to Maimonides, the <i>maḥba</i> is an instrument with which the branches of the olive tree are shaken to remove the olives still clinging to the branches. In all manuscripts of his tradition, the spelling is מחבה as a feminine, in accord with the feminine form of the verb. R. Simson and R. Abraham ben David explain <i>maḥba</i> as the leaves which hide olives and must be removed. Since Maimonides lived in olive growing societies, his explanation should be accepted. The root underlying מחבא seems to be Arabic ח̇בּא “to grow abundantly” (but cf. text at note 44).</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> Rebbi Eleazar said, so says the Mishnah: “of two rectangles” and he forgot it<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">The last expression, “and he forgot it,” is missing in the Rome manuscript. (However, “and he forgot it” is in the parallel <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Peah (Lieberman) 3:10" href="/Tosefta_Peah_(Lieberman).3.10">Tosephta <i>Peah</i> 3:10</a>). Rebbi Eleazar wants to emphasize that the correct reading is <i>two</i>, not <i>three</i> as in <i>Yalqut Shimoni</i> 937. Other explanations by J. N. Epstein in מבוא לנוסח המשנה pp. 92–93 are unconvincing.</i>. What are we dealing with? If it is because it is marked, are there not other olive trees<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">They also should be exempt since they are adjacent to a marked tree. <i>Two</i> is also the reading in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Peah (Lieberman) 3:10" href="/Tosefta_Peah_(Lieberman).3.10">Tosephta <i>Peah</i> 3:10</a>, <i>Sifri Deut</i>. 284.</i>? If it is because it is in a row<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">If it is aligned in a long row of seven trees and therefore not privileged, which is impossible since it may be considered the head of a row of four.</i>, it is considered a row in itself<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">Since it is not aligned with anything, it cannot be exempt from the law as a row.</i>. It must be because of row and of rows<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">It is in the middle of a row and its row is the middle row, following the explanation of Maimonides in the commentary to the Mishnah. The central position marks it.</i>.
Rebbi Yose said<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">It is unlikely that “R. Yose” here is correct, since the next paragraphs refer to R. Joḥanan. R. S. Cirillo has אמר ר׳ יוסי אמר ר׳ יוחנן but this clearly is Babli style (in the Yerushalmi it could be either אמר ר׳ יוסא ר׳ יוחנן or אמר ר׳ יוסי בשם ר׳ יוחנן) and, therefore, it is his interpolation.</i>, the Mishnah deals with an olive tree that can be shaken<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">I. e., the entire harvest can be done by shaking the tree; it is not necessary to climb up on a ladder to remove the olives still hanging.</i>. Rebbi Yose<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">This is either R. Yose the late Amora giving a ruling that in practice asserts the position of R. Yose the Tanna, or it is a justification of the position of the Tanna R. Yose from a practical, rather than a theoretical, point of view.</i> said, not only one that can be shaken; since olive trees usually are first checked by shaking, none can be under the law of forgotten sheaves.
Some Tannaïm<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">These Tannaïm are not the Sages of the period known as tannaïtic but are scholars who memorized tannaïtic statements (which at this time were not written down) to produce them when asked about them. The different versions are those of our Mishnah.</i> state: “it is found.” Some Tannaïm state: “It is found standing.” He who says “it is found” supports Rebbi Joḥanan<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">It turns out after the fact that the tree can be harvested simply by shaking.</i>. He who says “it is found standing” supports Rebbi Eleazar<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">It is known beforehand that the tree is standing at a certain privileged place.</i>. A <i>baraita</i><sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Peah (Lieberman) 3:10" href="/Tosefta_Peah_(Lieberman).3.10">Tosephta <i>Peah</i> 3:10</a>. The text here follows the Rome manuscript which is identical with that of the Tosephta. The Venice print has the positions of “recognizes” and “does not recognize” switched.</i> supports Rebbi Joḥanan: “When has this been said? If he does not recognize it. But if he recognizes it, he runs after it even 100 cubits<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">If the tree has a distinguishing mark, as in the case described by R. Eleazar, the entire Mishnah does not apply. Hence, we must deal with a particular property discovered after the fact.</i>.”
Hence<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">Here starts the discussion of the second sentence of the Mishnah.</i>, if it yields two <i>seah</i>, if it was forgotten it is not legally forgotten. It does not refer to this but to the first Mishnah: “Every olive tree that has a special name on the field, for example ‘a dripping olive tree’ in its time, if it was forgotten, it is not legally forgotten. Hence, if it yields two <i>seah</i>, if it was forgotten …”<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">Maimonides in his Mishnah commentary construes as follows: “Every olive tree that has a special name on the field, for example ‘a dripping olive tree’ in its time, when forgotten is not legally forgotten. When has this been said? Only if he did not start with it; but if he started harvesting it, even a <i>dripping olive tree</i> in its time, when forgotten is legally forgotten. But if it yields two <i>seah</i>, when forgotten it is not legally forgotten.”</i>
“Whenever he has under it, he has at its crown.” One may explain this in two ways. Whenever he has under it, he has at its crown; hence, before that even if there is nothing under it, he has at its crown<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">The poor may take the olives remaining on the branches even though the owner is not finished with collecting all olives shaken from the tree (Maimonides <i>Mattenot Aniïm</i> 1:12).</i>. Another explanation: Whenever he<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">The owner.</i> has under it, he has nothing at its crown<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">Since he will not start collecting the olives that fell off the tree until he has removed the remaining ones from the tree mechanically.</i>; by the time the <i>maḥba</i> was used, even though he has nothing at its crown he has under the tree<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">At that time, the poor may search the tree since the owner has completed his harvest.</i>.
It was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">Explaining the statement of R. Meïr and the word <i>maḥba.</i></i> in the name of the House of Shammai: When he puts away the knee cushion<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">Used to collect all the olives shaken from the tree. (Second explanation of <i>Arukh.</i>)</i> and finishes, then he still has at the top. Rebbi Abbahu in the name of Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish: The remainder<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">Arabic כִּדַּה “poor soil”, אַכִּדַּה “leftover pasture”.</i> of the harvest. Rebbi Abbahu said, <sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Maḥba</i> is …</i> that which takes care of the hidden ones: (<a class="refLink" data-ref="I Samuel 23:23" href="/I_Samuel.23.23"><i>1Sam.</i> 23:23</a>) “Find out and see all the hiding places where he could hide.”

Halakhah 3

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> What are dropped berries<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">Here starts the discussion of the duty to give single berries to the poor, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 19:10" href="/Leviticus.19.10"><i>Lev.</i> 19:10</a>.</i>? Anything that drops during vintage. If he was gathering grapes, cut off the bunch, it got mixed up in the leaves, fell to the ground and scattered<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote">By accident.</i>, that belongs to the proprietor. He who puts a basket under the vine while he is gathering grapes robs the poor, and about him it was said<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. Mishnah 5:5.</i> (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Proverbs 22:28" href="/Proverbs.22.28"><i>Prov.</i> 22:28</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Proverbs 23:10" href="/Proverbs.23.10">23:10</a>): “Do not displace an eternal boundary.”<br>What is a gleaning<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote">In the Torah, עלל is used exclusively for vines (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 19:10" href="/Leviticus.19.10"><i>Lev.</i> 19:10</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 24:21" href="/Deuteronomy.24.21"><i>Deut.</i>24:21</a>). In Arabic, the root means “going over the harvest a second time” in general.</i> of grapes? Anything that has no shoulder and no dropping<sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote">The Halakhah explains these terms.</i>. If it has either shoulder or dropping it belongs to the proprietor, in case of doubt it belongs to the poor. A gleaning on a cut branch, if it can be cut off together with a bunch it belongs to the proprietor, otherwise to the poor. An isolated berry, Rebbi Jehudah says, it is considered a bunch<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote">According to the Halakhah more than three isolated berries together form a bunch for R. Jehudah.</i>, but the sages say, a gleaning.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> This means that dropped berries become sanctified<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote">As food of the poor for which they do not have to give heave or tithes. If the berries were not the property of the poor while falling down, the vintner could not be accused of actively robbing the poor by putting a basket under the vine.</i> in the act of falling down. Does this not simply answer Hilfai<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">In the Babylonian Talmud, he is called Ilfa. He studied together with R. Joḥanan, was as great as R. Joḥanan in learning but preferred to earn a living in trade. He objected to the use of <i>baraitot</i> and <i>toseftot</i> and maintained that all necessary information could be obtained from the Mishnah.</i>’s question, since Hilfai asked: Do gleanings become sanctified in the act of falling down? Rebbi Samuel ben Eudaimon said: There is a difference, because he prevented them from reaching the ground<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote">Even if dropped berries become exempt from heave and tithes only when they touch the ground, still the vintner is guilty of robbery since without his intervention the berries would have touched the ground. However, it is reasonable to say that the berries do not become sanctified in falling and that the vintner, in addition to being a robber of the poor, is still under the obligation of giving heave and tithes from what he collected.</i>.
(<a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Peah (Lieberman) 3:11" href="/Tosefta_Peah_(Lieberman).3.11">Tosephta <i>Peah</i> 3:11</a>): “What are gleanings? Anything that has no shoulder and no dropping. If it has shoulder but no dropping, dropping but no shoulder, it belongs to the proprietor, if it is doubtful it belongs to the poor. What is a shoulder? Spread out<sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote">The etymology and meaning of פסיגין is not very clear. I am comparing it with Arabic פסג “to spread one’s legs.” The meaning of the sentence is that grapes must also grow upward from the spine.</i> one on top of the other. What is a dropping? They hand downward on the spine.”
Rebbi Abba in the name of Rav Jehudah: Only if they all touch the palm of his hand<sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote">A rudimentary bunch qualifies only as gleanings if it is so small that it fits into one hand.</i>. But did not Rebbi Ḥiyya say, it happened that they weighed a gleaning in Sepphoris and it was seven pounds<sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote">Greek λίτρα, Latin <i>libra</i>, “a pound” of 12 oz.</i>? Rebbi Ḥinena said, if they put it on a board, every grape must touch the board<sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote">Latin <i>tabula</i> “board, plank”; also “plot in a vineyard.”</i>.
A Levite who received unprepared tithes<sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote">First Tithes of which the heave of the tithe was not given to the Cohen. Tithe may not be eaten before the heave of the tithe is separated (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 18:26" href="/Numbers.18.26"><i>Num.</i>18:26</a>). The heave of the farmer has no minimum spelled out from the Torah, but it must be given from the harvest itself. The heave of the tithe is fixed at 10% but it may be given from anywhere (<i>Terumot</i> 2:1); hence, the Levite may use any fruit he has for that tithe.</i> and found in them gleanings may use them for the heave of the tithes even on other places. But do not gleanings belong to the poor? Rebbi Avin in the name of the rabbis from there<sup class="footnote-marker">63</sup><i class="footnote">Babylonia.</i>: I say that they are gleanings cut with the bunches<sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote">As spelled out in the Mishnah. The Levite does not commit a sin since he is not using property acquired illegally to satisfy a religious duty. The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Peah (Lieberman) 3:14" href="/Tosefta_Peah_(Lieberman).3.14">Tosephta (<i>Peah</i> 3:14</a>) simply states: “A Levite to whom grapes were given, among which he found gleanings, does not have to worry whether they might belong to the poor.”</i>.
Rebbi Simon said, the reason of Rebbi Jehudah (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Isaiah 17:6" href="/Isaiah.17.6"><i>Is.</i> 17:6</a>): “There will remain in it gleanings as when one plucks olives, two or three kernels<sup class="footnote-marker">65</sup><i class="footnote">Meaning that one, two, or three berries on one branch really belong to the poor even according to Rebbi Jehudah, but four or more, even though they grow out right from the stem and do not form bunches, are not called “berries” but “grape.”</i>.” More than that is a grape.
Clarification<sup class="footnote-marker">66</sup><i class="footnote">The following digressions are induced by the story told before of a few grapes in Sepphoris weighing together seven pounds. Most of these stories are also quoted, in changed form, in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Ketubot 111b-112a" href="/Ketubot.111b-112a">Babli <i>Ketubot</i> 111b–112a</a>; the first ones also, very much changed, in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 91b" href="/Bava_Batra.91b"><i>Baba Batra</i> 91b</a>.<br>כרם usually means “vineyard,” but in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Judges 15:5" href="/Judges.15.5"><i>Jud.</i> 15:5</a> כרם זית is a plantation of olive trees.</i>. Rebbi Abbahu, Rebbi Yose ben Ḥanina, and Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish passed by the orchard of Doron<sup class="footnote-marker">67</sup><i class="footnote">It is not clear whether this is a personal or topographical name.</i>. The sharecropper brought them one peach<sup class="footnote-marker">68</sup><i class="footnote">Greek περσικὸν μῆλον, τὸ, also περσικός, ὁ, “Persian apple.”</i>; they and their donkeys ate from it and left some over. They estimated it to be like a dish<sup class="footnote-marker">69</sup><i class="footnote">Greek λοπάς, -άδος, ἡ, “plate.”</i> from Kefar Ḥananiah<sup class="footnote-marker">70</sup><i class="footnote">A place in Galilee famous for its pottery, possibly Kafr Anan.</i> that contains a <i>seah</i> of lentils. After a time they passed by there, he brought them two or three peaches in one hand. They said to him, we would like some from that tree. He said to them, they are from it. On that, they quoted (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Psalms 107:34" href="/Psalms.107.34"><i>Ps.</i> 107:34</a>): “A fruit-bearing land into salt flats, because of the evil of its inhabitants.”
Rebbi Ḥanina said, when I immigrated here, I took my belt, my son’s belt, and the belt of my donkey to measure around a young carob tree of the Land of Israel and it was not enough. I cut one carob pod and it filled my hand with honey<sup class="footnote-marker">71</sup><i class="footnote">Carob syrup. In general, “honey” may mean both bee’s honey or syrup. In Arabic, דִבשׂ denotes only “sugary matter produced from a fruit, syrup, molasses”.</i>.
Rebbi Joḥanan said, the second quality fruit<sup class="footnote-marker">72</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. Arabic ספסאף “rejects, bad quality, junk.”</i> we ate in our youth tasted better that the peaches we ate in our old age, because during his lifetime the world changed<sup class="footnote-marker">73</sup><i class="footnote">He was born under the Principate, lived through the hyperinflation and the shrinking economy of the Military Anarchy, and died under Diocletian’s military absolutism. (Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 91b" href="/Bava_Batra.91b">Babli <i>Baba batra</i> 91b</a>.)</i>.
Rebbi Ḥiyya bar Abba said, one <i>seah</i> of Arbel<sup class="footnote-marker">74</sup><i class="footnote">On the high plain above Tiberias.</i> grain did yield one <i>seah</i> of fine flour, one <i>seah</i> of white flour, one <i>seah</i> of dark flour, one <i>seah</i> of bran, one <i>seah</i> of coarse bran, and one <i>seah</i> of wheat germ. But today, we do not even get one for one<sup class="footnote-marker">75</sup><i class="footnote">A <i>seah</i> of grain contains empty space between grains. In milling, by necessity something is lost. One <i>seah</i> of grain yields much less than a <i>seah</i> of flour. Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Ketubot 112a" href="/Ketubot.112a">Babli <i>Ketubot</i> 112a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sotah 17b" href="/Sotah.17b"><i>Sotah</i>17b</a>.</i>.
Rebbi Ḥuna in the name of Rebbi Avin: Cinnamon was goat feed when the Jews were growing it. Rebbi Ḥuna in the name of Rebbi Abin: The two daily offerings<sup class="footnote-marker">76</sup><i class="footnote">Two yearling sheep.</i> which were sacrificed every day used to be carried by a camel and their legs touched the ground. Rebbi Ḥuna in the name of Rebbi Idi: It happened that someone tied his goat to a fig tree; when he returned, he found honey<sup class="footnote-marker">71</sup><i class="footnote">Carob syrup. In general, “honey” may mean both bee’s honey or syrup. In Arabic, דִבשׂ denotes only “sugary matter produced from a fruit, syrup, molasses”.</i> and milk mixed<sup class="footnote-marker">77</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Ketubot 111b" href="/Ketubot.111b">Babli <i>Ketubot</i> 111b</a>, in the name of Rami bar Ezechiel, brother of Rav Jehudah, a wanderer between Galilee and Babylonia.</i>.
Rebbi said to Rav Periri<sup class="footnote-marker">78</sup><i class="footnote">He appears only here and the title “Rav” is impossible for anybody in the Yeshivah of Rebbi except Rav himself. R. S. Cirillo changes the name to Rebbi Pedat (who however lived long after Rebbi), R. Eliahu Fulda and R. Moses Margalit use “Rebbi Peridah,” a mythical figure from the Babylonian Talmud who is reputed to have lived for over 400 years. He would fit into the story but one might doubt that Rebbi would hurt him. I wonder whether the person in question is not Rav himself and פרירי is an (Aramaic-Arabic فورار) address of endearment “my lamb”. In that case, one has to translate: Rebbi said to Rav: My lamb, will you not show me.… Since Rav was a relative of the Heads of the Diaspora and rich, he could have owned real estate whose yield would sustain him during his years of study.</i>: Would you show me the bunch of grapes in your vineyard? He said to him, if you come out, I will show it. When he was still far away, he spied something like an ox. He said to him, does not the ox destroy the vineyard? He said to him, what you take for an ox is the bunch of grapes. He quoted for it (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Song of Songs 1:12" href="/Song_of_Songs.1.12"><i>Cant.</i> 1:12</a>): “As long as the King was at his round table, my nard gave its fragrance79;” the Temple is destroyed and you persist in your obstinacy? Immediately, they looked for it and it was never found again.
They brought before him two radishes from between New Year’s Day and the Great Fast<sup class="footnote-marker">80</sup><i class="footnote">Yom Kippur, the Tenth of Tishre. The radishes probably were offered for sale on Yom Kippur Eve.</i>. It was the year after a Sabbatical, and they were a full camel’s load. He said to them, are they not forbidden as aftergrowth<sup class="footnote-marker">81</sup><i class="footnote">One may not use any plant grown in the Sabbatical year for commercial purposes (and one may not use any produce if nothing of the same kind is left on the fields for wildlife.) Hence, the free growth of the Sabbatical is forbidden in commercial transactions even after the end of the Sabbatical year.</i>? He said to him, they were sown at the end of New Year’s Day. At that moment did Rebbi permit to buy vegetables immediately after the end of the Sabbatical year.
They asked before him, what is that which is written (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Joel 1:17" href="/Joel.1.17"><i>Joel</i> 1:17</a>): “Dried figs became mouldy under their shovels.” He said to them, we were raking honey, now we are raking rotten things instead. It happened that somebody had a row of fig trees; when he came, he found them surrounded by a fence of honey.
One person sowed turnips on his field; he used to cut them off and sell them<sup class="footnote-marker">82</sup><i class="footnote">It is still possible to replant the top of a large radish with a small part of the root and it will regrow a root, but not more than once.</i>. It happened that a fox made his nest in a turnip’s head. It happened in Shihin<sup class="footnote-marker">83</sup><i class="footnote">A village near Sepphoris renowned for its pottery. This story also appears in <i>Ketubot.</i></i> that one stalk of mustard plant had three leaves; one leaf was split off to cover a potter’s hut and they found on it three <i>qab</i><sup class="footnote-marker">84</sup><i class="footnote">About 7 quarts.</i> of mustard seed. Rebbi Simeon ben Ḥalaphta said, I had a stem of mustard plant on my property and I climbed on it the way one climbs a fig tree.
It happened that someone sowed a <i>seah</i> of peas and it produced 300 <i>seah.</i> They said to him, the Holy One, praise to Him, has started to bless you. He answered, get away from here, if bad dew had not descended on it, it would have produced twice as much.
Rebbi Simeon ben Ḥalaphta said, it happened that Rebbi Jehudah said to his son in Sikhnin<sup class="footnote-marker">85</sup><i class="footnote">A town in Galilee, birthplace of R. Joshua.</i>: Go and bring us dried figs from the barrel. He went and put his hand into the barrel, found it full of honey and said: Father, it is a barrel of honey. He said to him, put your hands deep into it and you will bring up the dried figs. It happened that Rebbi Yose said to his son in Sepphoris: Go and bring us dried figs from the upper floor. He went and found that honey was floating on the upper floor. Rebbi Ḥananiah was a dealer in bee’s honey; he also had honey obtained by roasting<sup class="footnote-marker">86</sup><i class="footnote">Following Levy, all the dictionaries identify <i>ẓeli</i> as a kind of dates, since vegetable honey is made from dates. However, the previous stories were all about the fact that earlier (before the war of Bar Kokhba but long after the destruction of the Temple) even figs produced sap so rich in sugar that it could be used as honey substitute. So one possible translation would be “of <i>ẓeli</i> figs”; it is unclear what kind they are. The translation assumes that it was bee’s honey, which may be obtained from the honey combs by heating. Then the “roasted” honey was lower quality honey obtained by roasting honey combs from which the first quality had been extracted by moderate heat, and honesty required that the customer be informed that the honey sold was of second quality only. “To roast” is Hebrew צלה, Arabic צלי. The word could also be derived from Arabic צוול “to clean out by washing”, צולה “chaff”, giving a similar meaning.</i>. After a time they passed by him. He said to them, in order not to trick you, you should know that the honey that I gave you was from roasting. They said, from that one we want more because it is good for our purposes. He set aside its value<sup class="footnote-marker">87</sup><i class="footnote">Greek τιμή, “value.” He set aside the difference in value between premium bee’s honey and <i>ẓeli</i> honey.</i> and built with it the House of Study of Sepphoris.
Rebbi Eleazar ben Rebbi Simeon went to a place where they brought him shrunk cabbage<sup class="footnote-marker">88</sup><i class="footnote">Either dried in the air or roasted over a fire.</i>. He said to them, you put a lot of honey into it. They said to him, we did not put anything in, it is in its natural state.

Halakhah 4

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> He who thins out vines<sup class="footnote-marker">89</sup><i class="footnote">So that the rest of them should grow more and larger grapes. The “vines of the poor” are those on which single berries are visible. One cannot say that it refers to the part of the vine that is designated as <i>peah</i> since thinning is done early in the growing season and <i>peah</i> is designated at the end of the harvest.</i>, just as he thins out his own so he thins out those of the poor, the words of Rebbi Jehudah<sup class="footnote-marker">90</sup><i class="footnote">The Halakhah explains that R. Jehudah considers the poor as partners of the proprietor while R. Meïr considers them as buyers.</i>. Rebbi Meïr says, he may do so for his own but he is not empowered for those of the poor.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> Everybody agrees that he who sells ten bunches to somebody else may not touch them<sup class="footnote-marker">91</sup><i class="footnote">To thin out after the sale.</i>. Everybody agrees in a partnership that just as he thins out his, so he thins out that of his partner<sup class="footnote-marker">92</sup><i class="footnote">Since it is a necessary part of tending the vineyard.</i>. Rebbi Jehudah considers him to be a partner, Rebbi Meïr considers him to be a seller<sup class="footnote-marker">93</sup><i class="footnote">The last clause is missing in the Venice print and the Leyden manuscript; it is added from R. S. Cirillo and the Rome manuscript on the testimony of R. Simson of Sens that it was in his Yerushalmi text.</i>. Rebbi Immi said to him<sup class="footnote-marker">94</sup><i class="footnote">The person addressed is not indicated. The usual partner of Rebbi Immi was Rebbi Yasa (Assi).</i>: Think about it, if a wild animal ate it, if you consider him a partner he is required to pay him<sup class="footnote-marker">95</sup><i class="footnote">If the wild animal ate all the gleanings of the poor person then, as in any partnership, the damage should be borne jointly by the two partners; hence, the farmer should pay to the poor person his part of the loss. (Since individual poor persons have no standing in court in this matter, probably he should give the equivalent to the local administrator of charity.)</i>!
Rebbi Joḥanan said, it seems that Rebbi Jehudah changed his opinion. There<sup class="footnote-marker">96</sup><i class="footnote">Halakhah 5:3, speaking of the farmer who wants to irrigate or fertilize his field immediately after harvest, before the poor had an opportunity to search for gleanings. There, the same question is asked in the other direction and answered differently.</i> he says, in both cases he takes them, deposits them on the fence and the poor comes and takes what is his; but here he says so<sup class="footnote-marker">97</sup><i class="footnote">That he can eliminate the part of the poor in the course of regular care of his vineyard.</i>! Because he squeezees them, they will produce more the next year. I should say also here, because he fertilizes it, it will produce more the next year. He said to him, it happens frequently<sup class="footnote-marker">98</sup><i class="footnote">A vine is a perennial and will always produce grapes, so the poor can wait for the next crop. But a field is subject to regular crop rotation and it is to be expected that every third year or so the field will be sown with vegetables not harvested at one time, exempt from <i>peah.</i> Hence, the interest of the poor in not in the next crop.</i> that he will sow vegetables and shelter them from the poor.

Halakhah 5

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> A vineyard in its fourth year<sup class="footnote-marker">99</sup><i class="footnote">It is forbidden to harvest a newly planted vineyard the first three years. In the fourth year, the grapes can be harvested but they (or the wine produced from them) must be brought to the Temple and be consumed in Jerusalem in a festive manner (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 19:23-24" href="/Leviticus.19.23-24"><i>Lev.</i>19:23–24</a>). If there is too much to be taken on a journey, it may be redeemed and the money taken to Jerusalem. The House of Hillel compare the yield of the fourth year to the Second Tithe that also has to be eaten in Jerusalem, since produce of the Second Tithe that is redeemed is subject to a surcharge of one fifth (from above, 25% from below). There are two kinds of removal the vineyard of the fourth year may be subject to; if it is compared to the Second Tithe it must be removed from the house at the end of the third and sixth years of every Sabbatical period; if the produce was that of a Sabbatical year, one may take it but only as long as wild animals find similar food on the field (in this case, in other unharvested vineyards) and it must be removed by being consumed before that time. The House of Shammai consider the yield of the fourth year as profane food, subject only to what is expressly spelled out in the verse.<br>The argument of the House of Hillel, that the verse compares the vineyard in its fourth year to the Second Tithe, is given in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Kiddushin 54b" href="/Kiddushin.54b">Babli <i>Qiddušin</i> 54b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sifra, Kedoshim, Section 3" href="/Sifra,_Kedoshim,_Section_3">Sifra <i>Qedošim Parašah</i> 3 #8</a>.</i>, the House of Shammai say, it is not subject to a fifth and is not subject to removal; but the House of Hillel say, it is. The House of Shammai say, it is subject to single berries and gleanings<sup class="footnote-marker">100</sup><i class="footnote">As any other profane food.</i> and the poor redeem for themselves, but the House of Hillel say, all goes to the winepress<sup class="footnote-marker">101</sup><i class="footnote">Since the second tithe is not subject to any gifts to the poor, neither is the yield of the fourth year.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> It was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">103</sup><i class="footnote">In a different form, Tosephta <i>Maäser Šeni</i> 5:17. The entire discussion is found in Yerushalmi <i>Maäser Šeni</i> 5:3.</i>: Rebbi says, the House of Shammai said this only in the Sabbatical year, but in all other years of the sabbatical cycle, the House of Shammai say that it is subject to a fifth and subject to removal. According to that Tanna, they learned the rules of the vineyard of the fourth year only from the Second Tithe; since you say that there is no Second Tithe in the Sabbatical year<sup class="footnote-marker">104</sup><i class="footnote">Since there is no private property of agricultural produce in the Sabbatical year, there may not be special uses for the farmer’s family.</i>, so there is no fourth year after planting<sup class="footnote-marker">105</sup><i class="footnote">The Mishnah speaks of כרם רבעי, a vineyard in its fourth year. The <i>baraitot</i> all speak of נטע רבעי, a planting (a planted tree) in its fourth year. The extension of the Biblical commandment about the vineyard to all trees is discussed in <i>Maäser Šeni</i>, Chap. 5.</i> in the Sabbatical year. But then should there be no holiness in it? Its holiness comes from the verse (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 19:24" href="/Leviticus.19.24"><i>Lev.</i> 19:24</a>): “Holy for praises,” it has the status of those holy fruits over which praises are said<sup class="footnote-marker">106</sup><i class="footnote">The First Fruits, for which the praises said in the Temple are spelled out in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 26" href="/Deuteronomy.26"><i>Deut.</i> 26</a>.</i>. And should it be permitted to the fresh mourner<sup class="footnote-marker">107</sup><i class="footnote">The mourner in the period between the death of a close relative and the burial, when he is forbidden any sanctified food even if he is not ritually defiled, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 26:14" href="/Deuteronomy.26.14"><i>Deut.</i> 26:14</a>.</i>? It is stated: This<sup class="footnote-marker">108</sup><i class="footnote">The verse declaring the vineyard of the fourth year as “holy for praises.” Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sifra, Kedoshim, Section 3" href="/Sifra,_Kedoshim,_Section_3">Sifra <i>Qedošim Parašah</i> 3 #9</a>.</i> implies that it is forbidden to the fresh mourner. And should it be subject to removal? Since Rebbi Simeon frees it from removal<sup class="footnote-marker">109</sup><i class="footnote">The House of Shammai will agree with Rebbi Simeon who states in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bikkurim 2:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bikkurim.2.2.1">Mishnah <i>Bikkurim</i> 2:2</a> that First Fruits and the vineyard of the fourth year are not subject to either kind of removal.</i>. And should it be redeemed while still connected to the ground<sup class="footnote-marker">110</sup><i class="footnote">This question is not answered. The Tosefta <i>Maäser Šeni</i> 5:19 states categorically that there can be no redemption while the grapes are still on the vine. However, see in <i>Tosefta Kifšutah Maäser Šeni</i> p. 786 the list of authorities who claim that this is only the required procedure, but that a redemption, if done on the vine, is valid after the fact.</i>?
Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel stated: Both in the Sabbatical year and in the rest of the years of the sabbatical cycle, the House of Shammai say, there is no fifth and no removal. According to that Tanna, they did not at all learn the rules of the fourth year after planting from the Second Tithe. But then<sup class="footnote-marker">111</sup><i class="footnote">If it is profane food. The rest of the paragraph is identical with the previous one except for the insertion of את ההלל missing here in the first version (but found in <i>Maäser Šeni</i> 5:3).</i> should there be no holiness in it? Its holiness comes from the verse (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 19:24" href="/Leviticus.19.24"><i>Lev.</i> 19:24</a>): “Holy for praises;” it has the status of those holy fruits over which praises are said. And should it be permitted to the fresh mourner? It is stated: This implies that it is forbidden to the fresh mourner. And should it be subject to removal? Since Rebbi Simeon frees it from removal. And should it be redeemed while still connected to the ground?
Rebbi Zeïra asked before Rebbi Abbahu: From where that it needs redemption<sup class="footnote-marker">112</sup><i class="footnote">If the produce cannot be bodily transported to the place of the Temple. The argument is quoted in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Berakhot 35a" href="/Berakhot.35a">Babli <i>Berakhot</i> 35a</a>. The word פדיון “redemption” is missing in the Leyden manuscript and the Venice print; it has been added from the parallel text in <i>Maäser Šeni.</i></i>? (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 19:24" href="/Leviticus.19.24"><i>Lev.</i> 19:24</a>) “Holy for praises,” holy for redemption. The rabbis never refrain from identifying ה and ח.<sup class="footnote-marker">113</sup><i class="footnote">In all Jewish dialects except Eastern European Ashkenazic, ח <i>(ḥ)</i> is very close to הּ. In Medieval German Jewish, as well in Talmudic Babylonian Jewish, <i>h</i> and <i>ḥ</i> were identical and חַ ,הַּ were used as rhyming sounds. The current Ashkenazic identification of the sounds of ח,כ <i>(ḥ</i>, <i>k</i>) comes from the fact that Polish has only one <i>ch</i> sound; this was adopted by the Jews from their Gentile surroundings. While Biblical Hebrew probably had the two Semitic ח sounds, corresponding to Arabic خ، ح, one <i>ḥ</i> as in יצחק Isaac (Septuagint Ισαακ), the other k as in רחל Rachel (Septuagint Ραχηλ), in Mishnaic times these distinctions had disappeared long ago.</i>
Rebbi Ayvu bar Naggari stated before Rebbi La following Rebbi Ismael (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 27:31" href="/Leviticus.27.31"><i>Lev.</i> 27:31</a>): “If a man redeems part of his tithes, he should add its fifth to it.” That excludes the fourth year after planting; one is not obligated by it for a fifth. Then he turned around and stated: There are two terms of redemption<sup class="footnote-marker">114</sup><i class="footnote">גָּאֹל יִגְאַל. One of these statements follows Rebbi, the other Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel.</i>, one for the Second Tithe and one for the fourth year after planting.
There we have stated<sup class="footnote-marker">116</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah <i>Terumot</i> 3:9: “The heave of Gentile and Samaritan is heave, their tithes are tithes, their dedication is dedication. Rebbi Jehudah says, there is no vineyard in the fourth year for the Gentile, but the Sages say, there is.” Even though the Gentile is not required to give heave, tithes, or to dedicate anything to the Temple, if he does it voluntarily, it must be treated according to all rules applying to heave, tithes, and dedications from Jews.</i>: Rebbi Jehudah says, there is no vineyard in the fourth year for the Gentile, but the Sages say there is<sup class="footnote-marker">117</sup><i class="footnote">This is connected with the disagreement between R. Meïr and R. Jehudah/R. Simeon whether the possession by a Gentile of arable land in the Land of Israel does free its produce from duties of heave and tithes or not, cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Peah 4:6:9" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Peah.4.6.9">Chapter 4, Notes 129–131</a>. The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Menachot 66b" href="/Menachot.66b">Babli (<i>Menaḥot</i> 66b</a>) declares R. Jehudah to agree with R. Meïr that the Gentile’s arable land is subject to all duties, but this is not the opinion of the Yerushalmi.</i>. Rebbi Eleazar said, so says the Mishnah: There is never a vineyard in the fourth year for the Gentile<sup class="footnote-marker">118</sup><i class="footnote">It is difficult to understand what R. Eleazar adds. R. S. Cirillo has: “There is no ‘vineyard of the fourth year’ at all in Syria,” interpreting the Mishnah in terms of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Terumot (Lieberman) 2:13" href="/Tosefta_Terumot_(Lieberman).2.13">Tosephta <i>Terumot</i>2:13</a>, where R. Jehudah is quoted that “a Gentile in Syria has no ‘vineyard of the fourth year’.” This follows the interpretation of R. Simson that R. Jehudah cannot speak of a Gentile in the Land of Israel, based on the Babli quoted in the preceding Note. However, R. Simson’s argument does not apply to the Yerushalmi; the direct testimony of R. Salomon ben Adrat (<i>Responsa attributed to Nachmanides</i>156) confirms the Yerushalmi text as it appears here, in <i>Maäser Šeni</i>, and in the Rome manuscript. It is true that in the end, one speaks only of Syria, but this cannot apply to R. Jehudah’s statement in the Mishnah or to R. Eleazar’s addition.</i>. Rebbi Bibi said before Rebbi Zeïra in the name of Rebbi Eleazar: According to the opinion of Rebbi, the statement of Rebbi Jehudah turns out to be like the statement of the House of Shammai. Since the House of Shammai said that they learned the rules of the vineyard of the fourth year only from the Second Tithe; since you say that there is no Second Tithe in the Sabbatical year, so there is no fourth year after planting in the Sabbatical year. Similarly, Rebbi Jehudah said that they learned the rules of the vineyard of the fourth year only from the Second Tithe; since you say that there is no Second Tithe in Syria<sup class="footnote-marker">119</sup><i class="footnote">Syria has a special status as far as agricultural laws are concerned. The definition of “Syria” here means all territory that was not conquered by the 12 tribes under Joshua and the early Judges but was included in David’s kingdom. Since it was never incorporated in the Kingdom of Israel, it is not subject to the agricultural laws from the Torah. But since it was part of King David’s empire, these laws apply, either as Rabbinical ordinance or as popular usage. In any case, all rules must be interpreted leniently in Syria. (In consequence of the persecution of Jews in Palestine, particularly under the Christian Roman empire, the center of Jewish population slowly moved from Galilee to Syria.)</i>, so there is no fourth year after planting in Syria. He said to him, look what he<sup class="footnote-marker">120</sup><i class="footnote">The House of Shammai, taken as a collective singular.</i> said! He said only, it is not subject to a fifth and is not subject to removal<sup class="footnote-marker">121</sup><i class="footnote">They did not say simply: The rules of the vineyard do not apply at all.</i>, hence, it is subject to all other rules; Rebbi Jehudah<sup class="footnote-marker">122</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Terumot (Lieberman) 2:13" href="/Tosefta_Terumot_(Lieberman).2.13">Tosephta <i>Terumot</i> 2:13</a>; this is R. Zeïra’s proof against R. Bibi, that R. Jehudah does not negate the obligation of the fourth year for Jews in Syria. However, the paragraph after the next will show that the Yerushalmi follows R. Bibi, that there is no such obligation in Syria.</i> says, there is no vineyard in the fourth year for the Gentile in Syria.
Samuel bar Abba<sup class="footnote-marker">123</sup><i class="footnote">A Galilean Amora of the third generation, student of R. Joḥanan, Rebbi Assi, and Rebbi Zeïra. He is not identical with the Babylonian Samuel, whose full name also was Samuel bar Abba.</i> asked: Since the House of Shammai said that they learned the rules of the vineyard of the fourth year only from the Second Tithe; since you say that there is no Second Tithe in the Sabbatical year, there is no fourth year after planting in the Sabbatical year. Similarly, in the third and sixth years of the sabbatical cycle, since there is no Second Tithe<sup class="footnote-marker">124</sup><i class="footnote">The second tithe is not called Second Tithe but Tithe of the Poor; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Peah 1:1:30" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Peah.1.1.30">Chapter 1, Note 127</a>.</i>, there should not be any fourth year after planting. Rebbi Yose<sup class="footnote-marker">125</sup><i class="footnote">The organizing principle for this and the following group of paragraphs is that Rebbi Yose solves the problems.</i> said, even though there is no Second Tithe in the third and sixth years, there is the tithe for the poor. In the Sabbatical year, there are no tithes at all.
Ḥeipha<sup class="footnote-marker">127</sup><i class="footnote">He seems to be identical with the Babylonian Fourth generation Amora ‘Aipha ben Raḥaba from Pumbedita. He and his brother Abime were known as “the sharp-minded ones from Pumbedita.”</i> asked: Since Rebbi Jehudah said that they learned the rules of the vineyard of the fourth year only from the Second Tithe; since you say that there is no Second Tithe in Syria, there is no fourth year after planting in Syria<sup class="footnote-marker">128</sup><i class="footnote">Even for Jews, following R. Jehudah in the Mishnah and against R. Jehudah in the Tosephta. This is accepted as genuine interpretation of R. Jehudah’s position.</i>. Similarly, they learned the rules of the heave of the thanksgiving sacrifice<sup class="footnote-marker">129</sup><i class="footnote">The rules of the thanksgiving sacrifice (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 7:11-15" href="/Leviticus.7.11-15"><i>Lev.</i> 7:11–15</a>) note only that four kinds of bread have to be brought and that one loaf each has to be given as heave to the officiating priest. The number of required loaves is not specified. The first heave has no fixed amount from the Torah; in the words of Samuel, one grain is enough for an entire silo. But since the number 1 is spelled out here and it is called heave, the natural inference is that the heave is the heave of the tithe given to the Levite, of which the Levite has to give 10% to the priest (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 18:26" href="/Numbers.18.26"><i>Num.</i> 18:26</a>). Hence, 1 is 10% of the required amount, and from each kind of bread 10 loaves have to be brought to the Temple (<i>Sifra Ẓav</i> 7:1).</i> only from the heave of the tithe; since you say that there was no heave of the tithe<sup class="footnote-marker">130</sup><i class="footnote">Since there were no tithes during the 40 years in the desert; tithes are only imposed in the Land of Israel.</i> in the desert, will it follow that there was no heave of the thanksgiving sacrifice in the desert? Rebbi Yose said, they learned from it only in regard of quantities.
Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Jehudah<sup class="footnote-marker">131</sup><i class="footnote">A son of R. Jehudah (bar Illaï) and companion of Rebbi.</i> stated: Rebbi Eleazar ben Rebbi Simeon said, Israel did become obligated for the fourth year after planting only after 14 years, seven during which they conquered and seven during which they divided up the land<sup class="footnote-marker">132</sup><i class="footnote">The computation which proves that the time from the death of Moses till the end of the land distribution under Joshua and the assembly at Shiloh was 14 years is given in <i>Seder Olam</i> (Chap. 11, in the author’s edition pp. 116–118.)</i>. Rav Ḥisda said, it turns out that the argument of Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Jehudah is identical with that of his father Rebbi Jehudah. Just as Rebbi Jehudah said that they learned the rules of the vineyard of the fourth year only from the Second Tithe, since you say that the Second Tithe started only after 14 years, similarly there was no fourth year after planting until after 14 years. Rebbi Yose said, he follows his son’s argument; Syria was inferred from “after 14 years<sup class="footnote-marker">133</sup><i class="footnote">If they were not obligated while they were in the Land before its distribution, then certainly they are not obligated in Syria which was conquered only by a king, not by the community of tribes, held only temporarily, and never was distributed among the tribes.</i>;” “after 14 years” was not inferred from Syria.
It is written (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 19:25" href="/Leviticus.19.25"><i>Lev.</i> 19:25</a>): “In the fifth year, you shall eat its yield<sup class="footnote-marker">134</sup><i class="footnote">This verse following the one which declares the yield of the fourth year of a new vineyard “holy for praises” reads: “But in the fifth year, you shall eat its fruit, to increase its yield to you.” Hence, the harvest is directly given to the owner of the vineyard. Also, “increase” presupposes prior yield for the owner.</i>.” Rebbi Yose the Galilean says, here one adds the fruits of the fifth to the fruits of the fourth year. Just as the fruits of the fifth year are for the proprietors, so the fruits of the fourth year are for the proprietors. Rebbi Zeïra, Rebbi Yasa, in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: It turns out that Rebbi Yose the Galilean argues like Rebbi Jehudah. Just as Rebbi Jehudah makes it his property<sup class="footnote-marker">135</sup><i class="footnote">This may refer to the earlier statement of R. Jehudah that the status of the yield of the fourth year is derived from the laws of Second Tithe and, as private property, is subject to the rules of single berries and gleanings. It also may refer to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 2:7:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.2.7.1">Mishnah <i>Qiddušin</i> 2:8</a> where R. Jehudah states that Second Tithe when wilfully and illegally diverted to profane use, such as bridal money to acquire a wife, becomes valid private property and the marriage is contracted, but if the same act was done in error, without intent to change the status of the Tithe, the Second Tithe remains holy, does not become profane, and the marriage is not contracted.</i>, so Rebbi Yose the Galilean makes it his property.
Rebbi Jeremiah asked before Rebbi Zeïra: According to those who declare it his property, should it not be subject to tithes? He said to him, according to what Rebbi Joshua ben Levi said<sup class="footnote-marker">136</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Yevamot 7:3:2-5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Yevamot.7.3.2-5"><i>Yebamot</i> 7:2</a>. This refers to the laws indicated in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 22:10-14" href="/Leviticus.22.10-14"><i>Lev.</i> 22:10–14</a>. A non-priestly woman married to a priest may eat <i>terumah.</i> If she is divorced, or she becomes a widow without issue, she returns to her former non-priestly status and may not eat <i>terumah.</i> The Mishnah states that the servants of a non-priestly woman who was married to a priest and became a widow while pregnant with her first child, should not eat <i>terumah.</i> The first Mishnah had explained that there are two kinds of property a bride brings to her husband. For “property of dowry” נכסי מלוג the ownership resides with the wife. “Iron cattle” צאן ברזל becomes the husband’s property completely in exchange for an obligation to return full value in case of dissolution of the marriage. In the Halakhah, the Mishnah is interpreted to mean that <i>her servants</i> are “property of dowry” servants, but “iron cattle” servants are <i>his</i> and entitled to eat <i>terumah</i> since only their value has to be returned. She herself may not eat <i>terumah</i> unless and until she gives birth to a male child. On this, R. Abin says in the name of R. Joshua ben Levi that one has to follow what people do, and they do not let <i>any</i> slaves brought by the wife eat in such a situation.</i>, as Rebbi Abin said in the name of Rebbi Joshua ben Levi, not only this practice, but in any practical question which is weak in court and you do not know how to decide, go out and see how the public acts, and act accordingly<sup class="footnote-marker">137</sup><i class="footnote">In <a class="refLink" data-ref="Berakhot 45a" href="/Berakhot.45a">Babli <i>Berakhot</i> 45a</a>, this is ascribed to Abbaie, an acquaintance of R. Abin.</i>. And we see that they do not give<sup class="footnote-marker">138</sup><i class="footnote">R. Zeïra seems to indicate that practice follows the House of Shammai in the interpretation of R. Jehudah. However, practice must follow the House of Hillel who consider the Second Tithe as Heaven’s money.</i>. Rebbi Mana said, that is, if the practice would follow the House of Shammai. But is there any public that acts according to the House of Shammai? Rebbi Abin said, they learned the rules of the vineyard of the fourth year only from the Second Tithe; just as you say that the Second Tithe is not subject to tithes, so the yield of the fourth year is not subject to tithes<sup class="footnote-marker">139</sup><i class="footnote">According to the House of Hillel.</i>.
Rebbi Abba, Rebbi Ḥiyya, in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan<sup class="footnote-marker">140</sup><i class="footnote">In <a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 37b" href="/Pesachim.37b">Babli <i>Pesaḥim</i> 37b</a>, this is stated by R. Assi, another student of R. Joḥanan.</i>: A dough of Second Tithe in Jerusalem, following Rebbi Meïr,<sup class="footnote-marker">141</sup><i class="footnote">Who in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 2:7:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.2.7.1">Mishnah <i>Qiddušin</i> 2:8</a> disagrees with R. Jehudah and states that one never may use the Second Tithe for bridal money since it belongs to Heaven rather than to the owner. The law of <i>ḥallah</i>(<a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 15:20" href="/Numbers.15.20"><i>Num.</i> 15:20</a>) states that it must be given to the priest as heave from <i>your dough</i>, and for R. Meïr it is not <i>yours.</i></i> is free from <i>ḥallah</i>, following Rebbi Jehudah<sup class="footnote-marker">142</sup><i class="footnote">In the Babli, R. Jehudah’s opinion is classified as that of the Sages, i. e. the operative practice, in accordance with the opinion of the Yerushalmi.</i> it is subject to <i>ḥallah.</i> Rebbi Jonah said, they said this only for Jerusalem, but not for the countryside<sup class="footnote-marker">143</sup><i class="footnote">Outside of Jerusalem, produce from Second Tithe may be eaten only if redeemed. Then the produce becomes profane and the sanctity is transferred to the money. Hence, even R. Meir must agree that the dough must acquire profane status and, therefore, is subject to <i>ḥallah.</i></i>.
Rebbi Abba bar Cohen asked before Rebbi Yose: He who declares it<sup class="footnote-marker">144</sup><i class="footnote">This must refer to the Second Tithe, not the fruit of the fourth year of a tree, since <i>ḥallah</i> is only due from dough made from grain.</i> obligated for single berries, does he also declare it obligated for <i>ḥallah</i>? He said to him, is that not Rebbi Jehudah? And it is our opinion that in all this practice, Rebbi Jehudah follows the House of Shammai<sup class="footnote-marker">145</sup><i class="footnote">Who declare both the Second Tithe and the growth of the fourth year as private property, subject to all its laws.</i>.

Halakhah 6

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> A vineyard that produces only gleanings, Rebbi Eliezer says, they are for the proprietor, Rebbi Aqiba says, for the poor. Rebbi Eliezer said (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 24:21" href="/Deuteronomy.24.21"><i>Deut.</i> 24:21</a>): “When you gather your grapes … do not take the gleanings after it.” If there is no vintage, where are the gleanings? Rebbi Aqiba told him (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 19:10" href="/Leviticus.19.10"><i>Lev.</i> 19:10</a>): “Do not take gleanings from your vineyard,” even if it is all gleanings. If that is so, why does it say “when you gather your grapes … do not take the gleanings after it;” the poor have no right to the gleanings<sup class="footnote-marker">146</sup><i class="footnote">Nor to enter the vineyard before the time of the harvest. The grapes might still grow to sit one row on top of the other so that at harvest time they are no longer gleanings.</i> before the vintage.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> Similarly, if there is no harvest there are no single berries<sup class="footnote-marker">147</sup><i class="footnote">Following R. Eliezer. Since in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 19:10" href="/Leviticus.19.10"><i>Lev.</i> 19:10</a>, gleaning and single berries are mentioned in one verse, they must follow the same rules.</i>, until there should be vintage<sup class="footnote-marker">148</sup><i class="footnote">When the proprietor gathers, the poor can have their grape gathering from the gleanings. However, if animals ate all grape bunches before the harvest, R. Eliezer declares the gleanings to belong to the proprietor.</i> along vintage, not if a wild animal ate it.
How much is a vintage? In the school of Shila<sup class="footnote-marker">149</sup><i class="footnote">An Amora of the first generation in Nahardea who already had his school when Rav returned from Galilee. In the Babli, he usually is given the title Rav, which however is an anachronistic usage by later generations.</i> they said, three bunches that make a quarter<sup class="footnote-marker">150</sup><i class="footnote">A quarter of a <i>log</i>, about 3.8 fl. oz. or 133 cm<sup>3</sup>; cf. <i>Berakhot</i> p. 296. [Note 164]</i> of wine.
Rebbi Aqiba certainly explains both verses. How does Rebbi Eliezer explain “do not take gleanings from your vineyard”? So you should not say that, since the poor have no rights before harvest time, the proprietor acquired rights to them<sup class="footnote-marker">151</sup><i class="footnote">That the owner of the vineyard could come and take all the unripe gleanings for himself before harvest time.</i>, it is necessary to say “do not take gleanings from your vineyard<sup class="footnote-marker">152</sup><i class="footnote">Anytime before, during, and after the harvest.</i>.”

Halakhah 7

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If someone dedicates<sup class="footnote-marker">153</sup><i class="footnote">Donates it to the Temple. Temple property is exempt from the laws of tithes and gifts to the poor and it is sinful to use any Temple property without first redeeming it by paying for it.</i> his vineyard before gleanings are recognizable, the gleanings do not belong to the poor<sup class="footnote-marker">154</sup><i class="footnote">Since nobody can give away what he does not possess, the owner of the vineyard cannot give away the property of the poor. As long as the gleanings are not recognizable, the poor have no rights to them.</i>. But after the gleanings are recognizable, the gleanings belong to the poor. Rebbi Yose said, they should give the value of their growth to the Temple<sup class="footnote-marker">155</sup><i class="footnote">To avoid using Temple property unlawfully, the poor who harvest the grapes have to give to the Temple the difference in value of the grapes now and at the time of dedication. (One cannot explain the statement to mean that they should pay part of the cost of tending the vineyard since the vineyard in the Sabbatical year may not be worked on.)</i>. What are forgotten grapes? On a trellis, anything he cannot stretch out his hands and take<sup class="footnote-marker">156</sup><i class="footnote">Since on a trellis the branches are stretched out widely, once he has moved he may not return. But single vines standing separately are units by themselves and they become forgotten only if the vintner moves to the next vine.</i>. On a single growing vine, once he is done with it.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> There<sup class="footnote-marker">158</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Pesachim 4:8:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Pesachim.4.8.1">Mishnah <i>Pesaḥim</i> 4:8</a> (Yerushalmi 4:9), <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Pesachim (Lieberman) 3:19" href="/Tosefta_Pesachim_(Lieberman).3.19">Tosephta <i>Pesaḥim</i>3:19</a>. Taking the fruits from dedicated sycamores is one of the three things the people of Jericho did and against which the Sages protested. In the Babli, the discussion is <i>Pesaḥim</i> 56 a/b. The entire Halakhah also appears in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Pesachim 4:9:2-9" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Pesachim.4.9.2-9"><i>Pesaḥim</i> 4:9</a>.</i> we have stated: “They permitted the use of sycamore figs<sup class="footnote-marker">159</sup><i class="footnote">גֻּמיז in Arabic. The Gaonic interpretation is “growth that comes out of the tree trunk.” (Pliny, <i>Naturalis</i> <i>Historia</i>, Book XIII, §56, already notes that the fruits of the “Egyptian fig” sit on the stem, not on branches.) The fruits are of such inferior quality that they do not qualify as objects of trade. (Cf. <i>Tosefta Kifšutah</i> I, p. 361.)</i> from Temple trees. The<sup class="footnote-marker">160</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Pesachim (Lieberman) 3:22" href="/Tosefta_Pesachim_(Lieberman).3.22">Tosephta <i>Pesaḥim</i> 3:22</a>.</i> Sages told them: Do you not agree with us that fruits from Temple property are forbidden? They said to them, when our forefathers dedicated them, they dedicated only the tree stems because of strong men who came and took them by force<sup class="footnote-marker">161</sup><i class="footnote">Sycamores produce inferior fruits but superior building material. The “strong men” are probably the Hasmonean rulers. The people protected their sycamore groves by putting them out of bounds of any human government.</i>.” Do the rabbis mean to say that they dedicated tree stems and their fruits? Even if you say that they dedicated only the stems but not the fruits, the rabbis wonder whether if somebody dedicates an orchard, he may reserve the growth for himself<sup class="footnote-marker">162</sup><i class="footnote">In Mishnah <i>Meïlah</i> 3:6, the anonymous Tanna declares that taking the fruits of a Temple tree does not constitute the crime of <i>meïlah</i> (larceny committed on Temple property.) In contrast, R. Yose declares the fruits to be covered by <i>meïlah</i>, therefore he requires in our Mishnah the poor to pay for the expenditures of tending the vines during the growing season. As the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 56b" href="/Pesachim.56b">Babli (<i>Pesaḥim</i> 56b</a>) points out, the Sages of the Tosephta, while agreeing that no felony is committed taking the sycamore figs, nevertheless must assume that taking them means overstepping a prohibition. However, this prohibition is not written in the Torah. While any condition that goes against a commandment of the Torah is invalid (Halakhah 6:9), a condition that goes against a rabbinic prohibition may be valid. It is unresolved whether the ancestors of the people of Jericho had the right to reserve for themselves the use of the sycamore figs.</i>. Let us hear from the following: “After the gleanings are recognizable, they belong to the poor.” That is different because nobody may dedicate anything that is not his own. Does that not mean that even if the gleanings were not yet recognizable, they should belong to the poor<sup class="footnote-marker">163</sup><i class="footnote">If the people of Jericho could not reserve the fruits, then it is clear that future growth is implicit in today’s tree, and the future gleanings should belong to the poor and not to the Temple.</i>? That is different, because it is a vineyard for the Temple, as it was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">164</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Peah (Lieberman) 3:15" href="/Tosefta_Peah_(Lieberman).3.15">Tosephta <i>Peah</i> 3:15</a>.</i>: “If somebody plants a vineyard for the Temple<sup class="footnote-marker">165</sup><i class="footnote">I. e., if he dedicates a newly planted vineyard to the Temple.</i> it is exempt from <i>orlah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">166</sup><i class="footnote">The prohibition to use the fruit of a newly planted tree during the first three years refers to the individual Jewish owner; it does not apply to the Temple which is common property.</i>, from the fourth year, and from gleanings, but it is subject to the Sabbatical year<sup class="footnote-marker">167</sup><i class="footnote">In which everybody is free to take its fruits.</i>.”
Rebbi Zeïra<sup class="footnote-marker">168</sup><i class="footnote">This paragraph explains the action of the Sabbatical year on Temple property. The text follows the parallel in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Pesachim 4:9:2-9" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Pesachim.4.9.2-9"><i>Pesaḥim</i> 4:9</a> (fol. 31b).</i> in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 25:2" href="/Leviticus.25.2"><i>Lev</i>. 25:2</a>): “The land shall observe a Sabbath for the Eternal.” The sanctity of the Sabbatical year falls<sup class="footnote-marker">169</sup><i class="footnote">Hence, the status of ownership has no influence on the duties of the Sabbatical year.</i> on anything that is the Eternal’s. Rebbi Ḥiyya bar Abba asked before Rebbi Mana: It is impossible<sup class="footnote-marker">170</sup><i class="footnote">In Mishnaic Hebrew, אפשר means “it is possible,” but איפשר is a contraction of אֵי אפשר and means “it is impossible.”</i> to eat it<sup class="footnote-marker">171</sup><i class="footnote">Any produce from Temple property collected in the Sabbatical year.</i> without redemption since Temple property cannot exit<sup class="footnote-marker">172</sup><i class="footnote">Its sacred status without the sanctity being transmitted to the money that goes into the Temple treasury.</i> without redemption. If one redeems and eats it, it would be as if one bought an axe with money from the Sabbatical<sup class="footnote-marker">173</sup><i class="footnote">Since it is written (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 25:6" href="/Leviticus.25.6"><i>Lev</i>. 25:6</a>): “The rest of the Land should be for you to eat,” we infer that the spontaneous growth is there to be eaten, not to be traded. It is possible to trade produce of the Sabbatical year as long as the final use is for food.
